Chapter 01

The wind off the Caspian came in under the window seal before dawn, carrying salt, oil, and the faint mineral sourness that seemed to live in the stone of Baku itself. Thomas stood in the half-light of the rented flat, one hand resting against the cold glass, and watched the avenue begin to move.

Neftchilar Avenue ran below him in a silver line of headlights. Taxis idled at the kerb. A street cleaner pushed a barrow along the promenade, his shoulders lifted against the morning wind. Beyond him, the sea broke in short, dirty folds against the concrete. It was not the blue water of postcards. It was grey, worked over, carrying the sheen of fuel and winter light.

Out on the horizon, the offshore rigs held their positions.

That was what Thomas noticed first in any city. Not the monuments. Not the restaurants listed in the company pack, or the views people photographed from hotel terraces. He looked for the points that held. Roads, cranes, harbour lights, the passage of supply vessels, the schedule behind the visible world. Cities introduced themselves through their systems if one knew where to look.

His flat had been chosen by procurement. Two bedrooms, serviced weekly, walking distance to the regional office, secure parking, sea view. The furniture had the blunt neutrality of corporate accommodation: pale sofa, glass table, heavy curtains that smelled faintly of dust when drawn. On the kitchen counter sat a bowl of fruit he had not ordered and would not eat. Three apples. Two oranges. A banana already bruising at the stem.

He opened the fridge. Bottled water, yoghurt, cheese wrapped in waxed paper, a carton of eggs. Someone had stocked it from a list.

Efficient enough.

He closed the fridge and checked his watch. Six fifteen. The driver was due at seven.

Thomas had been in transit for almost nineteen hours: Heathrow to Istanbul, Istanbul to Baku, the final descent over a darkened coast where sodium lights marked the curve of the bay. At passport control, the officer had examined his work visa for longer than seemed necessary, then stamped the page without looking at him again. The driver from the company had been waiting beyond the barrier with a board that read WARD, T. He had taken Thomas's bag and led him into air that smelled of rain on hot electrical wiring.

The city had appeared in pieces through the car window. Glass towers. Dark blocks of Soviet flats. A lit petrol station where three men smoked beside a pump. The Flame Towers shifting colour above the hill, bright and theatrical against the older streets below. Thomas had watched without forming an opinion. Judgement was unnecessary on arrival. The first job was intake.

He made coffee from the machine on the counter. The capsule dropped into place with a small plastic crack. The cup filled too quickly, the liquid thin and bitter. He drank it standing by the sink and reviewed the schedule on his tablet.

Local office induction. Port access briefing. Meeting with operations manager. Review of drilling equipment manifests. Inspection schedule for the offshore installation. Contractor compliance pack.

The list settled him.

At seven, the driver called from downstairs.

The lobby was marble and brass, warmed by lights set too high into the ceiling. A young guard in a black suit stood behind the reception desk, watching the street through the glass doors. He nodded when Thomas crossed the floor. Outside, the wind pressed immediately against Thomas's shirt collar.

The driver opened the rear door.

'Good morning, Mr Ward.'

'Morning.'

The car smelled of leather polish and stale cigarettes hidden beneath something lemon-scented. Thomas sat back and watched the city lift itself out of night. Baku did not wake softly. Metal shutters rattled upwards. Buses exhaled at junctions. Men in dark jackets crossed between lanes with one hand raised, not asking permission so much as announcing their passage.

The office was on the sixth floor of a glass building that looked across the harbour. It had been fitted to an international standard: access cards, white desks, meeting rooms named after oilfields, framed safety posters in English and Azerbaijani. In the reception area, a television played muted footage of a platform supply vessel moving through rough water. The subtitles ran too fast to read.

Rauf Mammadov met Thomas by the lift.

He was shorter than Thomas had expected, compact in the shoulders, with a close beard and a suit that sat perfectly on him. He shook hands with both palms, not warmly, not coldly. A measured grip. His eyes moved quickly, taking in Thomas's shoes, watch, travel-creased jacket, the laptop bag held in his left hand.

'You found the flat?'

'Yes.'

'Good. The view is better than the plumbing, but procurement likes it.'

Thomas looked at him.

Rauf smiled as if to show the remark had not required a reply. 'Come. I will show you the office before everyone arrives and begins lying about what has already been done.'

He led Thomas through the open-plan area. Desks stood in long rows, each with a monitor, keyboard, company phone, and a small plastic tray for documents that no one had yet learned to stop printing. The air conditioning hummed with a dry, uneven sound. Behind the glass wall, the meeting rooms were empty and bright.

'Engineering there,' Rauf said, pointing with two fingers. 'Procurement at the back. Logistics opposite. Government liaison uses the small room because they shout on the telephone.'

'Who handles permit tracking?'

Rauf made a sound through his nose. 'In theory, logistics. In practice, whoever is least busy when the Ministry asks where the missing form is.'

They stopped outside Thomas's office. It had a desk facing the window, two visitor chairs, a low cabinet, and a whiteboard already carrying the ghost of previous marker lines. Someone had erased in haste. Faint grids remained.

Thomas set his bag down and opened the blind. The harbour lay below, the water torn white around the small workboats.

'You will need help,' Rauf said.

'With what?'

'Local filings. Customs stamps. Driver slips. Translation when the yard men decide English is beneath them.'

'I can handle documentation.'

'You can handle your documentation. That is different.'

Rauf leaned against the doorframe. He did not enter fully. 'We have been using a temp from procurement. She has resigned. Or her cousin has died. I am not sure which story we are on today. It does not matter. We need someone here, in the chair, every day.'

'Hire someone.'

'I have interviews this afternoon.'

'Then why are you telling me?'

'Because you will reject all of them if I do not tell you first that competence here is not always where you expect it.'

Thomas took out his notebook. The paper was squared, cream-coloured, the corners unbent. He wrote Permit support, then underlined it once.

Rauf watched the movement. 'You write everything down.'

'Most things.'

'Good. Then write this. A woman who can get a Ministry stamp before lunch is worth attention. A woman who can make yard engineers lower their voices is worth more. If Excel is the weakness, teach her.'

'Excel is not optional.'

'In Baku, many things that are not optional become optional before ten in the morning.'

From somewhere beyond the glass, a printer began its first mechanical cough of the day. One sheet came out, then another. Rauf looked towards it as if it had offended him personally.

'The interviews are at two,' he said. 'Use Meeting Room Three. It has the least broken chair.'

By eight, the office had filled. Men arrived in dark coats, shaking rain from their shoulders. A woman in a red scarf unlocked a cabinet and took out a stack of folders bound with string. Someone argued over the coffee machine. The language around Thomas shifted between Azerbaijani, Russian, and English, sometimes within a single exchange. He listened for function rather than meaning. Request. Refusal. Delay. Defence.

At nine thirty, a procurement clerk brought him the current equipment manifests.

The first file was wrong by the second page.

Thomas placed a ruler beneath the printed rows and began marking discrepancies in pencil. A casing order had been logged in kilograms in one sheet and tonnes in another. A pressure valve shipment had been duplicated under two supplier codes. Customs clearance dates were entered in European format on half the forms and American format on the rest, turning March into a month of impossible approvals.

By eleven, his pencil had gone blunt.

He sharpened it over the waste bin and looked out at the water. A supply vessel moved slowly between harbour walls. On deck, orange containers were lashed in two neat rows. Men in hard hats stood near the stern, small figures braced against wind.

Systems failed in small places first. A column heading. A unit conversion. A date typed by someone who wanted the task gone. Failure rarely announced itself. It waited inside ordinary documents until pressure arrived.

At one forty-five, Rauf put his head through the door.

'Meeting Room Three.'

Thomas gathered the interview forms. 'How many?'

'Five. One is late already, which is efficient. Saves time.'

The first candidate had a folder of certificates and too much perfume. She spoke polished English and referred to herself in the third person when describing achievements. Thomas wrote down Composed, overprepared, no field sense.

The second had worked for a shipping agent near the port. He knew customs procedures and had a habit of answering before the question finished. He asked about salary three times in fifteen minutes.

The third candidate did not bring identification.

'It is in the taxi,' she said.

Thomas looked at Rauf.

Rauf did not look back.

The fourth arrived at two fifty-six.

She paused just inside the doorway as if checking whether the room permitted her. She wore a dark blue dress with frayed cuffs and a black coat too warm for the overheated building. Her shoes were polished, though the leather had cracked across the toes. Her hair was pulled back so tightly that it sharpened the bones of her face.

'Olesya Sokolova,' Rauf said, reading from the page in front of him.

The surname landed in the room and stayed there.

Thomas saw Rauf notice it. A small pause, no more than the width of a breath. The old Russian ending, carried into a city that had spent years turning itself towards other names, other loyalties, other futures. Olesya sat before either man could invite her.

Her hands folded in her lap. Not loosely. The right thumb pressed into the left knuckle until the skin whitened.

'You have administrative experience?' Thomas asked.

'Yes.'

Her English was careful, each word set down separately.

'Where?'

'A freight forwarding office. Then a drilling support contractor. Temporary work.'

'Why did you leave?'

She looked at Rauf. Then at Thomas.

'The contract ended.'

Rauf made a note. The paper scratched beneath his pen.

'The role requires permit tracking, equipment manifests, filing, translation, and local liaison,' Thomas said. 'There are moving parts.'

'I understand moving parts.'

It was the first thing she said without the caution of a rehearsed answer.

Thomas looked up from the form. 'Tell me about the drilling support contractor.'

'They rented tools to yards. I prepared delivery notes. Sometimes invoices.'

'Did you manage spreadsheets?'

'Yes.'

'What level?'

A small movement passed across her face. It was not quite a flinch. More the body drawing a curtain.

'I entered the data.'

'Formulas?'

'Some.'

'Pivot tables?'

She stared at him.

Rauf coughed into his hand.

Thomas crossed out the line on his form. 'That is not essential on day one.'

Olesya's thumb eased away from the knuckle.

'Russian and Azerbaijani?' Rauf asked.

'Russian. Azerbaijani. English. Some Turkish if spoken slowly.'

'Drivers here do not speak slowly.'

'Then I ask them to repeat.'

Rauf smiled. 'And if they refuse?'

'I ask the dispatcher who wants to be paid.'

The room shifted a little. Rauf leaned back. Thomas wrote: practical under pressure.

He placed a sample manifest in front of her. Ten rows, deliberately imperfect. Wrong unit in column D. Two missing supplier codes. A delivery date that fell on a public holiday.

'Review this,' he said. 'Tell me what concerns you.'

Olesya took the paper. She did not begin with the numbers. Her eyes moved over the page edges first, then the stamped header, then the signatures at the bottom.

'This signature is not the same as this one,' she said, touching the lower corner.

Thomas had not altered the signature.

Rauf leaned forward. 'Why do you say that?'

'The first is written by someone sitting. The second is written standing up, perhaps on a car bonnet. See here. The pressure changes.'

Thomas looked. She was right.

'Anything else?' he asked.

She tapped the date. 'No deliveries on this day. Holiday. The yard gates will be closed unless someone pays security to open them.'

'And the tonnage?'

She looked at the column. Too long. 'It is heavy equipment.'

'The unit is wrong.'

Her face closed again.

'Kilograms,' Thomas said. 'The manifest requires tonnes.'

'Yes,' she said.

She had not seen it.

Rauf's pen stopped moving.

Thomas took the paper back. 'Thank you.'

Olesya stood, gathering the strap of her handbag. The bag was old, black, carefully repaired near the clasp with dark thread. Her chair made no sound when she pushed it in. She placed it exactly beneath the table.

At the door, she turned.

'The security guard at the yard will open the gate for tea,' she said.

'Tea?'

'Not money. Money makes him nervous. Tea makes him important.'

She left the room.

The door closed without catching.

Rauf looked at the manifest, then at Thomas. 'She missed the unit.'

'She saw the signature.'

'We need Excel.'

'We need fewer forged delivery notes.'

'So?'

Thomas put Olesya's form on the top of the pile.

Rauf made the same sound through his nose as before. 'You will teach her Excel.'

'I will correct what needs correcting.'

'That is not the same thing.'

'No.'

They interviewed the fifth candidate, who had excellent software skills and no Russian. He kept checking his phone beneath the table. Thomas thanked him and did not write anything after he left.

Olesya started the following Monday.

She arrived at seven forty-eight with two plastic folders, a small packet of wet wipes, and a pot plant wrapped in newspaper. The office cleaner had not yet finished the corridor. Lights flickered awake above the desks. Thomas was in his office reviewing the weekend drilling report when he saw her pass the glass.

She did not knock. She went first to her desk.

The desk had belonged to the temp whose cousin had died or not died. There were coffee rings on the laminate, adhesive marks where labels had been pulled away, and a drawer that did not close fully. Olesya stood over it for several seconds. Then she took off her coat, folded it over the back of the chair, and began cleaning.

Thomas watched without meaning to.

She wiped the desk from left to right, lifting the keyboard rather than pushing it aside. She cleaned the monitor frame, the telephone receiver, the inside of the drawer. From one folder she removed a pen, a pencil, a ruler, a small notebook, and a packet of paper clips still in their cellophane. Each object took its place. The paper clips went in the top right corner. The notebook aligned with the keyboard. The pen lay parallel to the desk edge.

The plant came last.

It was a small fern in a chipped ceramic pot. She unwrapped the newspaper, folded the paper twice, and put it into the bottom drawer. The fern went near the window, close enough for light, far enough from the radiator.

At eight precisely, she walked to Thomas's door with a cup of black coffee.

'Good morning,' she said.

He looked at the cup. 'Thank you.'

'No sugar.'

'No.'

'Rauf said.'

She placed it on the coaster beside his keyboard. There had not been a coaster there before.

For the first week, she was early every day.

For the first week, something in the system bent out of shape behind her.

On Tuesday, a customs form went into the folder for the vessel rather than the supplier. Thomas found it after lunch, wedged between two delivery notes, its corner folded down with unusual care. The filing was wrong. The fold was exact.

On Wednesday, she entered casing lengths as individual units and doubled the order volume. On Thursday, she translated a yard foreman's complaint accurately but left out the insult that explained why procurement had stopped answering his calls.

On Friday, she sent Thomas a permit register with the dates aligned, the fonts corrected, the cells colour-coded in pale blue and green. Three formula errors sat beneath the prettiness of the sheet.

Thomas opened the file at seven fifteen that evening.

Most of the office had gone. The air conditioning had been turned down after complaints from the engineers, and the glass held the last heat of the day. Outside, the city lights had begun to show along the promenade, each one trembling in the dark water.

He corrected the first formula. Then the second.

When he found the third, he sat back.

The spreadsheet was beautifully arranged. That was the problem. It gave the appearance of control before the control existed. The borders were consistent. The headings clear. The colours restrained. If he had skimmed it, as Rauf might have skimmed it, the error would have travelled into the weekly report and sat there until some later failure exposed it.

Thomas stood and walked to Olesya's desk.

The physical files were perfect. She had relabelled the drawers in English and Russian. Delivery notes sat in clear sleeves, ordered by yard and date. A small handwritten list was taped to the inside of the top drawer: stamps, signatures, driver copies, Ministry receipts. No flourish. No decoration. A working list.

Beside the keyboard, the fern's soil was dark from recent watering.

He returned to his office and fixed the spreadsheet.

On Monday morning, he called her in.

She stood rather than sat.

'The permit register had three calculation errors,' he said.

'I checked it.'

'You checked the presentation.'

Her mouth tightened.

He turned the monitor towards her. 'Here. Column F is multiplying the unit cost by the wrong quantity. It should reference column D, not C.'

She leaned closer. Her perfume was almost absent, something clean beneath paper dust and cold air.

'I copied the formula from the row above.'

'The row above was also wrong.'

She looked at the screen for a long time. 'I will redo it.'

'I have corrected it.'

'Then I will check the next one.'

'You need to understand why it failed.'

She nodded, but her eyes had moved to the floor.

Thomas opened a blank sheet. 'Sit.'

She sat.

He showed her the cell references, first slowly, then again. She watched his hand on the mouse rather than the formula bar. When he asked her to repeat the process, she clicked the wrong cell and immediately withdrew her hand from the mouse as if it had become hot.

'Again,' he said.

She did it again. Wrong.

He waited.

The office outside his door had grown louder. Someone laughed near the printer. A phone rang unanswered across the room.

Olesya placed both hands flat on her knees. The knuckles had gone pale. 'Rauf says I am useless.'

Thomas looked at the spreadsheet. The active cell blinked.

'Rauf says many things.'

'He thinks I do not hear.'

'He knows you hear.'

She turned her head towards him then. The line of her jaw held, but only just.

Thomas moved the mouse back to the correct cell. 'The issue is not intelligence. It is sequence. You are copying from a broken row. Start from the source data.'

'And if the source is wrong?'

'Then nothing after it matters.'

She considered that.

The sentence had entered her differently than he intended. He could see it in the way her hands loosened, then folded again.

'Show me once more,' she said.

He did.

At noon, she watered the fern. At twelve ten, she sent him the corrected register. One formula was still wrong. He fixed it without calling her back.

That became the shape of the days.

Olesya arrived before the office had gathered its noise. She cleaned the surface of her desk, placed coffee beside his keyboard, checked the physical files, spoke to drivers, called clerks, waited in corridors where Thomas would not have known which door to knock on. She came back with stamps. She came back with signatures. She came back with explanations written on scraps of paper and names spoken too quickly for him to catch.

Then the files came to him.

He repaired the numbers.

Neither of them named the exchange.

There were other things he began to notice because systems trained the eye to notice irregularities. She wore the same coat every morning, though the lining had torn near the cuff. She ate at her desk but never before one o'clock, and never anything that required heating. Bread, cheese, cucumber wrapped in foil. She kept tea bags in a tin but used each one twice. When the office ordered lunch from the cafe downstairs, she declined before anyone had finished asking.

On the Wednesday of the second week, three engineers from the yard came in before lunch and filled the open-plan office with wet coats and cigarette smoke. They spoke over Olesya's head at first, then around her, their Russian hard and fast, their Azerbaijani quicker still when Rauf appeared from the corridor. One of them placed a folder on her desk without looking at her.

She opened it, read the first page, and closed it again.

'This is not for us,' she said.

The man closest to her laughed. He had a wide face, badly shaved, with a gold ring on his little finger. He said something Thomas did not catch. The other two looked towards the window.

Olesya stood. She did not raise her voice. She picked up the folder and walked to Thomas's office.

'The pressure test certificate is copied from another site,' she said.

Thomas took it from her. The format looked correct. The company stamp sat where it should sit. The supervisor's signature was legible.

'How do you know?'

'The mud on the photograph.'

He looked again. A small image had been stapled to the back page, showing a section of pipe on packed ground. Nothing about it had caught his attention.

'That yard has red aggregate,' she said. 'This is grey.'

Thomas went to the door. The three engineers had stopped talking. Rauf was beside the printer with a cup of tea in one hand, watching.

'This certificate is not for the Sumgait test,' Thomas said.

The man with the ring spread his hands. 'Same pipe specification.'

'Not the same test.'

'The pipe passed.'

'Not on this certificate.'

The man's eyes moved past Thomas to Olesya, who had returned to her desk and was aligning the edge of a paper stack against the surface. He said something under his breath.

Thomas waited. The office waited with him.

Rauf put down his tea.

'Bring the correct certificate by four,' Thomas said. 'Or the payment release waits until Monday.'

The three men left with their wet coats unbuttoned and the wrong folder under the ringed man's arm.

When the lift doors closed, the office resumed too quickly. Phones were lifted. Keyboards began again. Rauf came to Thomas's doorway.

'You understand what he called her?'

'No.'

'Good.'

Thomas looked across the room. Olesya had taken a cloth from her drawer and was wiping the place on her desk where the folder had been.

'Will they bring it?'

'Yes,' Rauf said. 'They like being paid.'

At four twenty, a courier arrived with the correct certificate. Olesya checked the photograph first, then the stamp, then the signature. Only after that did she enter the file into the register. She sent the digital copy to Thomas at four thirty-one. The subject line read: Sumgait pressure test, corrected.

The formula in the tracking sheet was wrong.

Thomas fixed it before he approved the release.

On the second Thursday, the air conditioning failed.

By mid-afternoon, the office had become a sealed box of heat, printer toner, and stale coffee. Men loosened collars. Someone propped open the stairwell door and let in the smell of cigarette smoke from below. The glass wall of Thomas's office held the sun until the room seemed lit from inside the window itself.

Olesya came in with a stack of permit forms against her chest.

'The Ministry wants the environmental attachment with the stamp on every page.'

'It is already stamped on the first page.'

'They want every page.'

'Why?'

'Because today they want every page.'

Thomas took the forms. A drop of sweat ran from his temple to the hinge of his jaw. He wiped it with the heel of his hand.

Olesya stood by the door, not leaving.

'What else?'

'You are from England.'

'Yes.'

'It is cold there.'

'Sometimes.'

She looked through the glass towards the harbour. The rigs were faint in the haze, reduced to dark strokes against a white sky.

'You travel always?'

'For work.'

'That is not the same answer.'

Thomas placed the forms in a neat pile. 'It is the only answer that matters.'

She accepted this with a small tilt of the head. Not agreement. Storage.

'In Russia,' she said, then stopped.

He waited.

'My father travelled for work. Before he came home, my mother changed the flat around him. Moved a chair. Cleaned windows. Put out plates we were not allowed to use.'

She looked down at the forms in his hand.

'And when he left?'

'The cup stayed by his side of the bed.'

Outside, someone struck the side of the printer. The machine resumed with a shudder.

Olesya reached for the forms, but Thomas held them.

'I will finalise these tonight.'

'I can do them.'

'I know.'

She looked at him then. The heat had brought colour to her face. A strand of hair had loosened near her ear and stuck to her skin. She did not thank him. She did not argue.

'The stamp must be blue,' she said.

'Not black?'

'Blue means original. Black means copy. Even when it is original.'

'Of course.'

For the first time, he saw the edge of a smile. It appeared and vanished before it could be offered.

She left the forms on his desk.

The office emptied slowly that evening. Rauf stopped at Thomas's door with his jacket over one arm.

'You are staying?'

'The environmental attachment needs rebuilding.'

'Olesya gave it to you?'

'Yes.'

Rauf looked across the open-plan office. Olesya was at her desk, shutting down her computer. She wiped the keyboard before covering it with a thin plastic sheet she had brought from somewhere.

'She is not good with data,' Rauf said.

'No.'

'You chose her.'

'Yes.'

'Why?'

Thomas looked at the stack of papers on his desk, then at the row of physical folders she had reorganised along the cabinet. Before she arrived, he had lost ten minutes twice a day finding the right document. Now every file came to hand before he knew he needed it.

'She sees different failures.'

Rauf followed his gaze. 'That sounds expensive.'

'Not if I know which failures are mine.'

Rauf laughed once. 'Careful. That is how Baku gets you. First you think you understand the problem. Then the problem invites you to dinner and asks for a favour.'

Thomas capped his pen. 'Good night, Rauf.'

'Good night, Mr Systems.'

Rauf left.

At six thirty, Olesya came to Thomas's door with her handbag over her shoulder.

'I am going.'

'Good night.'

She hesitated. 'The driver slips from Sumgait are in the green folder. The foreman signed only two copies. I put a note.'

'I saw it.'

'And the courier for the Ministry comes at nine. He will ask for me.'

'I will tell him.'

'No. He does not like speaking English.'

'Then I will point.'

This time the smile almost stayed.

'Good night, Thomas.'

She used his first name as if testing whether it would be refused.

He looked at the permit forms. 'Good night, Olesya.'

The door closed behind her.

The office settled. Without the movement of people, the building revealed its smaller noises: the tremor of air through vents, the lift cables somewhere in the shaft, the faint tick of the cooling glass. Thomas worked through the attachment page by page. He corrected the margins. He rebuilt the table. He checked the stamp placement against the sample Olesya had left clipped to the folder. The blue ink had bled slightly into the paper fibres.

At eight, the cleaner came through with a vacuum cleaner and did not enter his office.

At nine, the harbour lights became the only visible order outside.

He printed the corrected document and laid the pages across the desk to dry. The stack looked clean, almost inevitable, as if it had always existed in that form. No one at the Ministry would know the first version had been unusable. No one offshore would know a permit had nearly stalled in a room above the harbour because a stamp needed to be blue on every page.

The paper cooled under his hand.

By morning, it would look untouched.

Thomas gathered Olesya's original pages and put them in the shredder tray. Then he stopped.

Her handwriting ran along the top corner of the first sheet, small and upright.

Blue stamp every page. He will reject black.

No explanation. No apology.

He placed the note in his drawer instead.

On his way out, he paused beside her desk. The fern had turned slightly towards the window, each frond holding the last of the office light. Her ruler lay exactly parallel to the keyboard. The chair was tucked in. Beneath the monitor, on a square of folded tissue, she had left a piece of wrapped chocolate.

Thomas looked towards the dark glass of his office, where the corrected permit forms waited in their clean stack.

He picked up the chocolate and put it in his coat pocket.

Downstairs, the night guard unlocked the front door for him. The street smelled of diesel, rain, and the sea. Across the road, the Caspian moved in the dark without showing its depth.

Thomas walked back towards the flat with the document bag under his arm.

In the morning, the courier would come for Olesya.

Chapter 02

The courier came at nine carrying a plastic folder under one arm and a cigarette behind his ear.

He stopped beside Olesya's desk before looking anywhere else. That was the first thing Thomas noticed from his office. The man did not ask reception. He did not go to Rauf. He crossed the open-plan floor with the confidence of someone who knew which person in the room could make a document move.

Olesya stood before he reached her.

'Blue stamps,' she said.

The courier lifted the folder. 'All pages.'

'Show me.'

He made a sound of complaint, but opened the folder. Olesya turned the first page, then the second. Her finger paused on each stamp. She did not hurry because he wanted her to hurry. The office moved around them: phones, chairs, the click of keyboards, Rauf's voice from the corridor, the low cough of the printer coming to life again. Olesya stood in the middle of it and counted blue circles one by one.

When she reached the final page, she closed the folder and placed it on her desk.

'Wait.'

'I have other collections.'

'Then wait quickly.'

The courier laughed, but he stayed.

Thomas looked down at his screen. The permit register was open. Column H still held the wrong formula from the day before. He had corrected the live copy after Olesya left, but the version on her machine would still carry the error. If she updated the permit number from the courier's document and sent it to Rauf, the weekly report would pull through a false completion date.

He stood.

By the time he reached her desk, Olesya had taken out a receipt book. She wrote slowly, pressing hard enough for the carbon sheet to catch beneath. The courier leaned over her shoulder.

'Do not stand so close,' she said.

He stepped back.

Thomas placed one hand on the edge of the desk. 'Send me the register before you update Rauf.'

Olesya glanced up. 'I checked it.'

'Send it anyway.'

Her mouth tightened. Not refusal. Record.

She tore the receipt free and handed it to the courier. He tucked it into the folder, touched two fingers to his forehead in a half-salute, and left with his cigarette still behind his ear.

Olesya opened the register. Thomas waited beside her while the file loaded, the blue wheel turning at the centre of the screen. Her desk smelled faintly of lemon wipes. The fern had already been watered. A bead clung to the lip of the chipped pot.

'Column H,' he said.

'It is the completion date.'

'It is reading from the wrong cell.'

She looked at the formula bar. Her hand hovered over the mouse, then withdrew.

'I thought you corrected it.'

'On my version.'

'Then there are two versions.'

'Yes.'

'That is stupid.'

'It is common.'

She looked at him then, almost sharply. 'Common is not better.'

Thomas did not answer. He took the mouse and repaired the cell. Then he copied the formula down the column, checked the row references, and saved the file under the shared drive, where both machines would open the same version.

'Use this one now.'

'The other one?'

'Delete it.'

She moved the old file to the recycle bin with a decisiveness that pleased him.

At ten, Rauf came out of his office with the corrected permit pack in one hand.

'It passed.'

Olesya looked up from her desk. 'All pages?'

'All pages.' Rauf held the folder towards Thomas. 'The Ministry clerk asked who prepared it.'

'Why?'

'He said it looked like someone had finally learned.'

Olesya lowered her eyes to the keyboard.

Thomas took the folder. The blue stamps had dried darker at their edges. His rebuilt table sat exactly inside the margins. Olesya's handwritten note had done its work without being seen.

'Good,' he said.

Rauf watched him for a moment. 'There is another problem.'

'Of course there is.'

'Drill bits from Aktau. Customs hold. The yard is shouting because they need them by Friday.'

Olesya had already opened the logistics mailbox.

Rauf saw this and smiled without softness. 'Excellent. Everyone is awake.'

The shipment had been entered under the wrong tariff category. The vendor had used a general machinery code, the customs broker had copied it, and the local system had flagged the consignment as requiring secondary inspection. The drill bits were sitting in a bonded warehouse near the port, close enough for the yard men to point at and too far for anyone to use.

Olesya printed the documents and brought them to Thomas.

'The broker says we need the Ministry of Taxation to confirm the classification.'

'The broker should have checked it before the shipment arrived.'

'He says the vendor made the mistake.'

'He would.'

She placed the papers on his desk. The top sheet had already been marked with small pencil ticks beside the invoice number, supplier address, and shipping weight. She had missed the tariff code. She had seen the missing signature on the packing declaration.

Thomas pointed to the code. 'This is the hold.'

'I thought it was the signature.'

'The signature will delay release. The code prevents it.'

'Both matter.'

'Yes.'

The word hung between them longer than it needed to.

Rauf appeared in the doorway. 'Can we release the shipment?'

'Not yet.'

'That was not my preferred answer.'

'The vendor used the wrong tariff code. The broker copied it. Customs caught it. We need a correction letter from the vendor and a local classification note.'

Rauf looked at Olesya. 'Call the broker.'

'I called him.'

'And?'

'He says he sent an email.'

'Of course he did.'

'To the wrong address,' she added.

Rauf closed his eyes for one second. 'Find him.'

Olesya took the papers and returned to her desk.

Thomas watched her dial. Her Russian changed when she spoke to men who expected apology. It became lower, flatter, each sentence shorter than the last. She did not argue at first. She let the broker explain the wrong thing twice. Then she interrupted him.

The office quietened around her in patches.

'No,' she said. 'You sent it to procurement Azerbaijan. We are Baku regional support. Different mailbox. Different floor. Different people. Send it now.'

The broker spoke long enough for Thomas to hear the irritation in his voice through the receiver.

'No,' Olesya said again. 'Now.'

She ended the call and wrote something in her notebook.

Rauf looked across the room at Thomas. 'That is useful.'

The email arrived six minutes later.

It was addressed to the wrong person, attached to the wrong thread, and missing the correction letter.

Thomas stood behind Olesya's chair while she opened the attachments. There was a faint line of sweat along her hairline. The office air had not recovered from the previous day's heat. Outside, a low grey sky pressed down over the harbour, flattening the light.

'He forgot the letter,' she said.

'He did not forget.'

'No.'

'Call him again.'

She did.

By noon, the correction letter had arrived from the vendor in poor English and worse formatting. Thomas rewrote it. Olesya called the broker again to confirm the required stamp. Rauf carried the first version to the printer, read three lines, and brought it back.

'This says the items are non-critical.'

'They are critical,' Thomas said.

'That is why I brought it back.'

Thomas deleted the sentence and rebuilt the paragraph.

At twelve forty, the document was ready.

Olesya stood beside the printer, waiting for the pages. Her lunch sat unopened on her desk: bread wrapped in foil, a small cucumber, a boiled egg in a paper napkin. She had placed them neatly beside the fern, as if the food belonged to the desk more than to her.

'Eat,' Thomas said.

'After.'

'After what?'

'After the courier.'

'The courier can wait.'

She looked at him, not quite understanding the sentence.

The printer gave a soft chime. She took the pages and checked them against her list. Tariff code. Vendor stamp. Signature. Broker reference. Ministry address. She slid the set into a clear sleeve and pressed the edges flat with the side of her hand.

Thomas watched the motion.

There were people who moved paper as if it were an obstacle. Olesya moved it as if it could be made obedient.

The courier arrived at one ten. Not the same man. This one wore a leather jacket and smelled strongly of aftershave. Olesya made him sign the collection book before he took the sleeve.

'It must go to the Ministry first,' she said. 'Then customs.'

'I know.'

'Say it back.'

He laughed.

She did not.

'Ministry first,' he said. 'Then customs.'

Only then did she release the document.

At two thirty, the courier called from the Ministry. The clerk required a secondary stamp from the vendor's local representative. The representative was out of the office until Monday.

Rauf swore in Azerbaijani from inside his office.

The sound carried through the glass.

Olesya stood by Thomas's desk, holding the phone away from her ear.

'He says Monday.'

'Friday is the latest release date.'

'Yes.'

'Where is the representative?'

'At a funeral.'

'Is he?'

She listened again. 'No.'

'Where?'

'Lunch.'

Thomas held out his hand. She gave him the phone.

The man on the other end spoke quickly, lazily, with restaurant noise behind him. Thomas let him finish. He did not raise his voice. He asked for the representative's full name, the office address, the company registration number, and the name of the person to whom the delay should be attributed in the weekly vendor performance report.

The restaurant noise changed.

The man came back to the office.

At four fifteen, the secondary stamp was applied.

At four forty, customs released the drill bits.

At five, the yard confirmed receipt for delivery the following morning.

Rauf walked into Thomas's office with both hands in his pockets.

'You see?' he said.

'See what?'

'Baku.'

Thomas saved the release confirmation. 'This was ordinary incompetence.'

'Yes. That is Baku.'

Olesya appeared in the doorway. She held the final delivery note.

'The warehouse sent this.'

Thomas took it. The shipment weight was correct. The tariff code had been amended. The delivery address matched the yard. One line still listed the wrong internal cost centre.

He looked at Olesya.

She saw it a beat later.

'I will fix it.'

'No,' Thomas said. 'I will.'

Rauf raised his eyebrows.

'It affects the weekly cost report,' Thomas said.

'Then you will fix it,' Rauf said.

Olesya remained in the doorway after Rauf left. The delivery note trembled slightly in her hand. Not much. Enough.

'I missed it.'

'You caught the broker.'

'I missed this.'

'Yes.'

She looked down at the page. 'You fix the things after me.'

Thomas opened the cost report on his screen. 'I fix the things before they become expensive.'

'That is not the same.'

'No.'

He changed the cost centre. The cell accepted the correction and the total shifted by a small amount at the bottom of the sheet.

Olesya watched the number settle.

'Rauf will know?'

'No.'

'Why not?'

'Because the report will be correct.'

She folded the delivery note once, then unfolded it. A crease remained through the centre of the page.

'Thank you,' she said.

Thomas did not look away from the screen. 'For what?'

'For not making a public lesson.'

The phrase was too formal. It sounded translated from another room, another life.

'Public lessons waste time,' he said.

That was true enough to stand between them.

At six, most of the office began to close. Men pulled jackets over the backs of chairs. Someone wrapped leftover pastries in a napkin and carried them towards the lift. The harbour darkened early beneath the low clouds. Rain moved across the glass in fine diagonal lines.

Olesya was still at her desk.

Thomas could see only part of her through the open door: one shoulder, the side of her face, the pale oval of her hand moving between keyboard and notebook. Her lunch remained unopened. The cucumber had rolled against the fern pot.

He finished the cost report and sent it to Rauf.

When he looked again, Olesya had put on her coat.

She stood too quickly.

The movement was small, but not clean. One hand went to the edge of the desk. Her eyes closed for half a second, then opened.

Thomas came out of his office.

'Are you unwell?'

'No.'

'Sit down.'

'I am late.'

'For what?'

She began to gather her folders.

'Olesya.'

The use of her name stopped her more effectively than the instruction had.

She looked at the clock on the wall. Six twelve.

'The clinic closes at seven.'

'Then go.'

'I have to take the metro.'

'The metro is three blocks.'

'From here, yes.'

She placed the folders into her bag, then took them out again, noticing she had put them in the wrong order.

'Which clinic?'

'It does not matter.'

'It does if it closes at seven.'

She pressed the folders flat against the desk. 'Near Narimanov.'

Thomas knew the district only from the map on his tablet. Far enough in evening traffic. Too far on foot from the metro if the rain held.

'Why did you not go at lunch?'

'The shipment.'

'The shipment could have waited.'

She gave him a look that almost became disbelief. 'No. It could not.'

Outside, Rauf's office door opened. He came out with his phone in one hand and his coat over his arm. He saw Olesya standing with her bag, Thomas beside her desk, the untouched food still wrapped near the fern.

'Problem?'

'No,' Olesya said.

'Clinic,' Thomas said.

Rauf looked at her. Something passed through his face and was gone before it could be named.

'Go,' he said. 'The office will not collapse because you leave before the cleaners.'

'I finished the register.'

'Then vanish.'

She nodded once. Not gratitude. Compliance.

Downstairs, the lobby smelled of wet wool and floor polish. The guard watched a football match on his phone with the sound turned low. Outside, the rain had thickened, turning the streetlights soft around their edges.

Thomas had intended to call the company driver.

Olesya was already walking towards the metro.

'Wait.'

She stopped under the awning.

'I will get you a taxi.'

'No.'

'You will miss the clinic.'

'I have money for the metro.'

'That is not the same.'

The sentence landed badly. He could see it at once. Her chin lifted. Something in her had been handled too often by other people.

'I can take the metro.'

'I know.'

'Then why?'

He looked at the road. Cars moved slowly through the rain, tyres hissing over the wet surface. A taxi came past with its roof light on, then another without stopping.

'Because the clinic closes at seven.'

She held his gaze long enough to reject the first answer and accept the second.

Thomas stepped to the kerb and raised his hand. The third taxi slowed. He opened the rear door and gave the driver the district name. The driver repeated it, then named a fare too high by half.

Olesya answered in Azerbaijani before Thomas could speak. The driver shrugged. She answered again, shorter. He lowered the fare.

Thomas looked at her.

'He thinks you do not know,' she said.

'He is often right.'

The almost-smile returned. Briefly.

She got into the taxi. Thomas took out his wallet and pulled free two fifty-manat notes. Olesya saw them before he could hand them to the driver.

'No.'

'For the taxi.'

'No.'

The driver watched them in the mirror.

Thomas placed one note on the passenger seat beside the driver. 'To Narimanov. Wait there. Bring her back if she asks.'

Olesya's hand tightened around the strap of her handbag.

'Thomas.'

He closed the door before she could return the money.

The taxi pulled away.

Thomas stood under the awning until its brake lights disappeared into the traffic. Rain blew in beneath the glass canopy and darkened the front of his shirt.

Rauf came out behind him.

'You will learn,' he said.

Thomas did not turn. 'What?'

'In this city, help is never only help.'

'It was a taxi.'

'Yes.'

Rauf opened his umbrella. 'That is how it starts.'

He walked away towards the car park, the umbrella tilting against the wind.

Thomas stayed a moment longer, then went back upstairs.

The office was nearly empty. A cleaner had begun moving between the desks with a black rubbish bag. Olesya's desk remained as she had left it: keyboard covered, notebook squared to the edge, lunch still folded in foil. The fern leaned into the window. Beside it lay the clinic receipt she had forgotten to put away.

Thomas did not mean to read it.

The paper was face up.

Consultation. Medication. Laboratory fee. The total sat at the bottom in a boxed line, larger than he expected. Not catastrophic. Not nothing. Beneath it, in the same careful hand he had seen on the permit note, Olesya had written three numbers in a column and crossed out the first two.

He stood with the receipt between two fingers.

The cleaner's vacuum started at the far end of the room.

Thomas put the receipt back exactly where it had been.

Then he took his own notebook from his office and wrote the amount on the back page.

He waited until eight thirty before his phone rang.

'I am back home,' Olesya said.

There was traffic behind her. Or rain against glass. The line carried the soft interference of distance.

'Did the clinic close?'

'No.'

'Good.'

'The taxi waited.'

'Good.'

She said nothing for a while. Thomas looked through his office window at her empty desk.

'I will pay you tomorrow,' she said.

'For what?'

'The taxi.'

'No.'

'Thomas.'

'No.'

Another pause. Not silence exactly. He could hear her breathing, then something small being moved on a table.

'Then I will bring coffee.'

'You already bring coffee.'

'Better coffee.'

He looked at the machine near reception, its chrome surface smeared from a day of hands.

'That would be useful.'

'Good night.'

'Good night.'

The call ended.

Thomas remained in the office until the cleaner switched off the lights in the open-plan area. Only his room and the corridor stayed lit. He finished the cost report, sent a note to the yard, checked the customs release again, then opened the drawer where he had placed Olesya's blue-stamp note.

The wrapped chocolate from the previous evening lay beside it.

He had not eaten it.

Sarah called at nine fifteen.

Her face filled the laptop screen from a hotel room in London, the background all white bedding, polished wood, and the edge of a framed print hung too high on the wall. She had a pen in her mouth and was fastening an earring with one hand.

'You look tired,' she said.

'Long day.'

'Same here. Frankfurt moved the call twice, then pretended the delay was mine.'

She took the pen from her mouth and looked away from the camera. Somewhere beyond the frame, papers shifted. Thomas saw a neat stack of documents beside a glass of water, a blazer hanging on the wardrobe door, black shoes placed side by side under the desk. Sarah's rooms always became efficient within an hour of her arrival. Not homely. Operational.

'The consulting contract?' he asked.

'Likely. They want me in Geneva next week if the board signs off.'

'You should go.'

'I intend to.'

She smiled then, quick and bright, already past the decision. Sarah moved through the world as if doors existed to confirm her direction. Airports, contracts, trains, border checks, conference rooms. They did not resist her. If one route failed, she found another before the failure had time to settle.

'How is Baku?'

Thomas looked through the glass at the empty desk outside his office. The fern was a darker shape against the window.

'Complicated.'

'That means badly managed.'

'Mostly.'

'Good. You like badly managed. It gives you something to fix.'

He watched her turn a page on the desk beyond the screen. The earring caught the hotel light.

'And the people?'

'Capable in uneven ways.'

'That sounds diplomatic.'

'It is accurate.'

Sarah laughed under her breath. 'You and accuracy. I miss that, oddly.'

'Only oddly?'

'Do not make me sentimental before a board call.'

He smiled because she expected him to. She looked well. More than well. Focused, sharpened by movement, animated by the prospect of another city and another room where she would not have to ask anyone for permission to enter. Thomas admired that in her. He always had.

'The flat behaving?' she asked.

'Well enough.'

'And you?'

'Well enough.'

She studied him for a second. Not long. Sarah did not linger where no useful answer would be produced.

'Sleep, Thomas.'

'After I finish this.'

'You always say that.'

'It is usually true.'

'Good night, then.'

'Good night.'

The screen went dark.

Thomas sat in the reflection of his own face for a moment before closing the laptop. Outside the office, Olesya's desk remained ordered around its small plant. The tissue square beneath the chocolate was still there, empty now, a pale mark below the monitor.

Two rooms. Two women who never met inside them.

He turned off the desk lamp and left.

The next morning, Olesya arrived at eight exactly.

Her hair was still damp from the rain. She wore the same coat. The tear near the cuff had been repaired with thread a shade darker than the fabric. She placed a paper cup on Thomas's desk, this one from the cafe downstairs instead of the machine.

'Better coffee,' she said.

'Thank you.'

She turned to leave, then stopped.

'The taxi was too much.'

'It got you to the clinic.'

'Still.'

Thomas opened his drawer. The receipt was not there. The note was. The chocolate. His notebook. He took out two fifty-manat notes and placed them beneath a paperweight before she could step fully away.

Olesya looked at the money.

'For what?'

'Taxis.'

'How many taxis?'

'Future ones.'

She did not touch the notes.

The office noise gathered slowly behind her. A phone rang. Someone laughed near the kitchen. Rauf's door clicked shut.

'I am not your expense,' she said.

Thomas moved the paperweight half an inch, aligning it with the edge of the desk. 'No.'

'Then what is this?'

'A buffer.'

The word belonged to his world. It came out cleanly.

Olesya looked at the notes, then at the paperweight holding them flat. The word had changed their shape.

'For when the system fails,' he said.

She stood very still.

Then she picked up the notes, folded them once, and placed them inside the back cover of her notebook.

No thanks.

No promise to repay.

At noon, the fern was watered.

At five, the permit register arrived in his inbox. The formulas were still wrong in two places. The physical file reference was perfect.

Thomas corrected the numbers and saved the shared version.

The week arranged itself around that exchange.

On Monday, a driver arrived with four delivery slips and only three signatures. Olesya sent him back to the yard before Thomas had finished reading the first page. The man returned at noon with the fourth signature, angry enough to slap the paper down on her desk. She took a ruler from the drawer and moved the sheet towards herself until it aligned with the edge.

'Now it can be filed,' she said.

The driver looked at Thomas's office, then at Rauf's, then left without speaking.

The file she sent afterwards had the correct slips scanned in the correct order. The spreadsheet attached to it placed the delivery against the wrong vessel.

Thomas corrected the vessel code.

On Tuesday, Olesya secured a customs appointment no one else had managed to obtain. She did it by calling someone named Leyla twice, then a third time after lunch, her voice patient in a way Thomas would not have been patient. By three, the appointment was confirmed for Thursday morning.

The calendar invitation she created was set for the wrong time zone.

Thomas changed it before Rauf accepted.

On Wednesday, she brought coffee and a packet of small pastries from the cafe downstairs. She placed one on Rauf's desk, one beside the printer for the clerk who shouted at couriers, and one on Thomas's coaster. The office softened around the sugar. Even the procurement clerk who disliked everyone took one and said nothing cruel for almost an hour.

The purchase order she prepared that afternoon was missing the tax exemption line.

Thomas inserted it before sending the file.

Thomas kept the corrected files in the shared drive and the marked copies in his drawer. The room ran more smoothly that way. Olesya made it habitable. Thomas made it accurate.

Each believed the other was doing the lesser magic.

On Thursday morning, rain came hard against the harbour windows. Olesya arrived with the hem of her dress darkened and her hair damp at the temples. She took off her coat and hung it carefully over the back of her chair. The repaired cuff had opened again. A strip of lining showed through.

Thomas saw it when she reached across his desk to place the coffee down.

'Your coat is torn.'

She looked at the cuff as if it belonged to someone else. 'It catches on the bus seat.'

'You should have it repaired.'

'I did.'

The answer closed the subject.

At eleven, she went with Rauf to the customs appointment. Thomas watched them leave through the glass wall: Rauf walking quickly, phone already to his ear, Olesya beside him with the document sleeve held close against her ribs so the rain would not reach the paper. She had put a plastic bag over the sleeve and tucked the open end beneath her palm.

They came back after two. Rauf was wet to the shoulders and furious. Olesya was quieter than when she had left.

'Appointment was real,' Rauf said, dropping into the visitor chair in Thomas's office. 'Clerk was not.'

'Meaning?'

'Meaning Leyla exists. Leyla's supervisor has decided he requires a registration certificate that expired last year because he likes old paper.'

Olesya placed the sleeve on Thomas's desk. It was dry.

'We have the old certificate,' she said.

'Expired,' Rauf said.

'Still stamped.'

Thomas opened the folder. The old certificate was there, copied on thin paper, the stamp faint but legible. Olesya had anticipated the obstruction without knowing its exact form.

'This will work,' Thomas said.

Rauf looked at Olesya. 'Why did you bring it?'

'Because he asked for it once before.'

'When?'

'Not him. Another man.'

'Same office?'

'Same smell.'

Rauf stared at her, then laughed in a single burst. 'Same smell. Fine. I accept this classification.'

They resubmitted the pack by courier. The clearance came through at four fifty-two.

At five ten, Thomas found three arithmetic errors in the cost allocation sheet.

He stayed until seven correcting them.

The first note he left in her drawer was not large.

He chose the amount by making it small enough to deny and large enough to solve something. Two twenty-manat notes and one ten. He placed them beneath the spare staples in the top drawer, where she would find them only if she opened it fully. Then he closed the drawer and stood for a moment with his hand still on the metal handle.

The office was empty except for the cleaner in the corridor.

He told himself it was not payment. Payment would have required a ledger. It was not charity. Charity announced itself. This was a practical measure against lateness, rain, clinics, taxis, torn coat cuffs, and the thousand small frictions that made useful people unreliable.

A buffer.

The word held.

The next morning, the money was gone.

Nothing else had moved.

The staples sat where they had been. The notebook lay squared to the keyboard. The fern had been turned a little towards the glass.

Olesya brought coffee at eight.

'Good morning,' she said.

'Morning.'

No change in her voice. No blush of gratitude. No offence. She placed the cup on the coaster and returned to her desk.

Thomas opened the shared drive and found the permit register waiting for review.

Two weeks later, she came in wearing a warmer coat.

It was second-hand, he could tell that much. Dark wool, carefully brushed, a little too large across the shoulders. The buttons were mismatched and sewn on firmly. She hung it over the back of her chair and smoothed the sleeves before sitting down.

Rauf noticed at once.

'Promotion?'

Olesya looked at him.

'The coat,' he said.

'It was cold.'

'Good reason.'

Rauf went into his office.

Thomas did not look at the drawer.

He did not have to.

The pattern became almost invisible after that. A small amount, never on the same day twice. Sometimes beneath the staples. Sometimes inside the back of the spare receipt book. Once under the fern pot, where the damp ring marked the top note and forced him not to do that again.

The money disappeared.

Coffee appeared.

Files arrived ordered and wrong in ways he could repair.

No one mentioned any of it.

When he passed Olesya's desk on his way out, the notebook was open beside her keyboard. The back cover lay flat. Empty.

The money had moved somewhere he could not see.

The next morning, she arrived at seven fifty-eight.

The coffee was waiting before he sat down.

Chapter 03

Winter came down over the Caspian without drama.

It did not arrive as snow, not at first, only as a hardening of the air and a change in the sound of the harbour. Metal carried further. Chains struck against moorings in the dark. At dawn, the wind pressed itself against the office glass until the panes gave a low, almost animal complaint. The water beyond the promenade turned the colour of old lead.

Thomas noticed the change in the documents before he noticed it in his body.

Drivers were late. Yard signatures arrived smudged by rain. The port authority began attaching weather advisories to routine emails, each one written as if the sea could be persuaded by formatting. Offshore transfers were delayed by hours, then by half-days. Men who had shouted through summer now came into the office with their collars high and their hands red from the cold.

Olesya began arriving with her hair pinned under a wool hat.

The hat was grey and too small. It flattened the front of her hair and left a seam across her forehead when she took it off. She removed it at her desk each morning, smoothed the wool once with both hands, and placed it in the top drawer beside the receipt book. Then she cleaned the surface of the desk, turned the fern slightly towards the paling window, and brought coffee to Thomas at eight.

The paper cup from the cafe had become regular.

Not every day. Regularity too exact would have made the gesture visible. Monday, then Wednesday. Sometimes Friday. On other mornings she brought coffee from the machine near reception, black and thin, placed precisely on the coaster. The difference did not need naming. The cafe coffee came with a cardboard sleeve and a darker smell. The machine coffee came too hot and tasted faintly of metal.

Thomas did not ask which one she had brought.

He knew by the sound of the cup touching the coaster.

The first time he noticed this, he was reviewing a lifting schedule for the offshore crane. He heard the softer set-down, the faint drag of cardboard against cork, and said, without looking up, 'Cafe.'

Olesya stopped in the doorway.

'Yes.'

He looked up then.

She was watching the cup as if it had betrayed her.

'Thank you,' he said.

'The machine is bad today.'

'Only today?'

'Today especially.'

She left before he could answer.

After that, he found himself listening for small things.

Three steps from her desk to his door if she carried only paper. Four if she carried coffee, because she slowed before crossing the threshold. The drawer on her desk stuck on the left side and made a small wooden click when pushed fully shut. She typed quickly when copying words, slowly when entering numbers. She drank water at one thirty and again at four, never before lunch. If Rauf raised his voice in Azerbaijani, she reached for the nearest file and straightened it without looking down.

None of it mattered.

All of it entered the system.

At the end of the first week of cold weather, Thomas stayed late to reconcile the casing manifests against the offshore receipt logs. The office emptied in the usual stages: procurement first, because procurement believed any problem after five was a problem for tomorrow; engineering next, with arguments carried half-finished into the lift; Rauf last among the managers, stopping in Thomas's doorway with his coat over one arm.

'You are doing the monthly reconciliation?'

'Yes.'

'That is an ugly thing.'

'It is only ugly if the logs are wrong.'

Rauf laughed once. 'Then it is ugly.'

He looked across the open-plan room. Olesya remained at her desk, her coat still on the back of the chair, the grey hat folded beside her keyboard.

'She is still here?'

'Serial numbers.'

'You gave her serial numbers?'

'She asked for something she could finish.'

Rauf looked at Thomas for a moment, then at Olesya again. 'Be careful giving people finishable things. They begin to expect endings.'

'It is data entry.'

'Of course.'

Rauf left.

The building settled around the remaining light. The cleaners had not yet reached the sixth floor. Somewhere in the ceiling, the air system clicked and resumed. Thomas worked through the manifest line by line, pencil against ruler, the old habit steadying the eye. Outside, the harbour lights blurred in the rain.

At seven twenty, he heard Olesya's chair scrape.

Not the final scrape of someone leaving. A frustrated sound, too sharp in the emptied office.

He waited.

The chair moved again.

Thomas saved his file and stood.

Olesya was sitting with both hands in her lap, staring at the screen. A spreadsheet filled the monitor. Row after row of serial numbers, weights, supplier references, container codes. On the desk beside her lay a stack of delivery slips. The top one had been crossed through twice.

'What happened?'

'It jumps.'

'What jumps?'

'The numbers. I enter one, and the next row changes.'

Thomas leaned over the keyboard. The sheet had an auto-fill rule active in the wrong column. Each new entry was being treated as part of a sequence and altered as she moved down the file. Half the sheet had been corrupted by a function she had not known was there.

'Stop typing.'

'I have stopped.'

Her hands remained on her knees.

He pulled up a chair and sat beside her. The wheels caught against the seam in the carpet. He took the mouse, opened the version history, and restored the last clean save from twenty minutes earlier.

The spreadsheet jumped back.

Olesya let out air through her nose, very quietly.

'It is not gone?'

'No.'

'I thought I broke it.'

'You triggered a rule.'

'Same thing.'

'No.'

He showed her the small square at the corner of the selected cell.

'This fills the series. Useful if you want one, two, three. Bad if the number is a unique identifier.'

'Why is it there?'

'Because someone thought it was helpful.'

'Someone who does not use numbers.'

'Someone who uses different numbers.'

She looked at him. 'That is generous.'

'No. It is accurate.'

He opened an OCR tool, scanned the first slip, and extracted the serial number into the sheet. The process took less than a minute. He did the second one, then the third. Olesya watched without moving.

'You can do all of them like that?'

'Most of them.'

'I typed for two hours.'

'You did not know the tool existed.'

'Still.'

He handed her the mouse. 'You do this one.'

She did not take it at once.

'It will not break?'

'It may. Then we restore it again.'

She looked at the screen, then at the mouse. Her fingers closed around it carefully, as if the object required negotiation. She selected the next scanned slip, clicked the OCR icon, and waited while the text appeared. The first result misread an eight as a three.

'It is wrong.'

'So correct the eight.'

She clicked into the cell and corrected it.

'Now the next.'

They worked like that through twenty slips. Thomas did not touch the mouse unless she asked. The office darkened around them, the overhead lights too bright above the two desks, the rest of the room falling away into glass and reflection.

At the twenty-first slip, she made the correction without looking at him.

At the twenty-fifth, she saw the misread digit before the software finished highlighting it.

'There,' she said.

'Yes.'

The word was small. It warmed the space more than it should have.

At eight fifteen, the cleaner appeared at the end of the row and paused when she saw them still working.

'Sorry,' Olesya said.

The cleaner lifted one shoulder and moved to the far side of the office.

Thomas checked the completed sheet. Two errors remained, both in the weight column where the slips had used commas instead of decimal points. He fixed one and showed Olesya the other.

'You see it?'

She leaned closer. The sleeve of her cardigan brushed the edge of his jacket.

'Comma.'

'Yes.'

'It should be a point.'

'For this sheet.'

'Not for normal people.'

'For this sheet,' he said again.

She corrected it.

When the file saved, she sat back and looked at the screen as if waiting for it to betray her.

It did not.

'I am very slow,' she said.

'You are using tools you were never taught.'

'Rauf says the result is what matters.'

'Rauf is correct.'

She turned towards him.

'Then I am useless until someone fixes the result.'

The cleaner's vacuum began behind them, a low mechanical drag across the carpet. Thomas watched the cursor blink at the end of the final row.

'You saw the forged certificate last week.'

'That is different.'

'Yes.'

'This office does not pay me to see mud.'

'It should.'

She looked down at her hands. The nails were short, unpainted, one cuticle torn at the edge of her thumb. She pressed it with the opposite nail until the skin whitened.

'You fix my files,' she said.

Thomas closed the spreadsheet. 'I correct shared outputs.'

'No.'

The word came softly. It did not invite argument.

He looked at her.

'You fix my files.'

Outside, the wind pushed rain against the glass in a hard, slanting rush. For a moment the harbour disappeared behind it.

'The work gets done,' he said.

'Why?'

He could have answered with any of the useful words. Efficiency. Accuracy. Risk. Delay. Cost. Each one available, each one true inside its own small room.

'Because it is easier to correct a file than explain why it failed.'

Olesya watched him for longer than he expected.

'That is not the real answer.'

'It is one of them.'

The vacuum stopped. The sudden quiet left the office too exposed.

Olesya gathered the delivery slips and tapped them into a neat stack. She placed them in a clear sleeve, wrote the file reference on a label, and pressed the label flat with her thumb.

'I will do the next batch tomorrow.'

'Use OCR from the start.'

'Yes.'

'Check the commas.'

'Yes.'

She stood. Her chair rolled back and touched his knee before stopping. Neither of them moved for a second. Then Thomas shifted the chair aside.

'Good night,' she said.

'Good night.'

She put on her coat and grey hat. At the lift, she turned back once towards the row of desks, the lit office, the fern at her station, the folder now completed.

Then she left.

Thomas returned to his own office.

The casing reconciliation waited on his screen. He completed it after nine, sent it to Rauf, and shut down the computer. Before leaving, he passed Olesya's desk. The paper clips were aligned. The fern soil was dark. Beside the keyboard lay a small chocolate wrapped in gold foil.

He looked towards the lift.

The doors were closed.

He placed the chocolate in his top drawer beside the first one.

The next morning, the coffee was from the machine.

Olesya said nothing about the chocolate. Thomas said nothing about the spreadsheet. The morning took them back into its ordinary order.

That became the shape of the next weeks.

He taught her one tool at a time. OCR. Data validation. Freeze panes. Filters that could be cleared before they damaged a report. She learned the motion of each task slowly, with the same care she gave to sealing folders. She wrote instructions in a notebook as small commands:

Check source.

Lock header.

Clear filter before save.

Comma is not always comma.

At first, Thomas found the last note imprecise. Then he left it.

She never became good with the spreadsheets. The formulas remained suspect. A copied row could still carry the wrong hidden reference. A cell formatted as text could sit in a column of numbers and pass beneath her eye because it looked orderly. She began to know where danger might be. She stopped trusting clean surfaces.

That mattered.

Near the end of January, the old archive cabinet jammed.

It was a tall grey unit near procurement, dented along one side, with drawers that opened only if lifted from beneath and pulled at the same time. Most of the office avoided it. Files went in when no one knew where else to put them, and came out only under protest. The bottom drawer held vendor certificates from previous campaigns, some in folders, some loose, some still in courier envelopes with the corners torn.

Rauf needed a calibration certificate from two years earlier.

'If we cannot produce it,' he said from Thomas's doorway, 'the inspector will hold the lifting gear.'

'Why was it not scanned?'

'Because the scanner was broken.'

'For two years?'

'Spiritually, yes.'

Olesya came in behind him, holding a ring of keys.

'I know the cabinet.'

Rauf looked at the keys. 'Why do you have those?'

'The temp gave them to me.'

'The temp left before you started.'

'She gave them to Leyla. Leyla gave them to the driver. The driver gave them to me because they were in his glove box and he wanted the glove box for gloves.'

Rauf stared at her.

'Baku,' Thomas said.

'Do not start,' Rauf said.

The archive cabinet stood under the harsh strip lights at the back of the office. Olesya crouched before the lower drawer and tried three keys before the lock turned. The drawer shifted half an inch and stuck.

'Lift here,' she said.

Thomas took the handle from below.

'Not like that. You will bend it.'

He moved his hand.

'Here.'

The metal was cold beneath his fingers. She placed her hand beside his, smaller, dry from paper and winter air. Together they lifted and pulled. The drawer came free with a shudder that travelled up Thomas's arm.

Inside, the files had slumped into one another. Dust lay along the hanging folders in grey ridges. Someone had stored a cracked mug in the back corner. The mug contained three binder clips and a dead moth.

Olesya looked at it for a moment.

'Procurement system,' she said.

Thomas laughed before he could stop himself.

She glanced at him, quick and almost suspicious, as if laughter had not been the expected output.

Rauf, standing behind them with his phone in one hand, said, 'We need certificate TKL-44, not comedy.'

Olesya began removing folders. She did not read every label. She read the wear. The cracked spine of one folder. The newer tape on another. The faint oil mark on an envelope that had probably come from the yard. Thomas watched her sort by touch and smell as much as by text. The air around the cabinet filled with dust and old paper.

'This drawer was moved,' she said.

'How do you know?'

'The folders lean the wrong way.'

Rauf looked at Thomas. 'I am choosing not to understand that.'

'Probably wise.'

She found the certificate after twelve minutes, folded inside the wrong vendor's folder and clipped to a faded inspection photograph. The document had a stamp in violet ink, the signature half outside the box.

'There,' she said.

Rauf took it from her and held it up to the light. 'Alive.'

'Barely,' Thomas said.

'Barely is enough for inspectors if the stamp is old and ugly.' Rauf left with the paper, already dialling someone.

Olesya remained crouched by the open drawer.

'This should be fixed.'

'The cabinet?'

'The files.'

'It would take days.'

'Then days.'

Thomas looked at the drawer. It was a minor problem, old and local and unglamorous, the sort of thing no weekly report would capture until the day it stopped a shipment. Olesya was still touching the folder tabs with the back of one finger, reading the disorder.

'Make a list,' he said.

'A list will not fix it.'

'No. It will show the size of the damage.'

She looked at him. 'That is how you begin?'

'Usually.'

She stood, brushing dust from her skirt. A grey mark remained near her knee.

'I clean first.'

'And then?'

'Then the damage is less rude.'

He should have returned to his office. Instead, he helped her carry the first armful of folders to the empty meeting room.

They used Meeting Room Three because it had the large table and the least broken chair. Olesya wiped the table before placing the folders down. Then she set labels along the glass wall: vendor, year, equipment, missing, scan. Her labels were too small for the room, each word written in careful black letters.

Thomas brought his laptop.

'We can build an index.'

'First we find what exists.'

'The index will tell us that.'

'No,' she said, opening the first folder. 'The paper will.'

They worked in a rhythm that did not need agreement. Olesya opened folders, checked stamps, removed duplicates, and placed documents into piles. Thomas entered each item into a spreadsheet and assigned a scan status. When he tried to move too quickly, she tapped the table beside a document he had not recorded.

'You missed one.'

'It is a duplicate.'

'The stamp is different.'

He looked again. It was.

'Good catch.'

She passed him the next folder, already opened to the page he needed.

At four, the room smelled of dust, cold coffee, and the lemon wipes Olesya had used on the table. Rain moved down the glass in thin lines. Outside the meeting room, the office carried on without them. Phones rang. Rauf crossed the floor twice, each time looking in without interrupting.

By five thirty, they had indexed one drawer.

The spreadsheet was clean.

The room was not.

Folders lay in ordered stacks across the table. Olesya had dust on both cuffs. A strand of hair had slipped from its pin and rested against her cheek. She did not notice. Thomas noticed, then looked back at the screen.

'We should stop,' he said.

'This stack is almost finished.'

'Almost finished is not finished.'

'That is why I said almost.'

He smiled at the file name on the screen rather than at her.

They finished the stack at six ten.

When Olesya lifted the last folder, a torn envelope slid out and dropped under the table. She bent for it at the same time he did. Their hands met on the carpet. Not fully. Knuckles, the back of his finger against the side of hers. Both withdrew.

'Sorry,' she said.

'No.'

The envelope remained between them.

Thomas picked it up and placed it on the table.

Inside was a certificate for a valve assembly no one had asked for. Olesya smoothed it flat, checked the stamp, and added it to the scan pile.

'Hidden things are always expensive later,' she said.

Thomas looked at the certificate, then at the ordered stacks, then at her hands pressing the paper flat.

'Yes.'

He saved the index. The file name appeared in the shared drive: Archive_Certificates_Index_v1.

Olesya read it over his shoulder.

'V one?'

'Version one.'

'So there will be more.'

'There is always more.'

She gathered the used wipes and put them in the bin. Then she lifted the labels from the glass wall one by one, careful not to tear them, and stuck them to the front of a folder for the next day.

At the door, she stopped.

'The chocolate.'

Thomas looked up from shutting down the laptop.

'What chocolate?'

'The ones in your drawer.'

For a moment, the room seemed to lose its small sounds.

Olesya looked at the table, not at him. 'You do not eat them.'

'No.'

'You do not throw them away.'

'No.'

'Why?'

The question came lightly and stayed there.

Thomas closed the laptop fully. The click was louder than he intended.

'They keep.'

She nodded once, accepting the answer without accepting it.

'Good night.'

'Good night.'

She left the meeting room with the folder labels in one hand.

Thomas stayed behind to turn off the lights. In the dark glass, his reflection stood among the ordered files. Behind him, on the table, the torn envelope lay open and empty.

In return, the office began to arrange itself around her.

The courier with the cigarette no longer leaned over her desk. Drivers brought slips to her before Rauf asked for them. The procurement clerk who disliked everyone started leaving unsigned forms in her tray with a pencil mark where the missing stamp should go. Even the yard engineers, when they came in wet and impatient from Sumgait, lowered their voices by the second sentence.

Thomas watched this change with a private satisfaction he did not examine.

At noon, Olesya watered the fern.

At one thirty, she drank water.

At four, she opened the top drawer, checked the receipt book, and closed it again. On the days when money was there, the movement did not change. On the days when money was not there, the movement did not change. The drawer clicked exactly the same way.

This was how the arrangement protected itself.

No visible gratitude.

No visible giving.

Only the office, smoother than before.

One Friday, Sarah called during Thomas's lunch break. He took the call in his office with the blinds half open. Outside, Olesya was speaking to a driver at her desk, holding a delivery slip in one hand and pointing to a missing stamp with the other.

'You are distracted,' Sarah said.

Thomas turned slightly away from the glass. 'No.'

'That was too quick.'

'Then yes.'

'Baku?'

'Baku.'

Sarah smiled from whatever transit lounge held her that day. Behind her, people moved with wheeled bags, coats over their arms, passports ready in their hands. She wore a dark jacket, hair pinned back, no wasted softness in her face.

'I signed Geneva,' she said.

'Good.'

'Three months, possibly six. They want me in and out, not full-time on site.'

'That suits you.'

'It does.'

She looked down to check something, then back at him. 'You sound as if you are still at your desk.'

'I am.'

'Do you ever leave that building?'

'Occasionally.'

'Try doing it deliberately.'
