He glanced through the glass. Olesya had taken the driver's slip and was making him wait while she copied a number into her notebook. The driver shifted from foot to foot.

'I will.'

'You will not.'

'Probably not.'

Sarah laughed. It came cleanly through the laptop speakers, untouched by the airport noise around her.

'I miss your honesty.'

'Only that?'

'Mostly that.'

The driver outside Olesya's desk raised both hands. Olesya did not look impressed.

Sarah followed Thomas's glance. 'Am I boring you?'

'No.'

'Who are you watching?'

'A driver losing an argument.'

'Ah. The true theatre of oil and gas.'

'It is more exact than theatre.'

'You would say that.'

She was called away then, her name spoken by someone outside the frame. She turned, answered, then looked back at him.

'I have to go. Eat something that is not from a vending machine.'

'Yes.'

'That means no.'

'It means yes with qualifications.'

'Goodbye, Thomas.'

'Goodbye.'

The screen went dark.

Thomas remained turned away from the glass for a moment. Then he looked back.

Olesya was watching him.

Not openly. Her eyes moved from the laptop to his face and then back to the driver. It was nothing. Less than nothing. A glance caught between tasks.

He closed the laptop.

At three that afternoon, she brought in a folder of inspection photographs from the yard. The images were poor, taken on a phone with a scratched lens. Weld seams, pipe ends, stamped plates, the dull shine of wet steel. Thomas spread them across his desk while Olesya stood opposite him.

'These need matching to the report.'

'The filenames are wrong,' she said.

'Of course they are.'

'The yard sent them by message. Then email. Then message again.'

'Which set is current?'

'None.'

He looked up.

'They photographed the same pipe twice and forgot another section.'

Thomas went through the images. She was right. The missing section was listed in the report but absent from the photographs. Two images showed the same flanged joint from different angles, renamed as separate components.

'How did you see that?'

She pointed to the concrete beneath the pipe. 'Same crack.'

Thomas looked. A dark line ran through the wet concrete in both images, almost hidden beneath the shadow of the pipe stand.

'Yes.'

'They think if the pipe is different, the ground does not matter.'

'And does it?'

'The ground always tells.'

The sentence stayed in the room after she said it.

Thomas separated the photographs into two piles. Verified. Missing. Duplicate. Olesya took labels from her folder and wrote each word in block letters. Her handwriting was small but exact. When she leaned forward to place the first label, the cuff of her coat brushed the edge of the desk. The darker repair thread had held.

'You fixed the cuff,' he said.

'Yes.'

'Better this time.'

'I used stronger thread.'

She did not look at him.

'Good.'

They worked through the photographs until the light outside shifted from grey to black. Rauf came in once, saw the spread of images, and left without comment.

At five forty, Olesya's phone buzzed on Thomas's desk.

She looked at the screen and turned it over.

Thomas continued writing the missing-photo list. After a minute, the phone buzzed again. Then again.

'Do you need to answer?'

'No.'

The fourth buzz came almost immediately.

Olesya picked up the phone and stepped into the corridor. Through the glass wall, Thomas saw her stand near the printer alcove with one hand against her forehead. She spoke little. Mostly listened. When she came back, the colour had left her face.

'I have to go,' she said.

'Is something wrong?'

'No.'

She began gathering the photographs. Her hands moved too quickly, disturbing the piles they had made.

'Leave them.'

'They are not finished.'

'I will finish them.'

'No.'

'Olesya.'

She stopped.

'Leave them.'

The phone buzzed again in her hand.

She looked at it, then at the photographs, then at Thomas.

'My daughter is waiting outside school.'

It was the first time she had said daughter.

Thomas put down the pen.

'Go.'

'The bus is late.'

'Take a taxi.'

She looked at him.

There was no money visible between them this time. No notes beneath a paperweight. No drawer. No buffer that could pretend to be something else.

'I will take the bus.'

'Then she waits longer.'

Outside the office, the printer began feeding paper into its tray, page after page, though no one had sent anything to it. A delayed job from earlier in the day, arriving after its usefulness had passed.

Olesya looked towards the sound.

'Go,' Thomas said.

She left without her folder.

He watched her pass the glass, coat unbuttoned, grey hat still on her desk. At the lift she pressed the button twice, then stood with the phone tight in her hand.

When the doors opened, she stepped inside and disappeared.

Thomas finished the photograph list alone.

He found the duplicate. He logged the missing section. He sent the report to Rauf and copied the yard manager. Then he returned to Olesya's desk, placed the grey hat inside her top drawer, and paused with his fingers on the handle.

The drawer was empty except for the receipt book, spare staples, paper clips, and the small notebook where she wrote instructions for tools she did not trust.

He took out his wallet.

The notes lay too brightly against the dark bottom of the drawer.

He covered them with the receipt book.

On Monday, they were gone.

So was the hat.

The inspection photographs had been refiled in clear sleeves, each label rewritten in Olesya's hand. Duplicate. Missing. Verified.

Beside his coffee sat another chocolate in gold foil.

Thomas opened the drawer in his own desk.

The first two were still there.

He placed the third beside them and closed the drawer before the office filled.

At eight, Olesya came to his door.

'The yard will send the missing section today,' she said.

'Good.'

'I told them the ground tells.'

'Did that help?'

'No.'

The edge of a smile appeared and was gone.

She looked at the closed drawer of his desk, though she could not have known what was inside it.

'Good morning, Thomas.'

'Good morning.'

She went back to her desk. The fern caught the weak winter light. The coffee steamed between his hand and the keyboard.

Thomas opened the permit register.

The first formula was wrong.

Chapter 04

The Sumgait inspection was scheduled for a Thursday, which meant by Wednesday afternoon three people had already tried to move it.

The yard wanted Friday, claiming the weld team would still be finishing the final section. Procurement wanted Monday; the payment release had not been checked against the latest delivery note. Rauf wanted Thursday, while the defects were still visible and the money still mattered.

'Thursday,' he said, standing in Thomas's office with the inspection pack under one arm. 'Before they grind the ugly parts smooth and call it progress.'

Thomas took the pack. 'Who is translating?'

Rauf looked through the glass.

Olesya was at her desk, writing a courier reference into the collection book. Her grey hat lay beside the fern. The repaired cuff of her coat had opened again near the seam, a small dark mouth in the fabric.

'She knows the yard men,' Rauf said.

'Do they know her?'

'That is not always an advantage.'

Thomas looked through the glass again.

Olesya had opened the top drawer and was sorting the collection receipts by date. The drawer money, when it appeared, vanished without altering the order of anything around it. He had begun to notice this too. The absence left no mark. No shifted receipt book. No torn envelope. No note of thanks. The office simply continued the next morning with coffee on his coaster and a corrected physical file on her desk.

'What does Murat want?' Thomas asked.

'Payment.'

'Everyone wants payment.'

'Murat wants payment without inspection.'

'Then he will be disappointed.'

Rauf gave him a look. 'Disappointment is easy in an office. In a yard, it has tools.'

Olesya came back to the doorway with the collection book closed.

'What time tomorrow?'

'Seven thirty,' Thomas said.

'I will prepare the weld map.'

'Bring the photographs from last week. The missing section from D bay as well.'

She nodded.

'And the old test certificates,' Rauf said. 'Murat likes to claim memory has no paperwork.'

'Which certificates?'

'All of them.'

'That is not a number.'

'Then bring too many.'

She wrote it down in her notebook: too many certificates.

Thomas saw it and almost smiled. He did not.

That afternoon, she built the Sumgait pack on the long table in Meeting Room Three. Thomas found her there after five, sleeves rolled to her wrists, grey hat beside a stack of folders. She had arranged the documents in bands across the glass: current weld map, previous inspection photographs, pressure-test certificates, payment release, vendor correspondence, blank non-conformance forms.

Each stack had a paper label.

Thomas stood at the door.

'You have made a war room.'

'It is too small for war.'

'A skirmish room.'

She glanced up. 'That is closer.'

He walked to the table and checked the order. The current map was on top. The photographs had been marked in pencil at the corner, C seventeen, C nineteen, D two. The payment release sat beneath a red folder, visible but not accessible by accident.

'This is good.'

'It is complete.'

'Those are different things.'

'Today they are both.'

He picked up one photograph. The weld bead showed as a dark raised line beneath harsh yard light. Along the lower edge, mud had spattered the steel in small, dried flecks.

'This is the section from last week.'

'Yes.'

'The one the yard said was missing.'

'It was never missing.'

'Where was it?'

'Behind the wrong name.'

Thomas looked at her.

'The folder said auxiliary frame,' she said. 'The photograph said main support. They hoped the name would be enough.'

'And was it?'

'For someone who reads names, maybe.'

He put the photograph back.

The office outside had thinned to end-of-day sounds. A chair moved. A drawer closed. Somewhere near reception, the coffee machine expelled steam into its tray.

'You do not have to stay late for this,' he said.

'I know.'

'Then why are you?'

She aligned the edge of the red folder with the table.

'Tomorrow he will say we brought the wrong paper.'

'And if we bring every paper?'

'Then he will say paper does not build rigs.'

'Does it?'

'No.'

She pressed the label flat with her thumb. 'But it releases money.'

The red folder stayed under her hand.

Thomas watched her hand move across the page. Small letters. Firm pressure. No wasted space.

'Send her in.'

Rauf did not move at once. 'Sumgait is not the office.'

'I know.'

'No, you know the map. That is different.'

Olesya came to the doorway before Thomas could answer. She had the collection book still open in her hands.

'You need me?'

Rauf turned. 'Inspection tomorrow. Sumgait fabrication yard. You will interpret.'

The change in her face was almost nothing. A tightening near the mouth. Her fingers shifted on the spine of the collection book.

'Which foreman?'

'Murat.'

She looked down at the collection book.

'You know him?' Thomas asked.

'Everyone knows him.'

'That was not my question.'

'Yes,' she said.

Rauf took the book from her and closed it. 'Seven thirty. Company Land Cruiser. Bring the current weld map, the pressure-test photographs, and your warm coat.'

'My coat is warm.'

Rauf looked at the torn cuff.

Olesya put her hand over it.

'Seven thirty,' she said.

She left with the book held against her chest.

The next morning, Baku was still dark when Thomas reached the office car park. Rain had fallen during the night and left the concrete shining under the security lights. The Land Cruiser waited near the gate, white paint streaked with road dirt, tyres already carrying a skin of mud from some earlier trip. The driver smoked beside the bonnet with one hand tucked under his opposite arm.

Olesya stood by the rear door.

She wore the second-hand coat, the grey hat, and black gloves with the fingertips worn thin. A document sleeve was tucked inside the coat, protected from the damp. She had placed herself under the narrow shelter of the entrance canopy, close enough to the car to be ready, far enough from the driver that no conversation was required.

'Morning,' Thomas said.

'Good morning.'

The driver dropped his cigarette and crushed it beneath his shoe.

'Sumgait,' he said.

The word filled the car for a moment.

The city thinned as they drove north-west. Glass towers gave way to low blocks, repair yards, petrol stations, strips of waste ground where dogs moved through wet grass. The road carried buses, tankers, men in vans with fogged windows. Beyond the outskirts, the land flattened into a colourless industrial belt. Pipes ran beside the road on concrete supports. Smoke lifted from stacks and spread sideways under the low cloud.

Thomas sat in the front passenger seat with the inspection pack on his lap. Olesya sat behind him. In the rear-view mirror he could see her hands resting on the document sleeve. The gloves made them look smaller.

Rauf had not come.

That was deliberate. Rauf had left his familiarity at the office. A foreign engineer could say no in places where local refusals were folded into older obligations.

'Murat will say the welds are complete,' Olesya said from the back seat.

'Are they?'

'Some will be.'

'And the others?'

'He will say they are nearly complete.'

The driver laughed once.

Thomas turned a page in the pack. 'Nearly complete does not release payment.'

Olesya looked out of the window. 'He knows.'

The yard appeared first as a line of cranes against the grey sky. Then fencing, floodlights, stacked beams, the long ribs of unfinished structures, men moving between them in orange jackets. The Land Cruiser stopped at the security gate while the guard checked the entry list with a biro that would not write in the damp. Olesya spoke to him through the window. He answered without looking at Thomas.

Inside the yard, the ground was packed mud and crushed aggregate. Water gathered in ruts left by heavy tyres. Somewhere unseen, metal screamed under a grinder. The sound travelled across the yard in bright, hard bursts, stopping and starting like an argument.

Murat met them near Bay Three.

He was a large man with a shaved head, broad through the stomach, wearing a leather jacket beneath his high-visibility vest. His hard hat sat pushed back on his head, safety gear turned into ornament. Two welders stood behind him, both smoking, both looking at Olesya before they looked at Thomas.

Murat spoke in Russian.

Olesya listened without moving.

'He says the welds are complete,' she said. 'The inspection team is wasting time.'

Thomas opened the weld map. 'Tell him we will begin with section C seventeen.'

She translated.

Murat answered too quickly. The welders laughed.

Olesya did not translate.

Thomas looked at her.

'He says C seventeen is not accessible.'

'Why?'

She asked.

Murat pointed towards the bay and spoke again, longer this time. His hand moved through the air, cutting, dismissing, circling back towards Olesya and making her the obstruction.

'He says materials are stored there,' she said.

'Then the materials can be moved.'

She translated.

Murat laughed. He took a step closer to her. The mud sucked at his boot.

Thomas heard one word clearly. Russkaya.

Olesya's face did not change. Her thumb moved once against the edge of the document sleeve.

'Translate all of it,' Thomas said.

She looked at him.

'All of it.'

Her eyes returned to Murat. 'He says a Russian woman from an office does not tell yard men how to build for the sea.'

The welders stopped laughing before Murat did.

Thomas closed the inspection pack.

'Tell him the signature on the payment release is mine.'

Olesya translated.

Murat's mouth flattened.

'Tell him section C seventeen is cleared in ten minutes, or the release waits.'

She translated again.

Murat looked past her to Thomas and answered in broken English.

'You see weld. You pay.'

'I see weld pass inspection. Then payment is released.'

Murat spat into the mud, close enough to Olesya's boot for the splash to mark the leather.

Thomas stepped between them before deciding to.

The yard noise continued around them. Grinder, hammer, engine, wind through sheet metal. Murat looked at Thomas's chest, then his face.

'Ten minutes,' Thomas said.

The silence held for three breaths.

Then Murat turned and shouted towards the bay.

Men began moving steel.

Olesya looked down at her boot. A fleck of mud clung near the toe. She did not wipe it away.

They inspected C seventeen in a wind that drove grit against Thomas's cheek. The weld ran along a joint beneath a cross member, uneven in three places, with undercutting visible where the bead failed to meet the edge cleanly. Thomas crouched, wiped rainwater away with the back of his glove, and marked the defect on the sheet.

'Ultrasound,' he said.

Olesya translated.

Murat objected. She translated only the useful part. The equipment was at another bay. The technician was not available. The surface was wet. The test would delay work.

'The surface can be dried. The technician can be called. Work is already delayed.'

She repeated it.

The second weld failed visual inspection before they reached the end of the row. The third had been ground smooth. Too smooth. Thomas ran a finger along the cold metal, feeling for what had been hidden beneath the polish.

'Photograph this.'

Olesya took out her phone.

Murat said something to the welders.

Olesya's hand paused.

'What?'

'Nothing.'

'Olesya.'

She took the photograph. 'He says if I like pictures, I should photograph kitchens.'

Thomas looked at Murat.

Murat smiled.

'Take three photographs,' Thomas said.

She did.

At noon, the wind sharpened and the rain came in low from the sea. The yard office was a portable cabin with a heater that smelled of dust and hot plastic. Murat made tea for himself and the welders. No cup was offered to Olesya. Thomas declined before anyone could offer him one.

They stood around a metal table covered in drawings, inspection sheets, and old cigarette burns. Thomas marked the required retests. C seventeen. C nineteen. D two. Payment hold pending ultrasound. Photographic record attached. The pen dragged against damp paper.

Murat watched the marks accumulate.

'Too much,' he said in English.

'Enough.'

'You make problem.'

'No. I found one.'

Murat looked at Olesya. He said something low, almost conversational.

She translated with her eyes on the table. 'He says you should listen to local advice.'

'That is not what he said.'

She did not answer.

Thomas placed the pen down and looked at Murat. 'The release is held until the tests are complete.'

Murat's hand struck the table.

The drawings lifted at the corners.

Olesya did not step back. Only her shoulders changed, drawing in beneath the coat. Thomas knew the smaller signals now: the thumb on paper, the drawer click, the cup sound against the coaster.

'We are finished here,' he said.

Outside, the yard had become a blur of wet steel and moving jackets. The driver brought the Land Cruiser close to the cabin door. Olesya climbed into the back without speaking. Thomas took the front passenger seat again, the inspection pack damp at the corners.

No one spoke until the gate was behind them.

The driver turned up the heater. Warm air pushed dust from the vents. It smelled of old fabric and diesel.

Thomas looked through the side mirror. The yard receded behind fencing and rain.

'He knew I am Russian,' Olesya said.

The driver kept his eyes on the road.

Thomas turned slightly. 'Yes.'

'It matters there.'

'Only there?'

She looked out at the industrial land slipping past. Pipes, mud, low walls, men waiting under a shelter with cigarettes cupped in their hands.

'Some places say it. Some places only remember.'

The sentence ended there.

Thomas did not ask for more. The question would have required her to make a map for his benefit. She had already translated enough for one morning.

The road back towards Baku ran beside a drainage canal the colour of weak tea. Plastic bottles knocked against the concrete edges in the wind. Beyond the canal, housing blocks stood with their balconies enclosed in mismatched glass and metal, each one an attempt to claim a little more room from the weather. Laundry moved behind the panes. A woman in a red coat shook a rug over a railing and watched the Land Cruiser pass.

Olesya's reflection hovered in the side window, layered over the blocks and the canal and the low industrial sky.

'In school,' she said, 'they changed the language of the forms.'

Thomas waited.

'My mother had to fill everything again. Same address. Same child. Same building. New forms.'

'Because of Russian?'

'Because of many things.'

The driver adjusted the heater. The fan clicked louder.

'She made mistakes?'

'No.' Olesya looked down at the document sleeve on her lap. 'She made copies.'

The answer came with a small finality. A house rule from before Thomas knew her. If the city changed the form, you kept the old one. If a clerk denied a stamp, you produced the previous stamp. If a man in a yard pretended memory belonged to him, you brought paper.

Thomas looked at the inspection pack on his knees. Too many certificates.

Not too many.

Enough.

They drove through a district of repair shops and concrete walls painted with advertisements faded by salt air. The rain loosened and stopped. Puddles held the colour of the sky.

Olesya's phone rang.

The sound was thin in the heated car.

She looked at the screen and answered in Russian. At first she spoke quietly. Then the words came faster. Thomas caught only fragments: today, no, work, please, daughter, money. Dengi. Again. Twice more.

The driver glanced at her in the mirror.

Olesya turned her face towards the window.

When the call ended, she held the phone in both hands.

'Turn left here,' she said.

The driver looked at Thomas.

'Where?' Thomas asked.

'Please. There is a payment centre.'

'For what?'

She did not answer quickly enough.

The driver slowed at the junction.

'Turn left,' Thomas said.

The road narrowed into an older residential district, dense with low shops, balconies caged in metal, laundry hanging stiff in the damp air. Satellite dishes clung to walls, wet black bowls turned towards the cloud. The Land Cruiser moved awkwardly between parked cars and men pushing handcarts stacked with boxes. Children in dark school uniforms crossed through traffic without looking at the vehicle.

Olesya leaned forward and pointed. 'There.'

The payment centre sat between a pharmacy and a shop selling phone cases. Its sign was half-lit though it was only early afternoon. Inside, through the glass, Thomas saw a queue of people holding folded bills, plastic cards, scraps of paper. Fluorescent light flattened every face.

Olesya opened the door before the driver had fully stopped.

'Wait here,' she said.

She took her document sleeve but left the grey hat on the seat.

Thomas watched her enter the payment centre. She joined the queue near the back, then moved forward one place, then another. Her coat was dark against the pale walls. She held a paper in one hand and her phone in the other.

The driver lit another cigarette with the window cracked open.

Five minutes passed.

Then ten.

The queue moved. Olesya reached the counter. She spoke to the clerk, a woman behind scratched glass. The clerk shook her head. Olesya unfolded the paper, pushed it through the slot, then pulled it back when the clerk tapped the glass.

Thomas opened his door.

The driver looked at him. 'Problem?'

'Probably.'

Inside, the room smelled of wet coats, warm plastic, and the faint medicinal sweetness from the pharmacy next door. A wall-mounted television played a music video with the sound off. People in the queue watched Thomas enter, then looked away.

Olesya was still at the counter.

'What is the problem?' he asked.

She turned too quickly. The paper bent in her hand.

'Nothing.'

The clerk said something through the glass.

Olesya answered sharply.

Thomas took one step closer. 'Olesya.'

She looked at the paper, then at the clerk, then at the floor.

'Electricity.'

'What about it?'

'They will cut it today.'

The clerk tapped the amount printed on the bill with one polished fingernail.

Thomas read the figure. It was not large in the way corporate numbers were large. It was not an amount that required approval chains, cost codes, or a line in a budget. It sat there with the blunt force of a closed door.

'I can pay tomorrow,' Olesya said to the clerk in Russian.

The clerk shook her head.

'I said today,' Olesya said.

'How much?' Thomas asked.

'No.'

The word came out before he had finished the question.

The people in the queue had become still in the way people become still when pretending not to watch.

'How much?' he asked the clerk.

The clerk looked at Olesya first.

Olesya's face had gone pale beneath the harsh light.

Thomas took out his wallet.

'Please do not,' she said.

The clerk turned the bill towards him and pointed.

Thomas counted the notes and passed them through the slot.

Olesya did not move.

The clerk counted twice, stamped the bill, tore off a receipt, and slid it back. The stamp was blue. The same colour from the office had followed Olesya into this room.

Thomas picked up the receipt and handed it to her.

She took it by the edge.

Outside, the rain had stopped entirely. The street had brightened without sun. Water ran along the gutter, carrying cigarette ends, leaves, a strip of orange plastic from a shopping bag.

They walked back to the car.

Olesya opened the rear door, then stopped.

'I will pay you back.'

'No.'

'Thomas.'

'No.'

The driver watched them through the windscreen.

She folded the receipt once, then unfolded it. The paper had already softened from her fingers.

'This is not office.'

'Electricity affects work.'

'No.'

'If your electricity is cut, you do not sleep. If you do not sleep, you make errors.'

She looked at him. He had turned the gift into a work instruction.

'So it is for the spreadsheet?'

'Yes.'

The answer held.

Olesya looked down at the receipt. The blue stamp sat near the bottom, slightly crooked. Paid.

'I have money on Monday.'

'Then keep it.'

'For what?'

'The next thing.'

One corner of her mouth moved, then stopped.

'There is always a next thing.'

'Yes.'

They got into the car.

The grey hat lay on the seat beside her. She picked it up, smoothed it once, and held it in her lap for the rest of the journey.

Back at the office, Rauf was waiting by Thomas's door.

'Well?'

Thomas handed him the inspection pack. 'Three welds require ultrasound. Payment release held.'

Rauf opened the folder and read the first page. 'Murat will call me names.'

'He already did.'

'Good. Saves time.'

Rauf looked through the glass. Olesya had returned to her desk. She had taken off her coat and was placing the utility receipt inside the back cover of her notebook. Her movements slowed around the receipt.

Rauf closed the folder.

'Sumgait teaches quickly.'

'Apparently.'

'And slowly,' he said.

Thomas looked at him.

Rauf shook his head once and went back to his office.

The afternoon resumed around the held payment. Emails came from the yard before three. One from Murat, written in capital letters. One from the yard administrator, more polite and more dangerous; it copied senior people. Thomas replied to neither until he had attached the photographs, the weld-map references, and the payment clause.

Olesya translated the useful parts of each message and left the insults out.

'All of it,' Thomas said.

She looked at him across the desk.

'No.'

'Why?'

'Because this one is boring.'

He read the Russian line she had marked with her pencil. He knew enough now to recognise his own name and something about English hands.

'Boring?'

'Very.'

She took the printed page, placed it in the red folder, and aligned the corners.

At five, the ultrasound technician confirmed availability for the next morning. At five thirty, the yard accepted the hold in language that pretended it had been their idea. Rauf walked past Thomas's office and tapped the glass with two knuckles in approval.

Olesya stayed until six.

She did not bring the receipt to him. She did not mention the payment centre. When she shut down her computer, she opened the top drawer, placed the notebook inside, and closed it with the same small click as always.

The receipt was no longer visible.

Thomas passed her desk on the way to the printer and saw the notebook open beside the keyboard. Only the corner of the receipt showed from the back cover, the blue stamp cut in half by the paper edge. She had placed it there with the other things that had to be kept and not discussed.

He stopped long enough to read nothing.

On the top page of the notebook she had written the next morning's tasks:

Ultrasound confirmation.

Murat payment hold.

Driver slips.

The words sat in her usual narrow hand. Beneath them, separated by a line drawn with a ruler, she had written electricity.

Then she had crossed it out so completely that the paper had thinned beneath the ink.

Thomas went to the printer and collected the release memo. The machine was warm from use. The first page curled slightly in his hand.

When he came back, Olesya had closed the notebook.

At six ten, Sarah's name appeared on Thomas's phone.

He let it ring twice before answering. Olesya was still at her desk, winding the cord of her charger into a neat loop.

'You sound outside,' Sarah said.

'Office.'

'Then your office sounds like a road.'

Thomas looked towards the glass. The wind had risen again and was pushing against the building, making the seals murmur. 'Sumgait today.'

'Ah. The glamorous life.'

'Very.'

'Any casualties?'

'Three welds and one foreman's temper.'

'So a normal day.'

He could hear airport noise behind her: rolling suitcases, an announcement blurred by distance, the hollow acoustics of a terminal. Sarah was moving again. She always called between gates, between meetings, between one temporary room and the next.

'Where are you?' he asked.

'Geneva. Waiting for the late flight to London. They gave me an executive lounge voucher, which is how companies apologise without apologising.'

'Useful.'

'Barely. The wine is bad and a man beside me is explaining blockchain to someone trapped by politeness.'

Thomas watched Olesya lift her coat from the chair. The repaired cuff held.

'You sound tired,' Sarah said.

'Long day.'

'Come home for a weekend if you can. Or take one somewhere warmer. You do remember weekends?'

'In principle.'

'That means no.'

He smiled faintly. 'It means scheduling is complex.'

'Everything is scheduling with you.'

Olesya looked towards his office then, caught either by his voice or by the last small act of shutting down. She lifted one hand. Not a wave. A small practical acknowledgement, quick and exact.

Thomas lifted his hand in return.

Sarah said, 'Are you listening?'

'Yes.'

'No, you are looking at something.'

'Someone shutting down.'

'Important?'

The question was casual. It moved lightly through the line and left a small pressure at the base of his throat.

'A local administrator.'

'Then let her leave. You should too.'

'I will.'

'You will not.'

The terminal announcement rose behind her and swallowed the next words. She came back a moment later.

'Boarding. I will call tomorrow.'

'Safe flight.'

'Always.'

The call ended.

Olesya had gone back to her desk.

Her desk remained in its usual order. Fern turned towards the window. Keyboard covered. Receipt book absent from view. On the coaster beside Thomas's keyboard, the coffee cup from the morning still held a dark crescent at the bottom.

Thomas finished the release memo and saved it to the shared drive. The office lights caught his reflection in the window: white shirt, loosened tie, face blurred by the harbour darkness beyond the glass.

When he came out, Olesya was at the lift.

'Good night,' he said.

She turned.

'Good night.'

The lift arrived. The doors opened behind her.

She did not step in.

'The blue stamp,' she said.

'What about it?'

'At the payment centre. It was the same colour.'

Thomas thought of the Ministry documents, the yard pack, the electricity bill. Blue circles on paper. Permission attached to ink.

'Yes.'

'They stamp everything here,' she said.

'Most things.'

She looked at him for one second longer.

'Not everything.'

Then she stepped into the lift.

The doors closed.

Thomas returned to his office. On his desk, beside the keyboard, the weld photographs lay in a neat stack. C seventeen on top. Three angles. Mud at the edge of the frame. A dark line in the metal where the bead had failed to hold.

He placed the utility receipt number into his notebook, beneath the Sumgait release reference.

Then he crossed it out.

Chapter 05

Three days after Sumgait, Olesya did not come to work.

At eight fifteen her desk was still exact. The fern had been turned towards the window. The keyboard cover lay square over the keys. The courier book sat closed with a pencil placed along its spine, sharpened to a point fine enough to pierce paper. Only the chair gave her absence away. It had been pushed in by the cleaner, not by Olesya. The angle was wrong.

Thomas noticed it before he noticed the missing coffee on his coaster.

He stood inside his office with his coat still on, looking through the glass. Outside, Baku had gone flat under low cloud. Rain made a pale sheet across the windows and drew the harbour into a grey smear. The rigs beyond the water were reduced to outlines, uprights and cranes fading in and out as the weather moved.

Rauf came in with a folder under one arm.

'She is late,' Thomas said.

'Who?'

Thomas looked at him.

Rauf followed his gaze to the empty desk. 'Ah.'

'Did she call?'

'Not me.'

The answer gave him nothing useful. Rauf placed the folder on Thomas's desk and opened it to a delivery query from the supplier in Turkmenbashi. A pump skid had cleared customs, then uncleared itself in the way cargo sometimes did when a stamp was smudged, a date misread, or a clerk wanted to be reminded that his desk existed.

'We need the import manifest,' Rauf said.

'Olesya has the physical file.'

'Then we need Olesya.'

Thomas took off his coat and hung it behind the door. 'Where is the digital copy?'

Rauf smiled without much warmth. 'You are asking the wrong question.'

By nine, the customs broker had called twice. By ten, the supplier had sent four emails with the subject line changing shape each time. Thomas found the digital manifest on the shared drive, but the scan cut off the lower third of the page. The missing section contained the signature, the stamp, and the part everyone would pretend was most important since it was the part they could not see.

He went to Olesya's desk.

The top drawer opened with its usual small click. Receipts, courier slips, spare staples, two wrapped sweets, a folded bus ticket, the red notebook. Everything had been placed with a care that made touching it feel like trespass. He found the key to the lower cabinet in a narrow paper envelope marked stationery.

The physical file was in the second drawer, behind a stack of expired customs forms tied with cotton string. He knew it by the blue strip along the spine. He took it out and carried it back to his office.

The signature was present. The stamp was clean.

He scanned the page himself, attached it to the broker's reply, and waited for the cargo to become legal again.

At eleven thirty, Rauf appeared in the doorway.

'Still absent?'

'Yes.'

'You called?'

Thomas had not. He reached for his phone and saw, before he touched it, how absurdly visible the motion was. Rauf watched him with his head tilted slightly to one side.

'No,' Thomas said.

'Then maybe call.'

'I was going to.'

'Of course.'

Rauf left before the silence could settle anywhere useful.

Thomas found Olesya's number in the office directory rather than in his personal contacts. The distinction mattered for the length of time it took him to press call. It rang nine times. No answer. He put the phone face down on the desk and returned to the import file.

At one o'clock, she called.

He answered before the second ring.

'Olesya?'

There was noise behind her. A kettle boiling too hard. A child's cough, dry at the edges and deep in the middle. Then a chair scraped across a floor.

'I cannot come,' she said.

'Are you ill?'

'No.'

The kettle stopped. The silence after it was worse. The cough remained.

'Anya is sick. The school sent her home.'

Thomas looked through the glass at her empty desk. The pencil still lay against the courier book, too perfectly placed to belong to an emergency.

'Do you need a doctor?'

'It is fine.'

The cough came again, closer to the phone this time.

'That does not sound fine.'

'It is a cough.'

'How old is she?'

'Twelve.'

He had known she had a daughter. The file had held the fact safely until the sound came through the phone.

'Can someone stay with her tomorrow?' he asked.

'Tomorrow she goes back to school.'

'If she is still coughing like that?'

'She goes back.'

The flat behind her went quiet. Someone had heard herself being discussed and was holding still.

'We needed the manifest,' Thomas said.

'It is in the cabinet. Second drawer. Blue spine.'

'I found it.'

'Good.'

Her relief was too quick. It did not belong to the manifest. It belonged to the job.

'Take the day,' he said.

'I have taken the day.'

'I mean, do not worry about the hours.'

'I will make them up.'

'That is not necessary.'

'It is necessary.'

Another cough. Then Olesya moved the phone away and said something in Russian, low and brisk. The voice that answered was younger than Thomas expected, roughened by the throat but edged with embarrassment. He could not make out the words.

Olesya came back. 'I must go.'

'Call if you need anything.'

'We do not.'

The line ended.

Thomas kept the phone in his hand for several seconds. On his screen, her number sat without a name attached to it. He added the name. Then he deleted it. Then he added it again.

In the afternoon the customs release came through. The pump skid regained its official existence and was assigned to a truck that would arrive late, dented, and accompanied by three men who would insist nothing had been dented. Thomas signed the release, updated the shipment tracker, and sent Rauf the revised schedule.

At four forty, Sarah called from London.

Thomas answered from the corridor outside the meeting room, where the glass reflected him in separate panels, each one narrower than the last. Behind her voice, he heard crockery and the bright confusion of a cafe. She had signed the Lisbon contract. Portugal for ten days, then probably Berlin. Her life arrived to him in public spaces, each one already leading somewhere else.

'Are you eating properly?' she asked.

'Yes.'

'That means no.'

Thomas looked through the glass wall. Olesya's desk had become shadowed by the late afternoon. The fern still faced the rain.

'One of the administrators has a sick child,' he said.

'She will manage,' Sarah said. 'People do.'

Someone near her called her name.

'I should go. Do not spend the night in the office.'

'I will not.'

'That tone is not legally binding.'

The line closed into the corridor hum.

Thomas returned to his office and finished the last two emails. At five twenty, he opened the personnel folder and found Olesya's address. It was written in her narrow hand on the emergency contact form, the letters careful, the block number corrected once in darker ink. Her emergency contact was Anya. The telephone number beside it was the same number he had heard coughing behind.

He copied the address onto a yellow note. Then he looked at it until the act became impossible to explain as work.

At five forty, he placed the import manifest in a folder, added two blank signature pages, and put the folder into his briefcase.

Rauf was still in his office.

'You are leaving early,' he said as Thomas passed.

'I have to confirm a signature.'

'At this hour?'

'It is on the way.'

Rauf looked at the briefcase, then at Olesya's empty desk. He picked up his tea and blew across the surface.

'Of course,' he said.

The taxi took twenty minutes to arrive and another thirty to reach the district. Traffic moved in the slow, uneven pulses of rain. Cars pressed into gaps that had not been offered. Buses breathed steam at the kerb. The city changed as they drove east: the office towers fell behind, the pavement broke, the shopfronts lowered their eyes.

Thomas sat in the back with the briefcase against his knees and the address open on his phone. The driver glanced at it twice.

'Old block,' he said.

'Yes.'

'Lift maybe no work.'

'Fine.'

The driver laughed softly. 'Fourth floor always fine for people not living fourth floor.'

They stopped beside a row of Soviet-era blocks, long and tired, their concrete darkened by weather and exhaust. Satellite dishes hung from balconies like metal leaves. Wires crossed the courtyard in low loops. The ground was mud, broken tile, puddles, and the pale ends of cigarette filters. A boy kicked a flat football against a wall painted with numbers for a gas service no longer operating.

Thomas paid the driver and stood under the rain for a moment longer than needed.

The entrance door had no handle. Someone had tied a strip of cloth through the hole where the handle had been, and the cloth was wet enough to darken his fingers when he pulled it. Inside, the stairwell smelled of damp plaster, cabbage, gas, and the mineral dust of old concrete. The lift doors were closed. A handwritten note had been taped across them.

Do not use.

The words had been written in Russian and Azeri. Someone had added a third warning in pen, harder pressed, underlined twice.

He climbed.

On the second floor, a woman opened a door and looked at him through the chain. On the third, a television played behind a wall, the sound thin and metallic. By the fourth, the briefcase knocked against his knee with each step. He checked the number on the door and knocked.

Nothing.

He knocked again, softer.

Footsteps came close, stopped, then retreated. A lock turned. The door opened as far as the chain allowed.

A girl looked out at him.

She had Olesya's pale eyes and a face still unarranged by its own lengthening. Her hair was tied back badly, with strands caught under the elastic. She wore a school shirt under a cardigan too large in the shoulders. One sock was grey, the other white. Her cheeks were flushed, but the skin under her eyes had a bluish shadow.

'Hello,' Thomas said.

She did not answer.

'I am Thomas Ward. From your mother's office.'

The girl's eyes moved to the briefcase, then back to his face.

'Mama,' she called, and coughed halfway through the word.

The chain closed. More locks moved.

Olesya opened the door.

She wore a dark dress with a cardigan over it and a towel tucked through the handle of the oven behind her. Her hair had been pinned up, but pieces had escaped near her temples. Steam moved in the small kitchen at her back. The smell of boiled potato and lemon reached the hall.

'Thomas.'

The word was not a greeting.

'I brought the manifest,' he said.

She looked at the briefcase.

'To my home?'

'There is a signature discrepancy on the customs release.'

Anya stood behind her, one hand at her throat, watching both adults with the quick attention of a child who knew money and work could enter a room before they were named.

Olesya stepped back.

'Come in, then.'

The flat was small and warmer than the stairwell. Heat came from a portable radiator near the table. Shoes were arranged along the wall by size. Two women's coats hung on a hook, one Olesya's, one too small for her and too large for a child. The wallpaper had lifted near the window and been pressed back with clear tape.

No object lay where it had fallen. Even the worn things had been made to stand in line.

Olesya took the folder from him and placed it on the kitchen table. A pot simmered on the cooker. Beside it, a bowl of sliced lemon stood near the kettle. Anya sat on a low stool and folded her hands between her knees.

'This is Anya,' Olesya said.

'Hello,' Thomas said.

Anya nodded. 'Hello.'

Her English was careful. School English. Each sound placed in the mouth before being released.

'Your mother says you are unwell.'

Olesya looked at him.

Anya looked at the floor. 'It is just coughing.'

'Everyone says that.'

The corner of her mouth moved. The cough came before the smile could settle. It bent her forward, one hand pressed flat to her chest. Olesya crossed the room in two steps and put a cup into her hand.

'Drink.'

'It is hot.'

'Then hold it.'

Anya held the cup.

Thomas placed his briefcase against the wall and took the chair Olesya indicated. The table was covered with a plastic cloth patterned with faded cherries. The manifest folder sat between them, too clean for the room.

'Where is the discrepancy?' Olesya asked.

He opened the folder to keep the lie in place.

'The broker says the signature on the scan is not clear.'

She took the page, read it, and looked at him over the top edge.

'You already sent this today.'

'Yes.'

'Then there is no discrepancy.'

'No.'

The radiator clicked. Outside, a car passed through water in the courtyard.

Olesya lowered the paper.

'Why are you here?'

The question did not accuse him. It took the air out of the room.

'I wanted to see if you needed anything.'

'I said no.'

'I heard her cough.'

Olesya's hand tightened once on the paper. 'Children cough.'

'Has she seen a doctor?'

'The school nurse looked.'

'A doctor.'

'There is a clinic.'

'Have you gone?'

She placed the manifest back in the folder and aligned its edge with the tablecloth pattern. Cherry to cherry. Corner to corner.

'Not today.'

'Why not today?'

Anya lifted her eyes to her mother. Olesya did not look at her.

'The private doctor is not there.'

'And the state clinic?'

'The state clinic is there every day.'

The answer carried no contempt. It carried experience.

Anya sipped the hot lemon water and made a face. Then she saw Thomas looking and straightened.

'I am not very sick,' she said.

'You sound sick.'

'It sounds worse than it is.'

'That is what your mother says about invoices.'

Anya looked at Olesya. Olesya looked away first.

The pot lid trembled on the cooker. Rain tapped the window.

Thomas saw the schoolbooks stacked on the sill. There was no shelf. Mathematics, Russian grammar, English exercises, a science textbook mended in brown tape. A sheet of paper had been pinned above them with two drawing pins. Timetable. Homework. Chores. Medicine.

Medicine had been written in Olesya's hand, then crossed out, then written again.

'What medicine is she taking?' he asked.

Olesya moved to the cooker and lifted the lid. Steam rose, hiding her face for a second.

'Tea. Lemon.'

'That is not medicine.'

'No. It is tea.'

Anya looked into the cup.

Thomas leaned back. The chair legs were uneven. One had been repaired with tape wrapped tight around the lower part, building height where wood had worn away.

'I can pay for the clinic.'

'No.'

Olesya said it before the offer had fully entered the room.

'It is a doctor, not a favour.'

'It is both.'

'It can be work related.'

'My child is not work related.'

The words struck cleanly. Anya's eyes moved between them, quick and alert to the shape of adult things rather than their volume.

Thomas looked down at the manifest folder. The blue customs stamp sat visible at the top of the page. Permission attached to ink. Release granted by one square of colour in the correct place.

'No,' he said. 'She is not.'

Olesya turned off the cooker. The room became quieter at once.

'Then do not make her into work.'

The chair, the table, the child's cup, the distance from the office to here. All of it stood between them.

Anya coughed again.

It lasted too long. The cup shook in her hand and struck her tooth with a small sound. Olesya crossed to her and took the cup before it spilled. Anya tried to breathe through her nose. The effort made her eyes water.

Thomas stood.

'Where is the clinic?'

Olesya did not answer.

'Where?'

'Around the corner,' Anya said.

Her mother looked at her.

'It has a green sign,' Anya added, softer.

Olesya put the cup on the table. 'You should not have said.'

'He asked.'

'People ask many things.'

Anya lowered her head.

Thomas picked up his coat. 'I will wait downstairs.'

'Thomas.'

'Bring her coat.'

'No.'

'Then I will go to the clinic and bring the doctor here.'

Olesya stared at him. The colour had left the skin around her mouth.

'You do not get to order my home.'

He heard it then. Not refusal. Not pride. A boundary made from the last material she had.

He put the coat down.

'You are right.'

The radiator clicked again. Somewhere in the building, pipes knocked in the wall.

'I can give you the money,' he said. 'You decide.'

'And after?'

'After what?'

'After you give money, what is it called?'

He had no answer that would fit the room.

Anya coughed into her sleeve. Olesya closed her eyes for one beat, then opened them and took her bag from the hook.

'We will go,' she said. 'You do not come in.'

'Fine.'

'You wait outside.'

'Fine.'

'You do not speak.'

'Fine.'

Anya stood carefully, one hand pressed to her chest. Olesya wrapped the smaller coat around her, fastened each button, then took the grey hat from the hook and pulled it over her daughter's hair. It was too big for Anya. Olesya folded the edge twice.

Thomas looked at the table while she did it.

The manifest folder lay open beside the sliced lemon. The page did not belong there. Its stamp, signature, and printed clauses were absurd amongst the cup, the schoolbooks, the pot of potatoes. He had brought the office into the flat with no other way to enter.

The clinic was around the corner, as Anya had said. Its green sign flickered above a glass door filmed with condensation. A poster of lungs curled at the edges on the wall.

Olesya went in with Anya.

Thomas stayed outside under the narrow awning. Rain dripped from one broken corner onto the pavement in a steady line. People passed with bags pressed to their sides. Across the street, a bakery sold flatbread from a window fogged by heat. The smell reached him each time the door opened.

He checked his phone.

Sarah had sent a photograph from the Lisbon contract dinner: a long table, white plates, a glass of wine catching light, her face half turned towards someone out of frame. Beneath it she had written:

Proof of life.

Thomas looked through the clinic glass. Olesya sat with Anya beneath the lung poster. Anya had her head against her mother's shoulder. Olesya's thumb moved once over the seam of the coat.

He put the phone away.

Twenty minutes later, Olesya came out alone.

'Bronchitis,' she said.

'Antibiotics?'

'Yes.'

'Anything else?'

'Inhaler. Cough syrup. Rest.'

'Good.'

She held a folded prescription in one hand and the receipt in the other. The receipt was already damp along the edge.

'How much?'

'No.'

'Olesya.'

'No.'

The clinic door opened behind her. Anya came out wearing a paper mask. It made her eyes look larger. She held a small white bag from the pharmacy, but the shape inside was too thin for everything the doctor had named.

Thomas saw the omission before Olesya could hide it.

'Where is the inhaler?'

Olesya's hand closed around the receipt.

Anya looked at the street.

'They did not have that brand,' Olesya said.

'Which pharmacy?'

'It does not matter.'

'It matters if she needs it.'

'Thomas.'

He took the prescription gently from her hand. She let him with Anya watching. The inhaler was circled in blue by the doctor. Beside it, a price had been written in pencil.

Not large. Not small. A sum with teeth.

There was a cash machine at the corner by the bakery. Thomas withdrew the maximum it would allow in one transaction. The machine counted notes behind its metal face, slow and loud. He folded them once and placed them inside the prescription.

Olesya did not take it.

'For Anya,' he said.

'That does not make it simple.'

'No.'

'You think money makes things simple.'

'No.'

She looked at the folded prescription between them.

'You do.'

The rain thickened. Anya coughed behind the mask, smaller now, restrained by the effort not to draw attention from people passing on the pavement.

Olesya took the prescription.

She did not thank him.

They bought the inhaler from the second pharmacy. Thomas remained outside again. He watched through the glass as Olesya counted some of his money with her own notes folded around it, making the payment appear mixed, less clean, less entirely his. The pharmacist placed the box in a bag. Olesya checked the label before putting it into her handbag.

On the way back to the flat, Anya walked between them. She carried the medicine bag in both hands. At the block entrance she stopped and looked up at Thomas.

'Thank you,' she said.

Olesya's face closed.

'You do not need to say that,' she told her in Russian.

Anya answered in the same language. The sentence was too fast for Thomas, but the child's chin lifted at the end.

Olesya's eyes sharpened.

Anya lowered her chin.

'What did she say?' Thomas asked.

'Nothing.'

Anya looked at him through the paper mask. 'I said he bought medicine. People say thank you for medicine.'

Olesya opened the stairwell door.

'Inside.'

The climb was slower on the way up. Anya stopped on the second landing, breath catching. Olesya pretended to adjust the bag so the pause could belong to her. Thomas stood one step below them and looked at the paint peeling from the wall.

Back in the flat, Olesya made Anya sit by the radiator. She read the inhaler instructions twice, once in Russian, once in English, comparing the paper to the box. Thomas stood near the door with his coat still on. He could have left now. The clinic had been paid, the medicine bought, the immediate crisis reduced to instructions printed on paper.

But the flat had altered.

The heat seemed thinner. The tablecloth showed a tear near one corner. A bucket stood behind the curtain under the taped window, catching water from a leak he had not noticed before. Each drop landed with a soft, delayed tick. On the shelf above the cooker, two jars held rice and buckwheat. The rice was nearly gone.

Anya used the inhaler badly the first time. Olesya corrected her, too sharply. Thomas looked away until the second breath went in properly.

'Again tonight,' he said. 'And in the morning.'

'I can read,' Olesya said.

'I know.'

Anya leaned back against the chair and closed her eyes. The paper mask hung loose from one ear. The flush had begun to fade from her cheeks, leaving the skin pale and damp at the hairline.

Olesya folded the clinic receipt and placed it inside a small tin on the shelf. The tin already held documents: rent slips, school notices, payment receipts, folded forms with stamps in blue, purple, and black. She made room for the new paper by pressing everything flat with the backs of her fingers.

Thomas watched the gesture.

'Rent,' he said.

Olesya paused.

'What about it?'

'How much is it?'

'Paid.'

'For this month.'

'Yes.'

'And next?'

She closed the tin.

'Next month is next month.'

He heard Sarah's voice in that. Lisbon, Berlin, ten days, next month as a place one could enter with tickets and contracts already clearing a path. Here, next month waited behind a tin lid with the rent slips flattened beneath a child's prescription.

'Show me,' he said.

'No.'

'Olesya.'

'You are in my home.'

'Yes.'

'Do not inspect it.'

He stepped back from the shelf.

'I am sorry.'

The apology did not repair anything. It only sat between them, another object without a proper place.

Anya opened her eyes. 'Mama, he can sit.'

'He is leaving.'

'He brought papers.'

Olesya looked at the manifest folder still on the table. For the first time since he arrived, the absurdity of it touched her mouth.

'Yes,' she said. 'Very important papers.'

Anya looked at Thomas. 'Are you her boss?'

'No.'

'Rauf is boss?'

'Rauf thinks so.'

Anya considered this. 'Mama says Rauf talks too much.'

'Anya.'

'He does.'

Thomas took the chair by the door. Refusing would have made the girl's offer visible. Olesya served food once a person sat in her kitchen and the room demanded it. Boiled potatoes, a little butter, salt, black bread cut thin. She gave Anya the larger portion and placed a smaller plate in front of Thomas without asking.

'You do not have to feed me,' he said.

'Then do not eat.'

He ate.

The potatoes were plain and hot. The bread was dense, slightly sour. Anya ate slowly, measuring each bite against the cough. Olesya did not sit until both plates had been served.

'Do you like Baku?' Anya asked.

'Some days.'

'Mama says the wind has teeth.'

Thomas looked at Olesya. 'It does.'

Anya tore a small piece of bread and dipped it into the butter on her plate.

'Where are you from?'

'England.'

'London?'

'Sometimes.'

'How can you be from sometimes?'

Olesya's knife stopped against the plate.

'Work moves me,' Thomas said.

'Mama says men from rigs live in airports.'

'Your mother says many accurate things.'

Olesya lowered her eyes to her plate.

The phone on the shelf vibrated.

Olesya rose and checked it. Her face changed by not changing.

'School?' Thomas asked.

'Landlord.'

She put the phone face down.

It vibrated again.

Anya stared at her food.

Olesya did not touch the phone.

'Answer,' Thomas said quietly.

'No.'

'If it is about rent, answer.'

'It is not your call.'

'No.'

The phone stopped, then began again.

Anya pushed a potato into smaller pieces with the edge of her fork.

Olesya picked up the phone and went into the narrow hall. Her voice became low enough that the words blurred. Thomas heard the shape of Russian numbers. He heard tomorrow. He heard please, once. Then no again, harder.

The call ended.

She returned to the kitchen and took her seat.

'Eat,' she told Anya.

Anya obeyed.

Thomas looked at the tin on the shelf. Olesya saw him do it.

'Do not,' she said.

'How much?'

'Thomas.'

'How much?'

She pressed her hand flat on the tablecloth. The cherries beneath her palm distorted where the plastic had stretched.

'Two months,' she said. 'The school shoes and the doctor came together.'

'How much?'

She named the sum.

It was larger than the electricity bill. Still smaller than a single error in a procurement forecast. In this room, it sat at the table and took food from the plates.

Thomas opened his wallet.

Olesya stood so fast the chair struck the wall.

'No.'

Anya flinched.

Thomas stopped with his hand inside his jacket.

Olesya saw Anya's face and lowered her voice.

'No,' she said again.

'Let me solve this.'

'This is not a valve.'

'I know.'

'No, you do not.'

She took the plates to the sink though none of them had finished eating. Water ran hard over the metal, drowning the room. Her shoulders were straight. The tap splashed onto her sleeves. She did not move them back.

Thomas closed his wallet.

Anya looked at him. Her eyes above the mask were too old for the cardigan, the mismatched socks, the careful English.

'The landlord shouts in the stair,' she said.

'Anya,' Olesya said from the sink.

'Everyone hears.'

Olesya turned off the tap.

No one spoke.

Thomas took out his notebook instead of his wallet. He wrote the amount on a blank page, then the date, then rent, clinic, medicine. The words formed columns under his hand.

'I will not give it to you here,' he said.

Olesya watched him.

'Tomorrow, at the office, I will advance you two months' salary.'

'There is no form.'

'I will make one.'

'Rauf will know.'

'Rauf knows many things.'

'And accounting?'

'Accounting will see a salary advance.'

'It is not salary.'

'It will be.'

The lie had better paperwork than the manifest. Olesya looked at the notebook, then at the tin on the shelf, then at Anya's medicine bag.

'And after?' she asked.

'After, you work.'

'I work now.'

'Then you keep working.'

'And I owe you.'

'You owe the company.'

'No.' Her voice was soft. 'I will owe you.'

He did not deny it.

'I can manage the paperwork,' he said.

'That is what you like.'

'Yes.'

Anya lifted the inhaler box from the table and read the English label aloud, stumbling over corticosteroid. Thomas corrected the pronunciation once. She repeated it, better the second time. Olesya watched them from the sink with her hands wet and empty.

Outside, someone shouted in the stairwell. A door closed. The building settled around them in small sounds: pipes, rain, footsteps, the lift refusing service behind its taped warning.

Thomas left at eight.

Olesya walked him to the door. Anya had fallen asleep on the narrow sofa with a blanket pulled to her chin, the medicine bag on the floor beneath her hand. The paper mask lay on the table beside the manifest folder. He had forgotten the folder. Olesya picked it up and gave it to him.

'Your important papers.'

'Thank you.'

She opened the door but did not step into the hall.

'Do not come here again without asking.'

'I will not.'

'And tomorrow you do not say hardship.'

'What should I say?'

'Advance.'

'No soft words.'

'No soft words.'

She looked back into the flat. Anya shifted in her sleep and coughed once, weaker now.

'You like simple,' she said.

'I like things that can be fixed.'

'Same thing.'

He had no answer.

She held the door with one hand. Her cardigan sleeve had slipped down, exposing the pale ridge of her wrist.

'Tomorrow I come to work,' she said.

'If she is still ill, stay home.'

'Seven thirty.'

'Eight is fine.'

'Seven thirty.'

The desk, the pencil, the fern, the job still waiting in the office: she was putting the room back where it belonged.

'Seven thirty,' he said.

She nodded once and closed the door.

The stairs smelled colder on the way down. On the second floor, the woman with the chain opened her door again, looked at him, and closed it without a word. Outside, the rain had thinned to mist. The courtyard lights shone on puddles broken by floating scraps of paper. The taxi he had called was not yet there.

Thomas stood under the entrance awning and opened his banking app. Rent, medicine, salary advance, repayment schedule, discretionary allowance. He made a note in his phone and titled it O admin.

The taxi arrived with its headlamps low in the mist.

Before he got in, he looked up at the fourth floor. One window glowed behind a thin curtain. A shadow crossed it once, carrying something small from one room to another.

In the taxi, his briefcase rested on his lap. Inside it, the manifest had picked up a faint smell of boiled potato and lemon.

As they drove back towards the office towers and the harbour lights, the next failure already had a line item.

Upstairs, behind the taped window, Olesya opened the salary advance form he had left folded inside the manifest folder and read it twice. Then she took the money he had placed between the blank signature pages, smoothed each note against the table, and sorted it into three piles.

Rent.

Medicine.

School.

Anya slept under the blanket with the inhaler beside her hand.

Olesya folded the smallest pile once, tucked it into the tin beneath the stamped receipts, and closed the lid without sound.

Chapter 06

By the second week of February, the salary advance had become a folder.

Thomas found it in the shared drive under Payroll Exceptions, where the accountants placed anything that did not fit the ordinary rhythm of the month. The file name was bland enough to survive notice: O Petrova, advance request. Inside were four documents. His authorisation. Rauf's countersignature. A repayment schedule. A scanned copy of Olesya's receipt, stamped by payroll in blue.

He opened the receipt and looked at the amount.

The number had changed shape since the night in her kitchen. There, it had sat between the plates and the medicine bag. Here, it belonged to a column. It had a date, a reference, a line manager, a deduction plan. The company would take a little from her wages each month until the page repaired itself.

Outside his office, Olesya placed a courier envelope on the reception counter and pressed the flap down with the side of her hand. She was wearing the grey hat indoors. The heating had failed again on the north side of the floor. The torn cuff of her coat had been mended with darker thread. The repair was neat, visible, and meant to last.

Anya was back at school. The medicine line had disappeared from the note in Thomas's phone, and Olesya no longer left at four. She arrived at seven thirty, set the fern towards the window, removed the keyboard cover, checked the courier book, and restored the office one object at a time.

Nothing about her asked for acknowledgement.

That made acknowledgement harder to avoid.

At ten, Rauf came in without knocking.

'Your reassignment is confirmed.'

Thomas closed the payroll file.

'When?'

'End of month.'

'I thought they wanted me through commissioning.'

'They did. Then Iraq wanted you more loudly.'

Rauf dropped the printed transfer letter on the desk. Basra appeared twice in the first paragraph, once as location and once as cost centre. The language was clean and indifferent. Mobilisation date. Rotation close-out. Handover obligations. Travel itinerary to follow.

'You knew?' Thomas asked.

'Yesterday.'

'You waited until now?'

'I enjoyed one peaceful evening.'

Thomas read the letter again, though it contained nothing he had missed. Iraq had been possible for months. In oil work, possible often waited in a drawer until someone stamped it into fact.

'How long?'

'Six months minimum. Then it becomes a different six months.'

Rauf sat in the chair opposite him and looked through the glass.

Olesya was at the copier, feeding customs sheets through one by one. The machine pulled each page with a small shudder. She caught the copies before they had time to curl.

'Does she know?' Thomas asked.

'No.'

'Why would she?'

Rauf's mouth moved. Not quite a smile.

'Exactly.'

The copier jammed. Olesya opened the side panel, withdrew a crumpled page, and smoothed it on the glass with the flat of her hand. She did not call for help. She never called for help with machines after Thomas had shown her how to read the paper path.

Rauf stood.

'Handover plan by Friday. Inventory, open claims, customs issues, personnel notes. And do not leave your famous small disasters for me.'

'I do not have famous small disasters.'

'Everybody has them. Yours have labels.'

He left the letter on Thomas's desk.

Thomas turned it over so the printed side faced down.

At lunch, Sarah called from an airport.

The terminal noise reached him before her voice did: wheels over tiled floor, a gate announcement dissolving into static, someone laughing too close to the phone.

'You are officially moving again,' she said.

'News travels.'

'Company travel desk copied me on the itinerary request. Basra?'

'Looks that way.'

'Hotter than Baku.'

'Most places are one thing more than Baku.'

'And one thing less.'

He could hear her walking. Sarah always sounded clearer when she was in motion. Her breath adjusted to corridors, stairs, boarding queues, hotel foyers. Movement was the room she carried with her.

'Will you come through London?' she asked.

'Possibly for forty-eight hours.'

'Long enough to wash clothes and look troubled in the kitchen.'

'A useful skill.'

'You have many.'

Someone called her surname. She moved the phone away and answered, then came back.

'I have Geneva, then Lisbon again, then maybe home. We can overlap if the gods of scheduling are in a generous mood.'

'I will send dates when I have them.'

'Do that.'

There was a pause long enough for the terminal to enter it.

'You sound elsewhere,' she said.

'Office.'

'That is not what I meant.'

Thomas looked through the glass. Olesya had returned to her desk. She was writing numbers from a delivery note into the ledger, each digit narrow and exact. The torn cuff lay against the paper. Dark thread on dark wool.

'Handover,' he said.

'Ah.'

Sarah knew the word. It meant long evenings, boxes, passwords, unresolved problems disguised as lists. It did not mean what it meant at that desk outside his office.

'Do not carry Baku with you,' she said.

'I will carry the work.'

'That is not what I meant either.'

The announcement rose again behind her.

'Boarding,' she said. 'Send the dates.'

'Safe flight.'

'Always.'

The call ended.

Thomas left the phone face down beside the transfer letter. For several minutes he watched Olesya write.

On Friday, he began the handover.

He made the easy lists first. Open purchase orders. Cargo still in customs. Yard defects pending ultrasound. Spares delayed from Aberdeen. Vendor claims by severity. The system behaved when asked to describe steel, money, ships, valves. It became more resistant around people.

Personnel notes sat blank for half an hour.

At last he typed:

O Petrova. Administrative assistant. Strong physical records discipline. Weakness in digital systems and data entry. Requires review on spreadsheets before external issue. Reliable on courier, filing, translation, office logistics.

He stopped.

The sentence was accurate. It was also useless.

He added:

Good judgement under pressure.

Then deleted it. Too broad. Rauf would mock it. HR would not read it. Olesya would never see it. He wanted one piece of the file to hold something closer to the truth.

He typed:

Retain if possible.

The three words looked excessive on the page.

He left them.

By the following Tuesday, the office had begun to treat him as temporary. People knocked less. Documents arrived in small concluding stacks. Rauf came in twice a day with questions that were not questions.

'This claim from Sumgait.'

'Reject unless ultrasound attached.'

'This customs broker.'

'Do not let him call after four. He becomes theatrical.'

'This spare parts forecast.'

'Already padded by ten per cent.'

'You are learning local culture.'

'I am learning your handwriting.'

'Same thing.'

Olesya did not ask about the reassignment.

She took the transfer letter from the printer tray when he forgot it there, placed it face down on his desk, and said only, 'This was in the machine.'

'Thank you.'

Her eyes touched the Basra header. Then they moved away.

'Rauf needs the vendor file.'

'Which one?'

'The one he says he does not need until he needs it.'

'That narrows nothing.'

'Turkish valves.'

'Bottom cabinet.'

She nodded and went to retrieve it.

That afternoon, Thomas found a receipt on his desk.

It was from the clinic. The paper had been folded once. The blue stamp sat at the bottom, slightly smudged from wet fingers. Beside the amount, Olesya had written in pencil:

Deducted from advance.

No note. No thanks. No explanation.

He turned the receipt over. The back was blank.

For a moment he held a pen over it. Paid. Seen. Received. None of the words belonged there. He placed it in his top drawer with the chocolate from before winter and closed the drawer before the gesture could acquire a name.

At home that night, in the company flat, he took out his suitcase.

The flat had never settled around him. It had white walls, a sofa no one would remember buying, curtains that did not quite meet in the middle, and a kitchen equipped by someone certain all meals came from pans with lids. He had kept it efficient. He had never intended to belong to it.

Packing should have taken twenty minutes.

Work shirts. Field trousers. Boots. Passport. Yellow vaccination booklet. Laptop charger. Two notebooks. The stack moved from wardrobe to bed to suitcase in clean sequence. Then he stopped at the bedside drawer.

Inside were things that had avoided categorisation. A spare plug adaptor. Coins. An unopened packet of painkillers. Two receipts from the payment centre near Olesya's block. A school pharmacy slip folded into quarters. The old list from his phone, copied onto paper at some point without his remembering why.

Rent.

Medicine.

School.

He set the paper on the bed.

The categories had not ended. They had only waited.

The next morning, he went to the bank before work.

The branch sat behind mirrored glass on a road where traffic never stopped long enough to park cleanly. Inside, the air smelled of floor polish and paper notes. A security guard watched the queue with the expression of a man who had seen every version of need and no longer ranked them.

Thomas withdrew cash in two transactions to avoid a question from the clerk. The notes came out new and flat. He stood at the counter used for deposit slips and divided them beneath his hand.

Rent.

Medicine.

School.

Then he stopped and added more.

Not much. Enough to turn the envelope from calculation into something harder to defend.

He bought a plain white envelope from a kiosk beside the bank. The woman behind the counter sold cigarettes, chewing gum, batteries, and bus cards. She did not look at him while she counted his change.

In the office, he placed the envelope in the locked drawer of his desk.

That afternoon, Olesya asked to leave at three.

She stood in his doorway with the courier book against her chest and the question already prepared not to sound like a request.

'The school finishes early today.'

'Is Anya ill?'

'No. Teacher meeting.'

'Go.'

'I will finish the broker file from home.'

'No. Finish it tomorrow.'

'It is due today.'

'Tomorrow is fine.'

'You always say tomorrow is not fine.'

'Today it is.'

She looked at him. A rule had moved while she was using it.

'I can bring her here for one hour,' she said.

'You do not have to.'

'I have no one for one hour.'

The sentence came out flat, already stripped of any word he might try to soften.

'Bring her here.'

'She will sit in Meeting Room Three. She will not disturb.'

'Olesya.'

'What?'

'Bring her here.'

She nodded once and left before gratitude could be expected of her.

At four ten, Anya arrived with a schoolbag too heavy for her shoulders and a scarf wound twice around her neck. She had grown more serious since the night in the flat, or perhaps the office did that to children. Her eyes moved over the reception counter, the glass walls, the fern, the printer, the row of men passing with paper cups. She held herself carefully, reading the building for rules.

'Hello,' Thomas said.

'Hello, Mr Thomas.'

Olesya corrected her in Russian.

Anya looked at Thomas again. 'Hello, Thomas.'

'Meeting Room Three is free,' Olesya said. 'She has homework.'

'Good.'

Anya followed her mother through the office. The schoolbag brushed against her hip with each step. In the meeting room, Olesya set her at the far end of the table, away from the glass, and placed a bottle of water beside her exercise books. Then she returned to her desk, opened the broker file, and began typing with the same hard care she gave to all digital things that resisted her.

Thomas watched for longer than he should have.

Anya took out a mathematics book. The cover had been mended with clear tape at the spine. She sharpened a pencil over a folded tissue, gathered the shavings, and placed them in the bin. Then she bent over the page.

For half an hour, the office absorbed her.

Phones rang. Rauf swore softly at a supplier. The new engineer asked where a file was and received an answer from Olesya without her looking up. Anya worked through the noise, stopping only when a cough rose and settled again in her chest. The paper mask was gone. The inhaler sat in the open pocket of her schoolbag, its blue plastic cap visible.

At five, Thomas carried a stack of handover documents to the copier. On the way back, he passed the meeting room and saw Anya staring at one line in her book with the fixed stillness of someone refusing defeat.

He tapped lightly on the glass.

She looked up.

'Stuck?' he asked.

She covered the page with one hand.

'No.'

'Good.'

He moved on.

At his desk he lasted six minutes before standing again.

This time she did not cover the page quickly enough. Fractions. A train leaving one station at one speed, another leaving later from somewhere else, the kind of problem that pretended to be about trains and was really about time meeting itself.

'May I?' he asked.

Anya looked towards her mother.

Olesya's eyes were on the broker file, but she had gone still.

'It is homework,' Anya said.

'Yes.'

'Mama says homework is not for other people.'

'Your mother is usually right.'

'Usually?'

'Do not tell her I said that.'

Anya considered the bargain, then pushed the book a little towards him.

Thomas did not take the pencil from her hand. He drew two lines on a scrap piece of paper and marked the stations.

'Do not begin with the answer. Begin with what moves.'

She leaned closer.

'This train leaves first.'

'Yes.'

'Then it has more time.'

'Yes.'

'But this one is faster.'

'So the question is not where they are. It is when one catches the other.'

Anya looked at the page again. Her pencil hovered, then began to move. Olesya turned a page at her desk, though the file had not required it.

The new engineer came to the doorway.

'Sorry, where is the D bay ultrasound folder?'

Olesya answered before Thomas did. 'Third cabinet, orange tab, under current defects. Not historic defects.'

'Right. Thanks.'

Anya looked at Thomas. 'She knows all the files.'

'Yes.'

'But she says the computer hates her.'

'It does not know her.'

'She says it does.'

'The computer is poorly trained.'

Anya smiled down at the paper, small and private. Then the calculation required dignity.

She solved it three lines later.

'There,' she said.

'Good.'

'You are going to Iraq?'

He looked through the glass. Olesya's hands had stopped on the keyboard.

'Yes.'

'Is it far?'

'Far enough.'

'Will Mama still have this job?'

There was no polite way around the question. It had arrived from the place children watched from while adults pretended not to count money in front of them.

'Yes,' he said.

'Even if you go?'

'Yes.'

Anya wrote the answer to the train problem in the margin, then boxed it with a ruler.

'Good.'

Olesya stood and came to the meeting-room door.

'Anya, pack.'

'I have one more.'

'Pack.'

Anya closed the book without argument. Thomas stepped back.

'She solved it herself,' he said.

'Good.'

Olesya's eyes stayed on the schoolbag. She would not give the moment any more space than that. Anya packed the pencil, book, inhaler, water bottle. Each item went into the bag in order. Thomas saw where she had learned it.

At the lift, Anya turned.

'Goodbye, Thomas.'

'Goodbye, Anya.'

The doors opened. Mother and daughter stepped in together. Just before the doors closed, Anya reached for Olesya's hand. Olesya let her take it, but did not look down.

The office looked different after they were gone.

Not emptier. More exact.

The final week tightened around small things.

A replacement engineer arrived from Aberdeen with two suitcases, red cheeks, and a belief that the region would explain itself if spoken to slowly enough. Thomas walked him through the rig files, the vendor board, the customs delays, the map of personalities no one admitted was a map.

'Rauf knows every shortcut,' Thomas said. 'Listen when he pretends not to care.'

'And admin?'

The new engineer looked through the glass at Olesya's desk.

'Olesya manages the physical files.'

'Good with systems?'

Thomas glanced at the spreadsheet open on the man's laptop. Its columns were too wide, its headers mislabelled, its formulas already at risk.

'Define systems.'

The engineer laughed, assuming humour.

Thomas did not.
