At four, Olesya brought in the close-out binder.

It was black, newly labelled, and heavier than it looked. She placed it on his desk with both hands.

'For Iraq.'

'This is Baku.'

'Yes.'

He opened it. Dividers. Printed contacts. Customs reference numbers. Vendor escalation paths. Rauf's mobile written twice, once under operations and once under people who answer if you call again. At the back, in a clear sleeve, she had placed copies of the receipts related to the salary advance.

Clinic.

Medicine.

Rent.

School shoes.

Each one stamped, dated, flattened.

'You did not need to include these.'

'They are part of the account.'

'Not this account.'

'Yes.'

She stood beside the chair, hands clasped in front of her. Her cuffs were mended now, both of them. The darker thread made two narrow bands around her wrists.

'Olesya.'

She looked at the binder, not at him.

'I deducted the first amount from salary,' she said. 'Payroll will deduct next month also. The rent receipt is there. Medicine also. Shoes also.'

'I saw.'

'There is no missing paper.'

'I know.'

Only then did she look at him.

'Then it is clean.'

He closed the binder.

'Clean is not always the same as finished.'

'No.'

The word was quiet. It did not agree with him. It marked that she had heard.

Rauf appeared in the doorway and took in the two of them, the binder, the closed drawer.

'We are pretending this is a normal Friday?'

'It is Thursday,' Olesya said.

'Even worse.'

Rauf entered, put a stack of yard photographs on Thomas's desk, and addressed Olesya without looking at her.

'We need tea.'

She did not move.

Rauf looked at her then. A small correction passed between them.

'Please,' he said.

She left.

When she had gone, Rauf lowered himself into the chair.

'You are leaving money somewhere.'

Thomas said nothing.

'Do not look surprised. You have the face of a man who has put cash in an envelope and thinks paper makes it less visible.'

'It is temporary.'

'Most expensive word in any language.'

'She has a child.'

'Yes.'

'She has rent.'

'Also yes.'

Rauf picked up the top photograph and studied a weld defect he had already seen.

'And you have a flight.'

The sentence landed without force, which made it harder to move.

'I know.'

'Good.'

Rauf put the photograph down.

'Then know both things.'

Olesya returned with tea before Thomas could answer. She placed Rauf's cup first, then Thomas's. No cup for herself. The old office order restored for one more afternoon.

On his final day, the sky over Baku was clear and hard with cold.

The city looked sharpened by it. The harbour cranes stood black against the morning. The Caspian held a pale metallic light. From his office window, Thomas could see the supply vessels at their berths, lined by function and timing. Movement prepared before movement began.

His desk had little left on it.

Laptop. Phone. Passport. The close-out binder. The white envelope inside the drawer.

Olesya arrived at seven thirty.

She removed her coat, turned the fern, took off the keyboard cover, checked the courier book, and placed a fresh paper cup on Thomas's coaster. Coffee. No sugar. The lid fitted badly, as usual.

'You are early,' he said.

'So are you.'

'Last day.'

'Yes.'

She did not ask when the car would come or what time the flight left. He suspected she knew both. The travel itinerary had passed through the printer. The driver had signed the movement sheet. The office calendar had a block marked T Ward departure, and Olesya managed the office calendar with the care of a person guarding doors.

At ten, the new engineer asked where the customs archive was. Olesya answered without looking up.

'Third cabinet, lower shelf, blue tabs. Do not pull the green folder. It is holding the shelf level.'

The engineer laughed.

She did not.

At noon, Rauf insisted on lunch in the conference room. Someone bought flatbread, grilled meat, pickles, tomatoes, too much parsley. The room smelled of lamb fat and printer toner. People came in, ate standing, made jokes about Basra heat, asked for favours that were not jokes, and left again.

Olesya stayed near the sideboard, passing plates, collecting used napkins, restoring order behind appetite.

Thomas caught her wrist as she reached for a stack of cups.

Not hard. Just enough to stop the motion.

'Eat.'

She looked at his hand.

He let go.

'There is work.'

'There is always work.'

'Yes.'

She took one piece of bread and folded it around nothing. Then she left the room with it still in her hand.

At three, the driver came up to say the car was waiting.

Thomas signed the final movement sheet, handed the replacement engineer his access card, gave Rauf the close-out binder, and stood in the office doorway with his coat over his arm.

The room continued.

Phones rang. The copier clicked. Someone laughed near procurement. The fern on Olesya's desk had turned a little towards the weak sun. The courier book lay open under her hand.

'That is everything,' Rauf said.

'No it is not.'

'No. But it is what fits in a binder.'

They shook hands.

Rauf held his grip a fraction longer than needed.

'Do not start wars in Iraq. They already have enough.'

'I will try.'

'Bad answer.'

'I will not.'

'Better.'

Thomas picked up his bag.

Olesya stood.

For a second he thought she would come to the lift with him. She opened the top drawer of her desk and took out a blue folder. She placed it on the counter between them.

'For the airport,' she said.

'What is it?'

'Copies. Passport. Visa letter. Hotel booking. Driver number. Emergency contact.'

'I have these.'

'Now you have them twice.'

He opened the folder. Everything was in order. Of course it was. The copies were clipped by category, labelled in pencil, each corner aligned. At the back she had added a sheet with useful Russian and Azeri phrases written phonetically, though he would not need them in Iraq.

'This is Baku,' he said.

'Yes.'

Neither of them moved.

He set his bag down and opened the drawer in his office for the last time.

The white envelope lay under the clinic receipt.

He took it out and returned to her desk.

'This is practical,' he said.

Her face changed by one small degree. Not surprise. Not acceptance. A tightening around the mouth, a thread pulled from inside.

'No.'

'For the next month.'

'No.'

'For rent. Medicine. School.'

'You made categories.'

'Yes.'

'Of course.'

She looked past him to the office, to Rauf pretending to read a message near the conference room, to the new engineer bent over a file at the wrong desk, to the copier with its side panel still slightly loose.

'This is for leaving.'

'Yes.'

'Then it is leaving money.'

'It is a buffer.'

'For you.'

'For you.'

'No.'

The word did not rise. It stayed low and exact.

He held the envelope out.

'Take it.'

She looked at his hand. Then at the envelope. Then at him.

'If I take, what is it called?'

Advance. Help. Nothing at all.

'Whatever you want to call it.'

'That is not an answer.'

'No.'

The lift opened behind him. Someone stepped out, saw them, and moved away down the corridor with sudden interest in the wall.

Olesya took the envelope.

She did not put it in her drawer. She held it against the blue folder and pressed both to her chest, the way she had once held the collection book.

'You will come back,' she said.

It was not a question.

Thomas heard the driver in the corridor, clearing his throat. He heard Rauf's phone vibrate on the conference table. He heard the copier restart itself with a hard internal clack.

'The project is closed.'

'You will come back.'

He looked at her hands around the folder and the envelope. The darker thread at her cuffs. The grey hat on the desk. The fern turned towards a sun that would be gone in an hour.

'I do not know.'

'I know.'

She said it without heat, without pleading. A fact placed on the counter beside the courier book.

He picked up his bag.

'Send the receipts,' he said.

For the first time that day, something moved in her expression.

'Receipts?'

'For the rent. Medicine. School. Whatever you use it for.'

She looked down at the envelope, then back at him.

'Yes,' she said.

He had meant proof. He heard, too late, that she had heard continuity.

The driver cleared his throat again.

No smaller gesture offered itself. Thomas nodded once and walked to the lift.

At the doors, he turned.

Olesya was still standing behind her desk. Rauf had stopped pretending to read his phone. The new engineer looked at the file in front of him and saw none of it.

The lift doors closed before anyone moved.

The airport road ran along the water before turning inland. Baku slid past in layers: glass towers, old stone, cranes, housing blocks, petrol stations, hoardings with families too clean for the dust beneath them. The driver listened to news radio at low volume. Thomas sat in the back with the blue folder on his lap.

At the terminal, he checked in, passed security, and bought coffee he did not drink.

The blue folder stayed in his hand through boarding.

On the aircraft, before take-off, he opened it again. Passport copy. Visa letter. Hotel booking. Driver number. Emergency contact. The phrase sheet.

Thank you.

How much?

Where is the receipt?

The phonetic spellings were careful and mostly wrong.

He closed the folder.

The plane rose over the Caspian after sunset. For a while there was only dark water below and the wing light blinking red against it. Then the city tilted away, its roads breaking into lines of amber and white. Somewhere inside that grid, an office would be closing. Somewhere above a courtyard, a taped window would be catching the last of the day's cold.

His phone buzzed as the seatbelt sign went off.

One message from Olesya.

Rent paid. Anya medicine bought. School balance Monday.

Then, below it:

Thank you.

No initial. No soft word. A list with one human line attached.

Thomas typed:

Keep the receipts.

He watched the message send over the aircraft Wi-Fi, the small progress circle turning until it disappeared.

Below him, there was no visible ground.

In the flat on the fourth floor, Olesya sat at the kitchen table after Anya had gone to bed. The envelope was empty beside her. Three receipts lay in front of her, their blue stamps still damp at the edges. Rent. Medicine. School.

She opened the tin on the shelf.

The older papers were already there, flattened beneath the lid. She added the new receipts one by one, aligning each corner before pressing them down.

Then she took a fresh clear sleeve, labelled it in her narrow hand, and made space for the next page.

Chapter 07

London received him in rain.

This rain was not Baku's hard weather, driven sideways across the harbour until the cranes vanished. It was soft, persistent English rain, settling on brick, black railings, plane trees, taxis, the shoulders of people leaving the Underground. It made the terrace house in Fulham look narrower than he remembered. The front step shone under the streetlamp. The brass letterbox had dulled while he was away.

Thomas let himself in with the key that still worked.

The hall smelled of polish, old wood, and Sarah's perfume caught faintly in the coats. Two suitcases stood by the stairs. One was hers, unzipped, half packed with folded shirts and a pair of running shoes wrapped in a hotel laundry bag. The other was empty, waiting for him, a black rectangle with its mouth open.

He stood there with his Baku bag in one hand and Olesya's blue folder under the other arm.

Sarah called from upstairs. 'That you?'

'Yes.'

'Do not move. I am coming down with a travel adapter in my teeth.'

He set the bag down.

She came down barefoot, hair clipped at the back of her head, phone wedged between shoulder and ear, adapter in one hand, passport in the other. Her shirt was half tucked, half loose. She was speaking to someone in Geneva about room blocks, arrival times, and a dinner no one wanted to attend but everyone needed to be seen attending.

She kissed him on the cheek while still listening.

'Yes,' she said into the phone. 'Put me near the exit. I have Lisbon the next morning.'

Thomas stood still until she finished the call.

She dropped the phone onto the small table by the stairs and looked at him properly.

'You look like an airport kept you overnight.'

'Several tried.'

'Baku released you?'

'Provisionally.'

'Very generous of it.'

She put both arms around him. Her body was warm from the house, from movement, from the life that had continued around his absence. He held her with the blue folder trapped between them.

Sarah leaned back and tapped the folder.

'What is that?'

'Travel copies.'

'You and your paper.'

'Paper survives more things than phones.'

'That sounds like something a man says just before losing his phone.'

She took his coat and hung it on the peg. No questions about who had made the folder. No reason for one. Sarah's world produced papers every day: boarding passes, contracts, visas, invoices, receipts from hotels that charged separately for water no one drank.

In the kitchen, she had left soup warming on the hob. The table held two bowls, two spoons, a loaf still in its paper sleeve, and her laptop open beside a stack of printed itineraries. Geneva. Lisbon. Berlin. Home, written in pencil across one weekend, then crossed out.

Thomas placed the blue folder on the sideboard with his passport.

Sarah served the soup.

'Basra dates?' she asked.

'First week of March.'

'How long in London?'

'Nine days, if the medical clears tomorrow.'

'Nine whole days. We should alert the press.'

'I assumed you were leaving.'

'Geneva tomorrow. Back Friday. Lisbon Sunday. Berlin after that if the client stops pretending they can decide by committee.'

She sat opposite him and tore bread with both hands. A crumb fell onto the table. She brushed it into her palm.

'You can come to Lisbon for the weekend,' she said.

'I have Basra prep.'

'Basra will still be there on Monday.'

'That is not a comfort.'

She smiled into her bowl. 'No.'

They ate. The house made its small winter noises around them: pipes settling, rain against the front window, a bus changing gear beyond the terrace. Sarah spoke about flights, clients, a hotel that had mistaken her booking for someone named Mrs Barton and upgraded the wrong woman. Thomas listened, answered in the right places, and watched her passport lying open beside the laptop.

Pages filled with stamps.

Movement reduced to ink.

After dinner, Sarah went upstairs to finish packing. Thomas washed the bowls, dried them, and opened his phone.

There were three messages from Olesya.

School balance paid.

Receipt attached.

Anya says English teacher gave her extra work for missing days.

A photograph followed. Three receipts lay on the kitchen table in Olesya's flat. Rent, medicine, school. The blue stamps had dried darker at the edges. One corner of Anya's exercise book was visible beside them, the word fractions written in her careful hand.

Thomas enlarged the photograph until the receipt numbers filled the screen.

Sarah came into the kitchen carrying a black dress on a hanger.

'Is that work?'

'Receipts.'

'Always glamorous.'

He locked the screen.

'Do you need the iron?' she asked.

'No.'

'That was not a philosophical question.'

'Still no.'

She took the iron from the cupboard and left him with the dress sleeve trailing behind her.

Thomas opened the message again.

Tell Anya good work, he typed.

He deleted it.

Keep the receipts, he typed.

He deleted that too.

In the end he wrote:

Good. Send school receipt Monday.

The reply came after four minutes.

Yes.

Nothing else.

He placed the phone face down beside the sink and finished drying the knife Sarah had used for the bread. The blade had a small notch near the tip. He had forgotten it. Sarah had not replaced it. It still cut.

The next morning, he woke before the alarm.

Sarah was already dressed. She stood at the bedroom mirror fastening an earring, her suitcase closed at her feet, passport tucked into the front pocket of her handbag. The rain had stopped. Pale light entered through the curtains and lay across the floorboards in long, cold strips.

'Car is ten minutes away,' she said.

'I will make coffee.'

'Already made. Yours is in the pot. Mine is in the travel mug and therefore inferior.'

'Geneva today.'

'Geneva tonight. Lisbon Sunday. If you ask again I will assume you miss me.'

'I can read the calendar.'

'That has never stopped you checking.'

She came to the bed and kissed him. Quick, familiar, warm at the corner of his mouth.

'Doctor this morning?'

'Medical at ten.'

'Do not fail it. I have become accustomed to you being employable.'

'I will try.'

Her phone buzzed. She looked at the screen, then at the door.

'Car.'

He followed her downstairs in his dressing gown. The suitcase wheels clicked over the hall tiles. At the door, she turned and pressed two fingers to his chest.

'Come to Lisbon if you can. Forty-eight hours somewhere with sun would improve you.'

'I will check.'

'That means no.'

'It means I will check.'

'Fine. Lie elegantly.'

She kissed him again and stepped out into the morning with her passport, phone, suitcase, handbag, coat, all of it arranged for departure before the car reached the kerb.

Thomas watched her through the front window. The driver lifted her suitcase into the boot. Sarah folded herself into the back seat, already on the phone, already leaving before the car moved.

The house became quiet by degrees.

He drank coffee standing at the counter. Then he took the blue folder from the sideboard and carried it to the study.

The room had been his once, then became both of theirs, then became mostly Sarah's during the months he was away. Her files occupied the lower shelf. His technical manuals held the upper one. A framed photograph from their wedding sat behind a stack of tax documents. Sarah in cream silk, wind lifting the edge of her veil. Thomas beside her, younger, darker-haired, looking at the camera with the face of a man holding confirmed tickets.

He opened Olesya's blue folder on the desk.

Passport copy. Visa letter. Hotel booking. Driver number. Emergency contact. Phrase sheet.

Thank you.

How much?

Where is the receipt?

He took out the phrase sheet and placed it beside Sarah's Geneva itinerary. Two papers. Two kinds of travel. One built from gates and hotel names. One built from copied documents and wrong spellings.

His medical took less than an hour. Blood pressure, eyesight, chest, questions about medications, signature on a form. He passed. The company stamped him fit for Iraq with the same clean confidence it had used to move him out of Baku.

On the way home, he passed a travel agency near the station.

Turkey. Greece. Cyprus. Winter sun. The posters had been laminated so many times that the colours looked sealed away from weather. Istanbul appeared in the corner of one display: domes, water, a bridge lit at night. The city offered itself as a meeting point, neither Baku nor London, neither his home nor hers.

Thomas stood outside long enough for a woman with a pram to steer around him.

At home, he opened the airline website.

London to Istanbul. Direct. Three hours fifty.

Baku to Istanbul. Direct. Three hours.

He chose a weekend twelve days away. Long enough to prepare, close enough to remain real. Sarah would be in Berlin, if Berlin happened. His Basra mobilisation would still be waiting. He could tell work he needed a private weekend before deployment. No one would ask him to prove the need.

He did not book.

He opened Olesya's message thread.

Can you travel to Istanbul next month?

He watched the words in the box. They looked larger than their letters.

He deleted next month and typed:

Can you travel to Istanbul before I leave for Iraq?

He deleted that too.

At last he wrote:

I have a long weekend before Basra. Can you come to Istanbul?

He sent it.

The message showed delivered.

No reply came for thirty-seven minutes.

Then:

Why?

He had not prepared for that.

To see you, he typed.

The cursor waited.

He added:

And to discuss what happens next.

He deleted the second line.

To see you.

He sent it.

This time the answer came quickly.

I need to check passport.

The sentence carried no warmth. No refusal either. He read it three times, then opened the airline website again.

That afternoon, he made a spreadsheet.

Not a formal one. Just dates, flights, hotel options, estimated costs, currency, documents required. Istanbul moved from idea to grid. He chose a hotel near the water, not too grand, not too cheap, with two rooms on the same booking at first, then one room, then two again. He looked at photographs of white sheets, small balconies, a breakfast room with copper lamps, a street where cats slept under parked cars.

He did not press confirm.

At six, Sarah called from Geneva.

'The hotel put me in a room directly opposite the lifts,' she said.

'Ask to move.'

'I did. They gave me a room beside the ice machine. I am climbing rapidly through the hierarchy of bad rooms.'

'Geneva is efficient.'

'Geneva is passive-aggressive with good watches.'

He heard her open a wardrobe, hangers knocking.

'Did the medical pass you?'

'Yes.'

'Tragic. I was hoping for early retirement in a cottage where you learn to make jam.'

'You would leave after a week.'

'I would leave after two days and send postcards.'

He smiled at the desk. The Istanbul spreadsheet remained open on the screen.

'What are you doing?' she asked.

'Travel admin.'

'For Basra?'

'Partly.'

'There is no partly with you. There is only a spreadsheet pretending to be a feeling.'

He looked at the columns.

'That may be fair.'

'Come to Lisbon,' she said. 'No spreadsheet. Just book a flight.'

'You are there for work.'

'Everyone is somewhere for work. Come anyway.'

Olesya's reply appeared on his phone as Sarah spoke.

Passport not with me. Need office.

Thomas read it, then put the phone face down.

'Thomas?'

'I heard you.'

'No you did not.'

'Lisbon may be difficult.'

'It may.'

Sarah made a small sound into the line, then moved on to the next thing.

'I have dinner in ten,' she said. 'Send me Basra dates when final.'

'I will.'

'And eat something that came from a pan, not a packet.'

'Yes.'

'Liar.'

The call ended.

Thomas turned the phone over.

What office? he typed.

The reply came at eight twenty.

Passport office. They took it before. I must ask.

He stared at the word took.

Before what?

No answer.

He waited until the house had settled into night. A bus exhaled at the stop. Someone laughed outside the pub on the corner. Rain began again, fine against the study window.

At ten, Olesya sent:

I will go tomorrow.

He typed:

Good.

Then:

If there is a fee, tell me.

The reply sat marked delivered for eleven minutes.

Then:

No.

He leaned back in the chair. The study smelled of dust warmed by the radiator. Sarah's Geneva itinerary lay beside the phrase sheet. Two papers touching at one corner.

That night he built the trip without booking it.

He printed the flight options first. London to Istanbul. Baku to Istanbul. Arrival times within forty minutes of each other if she took the morning flight. He circled the overlap and wrote feasible in the margin. Then he crossed the word out. Feasible belonged to load-outs and pipe routes. It looked indecent beside her name.

He chose a hotel with two entrances.

That mattered after he noticed it. One door opened onto the main street, another through the courtyard to a lane with blue tiles and a bakery. The photographs showed polished floors, brass lamps, a breakfast terrace with winter covers pulled over the chairs. He enlarged the room images. A desk. A bed. A second chair. A balcony too small to sit on. He imagined placing two passports on the desk, two cups near the window, two sets of shoes by the door.

Then he opened another tab and chose a second room.

The price changed. The meaning changed with it.

He closed the tab.

In the kitchen, the dishwasher clicked through its cycle. Sarah's absence occupied the house more efficiently than most people occupied rooms. Her suitcase had gone, but her movement remained: the adapter missing from the drawer, the coffee mug washed and upside down on the rack, a dry-cleaning ticket beside the fruit bowl. She had left a note on the fridge:

Bin night Thursday.

Geneva number on calendar.

Eat something green.

He took the note down and put it on the counter while he made toast. Then he put it back in exactly the same place.

At midnight, Olesya sent a photograph.

Not the passport office. Anya's school receipt. A narrow slip with a stamp at the top and Anya's name in blue ink. Beneath it, Olesya had written:

Monday paid early.

Thomas looked at the receipt and then at the Istanbul printouts. The school receipt was plain, complete, and possible. The trip was three pages of waiting.

He typed:

Thank you.

Then added:

Tomorrow, take whatever documents they ask for.

The reply did not come until morning.

They always ask for something not there.

He read it standing by the kettle, still in yesterday's shirt.

Then bring too many certificates, he typed.

For the first time since Baku, three dots appeared and stayed long enough to make him put the phone down.

Her reply came:

That was Rauf.

He could see the meeting room in Baku at once. The red folder. The weld photographs. Her thumb pressing the label flat.

Then Rauf was right, he wrote.

No answer.

The next day, he worked through Basra medical forms, tax declarations, security briefings, a hostile environment training refresher that told him to vary routes, carry emergency cash, and avoid local entanglements. The last phrase appeared in a paragraph about transport and subcontractors.

He read it twice.

At three, Sarah sent a photograph from Geneva: a conference badge on a table, her passport beside it, a coffee cup with lipstick on the lid. Her message read:

Proof I still exist.

Thomas replied:

Passport looks busy.

She sent back:

So do I.

At three twelve, Olesya sent:

Queue.

Then a photograph of a paper ticket held between two fingers. 146. Behind it, a tiled wall, a plastic chair, the blurred sleeve of a man in a black coat. No face. No request.

Thomas placed the phone beside the laptop and tried to finish the security briefing. The training video showed a man in a clean shirt explaining checkpoint conduct beside an animated roadblock. Do not argue with local officials. Keep copies of all documents. Do not assume a payment request is unofficial. The man smiled while saying it. Thomas muted him.

At three forty, Olesya sent:

Still waiting.

At four:

They sent me to another window.

He typed:

Which window?

He deleted it. The answer would not help.

At four fifteen:

They say archive.

He wrote archive in the margin beneath Exit and left a square around it. The word had mass. It meant a room no one entered quickly, a shelf with names in the wrong order, a clerk who could say tomorrow without lying.

He checked Baku time. Three hours ahead. Working day ending. Office closing. Doors becoming doors again.

At four twenty-eight, his phone rang.

At four thirty, Olesya called.

He answered from the study with the door closed.

'What happened?'

There was traffic behind her. Wind across the microphone. She was outside.

'They did not give it.'

'The passport?'

'Yes.'

'Why?'

She said something to someone away from the phone. A man's voice answered, bored and official even at a distance. Then the sound changed as she moved into a quieter place.

'There is a restriction.'

'What restriction?'

'Exit.'

He wrote the word on the corner of the airline printout.

Exit.

'From what?'

'Court.'

'What court?'

'Thomas.'

'I need to understand.'

'No. You want paper.'

'Yes.'

'There is paper.'

'Then tell me what it says.'

The line crackled. He heard a bus pass close to her, then voices, then her breathing.

'Bank debt,' she said.

'Yours?'

'In my name.'

'From when?'

'Before.'

'Before what?'

'Before you.'

The answer stopped him. It made him aware of the room he was in: the desk, the wedding photograph, Sarah's files on the lower shelf, the rain moving over the glass.

'How much?'

No answer.

'Olesya.'

'I do not know the full amount.'

'How can you not know?'

'Every year it changes.'

He pressed the pen harder into the paper. The word Exit darkened under the nib.

'Original amount?'

'Small.'

'Now?'

'Not small.'

'Give me a number.'

'Ten thousand manat. Maybe more.'

The number entered the room and stayed there.

He converted it before he meant to. Pounds. Dollars. Salary. Bonus. Joint account. Personal account. Cash. Tax. Sarah.

'Who was the debt with?'

'Bank.'

'For what?'

'Flat. Anya's father signed. I signed also. He left.'

She said it without shape. A series of facts laid down where argument could not reach them.

'And they took your passport?'

'No. They hold it. It is different.'

'It sounds the same.'

'Here it is different.'

He heard a lighter click near her. Someone smoking. Someone waiting beside her. The world around the call remained public.

'Can it be cleared?'

'Everything can be cleared with money.'

'How much to lift the restriction?'

'I told you.'

'The whole debt?'

'They say yes.'

'Who are they?'

'The people behind glass.'

He looked at Sarah's passport in the photograph still open on his laptop. Its burgundy cover. The worn edges. A document that opened gates with the right country printed on the cover.

'Can you appeal?'

'I can stand in another line.'

'With a lawyer?'

'Lawyer costs money.'

'How much?'

'Thomas.'

'I am asking.'

'I know.'

The sound of traffic thinned. He pictured her outside an office with a ticket number in her hand, the blue folder perhaps in her bag, Anya at school, the rent receipt already filed. Paper had done what it could and stopped.

'You should have told me.'

'When?'

'Before.'

'Before what?'

He had no answer.

'Before Istanbul,' he said.

'There was no Istanbul before you wrote it.'

That was true.

He stood and walked to the window. The street below held wet parked cars, a cyclist waiting at the light, a woman carrying flowers under her coat. London moved without showing documents.

'I can come to Baku.'

'No.'

'Why no?'

'You left.'

'I can come back.'

'For what? To stand in offices?'

'If necessary.'

'It will change nothing.'

'We do not know that.'

'I know.'

The same words from the office, the day he left. You will come back. I know. Then, certainty had reached for him. Now it shut a door.

'Istanbul is not possible,' she said.

'Not this weekend.'

'No.'

'We can choose another place.'

'Thomas.'

'Georgia. Turkey later. Somewhere else.'

'I cannot leave Azerbaijan.'

The sentence was plain. No crack in it. No space for planning.

He wrote it under Exit.

Cannot leave Azerbaijan.

The words looked excessive. Four words could do what flights, hotels, routes, money, and wanting could not.

'What about Anya?' he asked.

'She has school.'

'That is not what I meant.'

'I know what you meant.'

He turned from the window. The wedding photograph looked back from behind the tax documents. Sarah's face half lifted by wind. A woman free to leave a room before anyone had finished speaking.

'Let me think,' he said.

'Do not make a project.'

'I am not.'

'You are already writing.'

He looked at the pen in his hand.

'I need to see the paper.'

'No.'

'A photograph.'

'No.'

'Olesya.'

'If I send paper, you will make a plan. If you make a plan, you will think this is solved.'

'It may be solvable.'

'You like that word.'

'Most things have a route through.'

'Not all.'

He heard a door open near her, an official voice calling a number. Olesya did not move.

'We will find a solution,' he said.

The line stayed open. He heard the wind again.

'You say we.'

'Yes.'

'There is you. There is me. There is a court. There is a bank. There is Anya. There is your wife.'

Sarah's passport photograph remained on his laptop, casual as evidence.

'Those are conditions,' he said. 'Not the end.'

'For you.'

'For us.'

She said nothing.

'Olesya.'

'I have to go.'

'Wait.'

'No.'

'We will find a solution.'

This time the line clicked before he had finished hearing himself.

He stood with the phone against his ear until the empty tone stopped.

For a while, he did not move.

The airline website remained open on the screen. London to Istanbul. Baku to Istanbul. Two direct lines meeting over a city where neither of them lived. The hotel booking still waited, selected but unconfirmed. His spreadsheet showed dates, costs, transfer options, room categories.

He printed the spreadsheet before he closed anything.

The printer in the corner woke with a small complaint and pulled the paper through unevenly. Page one listed flights. Page two listed hotels. Page three held a column called documents and another called risk. He had not remembered adding risk. It sat there with empty cells beneath it, waiting to be made useful.

He took the pages from the tray and laid them beside the phrase sheet.

Thank you.

How much?

Where is the receipt?

The questions looked different now. They no longer belonged to taxis and office counters. They belonged to rooms behind glass, debts that changed size while no one watched, passports held in drawers he could not open.

Thomas opened his banking app and checked his personal account. Then the joint account. Then the savings account Sarah used for tax reserves and travel float. The numbers were available if one moved enough labels around them. He closed the banking app before the calculation could complete itself.

In the hall, rainwater ticked from his coat onto the tray by the door.

He went downstairs and picked up the coat. In the pocket he found the travel agency leaflet from the station, folded without remembering the act. Istanbul in glossy blue. A couple on a terrace with cups between them. No luggage visible. No paperwork. No daughter. No bank.

He tore the leaflet in half, then stopped with the two pieces in his hand.

The tear had gone through the bridge.

He placed both pieces in the recycling bin and returned to the study.

The spreadsheet waited.

He closed the Baku flight tab first.

Then the hotel.

Then London to Istanbul.

The spreadsheet stayed.

That evening, Sarah called from Geneva before dinner.

'I have twenty minutes,' she said. 'Tell me something that is not about work.'

Thomas looked at the paper on his desk.

Exit.

Cannot leave Azerbaijan.

'I passed the medical.'

'That is work.'

'I ate lunch.'

'That is biological maintenance.'

'I saw a travel poster for Istanbul.'

'Better. Are we going?'

'No.'

'Ah. I like Istanbul.'

'You have been?'

'Twice. Once for a conference, once for three days between contracts. I bought a scarf I never wear.'

'Naturally.'

'You say that like scarves are a moral category.'

He looked at the phrase sheet from Olesya's folder. Thank you. How much? Where is the receipt?

'Maybe after Basra,' Sarah said. 'We can go somewhere that is not connected to oil, consulting, or your habit of looking at cranes.'

'Maybe.'

'That is the sound of a man not booking a flight.'

He did not answer quickly enough.

'Thomas?'

'I am here.'

'Yes. And elsewhere.'

He folded the paper with the word Exit on it until the ink disappeared inside.

'Just tired.'

'Then sleep.'

'You have dinner.'

'I do. A long table full of people who think strategy is a personality.'

'Good luck.'

'To both of us.'

The call ended.

He remained in the study until the room cooled. Outside, the rain stopped and the street became bright with reflected lamps. He opened the spreadsheet and added a new column.

Restriction.

Then another.

Debt.

Then another.

Unknowns.

He did not enter the amount. Not yet. The number sat in his head with the rest of the unfinished things.

Near midnight, a message came from Olesya.

Do not send money for this.

He read it once. Twice.

Then he typed:

I did not offer.

He watched the sentence and heard how small it was.

He deleted it.

He wrote:

I understand.

The reply arrived after a minute.

No.

Then nothing.

Upstairs, the bedroom was still made on Sarah's side. Her suitcase had left a rectangle in the carpet near the wardrobe. Thomas brushed his teeth, turned off the lights, and lay down without undressing fully.

In the dark, the house held its shape around him. The radiator ticked. A car passed. Somewhere in the pipes, water moved through walls he did not open.

His phone lit the ceiling.

One more message.

School receipt Monday.

He placed the phone face down on the bedside table.

In Baku, the passport would still be behind glass. In Geneva, Sarah's would be in the front pocket of her handbag, ready for morning. In London, the Istanbul spreadsheet sat open in the study, waiting for figures.

Thomas closed his eyes.

The route had failed before anyone had packed.

Chapter 08

For three weeks, he did not send money.

The sentence existed nowhere except in Thomas's head. It had no line in the spreadsheet, no entry in the banking app, no message thread that could be shown to anyone. It was not a promise. It was not discipline. It was a space he left open after Olesya wrote, Do not send money for this, and after he answered, I understand, and after she replied, No.

The no had stayed with him longer than the rest.

He kept working.

Basra preparations took the shape of forms. Medical clearance. Security briefing. Equipment manifest. Rotation schedule. Emergency contact. Indemnity. Travel insurance. Each document asked for the name of a wife, the address of a house, the telephone number of someone who would answer if the company called at night.

Sarah's details filled the boxes easily.

Sarah Louise Ward.

Fulham address.

Mobile number.

Passport number.

Relationship: spouse.

The word looked clean on paper. It asked for nothing more than accuracy.

In the evenings, Sarah came and went through London with the speed of a person whose life had many doors and no single threshold. Geneva became Lisbon. Lisbon became Berlin. Berlin became London for one night, then Edinburgh for a client who had chosen February for a workshop on renewal and resilience. Her suitcase lived near the stairs with its front pocket open and its tag still looped from the last airline.

Thomas's own suitcase remained in the study.

He packed for Basra in stages. Boots first. Field shirts. Lightweight trousers. Spare glasses. Yellow vaccination booklet. A hard drive. The blue folder from Baku stayed on the desk, not in the suitcase and not in a drawer.

When he opened the study each morning, it was there.

At first Olesya sent only receipts.

School receipt Monday.

Paid. Receipt attached.

Medicine finished.

Then:

Anya's cough better.

Then nothing for six days.

The silence did not behave like absence. It behaved like a closed valve under pressure. Thomas checked the thread between meetings, while the kettle boiled, once in the corridor outside a training room where a man from security was explaining how to recognise improvised checkpoints. The last message remained Medicine finished. No question. No request. No opening.

On the seventh day, Sarah came home from Berlin with a paper bag of pastries and a scarf she said she had bought in a railway station to punish herself for being early.

'You are hovering again,' she said.

Thomas looked up from the study desk.

'Am I?'

'Yes. You do that thing with your shoulders when you are pretending a screen has not offended you.'

'Basra logistics.'

'Naturally.'

She dropped the pastry bag beside his laptop and leaned over his shoulder. He closed the spreadsheet without hurry. It was the Istanbul file, now renamed Travel admin and stripped of the hotel tabs. Only the columns remained: restriction, debt, unknowns.

Sarah glanced at the file name.

'More travel admin?'

'Old notes.'

'You should delete old notes. They breed.'

'Noted.'

She kissed the top of his head, tasted one pastry, made a face, and carried the bag downstairs.

Thomas reopened the file after she left.

The debt column remained blank.

He did not enter ten thousand manat. It was not a number yet. It was a border.

On the Friday before his final Basra briefing, Olesya wrote at 05:13 London time.

Thomas.

Nothing else.

He woke to the message and sat up before he had decided to move. Sarah slept on her side of the bed with one arm outside the duvet, her fingers open on the sheet. Her phone lay face down on the bedside table, charging, ready for the morning flight to Edinburgh.

Thomas took his phone into the bathroom and closed the door.

What happened?

The reply came while the extractor fan hummed above him.

The landlord came.

He waited.

Then:

He says two months or leave.

Thomas stood barefoot on the cold tile. The mirror above the sink showed his face in the dark glass, lit from below by the phone.

How much?

No answer.

He typed again:

Olesya. How much?

She sent a photograph.

It was not a formal notice. A sheet torn from a receipt pad, written in a thick blue pen. Figures. Dates. A name half legible. No official stamp. No signature that would survive a dispute. At the edge of the photograph, Thomas could see a strip of tablecloth, a cup, Anya's pencil case.

Another message:

School also. They say Monday.

Then:

I was not asking.

He sat on the closed toilet lid and enlarged the photograph until the numbers blurred. Rent arrears. Current month. School balance. Late fee. There was no paper in the bathroom, so he wrote them on the back of a shaving-foam box.

When he added them, the total surprised him by being possible.

That was the first danger.

He looked at the bathroom door. Behind it, the bedroom remained dark. Sarah would wake in twenty minutes, shower in twelve, dress in six, leave in eighteen. Her suitcase would click down the stairs. The house would hold her shape for an hour, then release it.

Thomas typed:

I can send this.

The reply came at once.

No.

Then:

I told you after you asked what happened.

The extractor fan cut out. The silence filled the bathroom.

He typed:

If the landlord comes Monday, where do you go?

No answer.

Where does Anya go?

The dots appeared, vanished, appeared again.

School.

Then:

After school I do not know.

Thomas set the phone on the edge of the sink and turned on the tap. Water hit porcelain, too loud in the small room. He cupped his hands beneath it and washed his face without soap. The cold made his teeth meet.

When he looked again, Olesya had sent one more line.

I am looking for work.

He turned off the tap.

By the time Sarah knocked on the bathroom door, he had opened the banking app.

'You alive in there?'

'Yes.'

'You have gone very quiet.'

'Email from Basra.'

'Before six? Charming.'

'Give me two minutes.'

'You have one. I need the shower and a face that can convince Edinburgh I slept.'

He closed the app, opened his work email, and unlocked the door.

Sarah stood in the hall wearing his old university T-shirt and a pair of suit trousers. Her hair was pinned up with a pencil.

'You look hunted,' she said.

'Bad night.'

'You slept.'

'Badly.'

She looked at him, then past him to the sink, where his phone lay face down beside the shaving foam.

'Basra already doing this to you?'

'Apparently.'

'Say no to something today.'

'I will.'

She touched his cheek with two fingers as she passed. The gesture was warm, quick, already on its way to the next task.

Downstairs, while Sarah made coffee in a travel mug and argued with a taxi driver over the phone, Thomas opened a bank transfer page on his laptop.

International transfer.

Recipient.

He had not made Olesya a saved recipient. The fact appeared as a small ceremony of restraint. Every previous envelope, advance, and receipt had passed through office machinery or cash in hand. This would be different. A private line across banks, borders, and explanations.

He typed her full name.

Olesya Petrova.

Country: Azerbaijan.

Bank name.

Account number.

SWIFT code.

He did not have the details.

He messaged:

Send your bank details.

The reply came:

No.

He wrote:

This is for rent and school. Not the debt.

She did not answer.

Sarah came into the study with her travel mug in one hand and her passport in the other.

'Taxi is early. This is either a miracle or a trap.'

'Safe trip.'

'It is Edinburgh. I will survive by sarcasm and conference biscuits.'

She stopped at the door.

'Are you coming down?'

'In a minute.'

'Thomas.'

He looked up.

'If you are already at your desk before I leave, you owe the day something better than spreadsheets.'

'I know.'

'Do you?'

'Sometimes.'

She shook her head, not unkindly, and left.

The front door closed. Her suitcase wheels crossed the threshold. The taxi door shut. The engine moved away.

The house waited.

Thomas looked at the blank recipient screen.

At 07:12, Olesya sent a photograph of a bank card partly covered by her thumb. The account number was visible. Then another message:

Only this. Not more.

He entered the details.

The bank asked for purpose.

Family support.

He changed it to:

Personal support.

Then:

Education.

The field rejected education without an institution name.

He selected:

Other.

The next page asked for a reference. He typed Anya school and rent. It looked too naked. He deleted it.

He typed:

Support.

Then deleted that too.

In the end he wrote:

February.

The bank calculated fees, exchange rate, estimated delivery. Three working days. He paid extra for same-day processing. The fee was indecent and immediately acceptable. Money took a faster road when it was paid to hurry.

Before he clicked submit, he sat with his hand on the trackpad.

The amount on the screen was larger than any sum he had given Olesya before. Not impossible. Not reckless. Not clean.

His phone lit.

From Sarah:

Boarding. Edinburgh has rain and moral superiority.

He typed:

Good luck.

Then he moved his hand back to the laptop and clicked Transfer.

The confirmation page appeared.

Processing.

He saved the PDF before the page could change. Then he printed it. The printer took the paper with a dry pull and released one white sheet bearing the bank's logo, Olesya's name, the amount, the exchange rate, and the reference February.

There it was.

The reply he had not written.

He photographed the confirmation and sent it to Olesya.

Sent. Same-day. Should arrive today.

For nine minutes nothing happened.

Then:

Thomas.

He waited.

You should not.

He typed:

It is done.

The next message took longer.

I will pay back.

He wrote:

No rush.

Then, seeing the softness of it, deleted it.

Keep receipts.

He sent that.

No reply.

At ten, he attended the Basra briefing. The conference room had a glass wall and a tray of untouched sandwiches curling at the corners. A security consultant with clipped hair spoke about movement protocols, convoy discipline, and the difference between routine and predictability.

Thomas took notes in the margin of his printed transfer confirmation.

Avoid fixed pattern.

Carry cash.

Document all expenditure.

Do not improvise alone.

He underlined the last one by accident.

'Mr Ward?'

He looked up.

The consultant had paused at the screen. A map of southern Iraq glowed behind him, roads marked in red and yellow.

'Your previous rotation was Azerbaijan?'

'Yes.'

'Then you know the principle. Local conditions decide what procedure cannot.'

'Yes.'

The man moved on.

Thomas folded the transfer confirmation in half and put it inside his notebook.

At eleven thirty, his phone rang with the bank's number.

He left the conference room before answering. The corridor outside smelled of carpet glue and coffee. Someone had left a tray of used cups on the narrow table by the lifts. Brown rings had dried around the rims.

'Mr Ward?'

'Speaking.'

'This is International Payments Verification. We are calling regarding a same-day transfer initiated this morning.'

The woman on the line had a voice designed to remove weather from a room.

'Yes.'

'For security, I need to confirm the recipient name.'

'Olesya Petrova.'

'And the destination country?'

'Azerbaijan.'

'And your relationship to the recipient?'

The corridor narrowed.

In the conference room, through the glass, the consultant moved a red pointer along a convoy route. Men in chairs leaned over their papers. One of them reached for a sandwich and changed his mind.

'Personal,' Thomas said.

'I am sorry, sir, I need a little more detail.'

'A friend.'

The word was both too small and too large.

'And the purpose of funds?'

'Rent and school fees.'

'For the recipient?'

'For her household.'

Keys clicked on the other end of the line.

'Do you have documentation relating to the payment?'

Thomas looked down at his notebook. The transfer confirmation was folded inside it. Beneath the cover, in his own hand: Avoid fixed pattern. Carry cash. Document all expenditure.

'Receipts will follow.'

'From the recipient?'

'Yes.'

'Please understand that payments to certain jurisdictions can be delayed by intermediary checks.'

'It is marked same-day.'

'Same-day means the instruction is prioritised. It does not guarantee settlement in the recipient account.'

He had known that. He had paid the fee anyway.

'Can you tell me if it will clear today?'

'I cannot guarantee that.'

'Can you tell me what is missing?'

'At this stage, nothing is missing. The payment is under routine review.'

Routine. The word stood up in the corridor with its clean shoes and empty hands.

'There is time sensitivity.'

'I will add a note to the file.'

'What does that do?'

The woman paused. Not long enough to be rude. Long enough to tell the truth by omission.

'It records the information.'

Thomas looked through the glass again. The map of Iraq glowed behind the consultant. Red roads. Yellow roads. Movement permitted, movement discouraged, movement at convoy discretion.

'Please record that rent and school payment are due today.'

'I will add that.'

'And that delay creates risk.'

'I can add that the payment is time-sensitive.'

'Add risk.'

Another pause.

'I can add time-sensitive.'

He closed his eyes for one breath.

'Fine.'

When the call ended, he stood in the corridor with the phone in his hand. The lifts opened. Two men came out laughing about something on a screen. One of them nodded at him. Thomas nodded back and returned to the briefing.

On his notepad, beneath Do not improvise alone, he wrote:

Routine review.

Then:

Time-sensitive.

Then, after a moment:

Risk.

At lunch, Sarah sent a photograph of a conference table in Edinburgh. Lanyards. Coffee cups. A plate of biscuits with only the plain ones left.

She wrote:

Proof of hardship.

He replied:

Severe.

She wrote:

Send aid.

He looked at the words until the screen dimmed.

Then he put the phone away.

Olesya did not message in the afternoon.

Thomas checked the banking portal at one fifteen.

Processing.

At one forty-eight.

Processing.

At two ten, the status changed to additional checks.

He copied the phrase into the spreadsheet and stared at it until the words separated from meaning. Additional checks. More glass. More rooms. More people behind counters deciding whether the shape of a need matched the shape of a field on a screen.

He opened Olesya's message thread.

Payment delayed by bank checks.

He did not send it.

He typed:

Still processing.

He did not send that either.

If she was at home, the message would only give the room another sound. If she was out with the landlord, it would arrive as nothing useful. If she was at the school, it would become one more small paper held in her hand while someone waited.

At two twenty-five, she wrote:

Any news?

He answered:

Bank reviewing. I called. Still today.

The lie was not in the words. It was in the certainty he placed between them.

Her reply:

Landlord comes at six.

Then:

School office closes five.

Thomas looked at the clock above the conference-room door. The second hand moved with a small, mechanical tick, each tick making the same promise to everyone in the building.

He wrote:

I am watching it.

The phrase was useless. It was also all he had.

The transfer status changed from processing to released at two forty-three. He refreshed the page three times before leaving it alone. Released did not mean received. It meant his bank had let go. Somewhere between London and Baku, the money was travelling through correspondent accounts, currency exchanges, compliance checks, settlement windows, rooms full of servers no one had to see.

It was easier to imagine a physical object.

An envelope passing from hand to hand.

At four, he walked home rather than taking the Underground. The air smelled of wet leaves and bus diesel. Schoolchildren moved in packs along the pavement, swinging bags, kicking at puddles, eating crisps from shared packets. Outside a florist, a man arranged tulips in buckets. Red, yellow, white. The stems were wrapped in brown paper near the base, all of them leaning towards the street.

Thomas stopped at a cash machine and checked his balance.

The transfer had already left.

The number on the screen looked smaller, but not wounded. His life had absorbed the loss without visible damage. The mortgage would be paid. The bills would clear. Sarah's taxi from Edinburgh airport would charge to the joint card. Basra would pay hardship allowance from the day he entered country.

In Baku, the same number would decide whether a child came home from school to a locked door.

He took the printed balance slip and folded it into his pocket.

At home, he placed the transfer confirmation on the study desk beside the Istanbul spreadsheet. He should have shredded the spreadsheet. He slid the confirmation on top of it. The new paper covered the old route.

At five twelve, Olesya sent a photograph of the school office door.

Closed.

The word had been printed on a white sheet and taped to the glass from inside. Behind it, a corridor ran back into a building the colour of old chalk. A row of children's coats hung along one wall. The photograph was taken at an angle, quickly, before someone could ask why.

Thomas called her.

She did not answer.

He typed:

Tomorrow morning.

No reply.

At five thirty-nine, she sent:

Landlord waiting.

No punctuation. No photograph.

He opened the banking portal again.

Released.

Still released.

He called the bank and reached a different voice.

'The funds have left us, Mr Ward.'

'Where are they?'

'With the correspondent bank or the recipient bank.'

'Which?'

'We do not have visibility at that stage.'

A sound rose in his throat and stopped. Visibility. The money had become invisible at the exact moment he needed to see it.

'Can you recall it?'

'A recall may take several business days.'

'I do not want to recall it.'

'Then the payment is proceeding.'

'It is not arriving.'

'It may still arrive today.'

May. Still. Today.

He thanked her. Politeness ended the call more quickly than saying what the words were worth.

At six, no message came.

At six ten, Sarah sent:

Dinner with client. Pray for me.

He replied:

Good luck.

At six fourteen, Olesya wrote:

He is in stair.

Thomas stood from the desk. The chair rolled back and struck the wall. He picked up the phone, then put it down, then picked it up again.

Tell him transfer is released.

He sent the confirmation PDF again.

Show him this.

The ticks appeared. Delivered. Read.

No reply.

He stayed standing.

The house had become too quiet for London. Even the buses seemed to pass at a distance.

At six twenty-seven, she wrote:

He says paper is not money.

Thomas looked at the screen. In Baku, a man stood in a stairwell saying the one true thing about the system.

He typed:

I can speak to him.

Her answer:

No.

Then:

Anya home.

Then nothing.

For the next forty minutes he kept the banking portal open on the laptop and Olesya's thread open on the phone. He did not sit. He moved between desk and window, window and door, door and desk. Outside, people came home with shopping bags, gym bags, flowers, laptop cases. Lives returning to rooms that opened when keys turned.

At seven oh nine, Olesya wrote:

Received.

One word.

Then, ahead of his answer:

Landlord paid.

Then:

School paid.

A photograph arrived.

Two receipts on her kitchen table. The landlord's slip was still informal, but this time it had a signature and a date. The school receipt bore a stamp in blue ink. Anya's exercise book sat beside it, closed. The pencil case lay straight along the edge of the table.

Thomas enlarged the image.

The school stamp had caught the paper unevenly. Half dark, half pale.

Then another photograph.

Anya in a school coat, standing by the flat door. The photograph was slightly blurred. She was looking away from the camera, one hand on the strap of her bag. Her hair was tied back better than before. On the wall behind her, the wallpaper had lifted again near the taped window.

No caption.

Thomas looked at the child's face for longer than the image needed.

Then a message:

She knows.

He typed:

Knows what?

The answer came:

That rent is paid.

Thomas sat back.

The house had gone dark without him noticing. The study window reflected the room: his face, the desk lamp, the paper stacks, the blue folder, the phone in his hand.

He typed:

Good.

He deleted it.

He typed:

I am glad.

He deleted that too.

After a minute, he wrote:

Keep both receipts.

He sent it.

The response came at once.

I have.

Then:

Thank you.

No name.

No further line.

Thomas printed the transfer confirmation again.

He did not know why until the page was in his hand. One copy for records. One copy for the thing records did not hold. He placed the second copy in the blue folder from Baku, behind the phrase sheet, where Thank you and How much and Where is the receipt were written in Olesya's narrow hand.

The folder no longer belonged to travel.

At eight, Sarah called from Edinburgh.

'I am in a hotel room with a view of an extractor vent,' she said.

'Luxury.'

'And a minibar that thinks almonds are jewellery.'

'Do not eat the almonds.'

'Too late.'

He heard her shoes come off, one after the other, dropping onto carpet.

'How was the Basra briefing?'

'Predictable.'

'That sounds comforting.'

'They warned us not to become predictable.'

'Then it was unpredictable advice about predictability.'

'Something like that.'

She laughed softly. The sound loosened something in the room, then left it looser than before.

'You sound better,' she said.

'Do I?'

'Less like a spreadsheet with a pulse.'

He looked at the blue folder.

'Long day.'

'Mine too. A man used the phrase cultural alignment six times before lunch. I counted for survival.'

'Did it work?'

'Barely.'

She paused.

'Come up this weekend if you want. Edinburgh is wet, but in a theatrical way.'

'I have packing.'

'For Iraq, or for some internal exile I have not been briefed on?'

'Iraq.'

'Good. Pack socks. You always forget socks.'

'I do not.'

'You always buy socks at airports. It is your most consistent travel behaviour.'

They spoke for another seven minutes. Weather. A client dinner. Basra dates. Sarah's plan to come to the compound after her spring contracts, if the company housing was ready and the air route held. A visible future, provisional but named.

When the call ended, Thomas remained seated with the phone in his hand.

Olesya's message thread sat above Sarah's in the list.

Received.

Landlord paid.

School paid.

She knows.

Keep both receipts.

I have.

Thank you.

The sequence was cleaner than conversation. A problem had entered. A sum had moved. Paper had answered. The room in Baku had stayed available to the child who needed to sleep there.

He read the lines again. His breathing slowed around the order of them.

Problem. Sum. Receipt.

The shape held.

At midnight, he opened the spreadsheet.

Restriction.

Debt.

Unknowns.

He added another column.

Transfers.

Under it, he entered the date, the amount, the fee, the exchange rate, and the reference.

February.

Then he added notes.

Rent two months. School balance. Same-day. Receipts received.

He sat with the cursor blinking in the next empty row.

The transfer had solved nothing about the passport. It had not moved Olesya one mile closer to Istanbul. It had not touched the old debt. It had not explained Sarah, Basra, marriage, or the distance between a wife with a passport in Edinburgh and a woman with a child in a flat in Baku.

But the landlord had been paid.

The school had been paid.

Anya had gone home.

He saved the spreadsheet.

In Baku, Olesya opened the tin after Anya had fallen asleep. The new rent receipt went beneath the older one. The school receipt went into a separate clear sleeve, behind Anya's certificates and the first school photograph Thomas had never seen.

The flat had settled into its night sounds. The small refrigerator clicked, stopped, clicked again. Water moved somewhere inside the wall. In the other room, Anya turned once under the blanket and coughed without waking. Olesya waited until the cough passed before moving the papers.

The landlord's receipt had been written in the stairwell. His pen had pressed hard enough to bruise the paper. He had counted the cash twice, looked once at the confirmation on her phone, and then at Thomas's name. Not asked. Only looked. The school receipt was cleaner. Printed, stamped, signed by a woman who had not raised her eyes when Olesya paid the balance after office hours through the side door.

Olesya flattened both receipts beneath her palm.

The money had entered the day as delay, then paper, then cash, then stamps.

Now it had to become something else.

She smoothed the transfer confirmation on the table. He had sent it as an image, but she had printed it at a copy shop before paying the landlord. The paper had a faint vertical line where the printer toner had failed.

She did not put it with the rent.

She did not put it with the school.

For a while it remained alone on the plastic tablecloth, bank logo at the top, his name in one box, hers in another, the amount between them.

Then she folded it once and placed it behind the blue folder's fresh sleeve.

Not proof of payment.

Not exactly.

Chapter 09

Iraq did not begin with heat.

It began with dust.

Dust on the charter steps before Thomas had both feet on the ground. Dust lifting behind the baggage truck. Dust caught in the teeth of the perimeter fence, in the seams of the transport seats, in the damp at the corner of his eye. The airport buildings sat low and beige under a white sky. Men in uniforms moved between barriers with rifles slung across their chests. A convoy coordinator read names from a clipboard and ticked them off with a red pen.

Basra did not receive people. It processed them.

Thomas stood with the other arrivals beside a row of hard cases and watched his name become a tick. The air tasted of diesel and metal. Somewhere beyond the wire, a generator laboured with a thick, irregular cough.

'Ward,' the coordinator said.

'Here.'

'Majnoon compound. Vehicle three.'

Thomas lifted his bag.

'Helmet on before gate.'

'Yes.'

The helmet had been issued in Dubai, along with body armour, emergency card, radio instructions, and a laminated sheet explaining what to do if the convoy stopped unexpectedly. Do not exit the vehicle unless instructed. Keep head below window line. Follow security personnel. Do not improvise.

The final instruction had been printed in bold.

Inside the armoured vehicle, the windows were thick enough to tint the road. Basra passed in interrupted fragments: concrete walls, shopfronts, satellite dishes, a boy pushing a cart of gas cylinders, water pooled in tyre ruts, posters peeling from a blast wall. The driver did not speak. The security man in the front passenger seat watched the road with his rifle resting between his knees.

Thomas held his phone in his hand though there was no signal.

At the compound gate, two barriers lifted and one closed behind them before the next opened. Movement happened in boxes. Gate. Check. Barrier. Mirror under vehicle. Badge. Search. Barrier. Release.

The compound had lawns.

That was the first obscenity of it. Green strips under sprinklers. White modular buildings. Gravel paths. A dining hall with tinted windows. A gym. A clinic. A noticeboard advertising quiz night, yoga, convoy timings, and a warning about scorpions in boots.

His room was narrow and clean. Bed, desk, wardrobe, metal chair, small refrigerator, air conditioner already rattling at the wall. A laminated evacuation map was pinned beside the door. The window did not open.

He unpacked in the order the room allowed.

Boots under the bed. Field shirts on hangers. Laptop on desk. Passport in the metal lockbox. Blue folder in the top drawer.

He put it there, then took it out again.

There was no reason to keep Olesya's folder in Iraq.

That was why it stayed.

The first week ran on induction.

Security briefing. Site orientation. Permit system. Radio protocol. Emergency muster. Hydration requirement. Heat injury prevention. Access badge. Vehicle schedule. Rig familiarisation. The language of the place was restriction softened into procedure.

He learned the daily pattern. Breakfast before six. Convoy at six thirty. Operations office by seven. Field checks before the heat rose. Lunch in the dining hall under cold air and fluorescent lights. Calls with London in the late afternoon. Reports after dinner. Sleep in short pieces beneath the air conditioner.

At night, the compound hummed.

Generators. Water pumps. Air-conditioning units. Distant engines beyond the wall. Men laughing outside the smoking shelter. Doors opening and closing along the corridor with the same tired click.

Sarah called on the fourth night from London.

'You look boiled,' she said.

'I am indoors.'

'Then Iraq has found a way in.'

He had propped the laptop on two technical manuals to improve the camera angle. Behind Sarah, their Fulham kitchen was bright and narrow, the kettle steaming behind her shoulder. She had her hair tied up and a notebook open beside the laptop.

'How is the compound?'

'Secure.'

'That is not a description.'

'It has lawns.'

'Lawns?'

'Yes.'

'That is sinister.'

'Very.'

She wrote something in her notebook. 'Company housing sent me the arrival options. If I come after Lisbon, I can route through Dubai and land on the Tuesday convoy. They want my passport scan again.'

'They already have it.'

'Everyone always has it and no one can find it.'

She held the passport up to the camera, then put it beside her notebook.

'I will send the scan tonight.'

'Are you sure you want to come?'

'Want is a strong word. I want a great many things. Some of them involve a bath and a door that locks. But if you are there six months, I can work from there for a while.'

'It is restricted.'

'So is everywhere. Some places are just honest about it.'

Thomas looked at the window that did not open.

'Send the dates when confirmed,' he said.

'I will. And you send proof that you are eating something green.'

'There are lawns.'

'Do not eat the lawns.'

Her smile broke up slightly as the connection stuttered.

After the call, he opened the spreadsheet.

Restriction.

Debt.

Unknowns.

Transfers.

The last row still held February. Rent two months. School balance. Same-day. Receipts received.

Olesya had not asked again. She sent small practical updates, each one pared down before it reached him.

Anya back at school full week.

Clinic says inhaler morning and night.

New work possible. Cash weekly.

The job was at a stationery wholesaler near the bus depot. She packed boxes, checked delivery slips, and reconciled handwritten stock lists at a counter with no chair. It paid every Friday in cash. No contract. No insurance. No sick pay. No questions.

Thomas read the message twice.

He typed:

Do they give receipts?

Olesya replied:

For boxes?

He deleted his next question.

For two months, the system held at the edges.

He sent small amounts when a defined need appeared. Inhaler refill. School workbook. Winter electricity balance. Shoes after Anya's pair split at the sole. Each transfer had a note in the spreadsheet, each receipt arrived flattened on Olesya's table with the same care as before.

It should have been smaller than the first wire.

It did not.

Repetition made the line stronger.

In May, the compound clinic issued him a laminated card for heat stress.

He was meant to carry it in his shirt pocket during field visits. The front listed symptoms in block capitals. Dizziness. Confusion. Weakness. Collapse. The back gave actions. Move to shade. Remove excess clothing. Cool skin. Seek medical assessment.

He kept it beside his access badge and forgot it twice.

The clinic nurse stopped him at the dining hall door on the third morning.

'Card,' she said.

Thomas touched his empty pocket.

'Desk.'

'Your body is not on your desk.'

'Noted.'

'Noted does not lower core temperature.'

She handed him a spare card with the briskness of someone who had seen clever men become burdens.

That night, Olesya sent a photograph of Anya's inhaler box.

The dosage label had been torn at one corner. Beside it lay a pharmacy receipt and a strip of tablets Thomas could not read.

Doctor changed medicine.

He enlarged the label until the letters blurred.

Why?

Chest tight after school.

Clinic?

Yes.

He waited for the next line.

Cost more.

There it was. Not a request. A fact placed where a request would have been if she trusted need to arrive cleanly.

Send receipt, he typed.

Already did.

He looked again. The amount was on the pharmacy slip, bottom right, half hidden by the inhaler box. He entered it in the spreadsheet under medical. Then he sent the sum, plus enough for the next refill. The platform fee appeared, neat and shameless.

Olesya wrote:

Too much.

He answered:

Next refill.

For a long time there was no reply.

Then:

She has sports day next week. She cannot run.

Thomas looked at the heat-stress card beside his keyboard. Dizziness. Confusion. Weakness. Collapse. A body reduced to symptoms and action steps.

Ask doctor for note.

School wants official.

Then get official.

They charge for official.

He sent more.

The reply arrived after midnight.

Receipt tomorrow.

No thanks. No protest. A procedure accepted under pressure.

In the morning, Sarah emailed from London with her Iraq travel packet.

Subject: If I am going to live behind walls, I want decent coffee.

Attached were passport scan, vaccination record, work schedule, client call windows, and a list of things she intended to bring if the company housing allowed them: French press, running shoes, small speaker, two linen shirts, one black dress for whatever counted as formal inside a compound, and a framed photograph from the hall. Even modular prisons deserve evidence of prior civilisation, she wrote.

Thomas opened the files before breakfast.

Everything had a name. Sarah_Ward_Passport.pdf. Sarah_Ward_Vaccinations.pdf. Client_Calls_April_May.xlsx. Her documents behaved when summoned. They came clean, labelled, legible, complete.

Olesya's receipts arrived as photographs taken under kitchen light.

Rent paper beside a cup.

Clinic slip beside Anya's workbook.

Pharmacy bill held under Olesya's thumb.

Doctor note with a corner folded where it had been carried in a bag.

Thomas saved them in a folder called Baku medical, then changed it to Baku support, then changed it again to O admin.

The third name stayed.

On the first Friday of May, Olesya's new job paid her late.

She did not say this directly.

She sent a photograph of the bus card balance. Then:

Can walk Monday if not too hot.

He was in the field office when he read it. Outside, a mechanic was washing dust from a valve skid with a hose that turned the ground to paste. The thermometer near the door read forty-one.

Do not walk.

No reply.

He sent a small sum marked transport.

She replied:

This is foolish.

He wrote:

Heat is foolish.

Three dots appeared.

Then:

I will tell Anya you said this.

He left the message unanswered. It had a warmth he did not know where to put.

The bus receipt came the next day. Two trips. One for Olesya. One for Anya. The balance remaining was visible on the slip.

He entered it.

Transport.

By then the spreadsheet had changed character.

It was no longer a record of exceptions. It had begun to resemble a service log. Date. Category. Amount. Fee. Receipt. Notes. Rent. School. Medicine. Transport. Clinic. Refill. Official letter. Each line closed when paper arrived.

At work, he trusted closed lines.

A valve opened or it did not. A pressure test passed or failed. A cargo cleared or remained held. Men could argue, but the system recorded state.

Olesya's life began to present itself to him in the same grammar.

Need.

Transfer.

Receipt.

State changed.

The danger was not hidden. It was there in every clean row.

He liked rows that closed.

One evening, after a long call about corrosion allowance, Rauf messaged him from Baku.

I hear you are enjoying Iraq.

Thomas replied:

Enjoying is not the word.

Rauf:

Good. Then you understand it.

Thomas watched the message for a while, then typed:

How is the office?

Rauf answered after several minutes.

Less tidy. Your replacement prints emails.

Then:

Olesya left?

Thomas sat back.

Left what?

The dots appeared, vanished, appeared again.

Office. She is not with us now.

He read it twice.

When?

End of March. Temporary contract ended. You did not know?

Thomas looked at the spreadsheet. Inhaler. School note. Transport. The entries after March had no salary beside them, no office structure beneath them, no Rauf within reach.

No, he wrote.

Rauf replied:

Ah.

Then nothing.

The compound air conditioner rattled above him. Down the corridor, someone laughed at a film playing too loudly.

Thomas opened Olesya's thread.

Are you working today?

The answer came the next morning.

Yes.

Where?

Packing boxes.

Contract?

Cash.

He typed:

Why did you not tell me about the office?

The reply did not come until evening.

It ended.

He waited.

Then:

I found other work.

There was no apology in it. No invitation to ask what had happened in the gap. She had removed the missing month from the conversation as neatly as she removed an old receipt from a file.

Thomas opened the spreadsheet and added a new column.

Employment.

For every line after March he entered:

Cash.

The word looked insufficient.

By April, Thomas could read the shape of Olesya's messages before opening them. Thomas meant the matter had already passed the point where she could contain it. A receipt first meant closure by her own hand. Anya without a receipt meant something waiting.

On a Sunday evening, she wrote:

Anya's breathing not good.

Thomas was in the operations office with the night-shift superintendent, reviewing a stuck valve on a water injection line. Outside, the dust had risen during sunset and left a film on the windows. The office smelled of printer heat, old coffee, and the faint sourness of sweat trapped in work shirts.

He stepped into the corridor before replying.

Clinic?

No answer for six minutes.

Then:

Tomorrow.

He typed:

Today.

The dots appeared, vanished, appeared again.

It is Sunday.

Private clinic emergency.

Too expensive.

He wrote:

Go. I will send.

The reply came fast.

No.

Then:

If needed tomorrow.

He looked through the corridor glass at the superintendent leaning over the valve report, red pen in hand. A problem waiting for a decision.

Thomas typed:

It is needed today.

He sent an amount before she answered.

The payment platform showed the fee, the exchange rate, the recipient name. He pressed confirm. The screen turned white, then returned with a reference number.

He sent the screenshot.

Clinic today.

Olesya did not reply for twenty minutes.

When she did, the message was only:

We are going.

The next morning, he woke before five to a dust storm.

The world outside the sealed window had disappeared. The compound lights glowed in a brown fog. Fine grit hissed against the building. In the corridor, doors opened and closed as men checked convoy status on their phones.

By six, all movement was suspended.

The announcement came over the radio network and then through email. No road travel until visibility improved. Site teams to remain in accommodation blocks. Essential operations only by internal shuttle.

Thomas dressed anyway.

In the dining hall, the air-conditioning fought dust that came in on boots and shoulders. Men sat over eggs and tea, looking at the windows. Someone joked about being trapped in a snow globe built by a refinery.

Thomas's phone rang at six forty-two.

Olesya.

He answered before the second vibration.

'Tell me.'

Her breath was in the phone first. Not crying. Not words. Air failing to become either.

'Anya is worse.'

'Where are you?'

'State hospital.'

'Why state?'

'The private clinic sent us. They said attack too strong. Need hospital.'

The dining hall noise thinned around him.

'Is she admitted?'

'They put her in corridor. No beds.'

Something clattered behind her. Wheels. Metal. A voice shouting numbers.

'Can she breathe?'

'They gave oxygen. It stops, then starts. Thomas, they say wait.'

He stood. The chair legs scraped the floor.

'Go back to the private clinic.'

'They sent us away.'

'Call them. Tell them I will pay for a room and respiratory doctor.'

'They will not take her if hospital started.'

'Then ask what deposit changes that.'

She said something away from the phone. Russian, then Azeri, then Russian again. The languages crossed each other in the receiver.

Thomas walked out of the dining hall and into the corridor. Dust had gathered along the bottom of the door in a thin brown line.

'Olesya.'

'They say maybe private hospital, not clinic.'

'Name.'

'Caspian Medical.'

'Send address. Send billing contact. Send whatever they give you.'

'I do not have paper.'

'Then photograph the wall.'

The line stayed open. He heard footsteps, a cough, the squeak of wheels, Anya's name once, spoken away from the phone.

Then the call ended.

Thomas stood in the corridor with the phone in his hand.

At seven ten, Olesya sent three photographs.

A hospital corridor with green walls and metal chairs.

A signboard listing departments in Azeri, Russian, English.

A handwritten number on the back of an old receipt.

Below them:

They say deposit first.

He opened the payment platform. The amount she sent was not large by corporate standards. It was large in the way a door is large when it will not open.

The platform rejected the transfer.

Daily limit.

He stared at the message. Then tried a lower amount.

Rejected. Limit reached by previous transfer.

He opened his bank. International transfer. New recipient: Caspian Medical Services. Country: Azerbaijan. Purpose: medical.

The form asked for invoice number.

He had none.

He typed Anya Petrova deposit.

The form asked for patient relationship.

He sat still for one second too long.

Other.

The bank asked for supporting documentation.

He uploaded the photograph of the signboard, then the handwritten number, then the corridor.

The submit button stayed grey.

Required document missing.

He went to the operations office.

The compound outside had become a moving wall of dust. Men crossed between buildings with scarves over their mouths. The office generator had kicked into a higher pitch. Inside, only half the desks were occupied. The night superintendent looked up from a radio log.

'Roads are shut,' he said.

'I need the satellite line.'

'For work?'

'Medical.'

The man looked at him for one beat, then pointed to the small side office. 'Dial nine first.'

Thomas closed the door.

He called the number from the receipt.
