The first time, no one answered.

The second time, a woman answered in Russian.

'English,' Thomas said. 'Please. English.'

The phone moved. Voices. A hand over the receiver. Then a man came on.

'Caspian Medical.'

'My name is Thomas Ward. I am calling about Anya Petrova. She is being transferred from the state hospital. I need to pay the deposit.'

'Family?'

'I am responsible for payment.'

'Family?'

The question returned with the same weight.

'No.'

'Then we need family.'

'Her mother is there.'

'Mother signs. Payment before admission.'

'Send invoice.'

'We cannot make invoice before admission.'

'You cannot admit before payment.'

'Correct.'

Thomas pressed his free hand flat against the desk. Its surface was gritty.

'Then give me bank details.'

The man gave them slowly. Thomas wrote each line in block letters. Bank name. Account number. Tax number. Reference.

'How fast can you see payment?'

'When it arrives.'

'I can send confirmation.'

'Confirmation is not funds.'

The same sentence as the landlord, dressed in another voice.

'If I send confirmation, will you move her from the corridor?'

The man covered the receiver again. Thomas heard the room on the other side, phones and voices and one long electronic beep.

'Send confirmation to WhatsApp,' the man said. 'Doctor will decide.'

Thomas took the number.

He could not make the bank form accept the hospital without an invoice, so he used the payment platform in pieces. Three transfers. Different references. Medical deposit. Treatment. Oxygen. Each fee cut into the amount. Each confirmation arrived as its own PDF.

He sent all three to the WhatsApp number.

Then to Olesya.

Then he called the hospital again.

'Received confirmation,' the man said.

'Move her.'

'Doctor will decide.'

'Get the doctor.'

'Sir.'

'Get the doctor.'

The man's voice cooled. 'This is not England.'

'No,' Thomas said. 'It is a hospital.'

The line went quiet.

When the man came back, he said, 'Mother is signing.'

Thomas put the phone down carefully.

His hands had left damp marks in the dust on the desk.

For the next two hours, nothing worked except waiting.

The dust storm held the compound closed. The operations team argued over pressure readings from a field they could not reach. The satellite internet flickered. Sarah sent a message from London.

Housing confirmed Tuesday convoy if I come next month. Need your compound address exactly.

Thomas copied the address from his induction pack and sent it.

Sarah replied:

That sounds like a prison pretending to be a hotel.

He wrote:

Accurate.

Then the site manager came in looking for him.

'Valve meeting. Five minutes.'

'I need ten.'

'The well does not.'

Thomas looked at his phone. No message from Olesya. No change on the payment platform. No second call from the hospital.

He followed the site manager to the conference room.

The video link to the field team was poor. Dust turned every face into a brown smear. Men spoke over one another from three locations. The stuck valve had become a production issue, then a scheduling issue, then a cost issue. Someone in Dubai wanted a decision about whether to defer the line until visibility improved. Someone at the field wanted permission to send a mechanic before the dust settled.

Thomas listened with his phone face down beside his notebook.

'We wait,' he said.

The Dubai manager frowned through the screen. 'That costs us twelve hours.'

'Sending a mechanic blind costs us more.'

'We have pressure margins.'

'We also have people.'

The room quietened for one beat.

The site manager looked at him. 'So we hold?'

'We hold until visibility is back above threshold. Then we inspect before restarting.'

The decision moved into the minutes. Action assigned. Time stamped. Accepted.

His phone vibrated.

He did not touch it until the meeting ended.

Olesya had sent:

They ask previous medicine.

Then a photograph of the inhaler box from the flat, taken at an angle in poor light. The label was visible. The dosage was not.

He went back to his office and searched the O admin folder. Pharmacy receipt. Clinic note. Doctor note for school. Inhaler refill. Each photograph had been saved for accounting and now became medical history.

He sent four images.

Show doctor.

She replied:

He wants name.

Thomas zoomed in until the prescription line became legible.

Budesonide. Salbutamol.

He typed both words slowly.

Then:

Tell him refill every month. Worse with dust and running.

She did not answer.

He looked out at the compound. Dust had sealed itself to the glass. Men moved outside as pale shapes. The lawn sprinklers had stopped.

At ten thirty, the hospital number called.

'Mr Thomas?'

'Yes.'

'Doctor wants to know if child has allergy.'

'Ask her mother.'

'Mother says no. We ask payer for file.'

Payer.

The word entered the room without knocking.

'I do not have a full file.'

'You sent medicines.'

'Those are all I have.'

'Okay.'

'How is she?'

'Doctor reviewing.'

'Is she breathing better?'

The line carried a pause, a hand covering the receiver, a question passed to someone else.

'Oxygen continues.'

'That is not an answer.'

'It is answer now.'

The line ended.

Thomas wrote oxygen continues in the notebook under the valve decision. Then he drew a line through it. Then he wrote it again on a clean page.

At eleven, Olesya sent a photograph of her hand holding a consent form. Only the lower part was visible. Her signature sat near the bottom in a script he had seen on courier books, salary forms, receipts, school slips. A same hand, moved through too many kinds of permission.

He typed:

What did you sign?

Treatment.

Which treatment?

Nebuliser. Observation. Maybe injection.

Ask name.

I asked.

And?

She sent a photograph of a doctor's handwriting. Thomas could read none of it.

He forwarded it to the compound clinic nurse.

Can you read this?

Her answer came five minutes later.

Steroid. Bronchodilator. Standard for asthma. Why?

He wrote:

Friend's child.

The nurse replied:

Then tell friend child needs a doctor, not WhatsApp medicine.

He put the phone down.

By then, the conference-room valve decision had already become an email chain. Men copied him on objections, confirmations, modified schedules. The line could wait. It had pressure margins. It had procedures. It had people standing around it with radios and authority.

In Baku, Olesya waited beside a bed that did not yet belong to Anya.

Olesya sent:

They moved her.

Then:

Room.

Then a photograph.

Anya lay in a narrow hospital bed with a white sheet pulled to her chest. A clear mask covered her nose and mouth. Her hair had been tied back, but strands stuck to her forehead. The wall behind her was clean, painted pale blue. A monitor stood beside the bed. On the small table, a bottle of water, a folded jumper, and Olesya's phone charger lay in a line.

Thomas enlarged the photograph.

Anya's eyes were closed.

He looked at the oxygen tube. The clip on her finger. The clean sheet. The space around the bed where no one else waited on a chair.

The system had produced a room.

He saved the photograph, then immediately unsaved it, then saved it again under the hospital folder he had not meant to create.

Olesya wrote:

Doctor says night observation.

He answered:

Send invoice when they give it.

The reply came after several minutes.

Yes.

Then:

She is sleeping.

He looked at the words.

The dust scraped against the window.

At lunch, the dining hall served rice, chicken, cucumber, and oranges cut into quarters. Thomas ate standing near the coffee machine, phone on the counter beside his tray. The superintendent came in, saw his face, and did not make the joke he had been forming.

'Medical sorted?'

'For now.'

'Good.'

The man poured coffee and left.

For now. The only honest time period in the oil fields.

By late afternoon the storm began to thin. The compound emerged in pieces: the fence, the gravel path, the clinic sign, a row of boots left outside the accommodation block. Vehicles remained grounded. Dust lay over the lawns and turned them the colour of old canvas.

Sarah called at five.

'I have ten minutes before a client dinner,' she said. 'Tell me whether I should pack anything civilised for Iraq.'

'Define civilised.'

'Shoes that are not trainers.'

'Bring one pair.'

'One pair. This really is prison.'

'A prison with lawns.'

'The worst kind.'

She laughed. Behind her, he heard restaurant noise, cutlery, English voices, a door opening to traffic.

'You sound tired,' she said.

'Dust storm.'

'Are you inside?'

'Inside the wire.'

'That is not the same answer.'

'It is the answer I have.'

There was a pause.

'I sent the passport scan again,' she said. 'Housing says the Tuesday convoy is possible. I may arrive two days before your next field rotation.'

'Send me the itinerary.'

'Already did.'

An email appeared while she spoke. Sarah Ward travel pack. Dubai to Basra. Convoy Tuesday. Accommodation request attached.

'Got it.'

'Efficient wife.'

'Very.'

She smiled at him through the screen. 'Do not sound so surprised.'

'Never.'

'I have to go. Eat something. You look like the air has been sandpapering you.'

'Good dinner.'

'Unlikely.'

The call ended.

Thomas opened Sarah's itinerary.

Everything was present. Passport scan. Visa approval. Flight details. Company hotel in Dubai. Convoy number. Accommodation block request. Emergency contact. Dietary note, none. Her movement had formed itself into a packet the company could process without hesitation.

He downloaded it and saved it under Sarah Iraq.

Then he opened Olesya's thread.

The invoice arrived at six twelve.

The hospital had itemised the first night. Admission. Oxygen. Nebuliser. Medication. Respiratory consultation. Monitoring. Deposit applied. Balance due before discharge.

At the bottom was a stamp.

Blue.

The balance was higher than the money already sent.

Thomas opened the exchange-rate page.

Manat to pound.

Pound to manat.

Fees.

Same-day limits.

He moved from Sarah's itinerary to the hospital invoice and back again, two documents open side by side on the compound desk. One brought his wife through official channels towards him. The other kept a child breathing in a room he had paid into existence.

Outside, the last of the dust moved against the window in a thin brown veil.

He entered the new amount into the spreadsheet.

Hospital balance.

Then he checked the exchange rate again.

Chapter 10

The first monthly transfer did not look like a decision.

It looked like housekeeping.

Thomas made it on a Tuesday evening after the dust had settled and the compound had resumed its ordinary noise. Air conditioners rattled along the accommodation block. Men came back from the dining hall carrying paper cups and bruised fruit. Outside, the gravel paths held the day's heat. The lawns had been washed clean by sprinklers and already carried a new skin of dust.

In his room, Sarah's suitcase stood open beside the wardrobe.

She had arrived two days earlier on the Tuesday convoy, exactly as her itinerary had promised. Her passport had passed through three desks, two scanners, one armoured vehicle, and the compound reception without argument. She had been issued a badge, a room key, a site briefing, and a welcome pack containing a map, a dining schedule, and a warning about shaking out shoes.

By the second evening, she had rearranged the room.

Her laptop sat on the desk with a folding stand beneath it. His notebooks had moved to the shelf. The kettle had been placed near the socket that did not spark. Her shirts hung by colour. His field trousers stayed rolled in the lower drawer. No one, she said, needed to look at that much beige.

The room was still narrow.

It now had a centre.

'You have dust in your keyboard,' she said.

'Everything has dust in it.'

'Not everything. My suitcase is making a heroic stand.'

'Give it time.'

She was sitting cross-legged on the bed, laptop open, headphones around her neck. A client deck glowed on the screen. The words strategic capability framework sat above a diagram of boxes connected by arrows.

Thomas sat at the desk with his own laptop angled away from her.

Olesya's hospital invoice was open beside the spreadsheet.

The asthma attack had not ended cleanly. Nothing medical did. There had been discharge medication, one follow-up appointment, a nebuliser rental, and a school note stating that Anya was excused from physical exercise until reviewed. Each item came with paper. Each paper came with an amount.

The final hospital balance had cleared.

The room had existed. Anya had slept in it. Olesya had sent the discharge slip with the blue stamp at the bottom and the hospital bracelet lying beside it, cut open and flattened.

After that, there had been three days without a request.

Then:

Need inhaler refill next week. I will cover.

He had read it during a field-status call and answered after the meeting:

Send amount.

Olesya replied:

No. I said I cover.

He typed:

This should be budgeted.

He did not send it.

Budgeted was not a word that belonged in her kitchen.

He opened the spreadsheet.

Rent. School. Medicine. Transport. Clinic. Hospital. Refill. Official letter.

The rows no longer behaved like emergencies. They had begun repeating. Rent returned. Medicine returned. Transport returned. School appeared in uneven pieces, then larger ones. He could predict the next month before it arrived.

That was the second danger.

He created a new sheet.

Monthly.

Sarah spoke without looking up from her screen.

'If you are working, I am judging you.'

'You are also working.'

'Yes, but I am a hypocrite with a deadline.'

'That is a recognised category.'

'In consulting, it is most of us.'

She clicked through a slide, frowned, and deleted a line.

'What are you doing?'

'Budget.'

'Our budget?'

'Mine.'

'That sounds ominous.'

'It is mostly boring.'

'Boring can be ominous. It is where the serious things hide.'

She returned to the deck before he had to answer.

Thomas entered categories into the new sheet.

Rent.

Food.

School.

Medicine.

Transport.

Contingency.

The last word sat at the bottom looking more honest than the others.

He used conservative amounts. Then realistic ones. Then the amounts Olesya's receipts had already shown him. The total came out lower than expected, then higher after he included fees, then manageable after he removed any attempt to clear the old debt.

He did not touch the debt column.

He selected the first day of the next month and set a reminder.

Then he opened the transfer platform and scheduled nothing.

Scheduling would have made it visible in a way he was not ready for. He entered the amount in his calendar under a name that sounded like work.

Baku admin.

He looked at it for a while.

Then he changed it.

O admin.

The shorter name gave away less and meant more.

At the end of the week, Olesya sent the inhaler receipt.

She had paid it herself.

The photograph showed the receipt, a box of cartridges, and Anya's school timetable beneath them. The timetable had been altered in pen. Physical education crossed out for two weeks. Library added in the margin. Olesya's handwriting, narrow and exact.

Thomas sent the inhaler amount anyway.

For next refill.

The reply came the following morning.

You do not listen.

He was in the convoy yard when it arrived, waiting beside a row of vehicles with engines running and dust already lifting under their tyres. The security man checked names against a list. A mechanic banged a spanner against a tow hook. Someone shouted for water bottles.

Thomas typed:

I listened. I made it monthly.

He did not send it.

He typed:

Keep receipt.

He put the phone away before the dots could appear.

The field day ran long.

By the time he returned, Sarah had moved her laptop to the dining hall after the room's connection failed during a client call. He found her at a corner table under a television showing football without sound. She wore a white shirt, jeans, and the expression she used when pretending fluorescent lighting did not exist.

'There you are,' she said.

'Here I am.'

'You smell like machinery.'

'That is better than some options.'

She closed her laptop halfway.

'Eat.'

'I need to shower.'

'Eat first. You get strange when you are running on coffee and moral tension.'

'Moral tension?'

'It was either that or hydraulic fluid. I chose kindly.'

He sat opposite her with a tray of rice, lentils, chicken, cucumber, and a small orange still cold from storage. She watched him until he picked up the fork.

'Better.'

'You have become very compound.'

'I have become very bored. Different thing.'

The dining hall filled and emptied around them. Men in dusty boots. Engineers with badges turned backwards. Security staff in black shirts. Two women from finance carrying salads and speaking quietly about school fees in Aberdeen.

Sarah looked towards the television.

'I checked the joint account today.'

Thomas kept the fork in his hand.

'For what?'

'The Edinburgh hotel double-charged the card and I wanted to see if they had reversed it.'

'Had they?'

'Yes.'

She looked back at him.

'Your personal account is lower than usual.'

The fork touched the tray.

'I have had some Basra costs.'

'Basra reimburses.'

'Eventually.'

'How much eventually are we talking about?'

'Enough to be irritating.'

She opened the orange with her thumbnail, stripping the peel in one long piece.

'Do you need to move money from joint?'

'No.'

'Thomas.'

'No.'

'Fine.'

She placed half the orange on his tray.

'But do not subsidise a multinational out of your personal account. It offends my politics and our mortgage.'

'I will claim it.'

'Good.'

She resumed eating. The conversation closed under her hand. That was one of Sarah's mercies. She could leave a door unforced if the hinges looked strained.

Thomas ate the orange after the rice.

It was sharper than he expected.

On the first of June, the reminder appeared at 06:00.

O admin.

Sarah was asleep. Her breathing had adapted to the air conditioner's rattle. One hand lay open on the sheet, palm up. Her wedding ring had turned slightly on her finger during the night.

Thomas took the phone to the bathroom.

The amount was already in the spreadsheet. He opened the transfer platform and entered it without recalculating.

Recipient: Olesya Petrova.

Reference: June.

Purpose: Other.

Fee: accepted.

Delivery: same day.

He pressed confirm.

The screen gave him the reference number. He saved the PDF. He did not print it. Not with Sarah asleep in the next room and the compound printer in the office. He took a screenshot and sent it.

June.

The reply came at 09:18, while he was in the operations office reviewing pump vibration data.

What is this?

He stepped outside into a corridor that smelled of hot plastic and dust.

Monthly.

The dots appeared.

No.

He waited.

Then:

For what?

Rent. School. Medicine. Transport.

He could see her reading the list in the flat in Baku, or perhaps at the packing counter, phone held low so no one saw the screen.

The reply took eleven minutes.

This is not salary.

No.

What is it?

He typed:

Support.

He looked at the word and disliked its softness.

He deleted it.

Buffer.

He sent that.

Her answer:

For you.

He stood in the corridor with the phone in his hand. At the far end, a cleaner pushed a mop bucket through a strip of sunlight. The water inside it moved darkly.

For Anya, he typed.

This time the dots did not appear.

At noon, Olesya sent three photographs.

Rent receipt.

Bus card top-up.

Pharmacy balance.

Each had been placed on the plastic tablecloth, separated by a ruler, aligned for inspection. At the edge of one photograph, Anya's hand was visible, holding the ruler in place. The nails were bitten short.

Thomas entered the receipts into the June row.

The monthly system closed its first loop before lunch.

By the second month, the loop had a rhythm.

Reminder. Transfer. Screenshot. Receipts. Spreadsheet.

Olesya resisted in words and complied in paper.

July:

You sent too much.

Receipt attached.

The attachment showed three papers arranged on the tablecloth, each corner held down with something heavy. A chipped mug. A spoon. A small tin of tea. The rent receipt lay at the centre with its purple stamp pressed too hard into the paper. The bus-card slip sat below it. The pharmacy receipt had been folded and unfolded so often the print had begun to silver at the crease.

Thomas zoomed in.

The pharmacy total was less than she had expected. He knew that from the way the remaining cash was placed beside the receipt, notes aligned by size, coins gathered in a bottle cap. It was an answer before he had asked the question.

He entered the difference into the spreadsheet.

Under.

The word appeared in the variance column.

For three minutes he watched it sit there.

Then he changed it to:

Remaining.

In the evening, Sarah came back from the finance office with a brown envelope of petty cash and a printout stapled to it. She placed both on the desk and took off her badge.

'They reimbursed the hotel charge,' she said.

'Good.'

'And my taxi.'

'Also good.'

'And the airport lounge, which I had forgotten about and now feel rather fond of.'

She opened the envelope and tipped the notes onto the desk. The money lay between them with the loose confidence of money that had a proper route home.

'Do you need cash?' she asked.

'No.'

'Sure?'

'Yes.'

She counted the notes, wrote a figure on the printout, and clipped everything together. Her processes were not hidden. They were crisp and available, left in plain view amongst chargers, lip balm, and the compound map with her route to the gym marked in blue pen.

Thomas looked at the envelope.

Official money travelled with paperwork. It left marks in the correct places. It entered and left accounts wearing its own name.

On his screen, Olesya's July row glowed pale green.

Sarah leaned over his shoulder while the lid was still open.

'Spreadsheets after nine,' she said. 'That should be in the marriage contract.'

'I was checking one line.'

'That is how it starts.'

'What starts?'

'The decline. First one line, then tabs, then you are explaining conditional formatting at breakfast.'

He closed the laptop.

'Saved you.'

'You did.'

She kissed the back of his head and went to the bathroom. The shower pipes knocked inside the wall before water came through. A strip of steam appeared beneath the door.

Thomas reopened the laptop.

He moved the monthly sheet into a hidden tab.

August:

School books more this term.

I used contingency.

There were no photographs at first.

Only the message, sent at 22:41 Baku time. The hours between them had become another column in his head. When she sent late, he pictured the kitchen after Anya had gone to bed, plates soaking in the sink, the balcony door open a hand's width to let out the heat from the stove. When she sent early, he pictured the bus stop, the phone held in one hand, receipts folded inside the other.

Send receipt when you can.

Tomorrow.

The receipt arrived at 06:12 the next morning. Exercise books. Pens. A plastic geometry set. English reader. School uniform repair. Shoe glue.

The shoe glue made him stop.

It sat at the bottom, a small amount, nearly hidden by the pharmacy stamp from another shop on the reverse side of the paper. He enlarged the image until the pixels broke. The word remained legible enough.

Glue.

At breakfast, Sarah read emails on her phone whilst spreading jam on toast. She had stopped complaining about the dining hall bread and now treated it as a hostile material to be managed with butter.

'I have to extend a week,' she said.

Thomas looked up.

'Here?'

'Yes. Client moved the review. They want me in the same time zone.'

'Can you?'

'Already approved. Travel changed it. No drama.'

She said it whilst deleting a message with one thumb. A flight altered. A room extended. A badge kept active. The world rearranged itself for her with forms and confirmations, each one bearing her name.

'That works,' he said.

'Does it?'

'Yes.'

'You hesitated.'

'I was thinking about the seal replacement.'

'Romantic.'

'I do what I can.'

She smiled into the toast.

He nearly told her then.

The thought came without shape, more a movement in the mouth than a sentence. Olesya. Anya. Hospital. Rent. Monthly. The words lined up badly, none of them small enough for breakfast, none of them large enough for what had already happened.

Sarah's phone rang.

'London,' she said, and stood with the toast still in her hand.

The moment passed into the air-conditioning.

Thomas opened his phone beneath the table.

He sent the school amount.

September:

Anya needs dentist. I can use August left.

This time Olesya sent the receipt first.

It showed the dental clinic's headed paper and a small square of gauze still in its packet. At the bottom edge of the photograph, Anya's school shoe appeared, black leather cracked across the toe, one lace grey with dust.

Thomas added a note beside the September line.

Dentist.

Then he added:

Shoes soon.

He deleted it.

The spreadsheet should not predict everything. Prediction had a way of becoming permission.

In the operations office that afternoon, the printer jammed for the third time. Paper folded itself inside the tray with a dry tearing sound. The finance clerk stood beside it, holding a stack of expense forms and a paper cup of tea.

'Again,' she said.

Thomas opened the tray and freed the sheet.

'It needs servicing.'

'It needs retirement.'

She took the bent page and smoothed it against her hip. 'Yours?'

'No.'

'Then it might be approved.'

The men at the next desk laughed. The clerk did not. She wrote in red pen across the top of one form and stamped another. The stamp struck the paper with a hard little clap.

Approved.

Rejected.

Missing receipt.

Wrong code.

Every item had a box. Every box had a route. Even failure wore an official mark.

Thomas returned to his desk and opened the O monthly sheet. No boxes. No code. No line manager signature. Only a hidden tab and a woman in Baku sending proofs he had not asked her to learn.

When Sarah came in later with the revised travel documents, she placed them next to his keyboard.

'I printed yours too,' she said. 'Your next rotation.'

'Thanks.'

'You had three versions in your email. I picked the one that did not strand you in Istanbul for eleven hours.'

'You are a better logistics department than logistics.'

'Correct.'

She tapped the top page. 'Also, finance asked whether your Basra claim is still open. They say it has been sitting there for ages.'

'I will look at it.'

'You said that last week.'

'I know.'

'It is real money.'

He looked at the travel document. His name, route, departure, return. Each printed line clean and official.

'I will submit it tonight.'

'Good.'

She waited a moment, then left the office.

He did not submit the claim that night.

He reconciled September.

By October the calendar reminder no longer startled him.

O admin arrived amongst ordinary things. Generator inspection. Sarah London call. Water test. O admin. HSE review. Payroll query. The line sat there with the same grey text as everything else, neither larger nor smaller, absorbed into the machinery of the month.

He began doing it after tea, no longer before anyone woke.

The secrecy did not end. It changed posture.

At first he had taken the phone to the bathroom, held it low, kept one ear on Sarah through the door. In October he sat at the desk with Sarah reading on the bed and made the transfer whilst she turned a page. His hand covered the screen out of habit, then moved away. The transfer platform looked no different from banking, from bills, from anything a married man might do in the evening with numbers.

'You are quiet,' Sarah said.

'Just paying something.'

'Tax?'

'Worse.'

'Nothing is worse than tax.'

'School.'

The word came out before he had weighed it.

Sarah lowered her book.

'School?'

'An old colleague's daughter. They are in a tight patch.'

There it was.

A version small enough to live.

Sarah kept the book open against her knee. The cover had curled back in the heat.

'How tight?'

'Temporary.'

'How old a colleague?'

'Baku.'

'Oil world?'

'Adjacent.'

She looked at him in the light from the desk lamp. The air conditioner clicked, stalled, then resumed with a rougher note.

'You are helping them?'

'A bit.'

'With school fees.'

'Some costs.'

'Does this person have family?'

'Not useful family.'

Sarah's thumb moved along the page edge, bending paper and releasing it.

'You could have said.'

'It was messy.'

'Most decent things are.'

He nearly laughed, and did not.

She returned to her book, but not to reading. Her eyes stayed on the same line for a long while.

Thomas confirmed the October transfer.

The screen brightened.

Recipient: Olesya Petrova.

He angled the phone down.

No discussion. No announcement. He changed the number in the spreadsheet, then in the transfer. The increased sum moved through with its usual fee and reference.

Olesya noticed.

Why more?

Winter.

That is not an answer.

Heating. Medicine. School term.

You make seasons into lines.

He read the message twice.

Then he typed:

Lines pay bills.

Her reply:

Yes.

It was not agreement. It was a receipt for the fact.

The next day she sent photographs of the flat.

Not the whole flat. Never that. Parts of it, the room trusted only in pieces.

The heater against the wall, its enamel chipped at one corner.

The balcony door with a folded towel pushed along the frame.

Anya's school shoes on newspaper, newly blackened, the cracked toe still showing beneath the polish.

On the table lay the printed October confirmation with a pharmacy loyalty card on top of it. Olesya had weighed it down for the photograph. That was all.

Thomas saved the images to the wrong folder.

Pump Seals.

He saw the mistake and left it there.

Moving them would have required admitting that they had a correct place.

Sarah noticed again in November.

They were in the compound shop, a small windowless room that sold toiletries, batteries, chocolate, instant noodles, phone chargers, razors, and socks. Sarah had a basket over one arm. Thomas held a packet of coffee that had been imported at some cost from somewhere that did not drink it.

'We need to talk about your personal account,' she said.

He placed the coffee back on the shelf.

'Here?'

'No one is listening. They are all deciding whether toothpaste is worth twelve dollars.'

'What about it?'

'It is dropping every month.'

'I told you. Basra costs.'

'Basra costs do not arrive on the first of every month.'

He looked at the shelf. The labels were in English and Arabic. Mint. Whitening. Sensitive. Family pack.

'I am moving money into a reserve.'

'For what?'

'Contingency.'

She turned the basket handle against her wrist.

'That is the sort of word people use when they do not want a second question.'

'It is work-related.'

'Everything is work-related if you stand far enough away from it.'

He said nothing.

Sarah took the coffee from the shelf and put it in the basket.

'Fine. Keep your reserve. But if it touches joint money, tell me before it does.'

'It will not.'

'Good.'

She walked to the counter and paid with the joint card.

The receipt printed slowly. The cashier tore it off and handed it to her. Sarah glanced at it, folded it once, and pushed it into her pocket without looking.

Thomas watched the motion.

Some receipts proved nothing. Nothing had to be proved.

The stipend changed the messages.

Emergencies thinned. Updates took their place.

Anya passed English exam.

The heating works now.

Dentist says second appointment next month.

School wants parent meeting.

The money did not make Olesya expansive. It made her more exact. She sent fewer explanations, not more. Problems came already sorted, categorised, partially paid, waiting only for the column he owned.

Thomas began to answer less as well.

Good.

Send receipt.

Use contingency.

I will adjust next month.

The messages looked cold on the screen. They worked.

On Olesya's birthday in December, there was no reminder in his calendar.

The date arrived through a receipt.

She sent a photograph of a cake box on the kitchen table. The box was white, tied with red string, with the bakery name stamped in one corner. Beside it lay a receipt for a small amount and a handwritten note from Anya on lined paper.

Happy birthday Mama.

The writing had improved. The letters stood upright now, more certain in their spacing.

Olesya's message beneath it:

Anya used her own money.

Thomas was in a site meeting when it arrived.

The operations manager was explaining a pump seal failure. On the screen, a diagram rotated slowly, showing the part from every angle. Men argued about whether the replacement could wait until Sunday. Sarah sat two buildings away in the accommodation office, taking a client call with headphones on, her notebook open beside a cup of compound coffee she had stopped pretending to like.

Thomas looked at the cake box.

He had not known the date.

Or he had known once and let it sit outside the system until the system exposed it.

He opened the transfer platform under the table.

Supplementary amount.

Reference: Birthday.

The word looked wrong.

He changed it to:

December extra.

Then changed it back.

Birthday.

Purpose: Other.

Fee: accepted.

He pressed confirm.

The meeting continued around him. Seal. Pressure. Shutdown window. Procurement.

The confirmation arrived.

He took a screenshot and sent it to Olesya.

Happy birthday.

For dinner.

For seven minutes, nothing.

Then:

You did not have to.

He typed:

I know.

He deleted it.

He typed:

Enjoy.

He deleted that too.

In the end he sent nothing.

The screenshot sat above her message, brighter than the words beneath it.

That evening, when Sarah asked why he had skipped dinner, he said the pump seal meeting had run late.

'Did it?' she asked.

'Yes.'

She was brushing dust from the hem of her trousers with the side of her hand. The compound light made every thread visible.

'You have that face again.'

'What face?'

'The one that belongs to a calculation you have not shown me.'

'It is a pump seal.'

'Poor pump seal.'

She let it pass.

At midnight, Olesya sent a photograph.

The cake had been cut into three pieces, though only two plates were on the table. Anya's note had been moved beside the transfer confirmation. The confirmation was printed, folded once, and placed under the edge of the cake box, not with the receipts.

Olesya had written on the back in pencil. The pressure of the writing showed through the paper.

Birthday.

Thomas opened the spreadsheet.

December.

Rent. School. Medicine. Transport. Contingency.

He added one more line.

Birthday.

The cell accepted it.

Chapter 11

By January, the system had learned to move without noise.

It had no ceremony. It did not require invention each morning. It woke with Thomas before the alarm, already arranged in the places where a life kept its weight. Calendar. Accounts. Inbox. Receipts. A hidden tab. A second folder inside the first, named with letters only he would read correctly.

Outside, the compound sat under a pale winter sky. The heat had loosened its grip. Men walked to breakfast with jackets over their arms, complaining out of habit rather than need. Gravel clicked under boots. The palms at the edge of the accommodation block carried old dust in their fronds, the kind no rain here ever reached. The sprinklers came on at six and turned the paths dark for twenty minutes, then the dust returned and settled over everything with patient fingers.

Thomas liked the hour before the compound filled.

The dining hall opened at five thirty. At five forty, the coffee was still too bitter and the bread still warm at the centre. The televisions played silently above the serving line. News from London, football from Spain, an American weather map no one needed. The staff moved behind the counter with practised faces. Eggs. Beans. Flatbread. Fruit. A spoon struck the edge of a tray. Someone coughed into his sleeve.

Thomas took the same table near the window.

Sarah arrived seven minutes later with her laptop under one arm and her hair clipped badly at the back. She had begun sleeping later than he did and waking faster, a talent he still considered unfair. Her badge swung from her neck. Her shirt sleeves were rolled. She carried two cups of coffee and set one down in front of him.

'You are becoming predictable,' she said.

'Efficient.'

'That is what predictable people call themselves.'

'You brought me coffee.'

'I am investing in a civil morning.'

She opened her laptop before sitting and nudged his foot beneath the table with hers. The gesture was small. It landed exactly where it needed to.

Sarah had become part of the compound without asking permission from it. She had found the gym on the third day and the quiet office with the least unreliable connection on the fourth. By the second week she knew which finance clerk could get a driver booked without theatrical suffering and which security supervisor liked to discuss Arsenal until a form passed from one tray to another. Her client calls began before the first convoy left and ended after dinner. Her notebooks filled with diagrams, lists, names, arrows, figures. The compound took women poorly in theory and relied on them constantly in fact. Sarah moved through that contradiction with a clean impatience that opened doors.

It suited her.

That surprised him less than it should have.

At home, distance had made her sharp at the edges. Calls dropped. Time zones cut into sleep. Their house in Scotland had held too many unshared rooms. Here, in the sealed geometry of the compound, she was near enough to become ordinary again. Her toothbrush beside his. Her shoes under the chair. Her voice from the bathroom, asking whether the water had gone brown for him too. The narrow room did not give them space to dramatise anything. It required them to pass each other, reach around each other, negotiate drawers and sockets and towels.

Some marriages improved under scarcity.

At 06:00 his phone lit on the table.

O admin.

Sarah was editing a slide and did not look up.

Thomas turned the screen flat beneath his palm and let the reminder disappear.

He no longer made the transfer from bathrooms. That phase had passed. It had belonged to the first weeks, to the old voltage of exposure, to the childish belief that hiding in tile and steam changed the act. Now he waited until the breakfast plates were cleared. He walked to the operations office. He opened the transfer platform after checking overnight data. He made the payment with the same hand that approved a maintenance note and forwarded a pressure report.

The amount had become exact enough to look dull.

Rent.

School.

Medicine.

Transport.

Contingency.

He did not write Olesya's name in the calendar. He did not write Anya's. That was not cruelty. It was containment.

At 08:13, he confirmed the January payment.

Recipient: Olesya Petrova.

Reference: January.

Purpose: Other.

Fee: accepted.

Delivery: same day.

He saved the PDF to the folder marked O admin and sent the screenshot.

January.

Olesya replied at 10:02.

Received.

No argument. No correction. No You do not listen. No This is not salary. The word stood alone on the screen, professional and small.

At 10:09 came the first receipt.

Rent.

At 10:17, school transport.

At 11:41, pharmacy.

Each photograph had improved. The papers lay straighter now. The light was better. Olesya had started using the table by the balcony in the morning, when the winter sun came thinly through the glass and made the stamps legible. A ruler still appeared in some frames, though no longer in all. Sometimes Anya's hand held a corner down. Sometimes the corner lifted and cast a small triangular shadow across the total.

Thomas entered each amount.

The numbers behaved.

By lunch, January had closed.

That evening Sarah received a message from London and read it twice before passing him the phone.

'Look.'

He took it.

The client had approved her proposal. More than approved it. They wanted her to present the next stage herself and build the implementation plan from Iraq. There were three exclamation marks in the partner's reply, which Sarah considered vulgar but useful.

'That is good,' he said.

'That is very good.'

'How smug are you allowed to be?'

'Professionally? Modest. Internally? Unbearable.'

'I can work with unbearable.'

She took back the phone and placed it face down on the bed.

'They want me here through March.'

He folded a shirt and put it into the drawer.

'Can they do that?'

'They can ask. I can agree. Travel says no issue. Your company says guest extension is fine if security signs it. Security will sign it if I keep pretending to laugh at Colin's jokes.'

'Colin has no jokes.'

'That is what makes my sacrifice meaningful.'

She smiled, and the room changed shape around it. Not much. Enough.

Thomas put another shirt away.

'March works.'

'Does it?'

'Yes.'

'Say it with your face.'

He looked at her properly.

'March works.'

She watched him, weighing the answer by features rather than words. Then she nodded.

'Good.'

The matter joined the calendar.

Sarah extension.

O admin.

Site review.

Anya dentist follow-up.

London presentation.

Pump seal procurement.

Every item had its line. Every line had its day. There was no collision if he respected the edges.

This was the discovery he allowed himself in the weeks that followed: life did not have to be singular to be orderly.

A man could hold separate obligations without making them false. The mistake, he decided, had been disorder. Disorder made everything look like betrayal. Order revealed proportion. Sarah needed presence, clean accounts, shared time, the quiet reliability of a husband who made coffee and listened when her client used nonsense words. Olesya needed stability, money arriving before crisis, answers that did not vanish after the first heat of rescue. Anya needed school, medicine, shoes, a mother not counting coins beneath a yellow kitchen light.

None of those needs cancelled the others.

He wrote that nowhere.

He lived by it.

He tested it in small ways.

On Tuesdays, he walked with Sarah after dinner. Not far. The compound did not allow distance, only circuits. They passed the gym, the clinic, the laundry block, the low building where the drivers smoked by the service door, then circled back towards the accommodation under lights that made every face look tired. Sarah used the walks to empty the day out of herself. Names came first. Clients. Partners. Finance. Then the phrases that had offended her. Low-hanging fruit. Strategic alignment. Quick win. She recited them with solemn disgust until he laughed.

'You are too fond of hating your job,' he said one night.

'That is the correct way to have a job.'

'Is it?'

'Affection breeds complacency.'

'I will remember that.'

'Do. It may save you.'

Her hand found his sleeve as they crossed a strip of gravel where the light failed. She did not take his hand. That would have been too deliberate. She held the fabric at his wrist with two fingers until the path evened out, then let go.

On Wednesdays, he updated the monthly sheet.

Not in a rush. Not late at night with the screen dimmed and his shoulder turned to the door. He did it after the site call, tea cooling beside the keyboard, while the printer in the corner made its animal noises and men came in and out for forms. Rent line. School line. Medicine line. Notes. Variance. Next month.

The spreadsheet had become calmer as Olesya became better at it.

She no longer sent apologies with receipts. She no longer wrote too much explanation in the message beneath each photograph. The proof arrived stripped clean. A paper. A total. A date. Sometimes a single line.

Shoes repaired.

Teacher meeting Thursday.

Clinic says no change.

Paid gas.

There was dignity in the economy of it. He told himself that. The words allowed him to keep looking.

On Fridays, Sarah and Nisha sat outside with tea. On the same evenings, Olesya cleaned the flat. He knew this through fragments. A photograph of the gas bill with a wet cloth at the edge of the frame. Anya's schoolbooks stacked on a chair, floor still damp beneath it. A message sent later than usual.

Sorry. Cleaning.

No need to apologise.

I know.

Then, after a pause:

Still I do.

He did not answer that.

He opened the photograph again. The floor in the Baku kitchen shone in patches where the mop had passed. One chair had been turned upside down on the table. A blue bucket stood by the balcony door. At the window, the evening held the flat in a grey colour he knew from other winters there, smoke and sea air pressed together until the city looked rubbed with ash.

In Iraq, Sarah came in from the gym and dropped her towel over the back of a chair.

'You are miles away,' she said.

'No. Just tired.'

'You always say tired when you mean private.'

He put the phone down.

'Then I am privately tired.'

'Progress.'

She poured water into a glass and drank half of it without breathing between swallows. A line of sweat ran from her hairline to her jaw. She wiped it with the heel of her hand and looked at the phone on the desk, not long enough to ask, long enough to mark it.

'Walk tomorrow?' she said.

'Yes.'

'Early. Before the air turns criminal.'

'Agreed.'

She went into the bathroom. The shower knocked and hissed. Thomas turned the phone face down and left it there.

One object could belong to two worlds if no one picked it up.

February opened with rain in Iraq.

Not enough to clean anything. Enough to bruise the dust and leave the compound smelling of wet concrete, diesel, and the sour drainage channel beyond the fence. The gravel paths darkened. Puddles formed in shallow dips and reflected the security lights with a tremble that made the ground look less certain than it was.

Sarah walked to breakfast under his jacket, laughing at the uselessness of it. Rain clung to the loose hair at her temples. Her laptop bag knocked against his hip.

'This country has no idea what rain is for,' she said.

'It is doing its best.'

'Its best is mud with ambition.'

In the dining hall, she shook water from the jacket and hung it over the back of a chair. Thomas took trays from the stack. Eggs. Rice. Cucumber. Bread. Two coffees. A routine so ordinary it seemed to have been waiting for them long before they arrived.

His phone buzzed while he was pouring milk.

Olesya.

He left it until they sat.

Sarah was reading an agenda. He opened the message beneath the table.

Anya has marks.

Then a photograph.

The paper was creased from being folded into a schoolbag. Across the top, Anya's name had been written in blue ink. English: 91. Mathematics: 88. Azerbaijani: 84. Science: 90. Conduct: excellent.

Below the marks, the teacher had written a short note in Russian. Thomas used the translation tool only after trying to read the sentence himself.

Consistent effort. Improved confidence in spoken work.

He looked at that line for longer than the marks.

Sarah flicked his ankle with the toe of her shoe.

'You have gone somewhere.'

'Sorry.'

'Where?'

'School thing.'

'Old colleague's daughter?'

'Yes.'

'Good school thing or bad school thing?'

He turned the phone so she could see the marks, covering the name at the top with his thumb. It was a cowardly compromise and a practical one. He knew both truths at once.

Sarah leaned in.

'Those are good.'

'Yes.'

'Very good.'

'She worked hard.'

'Your face changed.'

He locked the phone.

'It is decent news.'

'Decent?'

'Fine. Very decent.'

Sarah smiled at her coffee.

'There he is.'

The phrase stayed with him through the morning.

There he is.

Not accusation. Not discovery. A wife catching a narrow, ordinary warmth in her husband and letting it stand. She had no reason to suspect the room behind it.

At noon, Olesya sent another message.

Teacher says she should enter English competition.

Then:

Fee is small. I can pay.

Thomas typed:

Use February.

Olesya answered:

Already planned.

Then:

She wants new notebook for it. Not necessary.

He stood in the corridor outside the control room, phone in hand, boots leaving damp marks on the floor. Through the glass, men watched pressure curves shift across a screen. The rain tapped at the metal roof with a sound like fingernails.

Buy the notebook.

Olesya did not answer for twenty minutes.

Then:

She will choose one after school.

That evening, a photograph arrived.

The notebook was red. On the cover, a gold line ran around the edge and met at the corners in small knots. Anya's hand held it open to the first page, where she had written her name in careful English letters, each one pressed hard enough to dent the paper.

Sarah was on a call with London, pacing the length of the room in bare feet. She used her hands while she spoke, cutting the air into sections, moving invisible pieces from one place to another. Thomas watched her, phone still warm in his palm.

Two rooms. One narrow strip of carpet.

It could work.

The thought did not arrive as argument. It arrived as evidence.

Sarah finished the call and dropped onto the bed.

'They bought it,' she said.

'The whole plan?'

'The whole plan.'

'They had sense enough.'

'Do not sound unsurprised. I require theatre.'

He put the phone face down.

'This is a magnificent commercial victory.'

'Better.'

'Your enemies are scattered.'

'Much better.'

She stretched one foot until it touched his knee.

'Dinner?'

'Dining hall?'

'Unless you have a better offer hidden in your drawer.'

He did not answer quickly enough.

Her eyes moved to the desk, then back to him.

'That was a joke.'

'I know.'

'Thomas.'

'Dining hall.'

She stood and reached for her shoes. The small pause remained in the room after she moved.

The presentation became an event without intending to.

Sarah booked the small conference room for three in the afternoon and spent the morning pacing between the bed and the desk, one hand holding her headset, the other moving through the argument in the air. Thomas watched her rebuild the same answer six ways. She took words out. Put them back. Cut two slides. Restored one. Moved a chart forward and then moved it back to its original place with a noise of disgust.

'Do not speak to me unless the building is on fire,' she said.

'What about localised smoke?'

'Submit a ticket.'

He made coffee and left it within reach.

At 14:50, she changed shirts. At 14:53, she changed back. At 14:56, she stood in front of the mirror and pinned her hair so severely that her face seemed newly made. Not softer. Not harder. More exact.

'How do I look?'

'Like you already know the answer.'

'Correct answer.'

'Like they should have read the deck.'

'Better.'

She took the laptop and walked to the conference room.

Thomas did not follow. He went to the operations office and opened a report on pump performance. The numbers ran across the page with perfect obedience and gave him nothing. At 15:08, Olesya sent a photograph of Anya's red notebook open to a page of copied English sentences.

My favourite place is the sea.

My mother says I was born looking at the sea.

One day I will speak English in another country.

The last sentence had been crossed out once, then written again below, straighter.

Thomas enlarged the picture.

The paper showed pressure marks from earlier pages. A child's work ghosting up from beneath the present line.

He wrote:

Good sentences.

Olesya answered:

She asks if country has to be real.

Thomas looked towards the conference room wall. Through it, somewhere beyond concrete and cables, Sarah was speaking to London, making people believe a plan could carry weight.

He typed:

Real enough for a sentence.

Olesya sent:

I told her same.

For a few seconds he let that sit. Same. A narrow plank across distance. He stood on one end of it and did not test the other.

At 16:12, Sarah came into the operations office and stood in the doorway.

No triumph in the entrance. No raised arms. Only her face stripped of its presentation mask, her headset in one hand, a red mark above her ear where it had pressed into the skin.

'Well?' he asked.

'They want phase two by Monday.'

'That sounds good.'

'It is good.'

'And inconvenient.'

'Deeply.'

She crossed the room, took his coffee without asking, and drank from it.

'Also,' she said, 'the partner apologised for underestimating the field constraints.'

'In those words?'

'No. In a coward's dialect. But I translated.'

'Historic.'

'I may frame the transcript.'

He stood and put a hand on her shoulder. It was meant to be brief. She leaned back into it and stayed.

The office continued around them. Printer. Phones. Chairs. A man asking where the stapler had gone. Sarah's shoulder under his palm, warm through cotton.

His phone was still lit on the desk beside the pump report.

Anya's notebook filled the screen.

Sarah looked down.

'Red notebook?'

'Yes.'

'Good choice.'

'Very.'

She touched the screen once with her finger, not opening anything, only acknowledging the colour.

'Phase two,' she said, and moved away.

The phone dimmed.

March came in warm.

The mornings lost their clean edge. By ten, the air outside the offices had thickened. The compound returned to its usual smells: fuel, hot plastic, boiled rice, damp towels, the sour sweetness of fruit left too long in the dining hall bins. Sarah complained with increasing precision. She had categories now. Bad heat. Office heat. Convoy heat. Shower heat. Heat with dust. Heat with flies. Heat that made a person reconsider every choice made since infancy.

Thomas listened and liked the catalogue.

It meant she was staying long enough to classify discomfort.

Her work settled into a pattern. She took calls in the quiet office. She wrote in the dining hall when the connection in their room failed. She ran in the gym late at night with headphones in and returned with her face flushed, her shirt dark at the collar, her breathing steady by the time she opened the door. She made friends with a woman from finance named Nisha, who had two children in Leeds and a laugh that carried through walls. On Fridays, Sarah and Nisha sat outside the accommodation block with paper cups of tea and spoke about flights, clients, schools, mothers, men, and the compound laundry that treated all fabric as a personal enemy.

Thomas watched from a distance once, walking back from the operations office.

Sarah had one knee drawn up, her arm resting across it. Nisha said something and Sarah tipped her head back. The laugh came loose and unguarded, not the polite laugh of calls, not the careful laugh she used when a contractor tried charm in place of competence.

He stopped before she saw him.

For a second, the scene contained no wound.

Then his phone vibrated.

Olesya:

Anya selected for competition.

He stood between the office and the accommodation block, Sarah's laugh behind him, Olesya's message in his hand, and the compound lights just beginning to come on above the paths.

Nothing broke.

That was the proof, he told himself.

Nothing broke.

He walked on.

The English competition was held on a Saturday morning in Baku. Olesya sent no messages during it. Thomas checked his phone more often than the absence required, then placed it screen down and finished a maintenance report he had avoided for two days.

At 13:37, the phone buzzed.

She spoke.

There was a video attached.

Thomas did not open it in the office. He took the phone outside to the side of the building where the generators covered smaller sounds. The concrete wall held the sun. A torn strip of plastic fluttered in the chain-link fence.

He pressed play.

Anya stood at the front of a classroom, red notebook held in both hands. She wore a white shirt and a dark skirt. Her hair had been pulled back too tightly at the sides. Behind her, a map of the world sagged against the wall with one corner loose. A row of children sat at desks, some watching, some not.

The sound was poor.

Her voice reached him through phone compression and generator noise, thin but steady. English words, carefully placed. Not perfect. Better than that. Worked for. Owned. She lifted her eyes from the page on the last sentence and looked towards someone out of frame.

The video ended.

Thomas played it again.

Then once more.

Olesya sent:

Third place.

Then:

She said her voice did not shake.

Thomas stared at the second line.

On the screen, his own face hovered faintly over the frozen image of the classroom.

He typed:

She did well.

Too little.

He deleted it.

Tell her I saw the work.

He looked at the sentence. It was plainer than the first one. More useful.

He sent it.

Olesya replied:

I will.

After that:

She asks if you saw the red notebook.

He typed:

Yes. It was the right one.

When he returned to the room, Sarah was changing for dinner. She had laid two shirts on the bed and stood between them in a vest, deciding which one belonged to the evening.

'Good news?' she asked.

He stopped at the desk.

'Yes.'

'School again?'

'Competition. She came third.'

'That is lovely.'

Sarah chose the blue shirt and pushed her arms into it.

'Did you tell her well done?'

'I told her mother.'

'Tell the child as well. Children remember that sort of thing.'

He looked at the phone in his hand.

'I do not have her number.'

'Then tell the mother to tell her properly.'

'I did.'

'Good.'

She buttoned the shirt, missed one, undid it, began again.

'You know,' she said, 'you are allowed to be decent without acting like you have been caught.'

He placed the phone on the desk.

'I am not acting.'

'That was not a denial.'

'No.'

She finished the buttons and stood in front of the mirror, tucking the shirt into her trousers. The mirror was narrow and bolted to the wardrobe door. It cut her into sections. Face. Collar. Hands. Belt. A wife assembled in pieces by bad accommodation furniture.

'I like this version of you,' she said.

'Which version?'

'The one who does something useful and then pretends it is admin.'

His mouth gave him away.

'Admin is useful.'

'Admin is where civilisation hides.'

'I may put that on a slide.'

'You may not.'

They went to dinner.

The dining hall had fish that night, which the whole compound treated as an event and a threat. Sarah took rice and salad. Thomas took the fish and regretted it by the third mouthful. Nisha joined them with a tray and stories from finance. Someone had filed the same expense three times under three different project codes. Someone else had tried to claim a suitcase as technical equipment. Sarah listened with the fierce attention she gave to administrative absurdity, laughing into her water glass when Nisha described the suitcase as mission critical.

Thomas sat between them and let the conversation pass over him.

His phone stayed face down beside his tray.

It did not move.

No crisis came.

The next morning, Olesya sent a photograph from the kitchen.

A cake stood in the middle of the table, small, round, unevenly iced. Pink icing at the edges. Three candles. Not birthday candles. Plain white household candles cut shorter, their bases pressed into the top until the icing gathered around them.

Anya was not in the photograph, only her hands. One held the red notebook. The other held the certificate. Third place. Her name printed badly, the ink faint at the bottom where the school printer had failed.

Beside the cake lay the monthly transfer confirmation, folded once.

Olesya's message:

We made celebration.

Then:

She says thank you for notebook.

Thomas sat on the edge of the bed and looked at the cake.

Sarah was still asleep. One arm lay outside the sheet. Her hand rested open near his pillow, fingers curled slightly from sleep. The air conditioner rattled, paused, rattled again. Light came through the curtains in a thin stripe and touched the floor between his bare feet.

He typed:

She earned it.

Olesya answered:

Yes.

A minute later:

She is eating too much cake.

Thomas smiled down at the phone.

Sarah stirred.

'What time is it?' she asked, voice rough with sleep.

'Early.'

'Then why are you smiling at machinery?'

'School celebration.'

'Third place?'

'Yes.'

'Cake?'

'Yes.'

'Good.'

She pulled the sheet over her shoulder.

'Wake me when the world becomes worth trousers.'

He placed the phone on the bedside table and lay back.

For once, the room did not divide itself.

Sarah beside him, warm under the sheet. Olesya's message cooling on the screen. Anya's cake in a kitchen hundreds of miles away. The compound settling into morning. Men in the corridor. A door closing. The first clatter from the dining hall. Everything in its place. Everything paid before it became a plea.

He closed his eyes.

Sleep came cleanly.

When he woke again, the phone was still beside him.

No new message.

The screen stayed dark.

Chapter 12

The compound made privacy a matter of angles.

There were no private rooms, not truly. Only beds behind thin doors, bathrooms with pipes that carried sound, offices with glass panels beside the handles, corridors where every footstep arrived before the person did. The place had been built to count people and move them. Badge in. Badge out. Convoy. Meal. Briefing. Return. Sleep. Repeat. Even silence had procedure.

Thomas had once found that useful.

By April, it had become a geometry problem with no stable answer.

Sarah was still there. Her extension had become another extension, then a second workstream, then an arrangement everyone spoke about with the casual tone of something already approved. Her name sat on security lists. Her badge opened the office door now without the receptionist looking up. Her laptop lived beside his. Her toiletries occupied the better half of the bathroom shelf. Her shoes multiplied under the chair in ways that suggested a supply line he had failed to detect.

She had not invaded the room.

She had made it habitable.

That was the difficulty.

On the morning the email arrived, the wind came from the south and carried dust low across the compound. It moved like smoke along the paths, gathering at door frames and the base of walls, working its way through any gap left open. The sky had turned the colour of old metal. Men came into the dining hall wiping grit from their eyelids. The coffee tasted of the urn and the air. Even the oranges had a film on them.

Sarah sat opposite him with her laptop open and a scarf over her hair.

'I look ridiculous,' she said.

'You look prepared.'

'Prepared is ridiculous with better stationery.'

She pushed a document across the table with two fingers. 'Can you read this before nine?'

'What is it?'

'The implementation note. Two pages. It grew overnight, like mould.'

'That happens when you feed consultants after midnight.'

'You should put that in a site bulletin.'

He took the document. The paper had already collected dust at the corners. Sarah held her coffee with both hands and watched the serving line through the steam. She looked tired in the practical way, tired with work still moving through her, not the hollow kind that emptied a face.

His phone lay beside the tray.

O admin was due the next day.

He had moved the reminder from 06:00 to 05:20, before Sarah woke, then moved it back after noticing the change made the thing look like concealment. He had considered deleting the reminder completely and using memory, then rejected that as vanity. Memory failed under heat. Systems did not.

At 08:07, he walked to the operations office with Sarah's document folded under his arm.

The office was already crowded. Dust had found its way in through the old seals around the windows and settled on keyboards, monitors, cups, the tops of men's boots. The air conditioners rattled with a new dry rasp. Someone had taped a cloth over the worst vent. The cloth ballooned and collapsed with each breath of machine air.

Thomas opened his laptop.

No connection.

He tried again.

The network icon spun, failed, spun again.

'External mail is down,' Nisha called from the finance desk.

Three men groaned in different languages.

'Internal still works,' she said. 'Try the old terminal.'

The old terminal stood against the wall near the printer, a company machine with a cracked keyboard and a monitor that made everything look faintly green. It was used for permits, printing boarding passes, emergency approvals, anything no one wanted to own. The browser remembered too much. Men logged in and out badly. The machine carried the residue of every rushed password in the building.

Thomas did not like using it.

At 08:23, he used it.

Sarah's document needed comments before her nine o'clock call, and his own laptop had become a sealed box. He logged into webmail, waiting while the screen loaded line by line. Inbox. Site reports. Pump seal confirmation. Travel update. Security weather advisory. Sarah's document sat under the wrong subject line, forwarded by her to him at midnight.

He downloaded it, opened it, and began marking the file.

The terminal keyboard stuck on the letter e. Each correction required a harder strike. Dust gritted under the keys. The printer beside him warmed itself with a smell of hot plastic.

At 08:41, the inbox refreshed.

One new message.

From: Olesya Petrova

Subject: March and April

Thomas's hand stopped over the keyboard.

The message line sat in the middle of the screen, ordinary as any other mail. Black text. White background. Sender. Subject. Time. The whole arrangement exposed by format.

He had told her to use messages.

Months ago, after the first transfer, he had said email was fine for receipts when messaging failed. Then the monthly rhythm had moved elsewhere, and email had fallen out of use. Old instructions had a way of waiting in the walls.

Nisha was speaking to someone behind him. The printer clicked. A chair rolled. Outside, a gust pushed dust against the window with a dry hiss.

He closed Sarah's document.

The inbox stayed open.

He moved the cursor to the message and did not click.

Sarah came in carrying her laptop.

'Tell me you have destroyed my note elegantly.'

He hit the browser close button.

The terminal froze.

Sarah stopped beside him. 'Ah. The old machine. Brave.'

'Network is down.'

'Naturally. The one morning London remembers Iraq exists.'

The browser window remained on screen, pale and unresponsive. Inbox visible. Olesya's name visible. Subject visible.

Thomas pressed close again.

Nothing.

Sarah leaned closer to see what the machine was doing.

He shifted his body in front of the monitor.

'Give it a second.'

'It never deserves a second.'

'I have the document.'

'Good. Where?'

'On the desktop.'

'Then move, engineer.'

She reached past him for the mouse.

He caught it first.

Too fast.

Her hand paused in the air.

The office noise thinned around them. Not stopped. Never stopped. Printer. Air conditioner. Phones. Men. Dust at the window. Still, the small space between Sarah's fingers and the mouse filled with something neither of them named.

'Thomas.'

'It is frozen.'

'I can see it is frozen.'

'Let me restart it.'

'I need the file.'

'I will print it.'

'From a frozen machine?'

He pressed the power button and held it down.

The screen went black.

For a second, both of their reflections sat on the dead glass.

Sarah's face beside his shoulder. His own behind the dust, less clear.

'That seemed dramatic,' she said.

'It was doing nothing.'

'It was showing your inbox.'

'Yes.'

'And?'

'And now it is off.'

She looked at him. The scarf had slipped back from her hair. A fine line of dust had gathered along one eyebrow.

'Was there something in your inbox I was not meant to see?'

He took his hand from the power button.

'No.'

The answer came too clean.

Sarah's mouth changed, not into a smile. The office door opened behind her, and the HSE supervisor came in asking for the incident form from last week. Nisha answered. Someone laughed near the printer. The ordinary world rushed back into the space and saved him badly.

Sarah stepped away.

'Email me the comments when your machines recover.'

'Sarah.'

'Nine o'clock call.'

She left with her laptop under one arm.

The terminal began restarting.

Thomas stood in front of it until the login screen returned.

He did not log in.

At 09:12, his phone vibrated.

Olesya.

Did you see email?

He walked out through the side door before answering. The dust hit his face and went into his mouth. He crossed the service path to the narrow strip between the operations building and the generator yard, where the noise covered speech and the cameras faced the gate, not the wall.

What email?

He sent it and could not bear the shape of it after it left.

Her reply came fast.

Receipts. March and April. School letter also.

Do not email.

There was a pause.

Then:

You told me email if files big.

Not now.

The three words looked smaller than the force behind them.

Olesya did not answer.

He looked back towards the office. Through the dusty glass, he could see Sarah in the conference room, headset on, one hand moving across the air as she spoke to London. Her posture was exact. Her mouth made shapes no one in the room could hear.

His phone vibrated again.

Why not now?

He typed:

Shared machines here.

Then:

Use messages only.

Olesya answered:

It has school stamp. Message lowers quality.

He turned the phone in his hand. The generator sound pressed against his ribs.

Send later.

No email.

Another pause.

Then:

You need paper but not path.

He read the line twice.

Dust moved against the wall in little strikes.

No email, he typed.

This time there was no reply.

The day did not give him a place to put the event.

Sarah's call ran late. The network came back. The old terminal sat with its login screen open, blank and accusatory. Nisha printed boarding passes. The HSE supervisor found the form he had asked for. A maintenance planner spilled tea near the plotter and blamed the table. Dust entered the office, was wiped away, entered again.

Sarah's document returned to him at 10:06 with no note, only the file attached and the words revised version in the subject line. She had taken his silence and worked around it. That was worse than a question. Questions still expected an answer. Workarounds accepted the shape of an obstruction and built a path around it.

He opened the file.

Her changes were clean. His earlier comments had been folded into the margins, not quoted, not credited. She had removed his phrasing where it sounded like field defence and replaced it with language London could repeat without injury. The document was better. It had passed through her hands and come back less argumentative, more usable, more exact.

He should have written:

Good changes.

He should have written:

Sorry about the terminal.

He wrote neither.

He corrected one figure in a table and sent it back with:

Pressure number updated.

The reply came three minutes later.

Thanks.

No punctuation after it.

He looked at the single word until the screen dimmed.

At 10:32, the old terminal printed a boarding pass for someone from drilling. The paper came out with one edge crumpled. The man tore it free and cursed the machine with affection, the way people spoke to objects that failed in known ways. Thomas watched the screen return to its login prompt. Anyone could have seen the inbox. Anyone could have asked who Olesya was. Anyone could have read the subject and forgotten it, or remembered it later while standing beside Sarah at the tea urn.

That was the new problem.

Not exposure as an event.

Exposure as residue.

A name left on a screen. A subject line caught by a passing eye. A download in the wrong folder. A notification brightening at the wrong second. A printer tray. A cache. A list of recent files. All the places where a system handled a thing after the person had stopped touching it.

The compound did not need to know secrets.

It only needed to keep traces.

At 10:47, Nisha came to his desk with a finance query.

'Do you know what cost code this should sit under?'

She placed a form beside his keyboard. Her fingernails were painted a dark red that had chipped at the tips. The form carried three signatures and a coffee ring.

'Which job?'

'Basra pump parts. Or maybe not pump parts. The invoice says general materials, which is how vendors confess to sin.'

Thomas read the line items. Seal kit. Coupling. Freight. Expedited handling. Documentation fee.

'Pump.'

'You are sure?'

'Documentation fee is nonsense, but the rest is pump.'

'Everything has a nonsense fee now.'

'That should be a separate cost code.'

She laughed and took the form back.

'Sarah is looking for you.'

He kept his face on the invoice.

'Now?'

'After her call. She said no rush, which means rush, but with manners.'

'Where is she?'

'Conference room.'

Nisha looked at him a fraction longer. Not prying. Not quite. People in compounds learned the difference between information and trouble, and they learned it early.

'Dust is making everyone strange today,' she said.

'That is one diagnosis.'

'Best I can do without medical cover.'

She left.

Thomas saved his work, closed the browser, checked the downloads folder again, and stood. The movement had become procedural. Close. Clear. Lock. Pocket. Walk. He could feel the order forming in his muscles.

The conference room door was half open.

Sarah sat alone at the far end of the table, headset beside her laptop, notebook open to a page filled with square boxes and arrows. The room smelled of stale coffee and dry marker ink. Dust had gathered along the sill behind her. Someone had drawn a smiley face in it with a finger.

'Nisha said you wanted me.'

'I did.'

'About the note?'

'No.'

She turned the notebook around.

At the top of the page she had written:

Baku school support.

Beneath it:

How much?

How long?

Who knows?

Any risk to us?

Thomas looked at the four questions.

'This is efficient.'

'I am predictable.'

'That was not my word.'

'No. It was mine.'

She did not sound wounded. That would have been easier. She sounded like a person opening a file in the correct place.

'I do not have all the answers here.'

'Start with one.'

'How much?'

'That seems traditional.'

He gave her the monthly figure.

Sarah wrote it down without looking up.

'Plus extras?'

'Sometimes.'

'How sometimes?'

'School. Medicine. One competition.'

'Competition?'

'English.'

Her pen paused at the paper.

'She came third.'

'I remember.'

Sarah wrote another note.

'How long?'

'I do not know.'

'That is not a system.'

'No.'

'It is an open-ended commitment.'

He said nothing.

She drew a line beneath the questions. The pen left a small dot where she stopped.

'Who knows?'

'Olesya. Me.'

'The child?'

'Some of it.'

'And now nearly anyone who walked past a shared machine.'

The air conditioner ticked dryly above them.

'I shut it down.'

'You shut down the machine. You did not shut down what happened.'

Her pen moved once between her fingers, end over end.

'Any risk to us?'

'Financially, no.'

'Thomas.'

'I do not know how to answer the larger version.'

'Try without engineering it.'

He looked at the dust-smiley on the window sill. One eye had already begun to collapse where the grit slid down the glass.

'If I stop, it damages them.'

'That is not what I asked.'

'If I continue badly, it damages us.'

'Better.'

She closed the notebook.

'I am not asking you to stop today.'

The words struck the table softly and stayed there.

'What are you asking?'

'To know before I am made part of something by accident.'

'You are not part of it.'

'I was standing in front of a frozen inbox this morning.'

He had no reply to that.

Sarah put the pen down.

'I have another call in twelve minutes.'

'All right.'

'And I need you to decide whether this is help, or whether it is a structure you built so you never have to decide anything.'

She opened the laptop.

The meeting was over.

Thomas logged into his own laptop at 11:20.

The email was there.

From: Olesya Petrova

Subject: March and April

He opened it.

The body was short.

Thomas,

Attached March receipts and April school letter. Also confirmation for extra notebook and competition. Monthly money was enough. Thank you.

O.

There were four attachments.

Rent.

Medicine.

School letter.

Competition certificate.

No confession. No heat. No sentence that said too much unless the reader knew how to read it. Monthly money was enough. A plain line. A wire under carpet.

He downloaded the attachments and saved them to the O folder. Then he deleted the email.

The message vanished from the inbox.

He opened deleted items.

It sat there.

He deleted it again.

The system asked if he wanted to remove it permanently.

He clicked yes.

Then he cleared the downloads folder.

Then the browser cache.

Then he opened the O folder and renamed the attachments:

M-03-A.

M-03-B.

A-04-S.

C-04.

The names looked like maintenance codes. He took a breath through his nose and tasted dust.

At 12:04, Sarah sent him a message.

Lunch?

He looked through the glass. She was still in the conference room, laptop open, head bent over the screen.

He typed:

Five minutes.

She answered:

Make it four. Fish day.

He wrote:

That is not an incentive.

She sent:

For speed, it is.

In the dining hall, she spoke about the call. London had found new concerns. London always found new concerns in the part of the work that belonged to someone else. She had three follow-up notes and one request so foolish she had written it down exactly for future prosecution.

Thomas listened.

He did the right things with his face.

The tray between them held rice, chicken, cucumber, yoghurt, flatbread. Sarah tore bread into pieces while she spoke, then forgot to eat them. Dust had found its way into the corners of the windows and drawn pale lines along the frames.

'You are quiet,' she said.

'I am listening.'

'Those are different.'

'I know.'

'The machine this morning.'

He picked up the yoghurt spoon and put it down again.

'I should not have shut it off like that.'

'No.'

'I handled it badly.'

'No.'

He looked at her.

Sarah's hands had gone still on the bread.

'You were guarding something.'

The sentence stayed on the table.

He could have denied it. The shape of denial was ready. Work. Confidential. Personal account. Old colleague. Messy. Each part fitted the next. A small bridge could be built from one half-truth to another if a man did not look down.

'It was an email from Baku,' he said.

Sarah waited.

'The school receipts. For the girl I told you about.'

'The old colleague's daughter.'

'Yes.'

'Why would that need guarding?'

'It has names. Amounts. Documents.'

'And?'

'It is not my story to put on a company machine.'

That was true.

Not all true.

True enough to stand.

Sarah looked at him across the tray. The dining hall noise moved around them with its forks, chairs, voices, trays sliding over metal rails.

'You are paying regularly.'

He did not ask how she knew.

'Yes.'

'Monthly?'

'Yes.'

'For how long?'

'Since the hospital.'

She looked down at the torn bread.

'You said a tight patch.'

'It became more than that.'

'You did not update the briefing.'

'No.'

A corner of her mouth moved at the phrase, then settled.

'Is this person in trouble?'

'They have limited options.'

'That is not quite an answer.'

'No.'

Sarah leaned back. A man at the next table laughed too loudly and struck his cup with a spoon. The sound rang out, bright and out of place.

'Are we in trouble?' she asked.

'No.'

'That was quick.'

'Financially, no.'

'I did not ask only financially.'
