He looked at the bread in her hands, torn into pieces she had not eaten.

'I have kept it separate.'

She breathed out through her nose.

'That is also not quite an answer.'

He had no better one that would not break the room.

Sarah gathered the bread pieces into a neat pile on the edge of her tray.

'Do not use joint money without telling me.'

'I have not.'

'Do not make me discover something by accident.'

'I will not.'

She watched him for another second, then stood.

'I need to go back.'

He stood too.

'Sarah.'

'Not here.'

She took her tray to the return rack.

He followed after a delay long enough to look accidental.

The afternoon narrowed.

Each hour made a smaller room.

The office became too bright. The bedroom too full. The corridor too exposed. His own laptop screen carried reflections from behind him. Sarah's phone lay on the desk while she worked, face up, every notification innocent and visible. His phone stayed in his pocket until it heated against his leg.

At 15:36, Olesya sent a message.

I cannot send school letter properly in message.

He was in the control room beside the senior operator, who was explaining a pressure fluctuation with a pencil against a printed graph. Thomas nodded, saw nothing. The phone pulsed again.

Olesya:

Email is normal.

Then:

You make me do wrong thing then say wrong.

The operator tapped the graph.

'Here,' he said. 'You see?'

'Yes.'

Thomas did not see.

At 16:05, he went to the bathroom at the end of the corridor and locked himself in the last cubicle. The floor was wet near the drain. Someone had left boot prints in grey arcs across the tiles. The extractor fan made a rough metallic buzz overhead.

He opened the app store.

The connection struggled, then caught.

He searched for the app he had used once for a contractor in Basra who would not send photographs through company channels. Green icon. Encrypted messages. Disappearing options. No email, no subject lines, no accidental preview on a shared terminal.

He downloaded it.

The progress circle moved slowly.

Outside, someone came in, washed hands, left. Water ran. Door closed. The circle reached the end.

He opened the app, registered, adjusted every setting he could find. No previews. Lock screen hidden. Archive unknown contacts. Disappearing messages after seven days. Then one day. Then he changed it back to seven. One day looked like alarm. Seven looked like housekeeping.

He sent Olesya the invitation link.

Use this from now.

Her reply in the old message thread:

Another app?

Yes.

Why?

Cleaner.

That is not reason.

It is enough.

No answer.

He waited in the cubicle until someone tried the door handle. The metal latch jumped under the pressure.

'Occupied,' he said.

The person outside muttered and moved away.

The new app lit with a request.

Olesya Petrova wants to connect.

He accepted.

Her first message arrived there.

I do not like smaller places.

He sat on the closed toilet lid, phone in both hands. The fluorescent light buzzed above him. The wet floor reflected the door in broken strips.

It is only for documents.

No.

For hiding.

He typed:

For keeping things contained.

The word looked precise and insufficient.

Olesya answered:

Contained for who?

He closed the app.

The phone remained in his hand.

At dinner, Sarah spoke to Nisha and did not mention the email. Thomas sat opposite them and watched the space her silence left. Nisha described a payroll error involving three men with the same surname and one bank account that belonged to none of them. Sarah laughed in the right places. She ate rice, drank water, asked one sharp question, then another.

Thomas could have mistaken it for pardon if he had been a more careless man.

He was careful now.

Careful had become the smallest enclosure.

After dinner, Sarah walked back to the room ahead of him. Her keycard worked on the second try. The lock blinked red, then green. She pushed the door open and went in without holding it for him.

The room had changed in his absence, though nothing had moved.

Her laptop on the desk.

His laptop beside it.

Two chargers in the wall.

Two phones on two different surfaces.

The bed made badly in the joint style they had developed: her side pulled tight, his side mostly persuaded into order. A towel over the chair. Dust along the window rail. The kettle beside the socket that did not spark.

Sarah sat on the bed and took off her shoes.

'I am not asking tonight,' she said.

He closed the door.

'All right.'

'That does not mean it has gone away.'

'I know.'

'Good.'

She placed one shoe beside the other with more care than the act required.

'I need sleep.'

'Yes.'

'And tomorrow I need you to read the revised note without destroying company property.'

'I can do that.'

'Good.'

She went into the bathroom.

The shower started.

Thomas stood in the room with the sound of water in the pipes and opened the new app.

Olesya had sent three files.

School letter.

Competition certificate.

April receipts.

Then one message:

Here. Smaller.

He saved the files to the hidden folder.

Then he changed the app name on his phone to Notes.

The icon remained green.

He moved it into a folder with bank apps, airline apps, utility apps, all the small machinery of an adult life. It sat there without complaint.

Sarah came out of the bathroom in a T-shirt, hair wet at the ends, face clean of the day. She placed her phone on the bedside table, screen up.

Thomas placed his face down.

She saw it.

Neither of them spoke.

Later, after the light was off and the air conditioner had settled into its night rattle, his phone buzzed once against the wood.

Not loud.

Enough.

Sarah did not move.

Thomas waited until the room returned to itself. Then he reached for the phone and opened the new app beneath the sheet, brightness lowered until the words seemed printed under water.

Olesya:

Do not make me disappear from places.

He typed nothing.

The message sat in the smaller dark.

Chapter 13

The siren began during the lunch queue.

Not loudly at first. A thin mechanical rise above the clatter of trays, the scrape of serving spoons, the low talk of men deciding between chicken and fish with the seriousness of people who had surrendered choice elsewhere. It climbed once, fell, then climbed again. This time every head lifted.

The dining hall changed without anyone speaking.

Forks stopped. A tray slipped sideways on the rail and struck the metal edge with a flat crack. The television above the drinks station continued showing a football replay to no one. A man from procurement stood with tongs in his hand, one piece of bread held in the air, waiting for a day that had already moved on.

Then the compound alarm found its full voice.

Incoming.

The word did not come from the siren. It came from men already moving.

Security entered through both doors. Black shirts. Radios. Hands out, not touching anyone until they had to. The dining hall staff stepped back from the counter. Steam rose from the rice. The smell of cumin, bleach, oil, and dust seemed to tighten inside the room.

'Shelter,' someone called.

Sarah was three places ahead of Thomas in the queue, holding a tray with salad, flatbread, and a cup of water. She turned once to find him.

He saw her face register the room before it registered him.

Then she put the tray down.

Good.

They had practised this twice in briefings and once in a drill no one had taken seriously enough. Move to the internal corridor. Stay low if outside. Do not return for belongings. Do not use lifts. Do not stand near glass. Wait for all-clear. The words had sat in welcome packs and laminated notices, useful in the abstract.

Now the dining hall doors looked too wide.

Thomas reached Sarah as the first thud arrived.

It was distant, or seemed distant, though distance changed inside a body. The sound did not crack like thunder. It struck the compound through the ground, a blunt pressure that moved through the soles of his boots and into his teeth. The lights flickered once. Someone swore. A woman near the drinks station dropped her phone, bent for it, and was pulled upright by a security guard.

'Move,' the guard said.

Not shouted.

No room for shouting.

Thomas took Sarah's wrist.

She did not resist.

They moved with the others through the side exit and into the internal service corridor that ran behind the dining hall towards the hardened shelter. The corridor was narrow, painted beige halfway up and white above, with old scuff marks where equipment trolleys had struck the walls. The air was hotter there. Bodies pressed close. The overhead lights hummed. A radio hissed against someone's shoulder.

Sarah's wrist was dry in his hand.

'Are you all right?' he asked.

'Walking.'

'Good.'

'Do not ask me questions with obvious answers.'

Her voice came level, clipped. He held onto that.

Another impact, closer.

The corridor compressed. A man ducked and struck his head against a fire extinguisher box. The metal rang. Someone laughed once, a short impossible sound, then stopped. Dust lifted from the top of the pipes and fell through the fluorescent light like sifted flour.

The shelter door stood open at the end.

Inside: concrete, benches, water crates, laminated instructions, a first-aid cabinet, three battery lights, a smell of stored plastic and old air. People entered too quickly and then had nowhere to go. Security pushed them along the walls. Sit. Sit. Keep the middle clear. Phones away unless instructed. Do not block the door.

Thomas and Sarah ended up on a bench near the back wall.

His shoulder touched hers. Her knee touched his. Across from them, Nisha sat with both hands flat on her thighs, staring at the floor between her shoes. The finance clerk from payroll counted people under her breath. A young contractor held a sandwich in its plastic wrapper, still unopened, the bread pressed flat in his grip.

The shelter door closed.

The sound of the compound changed.

Outside noise became something handled by walls. The alarm thinned. The impacts arrived without clear direction. Above them. Beside them. Somewhere beyond the concrete. Each one loosened small grains from the ceiling. The battery lights made everyone's skin the same colour.

Sarah leaned back against the wall.

'This is unpleasant,' she said.

'Yes.'

'I am making that my official incident statement.'

'Concise.'

'I know my audience.'

Her hand found his under the bench. Fingers first, then palm. She held him hard enough for the bones to meet.

He held back.

His phone was in his right pocket.

He had placed it there after lunch, face inward, new app hidden in the utility folder, notifications set to show no text. A soundless object. Managed. Contained.

It vibrated once.

Not a call. A message.

Sarah's hand tightened at the next impact. The vibration ended while he was still deciding whether she had noticed it.

He kept his eyes on the shelter floor.

Grey paint. Chips. Boot marks. A small dark screw near the base of the opposite wall, fallen from somewhere and ignored by everyone until now.

The phone vibrated again.

Then again.

Sarah turned her head a fraction.

'Is that you?'

'Yes.'

'Work?'

'Likely.'

'They can wait.'

'Yes.'

The third vibration came longer.

Not work.

He knew it with the old animal knowledge that had no need of proof.

Across the shelter, the security supervisor listened to his radio, head bent. Static. Numbers. A location code. A request repeated twice. No one in the room breathed loudly enough to compete with it. The shelter made every small sound public: the click of a swallow, the rub of fabric, a shoe sole shifting on concrete.

Thomas did not take out the phone.

He sat beside his wife and watched dust fall.

The shelter found its own order after the first ten minutes.

Strain did not announce itself. It entered through behaviour. A man who had spoken too loudly at lunch folded and unfolded the same paper napkin until it came apart in layers. The security guard nearest the door kept touching the radio clipped to his vest though it had not moved. Nisha wrote one name twice and crossed the second out so hard the pen tore the paper. Sarah's thumb counted the bones across the back of his hand, one at a time, then started again.

Thomas knew the compound systems well enough to picture the layers above them.

Perimeter cameras recording dust.

Operations freezing non-critical work.

Security logging all heads.

Medical standing by.

Admin waiting to send the official phrase once the phrase became available.

No immediate threat to personnel.

No casualties reported.

Routine precautions.

Words designed to travel cleanly to families, managers, insurers, regional offices, anyone whose job depended on distance staying legible. The words would leave out the sandwich flattened in the contractor's hand, the screw on the floor, Sarah's fingers tightening under the bench, the way impact entered bone before sound found language.

His pocket stayed quiet.

That gave him too much room to think.

Olesya's messages did not arrive like Sarah's. Sarah's were part of the visible world: calendar changes, meeting links, jokes about fish, a photograph of a ridiculous sign near the laundry. Olesya's messages came from below the floor of things. Rent. Medicine. Door. School. A child's breath. Problems that did not wait for the right hour.

He had moved her into the smaller app to contain the route.

Now the route had narrowed into a single dark channel in his pocket, and he was sitting on his hand while the channel called.

Sarah leaned closer.

'Your jaw is doing that thing.'

'What thing?'

'The thing where you become a locked filing cabinet.'

'It is probably the alarm.'

'Probably.'

She did not ask again.

The mercy in that was starting to cost both of them.

In Baku, Olesya stood in the stairwell with Anya's schoolbag in one hand and the grocery sack cutting into the fingers of the other.

She had not meant to be late.

The bus had stalled near Narimanov. The driver had opened the doors and half the passengers had stepped into traffic, preferring exhaust and shouting to the dead heat inside the bus. Olesya had waited three minutes, then followed with Anya close behind her. They had walked the last blocks past building sites, fruit sellers, parked cars with film over the windows, men smoking under balconies, the wind from the sea carrying dust and damp concrete.

Anya had talked about the English competition for two streets, then stopped when the grocery bag began to tear.

Now the key did not turn.

Olesya tried it once more, slowly.

The lock held.

From inside the flat came the thin sound of a television.

Not theirs.

She stepped back and looked at the number on the door.

Same door. Same scratch near the handle. Same cheap brass digit hanging slightly crooked. Same strip of old tape where the previous tenant had fixed a New Year decoration and torn paint away removing it.

Anya stood one step below, still in her school shirt. Sweat had flattened the hair at her temples. The red notebook stuck out of the schoolbag.

'Mama?'

'Wait.'

Olesya tried the key again.

No movement.

The neighbour's door opened.

Madina from the second floor looked out in slippers, one hand on the frame, the other holding a cigarette between two fingers. Her hair was wrapped in a towel. She looked at Olesya, then at Anya, then at the grocery bag.

'They changed it.'

Olesya kept the key in the lock.

'Who?'

'Owner's nephew came. With a man.'

'When?'

'Morning.'

'Why?'

Madina lifted one shoulder. 'Ask him.'

'Did they go inside?'

'They went inside. How to change lock from outside?'

Anya moved up one step until her shoulder touched Olesya's coat.

Olesya smelled metal from the key, onion from the torn grocery bag, old smoke from Madina's doorway.

'Did they take anything?'

'I did not stand and watch.'

Madina looked down the stairwell. A child was crying somewhere below. The building smelled of fried oil, damp plaster, drains, and someone's perfume sprayed too heavily in the morning.

'He said rent late.'

'Rent is paid.'

'Then call him.'

Madina closed her door halfway, not all the way. Enough to remove herself. Not enough to miss anything.

Olesya took out her phone.

No signal in the stairwell.

She climbed to the landing window, held the phone against the glass, watched one bar appear, disappear, return. She called the landlord. No answer. She called again. The line cut. She sent a message.

The rent is paid. Why lock changed?

No delivery tick.

Anya stood by the door with the grocery sack at her feet. A wet mark spread beneath it. Tomatoes, probably. Or yoghurt. Something soft giving up under pressure.

'Can we go inside?'

'Soon.'

'My uniform is inside.'

'I know.'

'My medicine.'

Olesya looked at the door.

The asthma medicine was in the kitchen drawer, beside the spoons. Spare inhaler. Nebuliser rental due back in three days. School uniform hanging from the balcony line. Passport copies in the blue folder. The transfer confirmations in the biscuit tin, under the old receipts.

All of it behind a door that now belonged to someone else's key.

She opened the secure app.

Thomas had not answered her earlier message about the school letter. The last thing in the thread was her line from the night before:

Do not make me disappear from places.

She typed:

Lock changed. We are outside.

The message sent.

No reply.

She typed:

Owner says rent late. It is not late.

No reply.

Then:

Anya medicine inside.

No reply.

In the shelter, Thomas kept his hand under Sarah's and counted the vibrations.

One.

Two.

Long gap.

Three.

Then nothing.

The all-clear did not come.

People had begun to arrange themselves into endurance. Someone passed water bottles from a crate. The young contractor finally opened the sandwich and took one bite without looking at it. Nisha wrote names on the back of a printed agenda, checking staff from finance by memory. The payroll clerk had stopped counting under her breath and started again on paper.

Sarah's hand loosened.

'If they keep us here long enough,' she said, 'I am charging this to the client.'

'Shelter hours?'

'Hostile working environment.'

'Accurate.'

'Billable trauma.'

He looked at her.

'Sorry,' she said. 'Bad joke.'

'No.'

'It was.'

She reached for the water bottle at her feet, twisted the cap, drank, then handed it to him. He drank from the same place her mouth had been. The water tasted of plastic and storage.

Another thud, farther this time.

The room stilled.

The radio came alive in fragments.

Impact outside perimeter.

No casualties confirmed.

Stand by.

Stand by.

Sarah's thumb moved over the back of his hand.

'This is close enough,' she said quietly.

'Yes.'

'When we get out, I want to call home.'

'Your mother?'

'Yes. Before she sees anything online.'

'Good.'

'And you should message your people.'

'My people?'

'Office. Field team. Whoever needs to know you still have a head.'

'I will.'

His pocket stayed still.

That was worse.

Still meant messages waiting. Still meant the phone had finished telling him and now required him to choose.

Olesya called the landlord from the pavement.

She had taken Anya downstairs after the stairwell air thickened and the groceries began to smell. The building entrance gave some shade. The courtyard was a square of broken paving, parked cars, washing lines, and one mulberry tree whose roots had lifted the concrete into ridges. Children were playing near the bins with a punctured ball. An old man sat on a stool outside the opposite block, peeling sunflower seeds and watching everything with the patience of unpaid surveillance.

The landlord answered on the fourth call.

'You changed lock.'

'You did not pay.'

'Rent paid. First of month.'

'Bank returned.'

'No.'

'Yes.'

'I have confirmation.'

'Then show bank.'

'Open door.'

'Pay first.'

'I paid.'

'It returned. My nephew saw. You pay balance, locksmith, penalty.'

Olesya closed her eyes once, opened them.

'My daughter needs medicine.'

'Pay, then door opens.'

The line ended.

Anya stood under the mulberry tree with the schoolbag against her chest. The grocery sack had finally split. Two tomatoes rolled under a car. The yoghurt tub had cracked and spread white across the paving. A stray cat came near, then thought better of it.

Olesya took a photograph of the lock.

Then of Anya's schoolbag by the door.

Then of the broken grocery sack.

She sent all three to Thomas.

No reply.

She called him in the new app.

It rang without sound on his side.

No answer.

Olesya ended the call before it could make her feel foolish.

The courtyard made witnesses of everything. Madina's half-open door upstairs. The old man by the opposite block. The children with the punctured ball. The cat returning now that the yoghurt had spread thin enough to lick. Even the mulberry tree seemed to lean over the scene, leaves dull with dust, its roots lifting the paving in slow public damage.

Anya had gone quiet.

That was worse than questions.

Olesya crouched beside the grocery sack and rescued what could be rescued. Bread in its plastic sleeve. One tin dented at the side. A packet of tea. The tomatoes under the car were gone, or else hidden beyond reach. The yoghurt had no second life.

'We can go to Marina auntie,' Anya said.

'She is at work.'

'Madina?'

'No.'

Madina was listening behind her door. That was not the same as opening it.

Olesya stood. Her knees made a small dry click. She brushed yoghurt from the side of the tea packet with her thumb and put it back in the torn sack.

The landlord's nephew arrived in a white car with one black replacement panel on the front wing. He was younger than Olesya expected, wearing sunglasses on top of his head and a shirt with a false designer logo printed too large across the chest. A locksmith came with him, carrying a canvas tool roll.

'You changed already,' Olesya said.

'Owner says change.'

'My things are inside.'

'Pay.'

'Rent paid.'

'Bank says no.'

'Show me.'

He held up his phone, not close enough for her to read. A banking screen. A returned line. Red text. Amount reversed.

The regular transfer had failed somewhere between Thomas's account, the landlord's bank, and the system of names that made every foreign payment slightly suspect. Or the landlord had found a way to make it fail. Or the nephew had a screenshot from another month. Olesya could not tell from three feet away in a courtyard with Anya behind her and the grocery bag leaking at her shoes.

'Open. Let me take medicine.'

'Pay.'

'The child needs inhaler.'

The nephew looked at Anya, then away.

'Pharmacy open.'

'Her inhaler is inside.'

'Pay, then inside.'

Olesya opened the app again.

Thomas had not read the photographs.

Only one tick.

She looked up at the building. Their flat was on the third floor. One window open a little at the top, white curtain moving behind the glass. Their curtain. Her curtain. The room breathing without them.

She typed:

They are here now.

Then:

He wants new rent and penalty.

Then:

Please answer.

The last word sat on the screen with its throat exposed.

She deleted it.

She sent only:

They are here now.

Thomas saw the missed call only after the all-clear.

The shelter door opened with a pressure sigh. Light entered in a hard strip. People stood too quickly, then had to wait while security counted them out. The corridor smelled worse than before, hot bodies and dust and something electrical that had overheated somewhere nearby.

Sarah stayed close beside him.

Outside, the compound looked both ordinary and altered. Dining hall doors open. Trays still on tables. A cup tipped on its side, coffee dried into a brown fan. Dust across the floor. Men speaking too loudly now that speaking had been returned to them.

The sky had cleared a little.

The world always had that indecency after impact.

Thomas took out his phone as they crossed the corridor.

Four messages.

One missed call.

Lock changed. We are outside.

Owner says rent late. It is not late.

Anya medicine inside.

Three photographs.

The concrete under him shifted without moving.

Sarah saw the screen light his face.

'Work?'

'No.'

'Baku?'

He did not answer quickly enough.

'Thomas.'

'They are locked out.'

'Who is locked out?'

'Olesya and Anya.'

The names stood between them without cover.

A door opened behind them. People came out of the shelter corridor in a slow line, blinking into the light. Someone asked where lunch had gone. Someone else laughed, too high and too soon.

Sarah's eyes did not leave him.

'Why?'

'Landlord says rent failed.'

'Did it?'

'It should not have.'

'Should not have is not did not.'

He opened the banking app.

The connection failed.

He tried again.

Failed.

The compound network had collapsed under everyone calling, messaging, reporting themselves alive. The signal bars appeared, disappeared, came back with no data behind them.

Olesya sent another message.

Landlord wants penalty. Locksmith. One month again.

Then:

She needs inhaler.

Sarah read it over his shoulder.

Her reading stopped before she moved away.

'Go,' she said.

'Where?'

'Wherever the network works.'

'You need to call home.'

'I can call home myself.'

'Sarah.'

'Go.'

No forgiveness in it.

Permission, perhaps.

He ran.

Not at first. Running inside the compound after an incoming alarm made security turn heads. He walked fast through the dining hall, past the abandoned trays, through the side exit, into the glare. Then faster along the gravel path towards the administration block, where the sat link sometimes held when the rest of the camp failed.

Behind him, Sarah called his name once.

He did not stop.

The compound after an alert had its own disorder. Not chaos. Never that. The place was too disciplined for chaos. It was procedure with dust in its mouth. Security men at junctions. Radios alive. Supervisors counting heads again after they had already counted them. Men trying to sound casual on calls to wives, parents, managers. The dining hall staff collecting uneaten food from trays with the flat expressions of people who had done this before and would do it again.

At the clinic door, two medics stood waiting with gloves on.

No one came to them.

That was good.

That was also obscene.

Thomas passed the clinic and saw himself reflected in the dark strip of its window. Shirt creased. Dust on one cheek. Phone in hand. A man moving urgently through one protected place to send money to another unprotected one.

His wife was somewhere behind him.

Olesya was outside a door.

Anya's medicine was in a drawer.

There was no neutral order for those facts.

His phone showed one bar.

No data.

He held it higher, giving height a power it did not have. The gesture made him look foolish. He kept doing it.

At the administration entrance, a queue had formed. Men with phones. A supervisor with a satellite handset. Two security guards controlling the doorway. Everyone needed a line out. Everyone had a wife, a mother, a manager, a crew list, a rumour to stop before it travelled.

Thomas stood behind a drilling engineer still wearing his dining hall apron.

The phone pulsed.

Olesya:

He says if not pay now he takes things out.

Thomas opened the bank app again.

This time the login screen appeared.

Then froze.

He pressed again.

Nothing.

The queue moved one person.

Inside the admin block, someone shouted for bandwidth discipline. The phrase travelled badly through the hall and came out absurd. A man in front of Thomas swore in Afrikaans. The drilling engineer kept refreshing a news site until the guard told him to stop.

Thomas's bank app opened at last.

Transfer blocked.

Security check required.

He stared at the line.

The bank offered reasons in small grey text.

Unusual recipient activity.

Unusual location.

Multiple recent transfers.

Protecting your account.

The words sat there with the patience of institutions. Protecting your account from the thing your account was for. Protecting the line from the hand reaching across it. Protecting a number in London from a locked door in Baku.

He pressed Continue.

The app asked for a call.

He pressed Call me.

No service.

He pressed again.

No service.

The queue moved half a step.

Olesya sent a photograph.

Anya sitting on the stair, schoolbag on her knees, one hand pressed against her chest. Not dramatic. Not collapsed. Sitting upright, chin lowered, the way a child behaves when adults have made the air too complicated to enter.

The image downloaded line by line.

Hair.

Forehead.

Eyes lowered.

Hand.

Schoolbag.

Then stopped before the bottom of the frame.

The incomplete image remained on his screen.

The regular payment had gone through three days earlier. The bank had accepted January, February, March, April. It had accepted birthday, school, pharmacy, dentist, the red notebook. Now, under a compound alert, with Olesya outside her own door and Anya's inhaler in a drawer, the bank had decided to become careful.

Confirm identity.

Receive call.

The call could not come.

Signal fell to nothing.

He closed the app, opened it, tried again.

Transfer blocked.

Security check required.

Behind him, Sarah appeared at the edge of the path.

She had not run. She had tied her scarf back over her hair and brought his laptop bag. The dust had marked one side of her shirt where she must have brushed the corridor wall.

She did not come closer.

He saw her take out her phone and hold it to her ear.

Calling home.

Good.

He turned away before she could see the bank screen again.

Olesya called.

He answered.

Her voice came in broken pieces.

'Thomas. He is here. He says ten minutes.'

'I am trying.'

'You said rent paid.'

'It was.'

'Door is locked.'

'I know.'

'Anya needs medicine.'

'I know.'

The line cracked. A horn sounded somewhere near her. Anya coughed in the background, once, then again, close enough to the phone for the sound to distort.

'Listen,' he said. 'I need to clear the bank block. It will take minutes.'

'We do not have minutes.'

The line dropped.

He called back.

Failed.

The queue moved.

He entered the admin building at 14:17.

Inside, the air was cooler and sharper, full of printer heat and men speaking into devices. A radio sat on the reception desk, giving updates in a calm voice that made nothing calmer. The sat terminal stood on a table near the wall, guarded by a sign-up sheet and a woman from logistics who had the expression of someone ready to deny a minister if he had not written his name down.

'Emergency family call?' she asked.

'Bank verification.'

She looked at him.

'After incoming?'

'Yes.'

'Two minutes.'

'I need five.'

'You have two.'

The room behind her was full of other emergencies wearing better paperwork.

A rig manager telling Aberdeen that no, the hit had been outside perimeter.

A medic checking a list against a radio call.

A young man in a company polo trying to get a line to his wife and saying baby twice before the call connected.

The sat terminal cable ran across the floor under a strip of yellow tape. People stepped over it carefully, giving the line the respect of something living.

Thomas wrote his name on the sheet after all.

T Ward.

Reason:

Bank verification.

The woman from logistics read it upside down.

'Two minutes,' she said again.

He took the handset.

The bank number took forty seconds to connect.

Recorded voice. Options. Security phrase. Date of birth. Last transaction. The line delayed after every answer, turning time into small cuts.

At one minute thirty, the woman from logistics tapped the table.

He lifted one finger.

At two minutes, she folded her arms.

'Sir,' the bank voice said, 'we blocked the payment due to unusual pattern and location.'

'Unblock it.'

'I need to confirm the recipient.'

'Olesya Petrova.'

'And the purpose?'

He looked at the wall.

A laminated poster showed the shelter route in red arrows.

'Family support.'

'Please repeat.'

'Family support.'

The woman from logistics looked away.

The phrase did not belong to him cleanly.

It belonged to Sarah in Scotland and Iraq, to shared accounts and mortgage payments, to a life visible enough for tax codes and Christmas cards. It belonged to Olesya outside a locked door and to Anya's schoolbag on a stair. It belonged to neither if anyone asked for proof.

The bank accepted it without interest.

The bank voice clicked, paused, returned.

'The block is cleared. Please wait ten minutes before retrying.'

'I do not have ten minutes.'

'The block is cleared.'

The line ended with weather, dust, locked doors, a child coughing in a courtyard, and the after-sound of mortars still moving through concrete all left outside the machine.

He handed back the phone.

'Thank you.'

'Next time write your name on the sheet.'

'Yes.'

Outside, Sarah had finished her call. She stood beneath the strip of shade by the admin wall, phone in one hand, laptop bag in the other. Neither of them moved towards the other.

Her face had changed since the shelter.

Not hardened. That would have been too simple. It had become more attentive, reading him at a smaller font size.

'Your mother?' he asked.

'Fine.'

'Good.'

'Your bank?'

'Cleared.'

'Then do it.'

He opened the app.

Signal one bar.

Enough.

Recipient: Olesya Petrova.

Amount: rent, penalty, locksmith, medicine.

Reference: April correction.

Purpose: Other.

Fee: accepted.

Delivery: same day.

He pressed confirm.

The circle turned.

Sarah watched the screen with him.

The circle kept turning.

Somewhere beyond the compound wall, a generator changed pitch.

The confirmation appeared.

Transfer sent.

He took a screenshot and sent it through the secure app.

Paid. Show him.

The message delivered.

One tick.

Then two.

No answer.

They waited.

Sarah shifted the laptop bag from one shoulder to the other.

'Will that open the door?'

'It should.'

'You keep saying should today.'

'Yes.'

'Does she have somewhere else to go?'

'Not easily.'

'Does the child have the medicine now?'

'No.'

Sarah looked towards the administration door. Men moved in and out behind the glass. On the radio inside, the official voice had begun naming normal operations by stages: dining hall reopened, non-essential movement restricted, convoy schedule pending review. The compound was repairing itself with language.

'You need to call her,' Sarah said.

'Signal.'

'Use the sat phone again.'

'They will not let me.'

'Then ask badly.'

He looked at her.

'You heard me.'

He went back inside.

The woman from logistics saw him and lifted a hand before he reached the table.

'No.'

'I need thirty seconds.'

'Everyone needs thirty seconds.'

'There is a child without medicine.'

That entered the room differently.

Not loudly. It did not make him noble. It made the logistics woman's mouth tighten and made the man beside the terminal lower his eyes to the floor.

'Thirty,' she said.

He called Olesya.

This time the line connected.

No greeting.

'Paid,' he said. 'Show him the screenshot.'

'I showed.'

'And?'

'He is calling owner.'

'Where are you?'

'Outside.'

'Anya?'

'Here.'

In the background, Anya coughed. Smaller now. Or farther from the phone.

'Can she breathe?'

'Yes.'

The answer was too quick.

He closed his eyes for one second and opened them before the darkness could gather.

'Olesya.'

'Do not say my name like this.'

The line crackled.

'Call me when door opens.'

'He says bank may reverse.'

'It will not.'

'You do not know.'

He did know and did not. The bank had made that plain.

'Call me when door opens,' he said again.

The logistics woman held up three fingers, then two.

Olesya said:

'You were gone.'

The line ended.

Thomas stood outside the administration block with dust in his mouth and Sarah beside him, close enough to touch, not touching.

The siren stayed silent.

His phone did not.

Chapter 14

The lockdown arrived by email before breakfast.

No siren this time. No thud through concrete. No shelter door closing with people on the wrong side of lunch. Only a message from regional security, copied to operations, logistics, medical, travel, and anyone senior enough to be made responsible by inclusion.

Effective immediately.

Movement restricted to essential personnel.

All outbound travel suspended pending review.

Rotations paused.

Visitors to remain on site.

Do not proceed to airport unless instructed.

The words came in black type on a white screen while the compound was still washing itself awake. Sprinklers clicked across the grass. The dining hall extractor fans started one by one. Men walked towards breakfast with towels around their necks, not yet briefed into the new rules. The sun sat low behind a veil of dust, flattening the accommodation blocks into pale shapes with hard edges.

Thomas read the email twice.

Then he read the recipient list.

Sarah was on it.

She stood in the bathroom, brushing her teeth with the door open. The mirror was fogged at the edges. Her phone lay on the bed, face up, charging from the socket that did not spark. It lit once, then again. The same email. A calendar alert beneath it. Travel review. Pack. Convoy to airport.

None of that belonged to the day any more.

'What?' she said from the bathroom.

He looked up.

'Travel is suspended.'

She spat into the basin and rinsed her mouth.

'Suspended how?'

'All outbound. Pending review.'

'Today?'

'Effective immediately.'

She came to the doorway with the toothbrush still in one hand. Her hair was wet from the shower and dark against her neck.

'My flight is tonight.'

'Not now.'

'I know what suspended means.'

She stepped past him, picked up her phone, and read the email without sitting down. Her face did not alter much. That was how he knew the thing had landed. Sarah moved first in the body, later in the face. Her left foot pressed harder into the floor. Her thumb stopped on the screen. A drop of water ran from her hair to the collar of her T-shirt and stayed there.

'How long?'

'No date.'

'There is always a date, even if it is a lie.'

'Not here.'

She scrolled, found the attachment, opened it, closed it.

'Regional?'

'Global wording.'

'That is worse.'

He said nothing.

She sat on the edge of the bed and placed the phone beside her. Her suitcase stood open under the window, half-packed. Two shirts folded. One pair of shoes wrapped in a laundry bag. A stack of documents in a plastic sleeve. A packet of oat biscuits she had bought for the flight, already softened by the heat.

'I was ready to go home,' she said.

The line did not ask for comfort.

He gave her none.

Outside, someone laughed in the corridor, unaware of the new size of the world.

By eight, the compound knew.

It knew through the official briefing in the dining hall, through travel coordinators with red eyes and printed lists, through men refreshing airline pages until the pages failed, through supervisors calling wives from stairwells, through the sudden value of cigarettes, instant coffee, spare chargers, extra medication. The compound had always been temporary. That had been one of its promises. Hard beds, bad food, dust in the teeth, but with a departure date attached.

The date had been removed.

Everything changed weight.

Sarah did not cry. She did not shout. She made three calls before breakfast and two after. London first. Then her mother. Then the travel desk. Then London again. Then a friend in Edinburgh who was supposed to collect her from the airport and now received instructions about heating, post, and the basil plant on the kitchen sill, already condemned by distance.

Thomas sat at the desk with his laptop open and no useful work inside it.

The official lockdown tracker appeared in the shared drive at 09:12.

Rows of names.

Nationality.

Role.

Location.

Room.

Medical notes.

Travel status.

Sarah's name sat amongst contractors, engineers, finance staff, visiting auditors, two trainers, and one vendor whose paperwork had been wrong even before the world closed.

Visitor.

On site.

Travel suspended.

Thomas looked at the word visitor until it lost shape.

Sarah stood behind him.

'Do not look at me like a row in a spreadsheet.'

He closed the file.

'Sorry.'

'I know it is your native language.'

'Still.'

She reached over him for her laptop. Her arm brushed his shoulder. The contact was ordinary and not ordinary.

'I have calls.'

'What do you need?'

'A room that is not this room.'

'Conference room?'

'Booked. Everyone wants to be trapped professionally.'

'Quiet office?'

'Nisha has it.'

'Operations side office.'

'Does it smell of printer toner and old socks?'

'Yes.'

'Perfect. No one in London will notice the difference.'

He gave her the side-office key and watched her leave with laptop, notebook, charger, phone, water bottle, scarf, and the careful expression she used when the thing requiring management was herself.

His own phone vibrated.

Olesya.

It is closed here also.

He opened the secure app.

School closed.

Packing closed.

Buses less.

Owner says no visits to flat without notice.

Anya cough again in night.

The messages had arrived in a stack, each one a smaller room than the last.

Thomas typed:

Medicine?

Olesya replied:

Two days.

Then:

Pharmacy expensive now.

Then:

My work says no shifts this week.

He looked towards the open door. Sarah's suitcase under the window. Sarah's phone charger still in the wall. Sarah's slippers beside the bed, one upright, one fallen sideways.

Send list, he wrote.

Olesya answered:

List is longer.

Send it.

The dots appeared and disappeared.

Then:

You are inside with her?

He held the phone still.

Yes.

No answer.

At ten thirty, the compound moved into lockdown rhythm.

Meal times split by block. Temperature checks outside the dining hall. Laundry reduced. Gym closed. Visitors instructed to stay near accommodation unless authorised. All non-essential meetings remote, which meant the same people sat in rooms beside each other and called into screens that failed every third sentence. The corridors filled with taped arrows, hand-written signs, plastic chairs placed at odd intervals to enforce space between bodies that had already breathed the same air for weeks.

Sarah returned from the side office at midday carrying her laptop against her chest.

'London wants me to keep working.'

'Can you?'

'That was not the question they asked.'

'What did they ask?'

'Whether I had sufficient connectivity to maintain delivery.'

'And?'

'I said sufficient is a theological term.'

She placed the laptop on the desk and looked at the suitcase.

'I should empty the suitcase.'

'Maybe wait.'

'For what? A miracle flight with legroom?'

He did not answer.

She knelt and took out the shoes in the laundry bag. Then the shirts. Then the plastic sleeve of documents. She laid each item back into the room with a deliberateness that made unpacking look more violent than packing had been.

'Do you know what the strangest part is?'

'What?'

'I am not stuck somewhere unfamiliar.'

She slid the suitcase under the bed with her foot.

'I am stuck in your life.'

The kettle clicked off.

Neither of them moved to pour it.

By afternoon, the first money problem arrived.

Olesya sent a photograph from the pharmacy window. The queue stood outside on the pavement, each person separated by a square of tape. The sign on the glass was in Azerbaijani and Russian. Restricted entry. Masks required. No credit for medicine. A small shelf near the door held bottles of hand gel priced like imported perfume.

Below the photograph came the list.

Inhaler.

Nebuliser liquid.

Cough syrup.

Paracetamol.

Masks.

Soap.

Rice.

Oil.

Eggs.

Internet for school.

The last item sat there with no price.

Thomas added the amounts in the monthly sheet.

The total was not impossible.

That no longer comforted him.

The cost itself had stopped being the point. The point was route. Bank. Reference. Recipient. Pattern. Sarah in the next room. Olesya in the app. The landlord who now knew money came from outside. The bank that had blocked him once and would learn from its own suspicion. Every transfer left a line. Every line wanted a name.

He opened the banking app.

Then closed it.

He opened the spreadsheet.

Then closed it.

He looked at the suitcase under the bed.

Then at Sarah's laptop on the desk, still warm from use.

The room had become too full of evidence.

He took his phone and went to the laundry block.

The laundry smelled of detergent, hot lint, and damp cotton. Machines turned behind scratched glass. Two dryers were out of order, though one still spun with nothing inside it. A hand-written roster had been taped to the wall, revised three times in different ink. Someone had left a sock on top of the payment box. Blue. Child-sized, though no children lived here.

Thomas stood between the machines and opened the banking app again.

Amount.

Recipient.

Reference.

Purpose.

He stopped at the reference line.

April correction had already been used.

Family support had been spoken aloud to the bank through a satellite handset with Sarah standing outside.

Rent looked like rent.

Medicine looked like medicine.

Other looked like what he had made of his life.

He typed:

Supplies.

Then deleted it.

Maintenance.

Deleted.

Household.

Deleted.

He left the reference blank.

The app would not allow it.

He typed:

April.

Purpose: Other.

The bank asked for additional verification.

Not blocked. Not yet. A softer gate. A code sent by text. A warning about fraud. A reminder never to transfer money under pressure.

Thomas stared at that line beside the washing machines.

Never transfer money under pressure.

A dryer changed direction with a dull internal thump.

He entered the code.

The transfer went through.

He sent the screenshot to Olesya.

For medicine and food.

Her reply:

This is too much.

Use it.

She wrote:

You say use like cupboard opens when you say.

He stood in the laundry block with the smell of hot lint in his throat and did not answer.

When he returned to the room, Sarah was sitting on the floor with the suitcase open again.

Not packing.

Rearranging.

She had divided the contents into piles.

Clean.

Laundry.

Work.

Home.

The home pile was smallest: oat biscuits, one book, a scarf, the plastic sleeve of documents, two wrapped gifts for people in Edinburgh, bought too early and now stranded. She held one of the gifts in her lap, turning it over.

'My mother will think I did this deliberately,' she said.

'Get trapped in Iraq?'

'To avoid lunch with my aunt.'

'That is an elaborate plan.'

'My family will respect the commitment.'

He sat on the bed.

'Did you get through to her?'

'Yes. She did the thing where she becomes calm and then sends eight messages afterwards.'

'Good.'

'One of them says basil dead.'

'I am sorry for your loss.'

'It died doing what it knew. Being neglected by me.'

She placed the gift in the home pile.

'Where did you go?'

'Laundry.'

'Without laundry?'

He looked at the empty chair.

'Needed space.'

'There is no space.'

'Less room, then.'

She nodded once. Not accepting. Marking.

'Baku?'

'Yes.'

'The child?'

'Needs medicine.'

'Did you send money?'

'Yes.'

Her fingers flattened the gift wrapping at one corner.

'From your account?'

'Yes.'

'Good.'

Then, after a pause:

'Do not make me ask every time.'

'I will not.'

'That means tell me before I ask.'

'I know.'

'Do you?'

He had no answer large enough for the room.

Sarah put the wrapped gift into the suitcase and closed the lid without fastening it.

That evening, the compound served dinner in paper boxes.

Block B between six and six thirty. Block C after that. Thomas and Sarah queued under the dining hall awning with taped marks on the ground keeping everyone apart by regulation, not by instinct. The wind had dropped. Heat held the dust low. A man ahead of them wore a mask below his nose and kept touching it with the hand that held his phone. Sarah watched him for three seconds, then looked away with visible effort.

'This will end badly,' she said.

'The food or the system?'

'Yes.'

They received rice, chicken, boiled vegetables, and an orange each. The boxes softened at the corners before they reached the room.

They ate on the bed, cross-legged, the desk occupied by laptops. Sarah's phone lay face up between them. His lay face down beside his knee.

She looked at it.

He turned it over.

No notifications showed. He had hidden them all.

'That is not better,' she said.

'What?'

'Blankness.'

'It is quieter.'

'For whom?'

He put the phone beside the food box, screen up.

The new app icon still sat inside the utilities folder, renamed Notes. No preview. No name. No text. A door without a handle.

Sarah watched him do it and said nothing.

The phone stayed dark until after dinner.

When it lit, it showed only:

Notes.

1 notification.

Sarah's eyes went to it before his hand did.

'Is that her?'

'Yes.'

'Then read it.'

He opened it.

Olesya had sent a photograph of Anya at the kitchen table. School on the screen of a small phone propped against a mug. The red notebook open. An inhaler beside her hand. A packet of masks on the table. Rice and oil behind it. The flat was theirs again, but the door still seemed capable of rejecting them.

Message:

She is inside.

Then:

Medicine bought.

Then:

Thank you.

Sarah saw the photograph.

Thomas did not move quickly enough to hide it. Or perhaps, by then, hiding had become the more visible act.

'That is Anya?'

'Yes.'

Sarah leaned closer, careful not to touch the phone.

'She is small.'

'She is ten.'

'That is small.'

He looked at the photograph again. Anya's shoulders drawn in. The red notebook under her hand. The inhaler close enough to be reached without standing.

'Yes.'

Sarah sat back.

'And the mother?'

'What about her?'

'What does she need?'

He looked at her.

'Sarah.'

'I am asking practically.'

'Everything is practical until it is not.'

'That sounds like something you say when you want to stop the question.'

The air conditioner rattled above them, caught, rattled again.

He closed the app.

'She needs the flat to stay hers. She needs school to continue. She needs work to reopen. She needs the landlord to stop pressing. She needs a country that does not make her smaller at every counter.'

Sarah looked down at the rice box in her lap.

'And you?'

'What about me?'

'What do you need?'

His phone screen went dark.

'I need the routes to keep working.'

Sarah's mouth tightened. She placed the lid on her food box.

'That was a very thin answer.'

He did not say the word back to her. Sentiment would have used air the room no longer had.

The next morning, the compound inventory began.

Each room received a form. Occupants. Medical requirements. Dietary restrictions. Work equipment. Current flight booking. Passport location. Emergency contact. Duration of medication available. Special considerations.

Sarah filled hers in at the desk while Thomas made coffee from the last of the jar.

'Emergency contact,' she said.

'Your mother?'

'Yes.'

'Mine is still you.'

Her pen stopped.

'Is it?'

'On the company system.'

'That is not what I asked.'

He poured water into the mugs.

'Yes.'

She wrote her mother's number and did not look up.

Thomas filled his form afterwards.

Emergency contact:

Sarah Ward.

Medical requirements:

None.

Special considerations:

None.

The word none repeated itself down the page with bureaucratic calm.

His phone vibrated from the bed.

Olesya:

School wants internet paid for three months. No monthly.

Then:

Owner says next rent cash only.

Then:

My work maybe not open this month.

The container tightened.

Thomas looked at the form.

Special considerations:

None.

He crossed out nothing.

At midday, he went to finance.

Not for money. For concealment.

The finance office had been moved into a larger room so people could sit apart. Chairs placed too far from desks. A printer on a side table. Hand gel at the door. Nisha sat behind a screen made from a clear plastic sheet that had begun to bow in the heat. Her eyes lifted above her mask when he entered.

'If this is about expenses, abandon hope.'

'Not expenses.'

'Then I am listening with one ear. The other is in a payroll abyss.'

He stood on the taped mark before her desk.

'Can I split salary allocation?'

'Between accounts?'

'Yes.'

'UK and offshore?'

'UK and another UK account.'

'Yours?'

'Yes.'

She looked at him over the plastic screen.

'Since when do engineers become interesting?'

'Since payroll abyss.'

'You want less going to the joint account?'

'No. Same to joint. More of the remainder separated.'

'That is not split salary. That is self-admin.'

'I want it clean.'

'Clean costs forms.'

'Then forms.'

She pulled one from a tray and slid it through the gap beneath the screen.

'Secondary account details. Payroll cut-off tomorrow. Do not make a mistake, or your money will spend a month in a suspense account and no one will admit owning the suspense.'

'Thanks.'

'Also, if you are hiding money from your wife, do not involve me.'

He looked at her.

Nisha's eyes did not soften.

'If you are protecting her from admin, still do not involve me.'

'It is not that.'

'It never is.'

He took the form.

The paper was warm from the printer.

Back in the room, Sarah was on a call, headphones in, speaking to London with one hand over her eyes. He placed the payroll form inside his notebook and sat on it until she finished.

When she took off the headphones, the room seemed to expand by an inch and no more.

'Bad?' he asked.

'Everyone has discovered the word continuity and is using it without licence.'

'Criminal.'

'Deeply.'

She rubbed the bridge of her nose.

'You?'

'Finance.'

'Expenses?'

'Payroll.'

She looked at him.

'What about payroll?'

'I am separating some of my personal account.'

'For Baku.'

'Yes.'

'To hide it from me?'

'To keep it from touching joint money.'

'Those are close neighbours.'

'I know.'

She removed the headphones from the desk and coiled the wire slowly.

'Show me the form.'

He took it from the notebook.

She read it without touching it.

Secondary account.

Allocation.

Signature.

Cut-off date.

'So I see less, and you call that protecting the joint account.'

'You see the joint account unchanged.'

'I see less of you.'

He said nothing.

She placed the form back on the notebook.

'Sign it if you are going to sign it.'

'Sarah.'

'No. Do not ask me to approve the shape of it. I understand the shape.'

She put the headphones back on though there was no call.

He signed the form in the operations office.

The pen did not work at first. He scratched it across the bottom of a printout until the ink started, then wrote his name on the payroll line with more pressure than needed. The signature came out darker than usual.

Afterwards, he opened the secure app.

From now I may send less often but larger.

Olesya replied after eleven minutes.

Why?

Less trace.

For who?

Everyone.

She wrote:

Less often means I wait more.

He typed:

It is safer.

Then deleted safer before sending.

It is cleaner.

Her reply:

For who?

The same question. Smaller now. Sharper.

He did not answer.

By the third day, lockdown had made the compound smaller than its fence.

The gym stayed closed. The dining hall served boxes. The smoking area became a place of rumours. The clinic called room by room for temperature checks. The internet slowed while everyone tried to keep lives alive elsewhere. Men who had once complained about flights now checked flight maps for planes that did not move. Sarah's world shrank to the desk, the side office, the bed, the corridor, the dining hall queue, and the square of sky visible through the dusty window.

Olesya's world shrank too.

Flat.

Pharmacy.

Queue.

Landlord.

School on a phone.

Messages at odd hours.

Receipts when shops still printed them.

Photographs when they did not.

The monthly sheet no longer fitted the month.

Rent had become rent plus penalty plus cash demand.

School had become school plus internet.

Medicine had become medicine plus masks plus delivery.

Food had become food before the prices changed again.

Contingency had become everything.

By the fifth day, the compound began rationing certainty.

Official updates arrived at nine and five, whether or not anything had changed. Travel remained suspended. External movement remained restricted. Local regulations remained under review. Medical screening remained mandatory. The repetition was meant to calm people. It did the opposite. It made language feel like furniture pushed against a door.

Sarah stopped asking when she could leave.

She asked better questions.

'Who signs off visitor medication?'

'Medical.'

'Who gets priority on laundry?'

'Field crew.'

'Who decides whether guests can use the office printer?'

'No one until someone complains.'

'Good. Then I am using it.'

She adapted with visible competence and invisible cost. She built a second version of herself from what the compound allowed. Calls before breakfast. Work in the side office. Food boxes at six. Ten minutes outside after dinner when the guards permitted it. A towel washed by hand and hung over the wardrobe door. A list taped inside the cupboard: coffee, soap, toothpaste, paracetamol, patience. The last word was crossed out by the second afternoon and replaced with batteries.

Thomas found the list whilst looking for a clean shirt.

He stood with the cupboard door open, reading the crossed-out word.

Sarah came in behind him.

'That was private stationery.'

'It was taped to a public cupboard.'

'Public to two people.'

'Still public.'

She reached past him and took out her black shirt.

'How is Baku?'

'Contained.'

'That word is doing too much work.'

'Yes.'

She waited.

He shut the cupboard.

'The school wants stable internet. The landlord wants cash. Her work is closed. The pharmacy prices moved again.'

'And you?'

'I am adjusting.'

'You are reducing.'

He looked at her.

'What?'

'That is what you do when a system tightens. You do not ask for more room. You make yourself smaller inside it.'

She folded the shirt over her arm.

'It is efficient. It is also not the same as living.'

She went to the bathroom to change. The door clicked shut without force.

The word remained.

Reducing.

In Baku, Olesya sent fewer photographs and more totals.

No receipt. Cash only.

Internet man wants three months.

Mask price different today.

Owner says he will come Friday.

Each message removed a layer of world. No tablecloth. No ruler. No Anya's hand holding a corner down. No pharmacy stamp. Only demand, amount, deadline. The paper trail thinned exactly when he needed it thicker.

He asked once:

Can you photograph cash receipt?

Her reply came after an hour.

There is no receipt if they do not give receipt.

Then:

You want proof more than door.

He put the phone down and went to the sink.

The room's tap had begun to drip. One drop every nine seconds. He counted it through the night when the air conditioner paused. Drop. Wait. Drop. Wait. The sound entered the spaces where answers should have been.

The next morning he took the kettle apart and cleaned the scale from the base with vinegar from the dining hall. The room smelled sharp and domestic. Sarah watched from the bed with her laptop on her knees.

'Is the kettle your enemy now?'

'It was underperforming.'

'A moral failing.'

'In appliances, yes.'

She looked at him over the screen.

'You slept at the desk.'

'I woke early.'

'You slept at the desk.'

He rinsed the kettle.

'Some of the night.'

'Why?'

'Did not want to wake you.'

'By sleeping?'

He dried the base with a towel.

'By moving.'

Sarah closed the laptop.

'You are allowed to take up space in a room you are also trapped in.'

He placed the kettle back on its stand.

The socket sparked faintly when he switched it on.

Both of them looked at it.

'That,' Sarah said, 'is not a metaphor. That is bad wiring.'

He unplugged it.

For once, the practical thing was only practical.

Thomas stopped using colours in the spreadsheet.

Green had become dishonest.

He removed the conditional formatting, one rule at a time, until the sheet sat plain and white. No good months. No bad months. No warning red. Only rows and numbers with their little grid lines, each cell pretending to contain what it could only point towards.

Sarah watched from the bed.

'Why make it uglier?'

'It was distracting.'

'It was yours?'

'Yes.'

'The Baku sheet.'

He did not close the laptop.

'Yes.'

'Show me.'

The request came quietly enough to be refused.

That was why it could not be.

He turned the laptop.

Sarah sat forward.

She read longer than he expected. Not the names. There were no names now, only categories. Rent. School. Medicine. Food. Internet. Owner. Penalty. Transport. Other. She read the months, the increasing totals, the notes cut down to initials, the blank places where explanation had been removed.

'You have made it bloodless,' she said.

'It needs to be manageable.'

'Does that help?'

'Sometimes.'

'Not today.'

No.

Not today.

She leaned back against the wall. Her face had the grey cast of the room's bad light.

'Where am I in your systems?'

'Sarah.'

'No, answer.'

'Everywhere visible.'

'That is not comforting.'

'It was not meant to be.'

'Then what was it meant to be?'

He looked at the sheet.

Joint account.

Emergency contact.

Room list.

Travel status.

Visitor on site.

Wife.

Visible life and visible risk.

'True,' he said.

She nodded once. The answer had confirmed something she had already written down elsewhere.

That night, he changed the settings again.

Not in the bathroom. Not in the laundry block. At the desk, while Sarah slept behind him with the sheet pulled to her waist and one arm over her eyes to block the corridor light.

He turned off previews on every account.

He moved the banking app into a folder labelled Travel.

He moved Notes into the second page of Utilities.

He removed Olesya's surname from the contact.

Then he removed the first name too.

O.

Too clear.

He changed it to:

Admin.

Then changed it again.

Supplies.

He looked at the word.

It made her smaller than a person.

He kept it.

For Sarah, he changed nothing.

Her name remained whole in his phone, her photograph beside it from a winter walk in Scotland: hair blown across her mouth, eyes narrowed against wind, one hand lifted to block the camera. The image had been taken before Baku, before the compound, before the monthly sheet. He considered removing the photograph, making her a name only, less exposed in the same machinery.

He did not.

He muted their shared calendar alerts.

Flights. Home. Dinner with Mark and Fiona. Dentist. Mortgage renewal. Birthdays. All the small visible ropes that tied their ordinary life together. None of them could happen from here. Letting them appear every morning was a kind of cruelty.

He turned them off one by one.

The phone became quieter.

The room did not.

At 02:14, Olesya messaged.

I see you made me supplies.

He sat very still.

The app had sent a contact-change notice.

The app had done exactly that.

He opened the thread.

I did not mean you to see that.

She replied:

But I did.

Then:

Good name. That is what I am now.

He typed:

No.

Deleted it.

He typed:

It is for privacy.

Deleted it.

Sarah turned in the bed behind him. The sheet moved against her legs.

Olesya wrote:

Do not answer. I know.

He held the phone in both hands until the screen dimmed.

In the morning, Sarah woke to find him at the desk.

'Did you sleep?'

'Some.'

'That means no.'

He placed the phone face down.

She saw the movement.

He placed it face up again.

That was the new rule. Face up in the room. Face down nowhere. No sudden closures. No dramatic exits from screens. He had made it to give Sarah less to suspect, and himself less room to invent innocence.

The phone showed nothing.

Sarah sat up and pushed hair from her face.

'You look terrible.'

'Thank you.'

'Not romantically.'

'Still.'

She looked at the desk, the laptop, the payroll form folded beside it, the phone face up and blank.

'What did you do?'

'Changed settings.'

'For her?'

'For everyone.'

'That usually means for her.'

He picked up the payroll form and held it out.

'I am submitting this today.'

Sarah did not take it.

'To protect two lives.'

'Yes.'

'By making yourself less visible in both.'

The sentence had no question mark.

Outside the room, the corridor loudspeaker clicked on and announced breakfast collection by block.

Block A.

Block B.

Block C.

The room waited through all three.

Thomas lowered the form.

His phone lit.

Supplies.

1 notification.

Sarah read the name.

Then she looked at him.

He did not touch the screen.

Chapter 15

The black box began as a second account.

It had no card. No joint access. No paper statements. No branch he would ever visit. It existed inside the bank app as eight digits, a balance, and a name he changed three times before leaving it blank enough to live with.

Reserve.

Then:

Personal reserve.

Then:

Secondary.

The last word had no weather in it. No kitchen. No child. No woman on a balcony trying to photograph a receipt in light thin enough for a bank to accept. It sounded administrative, which made it useful.

Thomas set the payroll allocation on a Thursday morning, sitting in the operations office with a mask under his chin and dust in the corners of the keyboard. The form had passed from Nisha to payroll, from payroll to regional, from regional to a portal that rejected his first signature. The scanned image was too pale. He signed again with a thicker pen and pressed hard enough for the point to mark the page beneath.

By noon, the change appeared in the system.

Joint account: unchanged.

Personal account: reduced.

Secondary: active.

The line looked clean.

That was the first seduction.

Clean lines made dirty things look handled.

The compound remained under lockdown. By then, the first shock had gone stale. People had stopped treating the rules as temporary and begun arranging complaints around them. Meal boxes. Temperature checks. Restricted movement. Work calls with frozen faces and broken audio. Laundry slots negotiated like mineral rights. A rumour every morning and an official correction every afternoon.

Sarah worked in the side office when she could get it, in the room when she could not, and on the floor when the desk became too narrow for both their laptops. She had stopped packing and unpacking. The suitcase stayed under the bed, closed but not forgotten. Once a day she checked flight updates and then closed the page before the spinning wheel finished embarrassing itself.

Thomas watched her adapt.

That was one of the cruelties. She was good at it.

She learned which dining hall boxes travelled badly, which shower hour gave warm water, which corridor had signal after midnight, which security guard would allow an extra five minutes outside if no one else was around. Her world had shrunk, and she had mapped it.

His had shrunk too, though no map would admit it.

At 08:40, Olesya sent:

Internet due.

No photograph.

No hello.

No school note, no red notebook, no Anya's hand at the edge of the table holding paper flat.

Only:

Internet due.

Amount?

Three months. 180.

Deadline?

Today.

He transferred it from Secondary.

Reference:

Internet.

The word stood too plainly in the line. He deleted it and wrote:

April.

Then changed it to:

Household.

The bank accepted the payment.

He sent the screenshot.

Delivered.

No answer.

He placed the phone face up on the desk, in accordance with the room's new law, and returned to a maintenance report. Pump seals. Pressure readings. Parts delayed by transport restrictions. Work had become a place where cause still carried effect with decent manners. A worn seal leaked. A blocked filter raised pressure. A missed inspection produced a fault that could be traced back through dates and signatures. The machinery did not pretend to be virtuous. It only demanded accuracy.

At 10:12, Olesya wrote:

Received.

Thomas entered 180 in the spreadsheet.

Not under School.

Not under Internet.

He created a new category.

Drawdown.

The word pleased him at once.

That should have warned him.

By the end of the week, Drawdown had swallowed four lines.

180.

95.

240.

60.

No subcategories. No notes. No pharmacy stamps, rent confirmations, school letters, ticket stubs, delivery slips. Money went out. Problems became quieter. The spreadsheet, once crowded with the small grammar of other people's days, simplified into a column of sums and dates.

It worked better.

That was the second seduction.

The less he knew, the faster he could move.

He began to prefer the messages that contained only sums.

That preference did not announce itself as cruelty. It arrived as lowered pulse, fewer open tabs, shorter delays between demand and payment. A message with a photograph took him into a room. A message with a number kept him at the door. The number could be handled. The room asked to be entered.

At first, he had wanted every receipt.

Order, he told himself. Documentation. Proof. A proper audit trail. He had asked for rent slips, pharmacy stamps, school notices, bus-card top-ups, doctor's notes, photographs taken in morning light with the total visible. Olesya had learned the discipline too well. The tablecloth, the ruler, Anya's hand at the corner, the careful framing of a life reduced to paper. Each image had arrived carrying more than the amount. It had brought chipped cups, balcony light, bad flooring, school shoes, the edge of a child's exercise book, a mother's thumb in the frame.

Now the shops did not print.

Now the landlord wanted cash.

Now school required internet paid in blocks.

Now medicine prices changed between morning and afternoon.

Now proof became another kind of work for the person already standing in the queue.

He allowed himself that reasoning.

It sounded decent.

At 06:25 on Monday:

340 today.

For?

Rent difference.

He sent 340.

At 11:10:

70.

For?

Delivery and masks.

He sent 70.

At 18:42:

120 tomorrow morning.

For?

School and pharmacy.

He did not ask for photographs.

The payments left Secondary in neat withdrawals. His personal account no longer lurched on the first of the month. The joint account remained unchanged. Sarah could open it whenever she liked and see mortgage, groceries, insurance, the familiar arithmetic of their shared life carrying on in numbers that did not flinch.

That was not honesty.

It was stability wearing honesty's coat.

The bank learned the pattern as he did. Payments to Olesya Petrova. Same recipient. Varying amounts. Same broad purpose. The warning screens appeared less often. The fraud questions softened. Sometimes the app sent a code. Sometimes it did not. Once, for 70, the payment went through so quickly that he stared at the confirmation, unsettled by the system's new trust in him.

The machine had accepted the shape.

Sarah had not.

On Tuesday evening, she found him copying numbers into a paper notebook.

'I thought you had a spreadsheet.'

'I do.'

'Then why paper?'

'Power went out in the office twice.'

'This room has power.'

'For now.'

She sat on the bed with her back against the wall, knees drawn up, laptop closed beside her. The lockdown had made her face leaner. Not thinner. More exact. The soft edges had been taken by poor sleep, box meals, and the constant need to sound composed into a camera that flattened everyone into delay.

'You are building a second record.'

'A backup.'

'A black box.'

He looked up.

'What?'

'A device that survives the crash and explains it afterwards.'

He turned the pen between his fingers.

'That is not what this is.'

'Then what is it?'

He looked at the notebook. Date. Amount. Transfer status. Balance. No category.

'A way to keep functioning.'

'For whom?'

The same question again. Olesya's question in Sarah's mouth, though neither had heard it from the other. For who? For whom? Different grammar. Same blade.

'All of us.'

Sarah's eyes stayed on the notebook.

'That is too many people for one small book.'

He closed it.

'Do you want me to stop?'

'I want you to stop making questions so large that the only possible answer is obedience.'

He had no reply.

She opened her laptop again, then closed it without waking the screen.

'Do you know what she bought today?'

'Masks. Delivery.'

'That is what she said?'

'Yes.'

'Do you know whether Anya is better?'

He looked at the phone.

'She has not said.'

'Did you ask?'

No.

The answer sat between them without needing to be spoken.

Sarah nodded once and turned away.

In Baku, the reduction had begun on Olesya's side before Thomas admitted its usefulness.

She had stopped arranging receipts on the table.

At first it was practical. Shops no longer printed. The landlord wanted cash and disliked photographs. The pharmacy wrote totals on scraps of paper torn from a pad, then stopped doing that when the queue outside reached the bakery. School fees arrived as messages from teachers, then amended messages, then voice notes played through the small phone speaker whilst Anya sat with her pencil ready and no page to copy from.

The old system had required light, paper, a steady hand, and enough time to make need look orderly.

Lockdown took those away.

Olesya sent one photograph after three days without images.

It was blurred. The kitchen table, but at night. A plastic bag of medicine. Rice in a clear sack. The red notebook closed. Anya's sleeve at the edge. The overhead bulb made everything yellow and poor. No ruler. No neat alignment. No total visible.

Below it she wrote:

This is not useful picture.

Thomas replied:

It is fine.

She answered:

No. It is not proof.

Then:

You like proof.

He sat with the phone in the laundry block, machines turning around him, and typed:

I do not need proof every time.

The answer took long enough for a dryer to finish and begin its cooling cycle.

Now you don't.

He read the line until the screen dimmed.

After that, she sent fewer images.

He told himself she had chosen efficiency.

Maybe she had.

Maybe she had also seen the room he had built for her in his phone. A small place with no table, no handwriting, no child's sleeve, no sound of a kettle on a gas ring. Amount. Deadline. Delivery confirmation. The rest outside.

The next message came the following morning.

220.

For?

She did not answer for seven minutes.

Then:

Necessary.

He should have asked again.

He sent 220.

That afternoon, a photograph arrived without explanation.

Not a receipt. The corridor outside the flat. The new lock scratched around the plate where tools had slipped. A small strip of tape across the doorframe, red, official-looking, though he could not read the writing from the angle. At the bottom of the frame, Anya's school shoes stood side by side on the mat.

He enlarged the tape.

Still unreadable.

He wrote:

What is tape?

No answer.

He wrote:

Is door all right?

No answer.

At 18:00:

Door is door.

Then:

Money received.

He entered 220 under Drawdown.

No note.

The omission was his.

He could have written:

Door tape.

He could have written:

Unknown.

He wrote nothing.

The blank cell did not resist.

It never did.

Sarah noticed the change before he named it.

She stood behind him one evening while he reconciled the sheet. The dining hall had sent chicken again, or something near chicken, in paper boxes that had softened at the corners by the time they reached the room. The air conditioner rattled with a loose screw. Outside, someone in the corridor argued with someone else about laundry powder, both voices weary enough to make the argument mostly ceremonial.

'What is Drawdown?' she asked.

He did not close the laptop.

'Baku.'

'That part I gathered.'

'Consolidated costs.'

'That is a phrase people use when they do not want the nouns examined.'

He looked at the screen.

'It is money for several things.'

'Which things?'

'Internet. Food. Rent. Medicine. Whatever is immediate.'

'Whatever?'

'Yes.'

Sarah stood with one hand on the back of the chair. The room light made the plastic food-box lids shine on the bed.

'Do you know?'

'Enough.'

'That is not the same as yes.'

'It does not help to ask for every receipt now. Shops are not printing. Cash is moving. Things are closed.'

'That is an explanation for fewer documents.'

'Yes.'

'Not fewer questions.'

He turned from the screen.

'Questions delay things.'

Sarah's fingers tightened once on the chair, then released.

'Listen to yourself.'

He did.

It sounded efficient.

It sounded monstrous.

Both could be true and still require the next payment.

'If she sends details, I start arguing with the details,' he said. 'If she sends the amount, I send the amount.'

'And that is better?'

'It is faster.'

'I did not ask whether it was faster.'

No.

She had not.

He closed the laptop this time, slowly, so it would not look like concealment.

The dark screen caught both of them in a poor reflection. Sarah standing. Thomas seated. Two people split by the black lid of a machine that had never cared what it carried.

'What does she call you?' Sarah asked.

'What?'

'In that app. What does she call you?'

'Thomas.'

'And what do you call her?'

He did not answer.

Sarah's eyes went to the phone on the desk.

'Supplies,' she said.

Not a question.

He looked at the food boxes on the bed. Rice cooling. Chicken skin gone dull. A plastic fork pressed into the sheet.

'To what?'

'O.'

'A large gift.'

The words had no heat in them. They landed colder than a raised voice would have.

The next morning, he changed it to Olesya before breakfast.

He did not tell Sarah.

Olesya saw the notice anyway.

Now I am myself again?

He typed:

Yes.

Deleted it.

The app notifies changes.

800 soon.

Many things.
