No list followed.

The number stayed with him through the afternoon.

Eight hundred was too large to belong to the new rhythm and too round to be accidental. It sat outside the small arithmetic of lockdown. Not 70 for masks. Not 180 for internet. Not 340 for rent difference. Eight hundred had the quality of a door closing, a hand held out, a debt that had travelled through several rooms before reaching him.

He checked the secondary balance.

Enough.

That should have been the only question.

It was not.

At 14:05, he opened the app and typed:

What happened?

He deleted it.

At 14:07:

Is Anya all right?

He deleted that too.

At 14:10:

Send details.

He did not send it.

Details had become a word with teeth. If she sent them, he would have to look. If she did not, he could tell himself he had not been given the choice. The black box protected him from the image and punished her with its silence.

The side office door opened. Sarah came out with headphones around her neck and the hollow look of someone who had spent an hour teaching senior people something they planned to misunderstand later.

'Any news?'

'No.'

'From Baku?'

'No.'

'From London, there is only bad theatre.'

'What now?'

'They want a continuity framework for a situation with no continuity.'

'Sounds on brand.'

'I told them so, with manners.'

She came into the room and washed her hands in the basin longer than required. The tap had been repaired badly. It no longer dripped, but when opened it coughed, then spat brown water for two seconds before clearing.

Sarah watched the water change colour.

'This place is held together by notices.'

'And cable ties.'

'I have seen the cable ties. They inspire less confidence than the notices.'

She dried her hands on a towel already stiff from use.

'Have you eaten?'

'No.'

'Then do that before you become a doctrine.'

He took the food box she handed him and opened it. Rice. Beans. A small piece of chicken. Cucumber that had surrendered. He ate while she stood by the window and looked through the dusty glass at the compound path.

'Sometimes,' she said, 'I miss rain that belongs to home.'

He looked up.

'Yes.'

'That is a specialised illness.'

'No. Scottish rain has purpose. This dust just relocates itself.'

She turned from the window.

'Do you miss anything?'

The obvious answer was home. The true answer arrived in pieces and refused assembly: a kitchen in Baku before money had entered it, Sarah in their house before calls replaced rooms, Olesya's receipts before he wanted them gone, the first night in the compound when his lies had still required effort.

'Quiet,' he said.

'This room is quiet.'

'No.'

She took that far enough and did not press.

His phone lay face up on the desk.

It did not light.

800 soon.

In the evening, he made the mistake of opening the old photographs.

Not photographs of Olesya herself. Those he had moved elsewhere and then stopped opening. Receipts. Rooms. Objects. Anya's red notebook. The birthday cake with two plates. The heater with chipped enamel. The school marks folded from a bag. A photograph of a rent receipt held down by a spoon, the spoon bent slightly at the handle. Nothing intimate, if intimacy meant skin. Everything intimate, if intimacy meant evidence of use.

He opened one image at random.

The kitchen table before lockdown.

At the edge of the frame, only partly visible, Anya's sleeve. Yellow. A small stain near the cuff. Olesya's hand holding the receipt down. A line of flour on the table. Not much. Enough to say something had been made there.

He closed it.

The picture had done what pictures did.

It had made the number unacceptable.

He opened the banking app to prove he could still act without looking.

Tell me deadline.

Olesya answered after four minutes.

Today.

For?

Landlord cash. Internet. Medicine. Food. School. Debt to Marina. Shoes.

The list came without punctuation, without photographs, without hierarchy. A heap emptied onto the floor.

For the first time in days, the list made him see too much.

Landlord cash: the nephew's hand at the door, palm out, the cheap logo on his shirt, the old lock sitting useless inside the flat.

Internet: Anya bent over a phone at the kitchen table, teacher's voice flattened by speakers, red notebook open, her eyes moving between screen and page.

Medicine: the pharmacy window, tape squares on the pavement, Olesya counting notes before joining the queue.

Food: rice, oil, eggs, the torn grocery sack in the courtyard.

School: fees hidden inside other words.

Marina: a borrowed sum written nowhere.

Shoes: the cracked toe he had seen months earlier and then deleted from the note.

He had tried to build a box for money.

The box had filled with rooms.

Landlord cash. Internet. Medicine. Food. School. Debt to Marina. Shoes.

Every item carried a life inside it, and still the list did not have enough detail to force him to imagine the room. That was the moral convenience of it. He could see categories and avoid seeing Anya's foot in the worn shoe, Olesya counting borrowed notes in Marina's kitchen, a landlord waiting at the door with a nephew and a locksmith. He could fund the consequences and refuse the scene.

The transfer failed twice.

Not blocked. Timed out.

The compound internet dropped each time at the confirmation screen. The bank app spun, went blank, returned him to login. The room grew hotter. Sarah sat on the edge of the bed, no laptop now, no orange, watching him with her hands pressed flat beside her thighs.

On.

Evening collected itself slowly.

The compound had learned to dim without becoming night. Security lights came on before the sun had finished leaving. The paths turned white under them. Dust lifted from boots and hung in the beams. In the room, the air conditioner fought the heat with a sound like an old throat clearing. Sarah took her last call of the day from the bed, laptop balanced on her knees, speaking to London in the careful tone of someone feeding a wire through a wall.

Thomas sat at the desk and kept the secure app open.

No reply.

He closed it.

Opened it again.

No reply.

At 19:03, he wrote:

Confirm when received.

He did not send it.

At 19:05:

Please confirm.

Deleted.

At 19:09:

Did you get everything?

Deleted.

A thank you would turn the payment back into care. Without it, the money remained money, and the room behind the number remained closed.

Sarah finished her call and removed the headphones.

'Still nothing?'

'Nothing.'

'Maybe she cannot answer.'

'Maybe.'

'Maybe she will answer later.'

'Yes.'

Sarah placed the headphones beside her and rubbed the dents they had left near her ears.

'You know what is strange?'

'What?'

'I do not think you are waiting to know whether they are all right.'

He turned.

She looked back at him without moving from the bed.

'I think you are waiting to know what the payment made you.'

The sentence entered softly.

That made it harder to push out.

'That is unfair.'

'Probably.'

'Sarah.'

'I said probably.'

She closed the laptop and set it on the floor.

'I am tired enough to be accurate and inaccurate at the same time.'

The word tired was allowed. It described the room as much as her.

He looked at the phone.

No reply.

At 20:12, the compound loudspeaker announced a water-pressure issue in Block C and instructed residents to minimise non-essential use. The announcement repeated twice, each time with a small burst of static before the first word. Sarah laughed once from the bed, a dry sound.

'Even the water has had enough.'

He smiled without much force.

They brushed their teeth with bottled water from the crate under the desk. Sarah went first, then handed him the bottle without speaking. A domestic relay, stripped down to object and hand.

When she lay down, she turned towards the wall.

He remained at the desk.

The phone stayed face up.

The afternoon went on with the particular cruelty of a day that had already spent its event.

Sarah took a call with London. Her voice carried from the side office through a connection that fractured every other sentence. Thomas worked on a report and corrected the same line three times without changing it. The compound announced dinner collection by block. A maintenance request came in for a leaking basin in C wing. Someone taped a new notice over an old notice, leaving the torn corners visible beneath.

At 17:22, Olesya read the message.

The ticks turned blue.

Thomas saw it happen.

He waited.

The typing dots did not appear.

At 17:46, he opened the thread again.

Nothing.

At 18:10, Sarah returned with two food boxes and set one beside him.

'Anything?'

'Read.'

'No reply?'

'No.'

She sat on the chair, not the bed. That had become one of the room's measurements.

'Does she usually say thank you?'

'Sometimes.'

'Do you need her to?'

He wanted to say no. The correct answer was no. The money was not a transaction if he did not require gratitude. It was care. It was support. It was structure.

But his eyes stayed on the blank space beneath 800 sent.

Sarah opened her food box.

'That is also an answer,' she said.

He put the phone face up on the desk.

The screen dimmed.

At midnight, it was still blank.

At 02:00, he woke in the chair with his neck stiff and the phone under his hand.

No new message.

Sarah slept on the bed with her back to him, one shoulder uncovered, the sheet gathered at her waist. The room smelled of stale air, cold rice, and the vinegar he had used on the kettle two days earlier.

Thomas opened the spreadsheet.

May.

800.

Category:

He typed:

Drawdown.

Then he moved the cursor to Notes.

His fingers waited above the keyboard.

He wrote nothing there.

The black box held.

Chapter 16

The end of Iraq came without farewell.

It came as a schedule change, then a rumour, then a line on the company tracker that moved Thomas and Sarah from locked-in to redeploying without asking either of them whether the word was accurate. Regional hub transfer. Delhi. Temporary corporate housing. Essential personnel and dependants. Travel corridor approved.

Dependants.

Thomas saw the word on the list before Sarah did.

She stood beside him in the operations office with a paper cup of tea and dust along the edge of her sleeve. The lockdown had held them in Iraq long enough for departure to become theoretical. Now it had returned wearing paperwork and no apology.

'Dependants,' she said.

'Standard category.'

'For wives, children, and women with laptops who overstayed a consulting trip by global event.'

'That may be too specific for the drop-down menu.'

'Their loss.'

She took the printed sheet from his hand and read the row again. Her name. His name. Passport status. Medical clearance. Destination: Delhi. Flight: charter. Date: provisional.

'Provisional means real until it isn't,' she said.

'Yes.'

'I dislike how easily I now speak logistics.'

'You are fluent.'

'Do not make this worse.'

Her voice was light, but her eyes stayed on the word destination.

Delhi.

Thomas had worked through Delhi before, never lived there. Airports, hotels, one office tower with over-watered plants, a driver who had known six routes to avoid six different kinds of delay. The city in his memory was heat, horns, wet dust, cardamom tea in paper cups, a hotel corridor smelling of polish and old air-conditioning. It had been transit then. A place crossed through.

Now it was the next container.

The compound spent three days disgorging people in batches.

No one called it leaving. They called it movement. They called it rotation adjustment. They called it hub consolidation. The words kept everything technical and therefore bearable. Bags appeared in corridors. Printer queues formed outside admin. Medical stamped forms until the ink pad dried. Security checked names against lists that changed every hour. Men who had wanted nothing more than to leave now distrusted the flight that allowed it.

Sarah packed with a precision that put the room under accusation.

Work clothes. Travel clothes. Documents. Chargers. Medication. Gifts still stranded from Scotland. The dead basil plant at home had become a joke in messages from her mother and then stopped being a joke. Sarah folded the printed lockdown form and slid it into the plastic sleeve.

'Do we need the room inventory?'

'Probably not.'

'That means yes.'

'Put it with passports.'

'Listen to us.'

She tucked it away.

'We have become the sort of people who travel with room inventories.'

Thomas closed his laptop.

'It is a stage of marriage no one warns you about.'

'The vows should mention laminated documents.'

She lifted the suitcase off the bed and set it upright. The zip held. Barely.

His phone lay face up on the desk.

Olesya had not replied to the 800.

The blue ticks remained. No message beneath them. The silence had become a surface he could touch without leaving a mark. He opened the thread less often now, or told himself he did. He knew the last line by position, by the exact amount of white space below it.

800 sent.

Read.

Nothing.

Sarah saw him look.

'Still no answer?'

'No.'

'And the next payment?'

'Due next week.'

'Can you send early?'

He looked at her.

'Why?'

'We are changing countries. Banks enjoy becoming stupid at borders.'

That was why she was good in crisis. She noticed the door before it closed.

'I can try.'

'Try before the airport.'

'It is monthly.'

'The month does not care where you are standing.'

He opened the banking app.

Secondary balance: enough.

Recipient: Olesya Petrova.

Amount: monthly plus buffer.

Reference: June.

Purpose: Other.

The compound connection flickered. The app paused, recovered, asked him to confirm.

He pressed confirm.

The circle turned.

Then the screen went white.

Session expired.

'Signal?' Sarah asked.

'Probably.'

'Try again.'

He did.

This time the app accepted the login, loaded the transfer page, then returned him to accounts without warning.

No payment.

He checked activity.

Nothing pending.

The air conditioner rattled above them with the loose screw he had never reported.

'Later,' he said.

Sarah looked at him.

'Before the airport.'

'Yes.'

He tried three more times before the convoy.

Each attempt failed differently.

Session expired.

Connection lost.

Unable to process.

Please try later.

At the fourth attempt, the app asked him to contact the bank.

That was new.

The convoy to the airport left at 05:40.

The compound was colourless at that hour. Floodlights, dust, men in masks, luggage lined beside armoured vehicles, a security supervisor reading names from a clipboard in the tone of a man who had lost all faith in names. Sarah stood beside Thomas with her backpack on both shoulders and her passport in the front pocket of her jeans.

'Did it clear?'

'No.'

'What does no mean?'

'It asked me to contact the bank.'

'That seems like a bad no.'

'Yes.'

'Thomas.'

'I know.'

He did not know. Not yet. He knew only the shape of the gate, not the lock inside it.

The road out of the compound ran past the perimeter wall, the empty clinic, the generator yard, the gravel space where men still smoked out of habit though the convoy was waiting. Beyond the gate, Iraq opened in hard morning light. Flat ground. Low buildings. Concrete barriers. Roadside dust lifting behind the lead vehicle and folding back over the rest of them.

Sarah sat beside him in the second vehicle.

She put her hand on his knee once when they passed the place where the mortar smoke had been visible weeks earlier. No speech. Just the pressure of her fingers through the fabric. Then she took her hand away and checked her passport again.

His phone had no service.

At the airport, service returned in fragments.

He opened the banking app while Sarah queued for document check.

Account under review.

The words sat at the top of the screen in a red banner.

He tapped details.

International transfer activity requires additional verification.

Please contact us.

He opened chat.

The queue in the terminal moved forward by inches. Around him, contractors held passports, boarding letters, medical forms, phone chargers, duty-free bags, all the small documents and objects required for a person to be allowed through a door. A child from another company's group slept across two seats with her head on a rucksack. The public address system announced a flight in Arabic, then English, then a burst of static.

Bank chat connected.

Automated assistant:

How can we help today?

Thomas typed:

Transfer blocked.

The assistant offered three buttons.

Card issue.

Fraud alert.

International payment.

He selected International payment.

The assistant asked him to describe the recipient relationship.

He stared at the question.

Sarah turned from the queue.

'Any progress?'

'Bank chat.'

'Do you need anything?'

'No.'

She looked at his screen from where she stood. Too far to read. Close enough to know he had angled it away.

The assistant repeated:

Please describe your relationship to the recipient.

The terminal moved around him. Wheels on tile. Shoes. Announcements. Security staff. A man laughing too loudly into a phone. A trolley with a squeaking wheel moving in circles around a pillar.

Thomas typed:

Friend.

The assistant replied:

Please provide supporting documentation establishing the relationship and purpose of ongoing transfers.

He deleted the chat.

Then reopened it.

The same red banner remained.

Sarah reached the document desk. The officer checked her passport, then his, then the company letter, then the list. Stamps. Screens. A second officer called someone over. The two men compared names. Sarah kept her face still. Thomas kept the phone low in his palm.

Olesya messaged at 06:32 Baku time.

Monthly?

He did not answer until they were through security.

Trying.

Her reply:

Need today.

He typed:

Bank review.

The dots appeared immediately.

Then:

What means?

Blocked until documents.

What documents?

He stood beside the departure gate with the phone in his hand and a boarding pass between two fingers. The gate windows showed the charter aircraft sitting in a haze of heat and exhaust. Men in high-vis vests moved around it with cones and hand signals. Sarah bought two bottles of water from a kiosk and placed one against his wrist.

'Drink.'

He did.

Olesya sent:

Send document then.

He closed his eyes for one second.

There is no document.

The message delivered.

One tick.

Two.

No reply.

At the gate, he tried the chat again.

The bank's human agent this time was called Priya, or said so in the small circle beside the message. Her replies came too neatly to feel human, though not neatly enough to be a machine. She asked for the recipient relationship again. Then proof of purpose. Then source of funds, even though the funds had come from salary paid into the same bank for years. Every answer opened a smaller door.

Recipient relationship:

Personal.

Please be more specific.

Long-term personal support.

Please upload supporting documentation.

There is no contract.

Please upload a written declaration.

Thomas opened a notes app and tried to make the sentence.

I provide regular support to Olesya Petrova for living costs.

He read it.

It was true in the way a skeleton was true.

He added:

This includes rent, food, medicine, and school costs for her daughter.

That was also true.

Then the document asked, silently and without mercy:

Why?

There was no field for why. That was the bank's genius. It wanted a relationship, not a reason. It could process wife, child, parent, invoice, employee, tenant, contractor, loan. It had boxes for the world's recognised routes. It had no box for a woman he had turned into an obligation without giving her a name the system would accept.

Sarah returned from the kiosk with water and a packet of almonds.

'You have gone very still.'

'Declaration.'

'Show me.'

He handed her the phone.

She read the two sentences.

'This is accurate.'

'It is not enough.'

'No.'

'What would be enough?'

She looked at the gate windows. Outside, a baggage cart moved beneath the aircraft with orange lights blinking.

'For a bank? Marriage certificate. Birth certificate. Rental agreement. Invoice. Employment contract. Something with a line they know how to read.'

'There is nothing.'

'There is something. There is no document.'

She handed the phone back.

'Those are different.'

The agent sent another message:

Please confirm whether recipient is family member.

Thomas looked at the words until the letters lost their place.

Sarah read them over his shoulder.

'Do not lie to a bank.'

'I know.'

'I mean it.'

'I know.'

He typed:

No.

The agent replied:

Please confirm reason for ongoing support to non-family recipient.

Boarding began.

Rows one to ten.

The queue formed badly at first, then corrected itself under the pressure of staff and tired people with printed boarding cards. Sarah stood and lifted her backpack.

'Thomas.'

'One minute.'

'They are boarding.'

He typed:

Personal humanitarian support.

The phrase looked like a notice on a charity box.

He sent it.

The agent replied:

Your case requires review by specialist team. Estimated response time: three to five working days.

He stared at the screen.

Three to five working days.

Olesya had written today.

The boarding line moved.

Sarah took his bag from the seat and handed it to him.

'Come on.'

'Three to five days.'

'I saw.'

'They do not have that.'

'No.'

She held his gaze for a second.

'Neither do we, if you miss this flight.'

He locked the phone.

The charter flight to Delhi had no ceremony either.

People boarded with the worn faces of evacuation and the thin manners of business travel. The cabin smelled of coffee, stale air, disinfectant, and bodies that had slept badly. Sarah took the window seat and pressed her forehead briefly to the plastic shade before lifting it. Thomas sat beside her, phone in aeroplane mode, the bank app useless behind the screen.

'Can you fix it from Delhi?' she asked.

'I can try from a proper connection.'

'That is not what I asked.'

'I know.'

'Can you?'

'I do not know.'

She nodded.

The plane taxied.

On the runway, his phone buzzed once before losing signal completely.

Olesya:

Electricity today.

Then another, arriving in the same breath:

If no payment they cut.

The phone went dark.

The aircraft lifted.

Iraq fell away beneath a film of dust, grids and roads and compounds flattening into colour and line. Thomas watched until cloud took the ground. Beside him, Sarah closed her eyes but did not sleep.

In the air, there was nothing to do.

That should have been impossible.

He had built his life around doing: transferring, categorising, replying, adjusting, moving funds from one account to another, naming things just enough for machines to allow them through. At altitude, cut from signal, his entire structure reduced to intention. Intention did not keep lights on. Intention did not satisfy banks. Intention did not stand in a queue, sign a form, explain a relationship, pay a bill.

Sarah opened her eyes.

'You are counting.'

'What?'

'Your thumb. On the armrest.'

He stopped.

'Sorry.'

'Do not apologise. Count something useful.'

'Such as?'

'Ways Delhi can be better.'

'Better than what?'

'Than a locked compound with box chicken.'

'That is a low bar.'

'Good. We need the win.'

He looked at the seatback in front of him.

'Company housing is supposed to be good there.'

'Define good.'

'More space. Better connection. Kitchen.'

'Kitchen sounds ambitious.'

'Regional staff. Drivers. Local admin.'

'You make it sound like a machine shop.'

'Maybe that would be useful.'

She turned her head towards him.

'Useful for us, or useful for the thing you need to fix?'

He did not answer.

She closed her eyes again.

'Fine. Count kitchens.'

The flight served breakfast in foil trays.

Sarah ate the yoghurt and left the eggs. Thomas ate nothing. He opened the in-flight magazine, looked at a map of routes, closed it. The line to Delhi curved neatly across the page. A clean arc. No blocked transfers. No child doing schoolwork under a failing bulb. No woman in Baku holding a phone up to a window for signal. Maps were dishonest in their politeness.

Halfway through the flight, the cabin lights dimmed.

Sarah slept at last.

Thomas took out the paper notebook from his bag.

Date.

Monthly transfer failed.

Reason:

He wrote:

Bank review.

Then:

Documentation required.

Then he stopped.

Relationship:

The pen rested against the page.

Friend.

Support.

Recipient.

None of them passed through the real gate.

He wrote nothing in that line.

The plane landed in Delhi in a white afternoon.

Heat came at the aircraft before the doors opened, visible through the windows in shimmer and exhaust. The terminal smelled of floor polish, coffee, damp fabric, and something floral from a cleaning solution sprayed too heavily near arrivals. Thomas's shirt stuck to his back before they reached passport control.

Delhi did not receive them as a new life.

It received them as processing.

Queue.

Form.

Stamp.

Thermal camera.

Company representative holding a printed sign.

Luggage delayed.

Phone signal strong and overloaded.

He did.

Red banner.

Account under review.

He opened chat again.

This time a human agent joined after six minutes.

Good afternoon. I can help with your international payment query.

I need to release a payment urgently.

The agent replied:

We need additional information due to repeated transfers to Azerbaijan. Please upload documentation confirming your relationship with the recipient and the purpose of the funds.

He wrote:

Personal support.

Please provide documentation.

What documentation?

Examples include invoice, contract, tenancy agreement, family relationship document, or written declaration explaining payment purpose.

The baggage belt began moving. Suitcases emerged in a slow procession of other people's lives. Black cases. Blue cases. A cardboard box wrapped in plastic. A pushchair. Sarah's suitcase appeared with the laundry tag still tied to the handle. She pulled it off the belt and looked back at him.

'Well?'

He showed her the screen.

She read it.

'Written declaration.'

'Yes.'

'Can you write one?'

'Saying what?'

Sarah did not answer quickly.

That was the answer.

He closed the chat.

'Do not close it.'

'I cannot write it here.'

'You may not get a better place.'

The company representative approached, smiling with professional strain. 'Mr Ward? Mrs Ward? We have car ready. Bags only these?'

Mrs Ward.

Sarah looked at Thomas.

'Yes,' she said. 'Only these.'

The driver led them through arrivals into heat and horns.

Delhi moved around them with a force that made the compound seem like a diagram. Cars leaned into gaps that did not exist. Motorbikes slid between buses. Street vendors stood beside traffic with water bottles, phone chargers, masks, cut fruit under plastic. Stray wires looped between poles. Billboards rose above flyovers. A man pushed a cart stacked with green coconuts through a stream of vehicles that rearranged themselves around him without slowing.

Sarah sat beside Thomas in the back seat.

The air-conditioning worked hard and badly.

'Well,' she said.

'Yes.'

'It is not Iraq.'

'No.'

'That may be enough for today.'

He looked at his phone.

Olesya had sent four messages during landing.

Thomas?

They say today.

I asked Marina again.

She cannot.

I landed. Bank still blocked.

No accusation. No punctuation after it.

Just the fact.

She replied:

Trying is not light.

He held the phone low against his thigh.

Sarah watched Delhi through the window. A man on a scooter balanced a child in front of him, the child's hands resting on the handlebar between his. Both wore masks under their chins. At the roadside, laundry hung from a balcony above a pharmacy sign. A dog slept in the strip of shade beneath a parked truck.

International bill payment Azerbaijan.

Azerbaijan.

The car moved into a wider road lined with trees whose leaves had turned grey at the edges from dust. Delhi heat pressed against the windows.

Receipts. Rent. Pharmacy. School. Cake. Door. Red notebook. Lock. Food. Medicine. Blurred kitchen. Door tape. Nothing with electricity account number. He had never asked for it. Electricity had been a line in the sheet, not a document. It had arrived as amount, been paid, vanished.

Too many people.

He knew that. The words made him look at it.

Hotel required passport, movement, permission, a woman arriving with a child and no husband, no local man, no clean explanation. It required crossing a city under rules that changed at each counter. It required Olesya to stand somewhere new and be assessed by someone with a pen.

The results repeated themselves, each one either unusable without the account number or too polished to trust. One site asked for recipient phone number and promised bill lookup. She opened the gallery and began moving through the images with her thumb. Receipts. Lock. School letter. Medicine. Food. The blurred kitchen. Door tape. Her face stayed still. Her breathing altered. He did.

This time it delivered.

No answer.

The company flat was on the ninth floor of a serviced block near a road that did not stop sounding. Reception had marble too polished for the hour, a guard with a temperature scanner, and a bowl of green apples no one had touched. The flat itself had two bedrooms, a small kitchen, a sitting room, a balcony with a view of construction cranes and traffic, and air-conditioning that worked in several moods of loud.

Sarah stood in the doorway and looked at the space.

'This is obscene.'

'Compared with Iraq?'

'Compared with most things.'

The floor was cool underfoot. The fridge held bottled water, milk, eggs, butter, bread, and three small jars of jam. A welcome card sat on the counter with both their names printed correctly. In the bedroom, the bed was made with white sheets and four pillows. The bathroom had towels folded into a stack.

Thomas put his bag on a chair and opened his laptop.

Sarah did not empty her suitcase.

She stood beside the kitchen counter with one hand on the welcome card.

'Housing,' she said.

'What?'

'On the plane. You said Delhi housing would be better.'

'It is.'

'Yes.'

Her voice had gone flat.

He looked at the card.

Thomas Ward.

Sarah Ward.

Welcome.

His phone lit.

Olesya:

Lights off.

Then:

Anya keeps asking.

The second word hit the room and broke there.

Thomas opened the bank chat again.

Upload documentation.

He opened a blank document.

Bank compliance declaration.

He stopped.

He could write the money. He could write support. He could write recipient. He could write education, food, medicine, rent, electricity. He could not write the relationship in a way that would satisfy a bank without making a claim he had no right to file or exposing a truth his own life could not hold.

He tried anyway.

I have provided regular financial support to Olesya Petrova since...

Since when?

Since the hospital. Since the first transfer. Since the moment he had discovered money could cross the distance faster than any answer he was willing to give.

He deleted the line.

I am responsible for supporting...

Responsible.

The word looked legal, nearly marital, nearly paternal, nearly everything he had avoided becoming in public. He deleted it.

The payments are charitable.

That was worse. That took Olesya's kitchen, Anya's notebook, the lock, the medicine drawer, and pushed all of it into a box marked benevolence.

He deleted it.

Sarah stood behind him, silent enough to make the keyboard sound larger.

'You cannot write it,' she said.

'I can write something.'

'Something is what the bank rejected.'

He did not turn.

On the balcony, Delhi traffic continued below them, horns rising through the glass. The city was lit in pieces. Flats. Offices. Food stalls. Headlamps. A construction crane with red lights blinking at intervals. Electricity everywhere he looked.

His phone buzzed.

Battery 8.

Then:

If phone dies I cannot see.

No reply.

Sarah came to stand behind him.

She read the blank page.

He looked at the cursor.

'I do not know.'

'Then that is the block.'

Outside, a horn sounded in the road below, long and furious, then was swallowed by ten others.

Olesya sent:

We are in dark.

Thomas looked at the message.

Then at Sarah.

'The company housing is good,' he said.

Sarah did not move.

Then she turned her face towards the balcony, towards the cranes, the traffic, the city lit in every window it could afford.

'Yes,' she said.

The cursor blinked, asking for a name.

Chapter 17

The cursor was still blinking when the inverter in the Delhi flat changed note.

It gave a small mechanical click from the cupboard near the dining area, then settled again into its soft electrical hum. The lights did not dip. The air-conditioning carried on pushing cold air across the polished floor. Outside, the compound generator answered some brief weakness in the street grid with a deeper growl, and the ceiling lamps above the table held their white glare without flicker.

Thomas kept his hand on the laptop.

The blank declaration waited.

Name of beneficiary.

Relationship to remitter.

Purpose of support.

It was not a long form. That made it worse. The bank had found the few lines his whole arrangement could not cross.

Sarah was asleep in the main bedroom behind a door that had been left open for the air. She had unpacked with a speed he still found faintly theatrical, though there was nothing theatrical in the room now. Shoes aligned beneath the built-in cupboards. Cotton dresses hung by colour. The small travel steamer rested on a towel beside the sink. Her Delhi had already begun to make sense. Driver. Market. Grocer. Gym. Two women from the high commission circuit who had sent her numbers before the car from the airport reached Vasant Vihar.

On the table beside his laptop, Olesya's last message sat on the secure app.

We are in dark.

No punctuation.

He touched the screen once so it would not sleep.

The app showed her name at the top, then the minute mark, then the message. There was no photograph. She had removed it months earlier, after a complaint from an aunt or a neighbour or a woman in the building who had asked too many questions. The blank circle had served him well. It made her easier to place in the part of his life built from transactions and late replies, from numbers arriving in small white boxes, from the discipline of not imagining the corridor outside her flat when the lights failed.

Now the blank circle looked like a hole cut in the screen.

He went back to the declaration.

Name of beneficiary.

Olesya.

He typed her full name, then stopped before the next field.

Relationship to remitter.

The cursor waited there.

He put his fingers on the keys.

Friend.

He did not press enter.

Friend would pass through the bank perhaps. It had the stale usefulness of a word no one needed to defend. Friend was how men described women whose numbers they kept under false folders. Friend was how the world made illicit things sound harmless until the document reached a human desk.

He selected the word and deleted it.

Personal support.

The form rejected the field before he submitted it. Please select from approved category.

Relative.

Employee.

Education.

Medical.

Maintenance.

Gift.

He selected gift and the next field opened.

Occasion.

He sat back.

From the bedroom, Sarah turned once under the sheet. The mattress made a low cotton sound. The city beyond the balcony remained awake in layers: horns from the outer road, a motorcycle opening its throttle, a dog from somewhere behind the compound wall, the clatter of a metal gate dragged over concrete.

His phone vibrated.

Battery 4.

Then another line arrived.

Anya with neighbour now.

Then:

Cold in flat.

He looked at the air-conditioning remote lying near Sarah's keys. Its blue display showed twenty-one degrees.

He stood and crossed the flat in his socks.

The living area had more space than the Baghdad rooms, more than the Baku hotel suite where he had first learned how silence could become a form of arrangement. The company had taken a serviced flat with a balcony, two bedrooms, a kitchen with glossy cupboards and a fridge already stocked by the relocation agent. There were six bottles of mineral water in the door, butter from France, green apples waxed to a display shine, a small bowl of limes no one had requested.

The place smelled of new paint, cooled dust, and cardamom from the welcome tray near the sink.

He opened the fridge, then closed it without taking anything.

His phone vibrated again.

Phone will die.

He typed:

I am fixing it tonight.

He sent the line, then read it.

It had the blunt polish of all his worst promises. I am fixing it. Not I can fix it. Not I hope. He had offered certainty to a room with no electricity.

Olesya did not answer.

He returned to the table and opened the bank portal again. The rejected declaration sat in the queue with a red mark beside it. A message from the compliance officer had arrived in the portal inbox at 22:17 Delhi time.

Kindly attach documentation establishing the beneficiary relationship and the lawful basis for recurring personal remittance.

Kindly.

He had seen that word in too many letters. It was the word people used before refusing you. Kindly submit. Kindly confirm. Kindly note. Kindly arrange. It had the manners of a closed gate.

He downloaded the template affidavit and opened it.

I, the undersigned, declare that the above beneficiary is my...

The blank line sat after my.

He closed the file.

The first call went to the bank's international support number. He had called it twice already from Iraq and once from the airport lounge in Doha. Each time the music sounded thinner, flattened by distance into a series of chimes that made his molars tighten. He put the phone on speaker and lowered the volume to one bar.

The call counter passed twelve minutes.

On the balcony, the Delhi night pressed against the glass. It was not dark in the way Baku was dark when the power failed. Delhi carried light even where it was weak. Sodium lamps over the compound road. Blue television glare in a guard hut. Headlights travelling through the neem trees in short white cuts. Beyond the wall, a kiosk still open, its sign flickering between two letters.

His own reflection floated over the city. Laptop light under his chin. Phone on the table. The careful man in the rented flat.

The line clicked.

'International support, this is Naveen speaking. May I have your customer reference?'

Thomas gave it.

Keys sounded on the other end. A cough moved away from the receiver. Somewhere behind the agent, another voice repeated a script.

'Mr Thomas, the account is subject to review for beneficiary verification. This is for your protection.'

'The beneficiary has no electricity.'

'I understand, sir.'

He did not. The line would not carry it. Olesya's dark flat could not enter the headset, could not cross into the little cubicle where Naveen sat beneath fluorescent panels and a wall clock.

'The transfer is a monthly personal support payment,' Thomas said. 'There is a history. The account has processed this before.'

'Prior processing does not remove documentation requirement.'

'It is urgent.'

'Sir, I can mark the case urgent.'

'That does nothing.'

A pause followed. Not offence. Procedure rearranging itself.

'Sir, our instruction is affidavit of support with relationship evidence.'

'What evidence?'

'Marriage certificate, birth certificate, adoption certificate, employment contract, education invoice, medical invoice, tenancy agreement with shared obligation, court order, maintenance agreement.'

He wrote the list on a company notepad with a pen taken from the airport hotel.

Marriage certificate.

Birth certificate.

Adoption certificate.

Employment contract.

Education invoice.

Medical invoice.

Tenancy agreement.

Court order.

Maintenance agreement.

The list looked absurdly tidy. A clean row of doors, each one locked from the other side.

'I can provide bank history,' he said.

'Bank history shows prior transfer activity. It does not establish relationship.'

'What about gift?'

'Recurring gift above threshold will still trigger review.'

'How long if I submit the affidavit?'

'Standard review is five to seven business days.'

He looked at Olesya's message again. Battery 4.

'She does not have five days.'

'Sir, I can mark the case urgent.'

Thomas closed his eyes, not long enough to make it prayer.

'Mark it urgent.'

'Certainly, sir.'

The line returned to hold music whilst the agent typed. Thomas watched the notepad. The words had started to blur at the edges, not from tears, only from staring too long at black ink under white light.

Sarah appeared in the passage.

She had wrapped the hotel robe over her nightdress. Her hair was flattened on one side, and the sleep crease from the pillow ran down her cheek. She stood with one hand on the doorframe and looked at the laptop, the phone, the notepad.

'Still awake?'

He muted the call.

'Bank.'

'For Baku?'

He nodded.

Sarah came into the room but did not sit. The robe belt hung loose. A mosquito moved near her ankle and she bent to slap it, missed, then straightened again.

'Can they release it?'

'They want documentation.'

'Of what?'

'Relationship.'

The word stayed between them.

Sarah looked at the notepad. Her eyes moved over the list.

'And you have none.'

'Not the kind they accept.'

The phone speaker clicked.

'Sir, I have escalated the case. You will receive confirmation by email.'

Thomas unmuted.

'Is there anything else you can do tonight?'

'No, sir.'

'Then send the email.'

'Certainly.'

The call ended.

Sarah took the pen from the table and turned the notepad towards herself. She read the list again, more slowly this time, then placed the pen down exactly where it had been.

'What will you do?'

'Find another route.'

'From here?'

'Not necessarily.'

She gave a small nod. It was not agreement. It was the shape her face made when she was moving an answer to a place where she could look at it later.

'Thomas.'

He waited.

'Do you know what you are asking the world to do?'

He looked at her.

'I am trying to send money.'

'No.'

The mosquito returned. It hovered near the lamp, frantic in the heat thrown from the bulb.

'You are asking every system to treat her as important without letting any system know who she is.'

He did not answer.

Sarah folded her arms inside the robe, pulling the fabric close at the throat. The city horns rose outside, then thinned.

'I am going back to bed,' she said.

She left the room with the list still on the table.

Thomas waited until the bedroom door clicked half shut, then opened a browser in a private window.

European remittance Azerbaijan card urgent.

He deleted urgent.

Urgent would bring scams.

He searched again with country names, currency codes, bank card payout, cash pickup, transfer time, beneficiary verification, sender from India, sender non-resident. The results arranged themselves into a marketplace of fees and promises. Same day. Instant. Low cost. Trusted by millions. Logos in blue, green, orange. Smiling people in photographs, all of them holding phones in kitchens full of morning light.

He opened six tabs.

The first platform did not support Azerbaijan.

The second supported it only from the European Union.

The third offered bank deposits but required the sender address to match card billing country.

The fourth allowed credit card funding from foreign cards but capped the first transfer at two hundred.

The fifth had poor reviews on payout delays.

The sixth required a video verification and a utility bill less than three months old.

He went back to the fourth.

Two hundred would not be enough. It would buy time measured in hours. Yet the platform raised limits after the first transfer cleared. He read the terms twice. It allowed multiple sends to the same beneficiary after card verification. It charged the fee up front. The exchange rate sat badly, four points under the bank rate.

He opened a calculator.

The numbers came out obscene.

Three months of support through this route would cost more than a return flight to Baku had cost in the old days, before exit restrictions and debt and the quiet hardening of borders around Olesya's life. It would pass over his main bank, touch a credit card, pass through a payment processor registered in Lithuania, convert twice, and land in a Baku bank account that might then ask its own questions.

Still.

It moved.

He created an account.

The platform asked for his residential address. He used the Norwegian address still tied to the card. It asked where he was currently located. He selected India. It asked for purpose. He selected family support, then changed it to personal support, then changed it back. Family was stronger. Family was also untrue in the only way that mattered on a form.

He selected personal support.

The platform asked for the beneficiary's bank account.

He opened the secure app.

Send full bank details again. IBAN, card number, address exactly as bank records show it.

The message did not deliver.

Battery dead.

The small grey clock sat beside his words.

Thomas leaned back.

The flat held its white light.

He could go to bed and wait. Morning in Delhi would be early in Baku. Anya would charge the phone at school if the school had power. Olesya would find a neighbour or a kiosk. The details would arrive.

He stood so quickly the chair legs scraped.

In the kitchen he filled a glass from the tap, stopped, emptied it, then took a bottle from the fridge. The plastic crackled in his hand. He drank too fast. Water ran cold down his throat, then sat badly in his stomach.

The secure app remained unchanged.

He opened his old files. Olesya had sent account details before, years earlier, back when transfers still had the intimacy of novelty. He had saved everything then. Receipts, screenshots, exchange rates, the first monthly spreadsheet. A folder called Baku admin sat inside a work archive, two levels below a directory of old project files. He had made it dull on purpose.

Inside were thirty-seven PDFs.

He sorted by name.

Rent_Oct.

School_A.

Medical_Asthma.

Bank_card_front.

He opened the bank card file.

The photograph was poor. Olesya had taken it under yellow light, her thumb covering the corner. The card number was visible. Her name was embossed in silver, one letter catching the flash.

He entered the number into the platform.

The platform asked for date of birth.

He had that too.

He entered it.

Address.

He had three addresses for her. The old flat near the office. The Sumgait address where her mother had been registered. The later Baku flat with the failing lift and the metal front door that never quite closed unless pulled hard.

He chose the later Baku flat.

The platform accepted it.

It asked for amount.

He sent two hundred first.

Credit card one-time password.

The bank sent the code to his Norwegian number. The phone searched for signal, found one bar, lost it, found it again. The code arrived after sixty seconds.

He typed it.

Processing.

The word spun in a circle.

He watched it without moving. The balcony door had not sealed properly and a thread of warm air reached his left wrist. The laptop fan rose. Somewhere in the service area, a pipe knocked once in the wall.

Transfer submitted.

Estimated delivery: within minutes.

He exhaled through his nose. Nothing in the room changed.

He took a screenshot and saved it to a folder named Delhi utilities.

Then he waited.

The platform showed pending.

At 01:12, it changed to processing.

At 01:19, it asked for card verification.

He uploaded the front of the credit card with the middle digits covered. The platform rejected the image. The corners were not visible. He placed the card on the table beside Sarah's keys and photographed it again. The keys, a brass Ganesh charm from the relocation agent, and the card all appeared in the frame. He cropped the charm out.

The second image was accepted.

At 01:31, the platform froze his account for identity verification.

He laughed once.

The sound came out too loud.

From the bedroom Sarah shifted again. He listened for her voice. None came.

The verification screen wanted a passport photograph and a live face scan.

He found his passport in the black document wallet. It still had the Iraq entry stamp near the middle, a rectangle of purple ink that had bled into the page. The photograph inside showed him with shorter hair and a tighter jaw, a man trained to think forms became harmless when filled correctly.

He held the passport to the webcam.

Too dark.

He turned on the dining pendant and tried again.

Glare detected.

He moved to the hallway, where the light was flatter.

Document accepted.

Now face scan.

He stood in front of the laptop and centred his face in the oval.

Turn head left.

Turn head right.

Blink.

He blinked.

The oval turned green.

Identity under review. This may take up to twenty-four hours.

He stood in the hallway with the passport open in his hand.

Twenty-four hours.

Olesya's phone was dead. Her lights were out. Two hundred had not moved. The bypass had led him to a new gate, cleaner and brighter than the last.

He returned to the table and opened the support chat.

Need urgent release. Beneficiary without power. Transfer for household utility.

The chat bot answered immediately.

Please choose from the following options.

He selected verification.

A new menu opened.

He selected transfer delay.

Another menu.

He typed agent.

The bot wrote:

Our team is here to help. Current waiting time: 47 minutes.

He kept the window open.

At 02:04, an agent named Mira joined the chat.

Thank you for contacting support. I see your account is under standard identity review.

He typed with two fingers.

Can this be accelerated? It is an emergency household payment.

Please upload proof of address.

I uploaded passport.

Passport confirms identity. Address proof required for higher confidence review.

He stared at the screen.

The Norwegian utility bill was in his cloud drive. It showed his name, the address, the customer number, the usage graph for a house Sarah had chosen for its view of water. He downloaded it, then stopped with the file icon sitting on the desktop.

The address tied to the card.

The wife who lived there.

The woman in Baku whose electricity had failed.

All of it crossing one upload box.

He sent the bill.

Mira wrote:

Thank you. Review in progress.

The chat went silent.

Thomas stood and walked to the balcony. He opened the sliding door enough to step outside.

Delhi entered the flat at once. Heat, exhaust, damp leaves, a sweetness from flowering shrubs planted along the compound path, then the sour edge of open drain somewhere beyond the wall. The air had weight. It sat on his skin. Below, the night guard leaned back in a plastic chair with his lathi across his knees, one shoe half off, his face lit by his phone.

Across the compound, other company flats glowed. Curtains open, lamps on, plates left near sinks, lives granted current and water and people paid to collect what broke. A woman in the opposite block stood at her balcony rail smoking. The ember moved to her mouth, then away. She looked over the compound without seeing him.

Thomas's phone vibrated.

The message had come through.

At neighbour. Charging.

Then:

No power yet.

Then:

Anya sleeping there.

He typed:

I found another route. Small amount first. Then more.

Olesya answered after a minute.

How long?

He looked back through the glass at the laptop.

Soon.

He deleted it.

I am waiting for verification.

Her next reply came faster.

What verification?

Payment company.

You said fixing.

He let the phone lower against his thigh.

Below, the guard stood and walked to the gate. A car had pulled up outside, dark windows, driver in a white shirt. The gate opened just enough. The car slid in without speaking to the street.

Thomas typed:

It is moving.

Olesya wrote:

Moving is not paid.

The line stayed on the screen.

Behind him, the laptop chimed.

Mira:

Your verification has been approved. Please note your first transfer remains subject to payout partner acceptance.

He went back inside.

The platform had changed. The two-hundred transfer now showed sent to partner.

He initiated a second transfer. Larger. The platform allowed it, then asked for the card code again. The Norwegian bank delayed the code. He moved the phone along the window, searching for signal. One bar. No code. He switched it off airplane mode and on again. Two bars. The code arrived in a stack of three duplicate messages.

He entered the latest one.

Payment declined by issuer.

He opened the banking app.

Suspicious transaction detected.

Confirm this was you.

Yes.

Try again.

He tried again.

Payment declined.

The app displayed a fraud desk number.

He called.

Another wait. Another line. Another person needing him to identify himself before admitting the shape of the obstacle.

'We can see unusual foreign online activity, Mr Hart.'

'I confirmed it in the app.'

'The system may still prevent repeated attempts.'

'Can you clear it?'

'I can note the account.'

'I need the payment to go through now.'

'What is the merchant category?'

'Remittance.'

'And the beneficiary country?'

'Azerbaijan.'

The agent paused. The pause had training inside it.

'Mr Hart, I can remove the card hold, but repeated high-value attempts may trigger further review.'

'Remove it.'

'For your security I need to ask the purpose.'

He looked towards the bedroom. Sarah had closed the door fully at some point. A thin strip of darkness showed beneath it.

'Household support.'

'For family?'

The word sat there again, offered by another stranger.

'Personal household support.'

'I will record that.'

He wanted to ask what the category would mean later. He did not.

The agent removed the hold.

He ran the second transfer.

Processing.

This time the spinner did not stall.

Sent to partner.

He ran a third, smaller, then a fourth. The fee increased on each one. The exchange rate worsened. A warning appeared after the fourth transaction: You are approaching your daily limit. He stopped there.

Total sent, less fees, would cover the electricity, the rent arrears Olesya had not yet named, and perhaps the neighbour who had taken Anya in for the night. It would not repair the thing the outage had exposed. It would not return the television. It would not make Anya forget the cold flat or the embarrassment of carrying a school bag into another person's home.

He sent Olesya the screenshots.

Four messages delivered.

No answer.

At 03:06, the first payout changed to completed.

He sent:

Check your account.

Olesya answered:

I see 200.

Use for electricity now.

Need all.

More coming.

When?

Tonight.

She did not answer.

The second transfer stayed at sent to partner. The third showed processing. The fourth showed pending review.

He watched the statuses, refreshing each tab until the platform warned him that too many refreshes could slow processing. He sat back and put both hands flat on the table.

Sarah came out again just before four.

This time she had dressed. Linen trousers, grey T-shirt, hair tied without care. She had the look of someone who had stopped pretending sleep would come back. She went to the kitchen, filled the kettle, then remembered the bottled water and emptied it into the sink. The kettle clicked on.

'Tea?' she asked.

'No.'

She made one anyway and placed it near his elbow.

'Did it work?'

'Partly.'

Sarah looked at the laptop.

'How many screens does one payment need?'

'More than it should.'

She sat opposite him.

For a while neither of them spoke. The kettle ticked behind them. Dawn had begun to lighten the curtains with a thin grey wash. Somewhere down in the compound, a broom moved over paving stones in regular strokes, dry leaves scraping into a pan.

Sarah reached for the notepad and turned to a clean page.

'What did this cost?'

'Fees are high.'

'How high?'

He gave her the number.

She wrote it down.

'And the bank transfer is still locked?'

'Yes.'

'So this is the new way?'

'For now.'

Sarah nodded, then drew a line under the fee figure.

'For now has become expensive.'

He rubbed the side of his thumb against the table edge. The veneer had a slight ridge where two boards met.

'The money moved.'

'Some of it.'

'Enough to turn the power back on.'

'That is not the same thing.'

He looked up.

Sarah did not soften the line. She had never been cruel with him. That was one reason his arrangements had survived so long. She had left space where another woman might have demanded inventory. She had asked less than she could have asked, trusted the shape of him, or perhaps trusted the version of him that came home with travel documents in order and invoices paid.

Now the table held too many facts.

'What do you want me to do?' he asked.

The question landed badly. He heard it at once.

Sarah looked at the tea, then back at him.

'Do not make me the person who answers that.'

The laptop chimed into the space between them.

Second transfer completed.

Third transfer completed.

The fourth remained pending.

He messaged Olesya.

Two more completed. Check.

The ticks turned blue.

One minute.

Two.

Then:

I see.

Pay electricity first.

Already paid.

Good.

It was not gratitude. It was a line item closing.

He stared at the word until the letters separated from their meaning.

Sarah stood.

'I have a driver at nine.'

'Where are you going?'

'The market. Then lunch with Meera from the relocation office.'

She took her cup to the sink, rinsed it, and set it upside down on the draining board.

'You should sleep before work calls start.'

He nodded.

She went back to the bedroom.

The flat changed with morning. The compound generator shut off. A brief tremor passed through the lights, then municipal current took the load. Outside, gardeners began dragging hoses across the grass. Water struck leaves in hard silver bursts. A man shouted up to someone on a balcony. A pressure cooker whistled from another flat, sharp and domestic.

Thomas stayed at the table.

The fourth transfer completed at 06:48.

He sent the final screenshot.

All sent.

Olesya did not answer until 07:23.

Thank you.

Two words.

No name.

No photograph.

No question about his night, no mention of Anya beyond what had already been given, no softening after the money arrived. The message sat underneath four screenshots, each one carrying reference numbers, payout codes, exchange rates, fees, and the mark of a company whose name he had not known twelve hours earlier.

The bridge had held.

He could see every bolt.

At eight, Sarah left for the market.

The driver rang the bell, though he had been told to wait downstairs. He stood in the corridor in a pressed white shirt, holding the car keys with both hands. Sarah signed something on his phone and asked him to give her two minutes. She checked her bag at the hall table: purse, sunglasses, hand sanitiser, folded list from the relocation office. Thomas watched from the dining table with the laptop still open.

'Will you be here?' she asked.

'Yes.'

'The cleaner is coming at ten.'

'Fine.'

'Please move anything private.'

She did not look at the laptop when she said it.

He closed the screen.

Sarah nodded once and left.

Through the door he heard her speak to the driver in the corridor. Her voice had already found the easy register she used with new cities, polite and firm, ready to receive service without apology. The lift doors opened. Closed. The corridor returned to its cooled silence.

Thomas opened the laptop again.

The platform dashboard showed all four transfers completed. Under each one, a button invited him to repeat payment. Faster next time, it promised. Save beneficiary for future use.

He clicked save.

The beneficiary profile opened. Olesya's name, her card number, her address, her date of birth, her country, her receiving currency. A neat little file made by a company in Europe for a woman in Baku, funded by a man in Delhi with a card tied to Norway and a wife downstairs in a car.

The system had accepted her at last.

Only after he made her smaller.

He changed the beneficiary label to Utilities.

Then he sat very still, listening to the cleaner's cart begin its slow movement along the corridor outside.

Chapter 18

For two months the European platform behaved.

It sent small green confirmations to his email before breakfast. It held Olesya's card details in a beneficiary profile labelled Utilities. It remembered the Norwegian billing address, accepted the Delhi location, charged the credit card, converted at an ugly rate, and placed money into Baku with a speed that let him look away again.

The first week after the outage, he checked every transfer line by line.

Reference number.

Payout partner.

Fee.

Exchange rate.

Beneficiary.

By the fourth week he checked only the green tick.

The platform trained him in reduced attention. That was its first kindness. It narrowed the act until it could be done between a call with the drilling superintendent and Sarah asking from the bedroom whether he had seen her linen shirt. It made no moral demand. It did not ask who Olesya was after he had finished pretending not to answer. It stored the pretence and offered to repeat it.

Faster next time.

By then Delhi had arranged itself around him. The company car collected him at 07:10 when he went to the office and later when he had to visit the supplier yard near Faridabad. The driver knew the better route when the ring road had water standing in the low lanes. Security at the gate had learned his face. The cleaner came on Tuesdays and Fridays, always wearing plastic sandals that slapped softly along the passage, always moving his papers into neater piles than he had left them.

Sarah learned the city through errands and other women.

She came back with names he could not place, with jars of spices from INA Market, with a tailor's card, with a warning about a dentist, with invitations written in the light voice of people who had learned how to be temporary without looking lost. She found a yoga studio above a pharmacy. She complained about the traffic with the easy entitlement of someone who knew a driver would sit in it for her.

Olesya sent less.

Electricity paid.

School fees due 12th.

Rent paid.

Need winter clothes for Anya.

Thomas answered in figures.

Send invoice.

How much?

Transfer Friday.

Paid.

The old sentences did not return. No photograph of Anya with a schoolbook. No complaint about the lift. No small domestic reports that had once entered his day without asking permission. The messages had become clean. They did not leave marks on the table.

He told himself clean was better.

Clean meant he could put it into the spreadsheet.

He created a new tab called Route B and hid it behind the older monthly sheets. The columns were narrower than they needed to be. Date. Amount sent. Fee. Amount received. Platform reference. Card charge. Confirmation. He shaded nothing, added no colours, and used numbers rounded to the nearest unit so the sheet would not look too attended if Sarah ever saw it over his shoulder.

The first month balanced.

The second did not.

An extra fee appeared on the receiving side. Olesya sent a screenshot of the deposit with the bank name cropped out. He asked for the full image.

Why?

Need to reconcile.

What reconcile?

Check amount.

Amount is there.

The exchange loss?

You sent. I received.

He left the row marked with a small asterisk and told himself he would correct it later.

Later became a place where small wrong things were stored.

Sarah noticed the credit card balance on a Sunday morning.

She was sitting at the end of the dining table with her laptop open, paying for flights to Singapore for a weekend she had described as necessary before asking whether he wanted to come. He had said he could not. Work. She had accepted the answer without pressing it into shape.

'Your card is high again,' she said.

He did not look up.

'Delhi setup costs.'

'Those went through the company.'

'Some were personal.'

She tapped something on her keyboard. Her nails were unpainted. One had split near the edge and caught briefly on a key.

'It is not my card,' she said.

'I know.'

'That was not an objection.'

He entered a formula in the wrong cell and undid it.

'I am managing it.'

Sarah closed her laptop halfway, enough to make a darker rectangle of her face in the screen.

'You always are.'

He waited for more. She gave him nothing further. The laptop opened again. Her flight confirmation arrived with a soft chime and printed itself into their shared calendar, visible, named, accounted for.

His transfers remained elsewhere.

That afternoon Olesya sent:

Anya needs exam registration.

How much?

She gave the number.

Invoice?

No invoice yet.

Then send when available.

Deadline tomorrow.

He stared at the line. The old version of him would have sent the money and asked for the paper later. The current version had learned that every missing document became a weak place in the bridge. Yet Anya existed on the other side of the weak place, older now, less visible, her life reduced to fees, deadlines, and the need for proof after payment.

He sent.

Olesya replied six hours later.

Received.

He typed:

What exam?

The answer came the next morning.

English.

No photograph followed. No joke about his voice. No complaint from Anya that he pronounced things wrongly. Only the subject, then silence.

He moved the entry into the spreadsheet and labelled it Education. The label looked cleaner than the exchange that had produced it.

On the evening the platform failed, Sarah had taken him to a corporate dinner at a hotel in Chanakyapuri.

The building sat behind layers of security, all metal gates and mirrored glass, a cooled island in the heavy dust beyond the road. At the entrance, a guard leaned towards the car and looked under it with a mirror fixed to a pole. Another opened the boot. The driver kept both hands visible on the wheel.

Inside, the lobby smelled of lilies, polish, and chilled air. Marble held the sound of shoes. Women in silk moved through the light with small handbags tucked under their arms, and men from the company stood in loose circles with their lanyards removed but not their office posture. A pianist played something harmless near the bar. The notes reached the table already made thin.

Sarah wore a dark green dress he had not seen before. She had bought it in Delhi and had the waist altered. It sat well on her. Men looked at her, then corrected themselves when they saw him beside her.

'Don't take calls at the table tonight,' she said whilst they waited for the host to notice them.

'I won't.'

'Messages either.'

He put the phone face down.

'It is one dinner.'

'I know.'

She gave him the briefest look. Not accusation. A measurement.

At the table the conversation moved through oil prices, visas, the difficulty of moving equipment through port authorities, the weather in Stavanger, schooling, air purifiers, a minister no one trusted, and which airline had become unusable. Sarah sat between the regional finance director and a woman from procurement who had once lived in Muscat. Thomas cut his food into even pieces and listened for the few words that mattered to him.

Audit.

Compliance.

Threshold.

Review.

The finance director spoke about a contractor in Chennai who had tried to route payments through three shell vendors and had been caught when a junior clerk matched two phone numbers. Everyone at the table laughed with the looseness of people who had not been the clerk.

Thomas lifted his glass and drank water.

His phone vibrated against the tablecloth.

Once.

Then again.

Sarah's hand was beside her plate. Her wedding ring caught the candlelight when she reached for her glass. She did not look down.

He waited.

The phone vibrated a third time.

'Excuse me,' he said.

Sarah did not turn her head.

In the corridor near the toilets, the hotel cooled the air so hard that the back of his neck tightened. He stood beside a brass planter holding a palm with polished leaves and turned the phone over.

The first message was from the European platform.

Account suspended pending beneficiary verification.

The second:

Action required: recipient identity documents.

The third:

Your scheduled payment has not been processed.

He opened the email.

Dear Customer,

As part of our ongoing review obligations, we require additional information from your recipient before further payouts can be completed. Please upload a clear photograph of the recipient's passport or national identity document, proof of residential address, and a live verification image through the secure link below.

Failure to complete this request within seven calendar days may result in permanent closure of your account.

The hotel corridor had no windows. Mirrors repeated the light back at him. Behind the toilet door, water ran into a basin, stopped, ran again.

He read the line a second time.

Recipient's passport.

Proof of residential address.

Live verification image.

Olesya had once refused to send a photograph of her building entrance after a debt collector used it to prove she was still living there. She had covered her bank card number with her thumb in the old picture. She had removed her face from the app. She lived in a country where documents could be used by people who had no right to them and still win.

He could not ask her for a live verification image.

He opened the platform dashboard. The monthly payment sat in red.

On hold.

Beside it, a button waited.

Request recipient documents.

He locked the phone and pressed it against his thigh.

At the table, a waiter had cleared his starter and replaced it with fish under a pale sauce. Sarah looked at him once when he sat down. He gave the small head movement that meant later. She picked up her fork.

The finance director was still talking.

'The thing about informal routes,' the man said, 'is that sooner or later they behave like informal routes.'

Thomas looked down at the fish. A caper sat in the sauce like a small green eye.

He stayed until dessert.

In the car home, Sarah sat beside him rather than in front. The driver kept the partition open. Delhi moved past in sodium light and honking layers, scooters coming close enough to show the pale dust on a passenger's trousers, roadside fires burning in metal drums, a boy asleep under a tarpaulin beside stacked crates of oranges.

Sarah looked out of the window.

'Was that Baku?'

'Payment platform.'

'Same thing now, isn't it?'

He did not answer.

The driver changed lanes, braked, then found a space between two buses. A horn stayed under his hand for a long time. Thomas watched the driver's eyes in the mirror, fixed and flat, giving away nothing.

'Is it blocked again?' Sarah asked.

'Suspended.'

'Can you clear it?'

'They want her documents.'

Sarah turned then.

'Will she send them?'

'No.'

'Have you asked?'

'No.'

Sarah's face passed in and out of streetlight.

'Then you know.'

He looked at his phone.

'I know enough.'

They drove the rest of the way without speaking. At the compound gate, the guard came out with his clipboard. The driver lowered the window. A mosquito entered the car and moved in the glow from the dashboard, its legs fine as threads.

In the flat, Sarah took off her earrings at the hall table and placed them in a small ceramic dish. One earring missed the dish and struck the wood. She picked it up, set it with the other, then went into the bedroom.

Thomas stayed in the living area.

He opened the platform again. The same red hold. The same request.

He wrote to support.

Recipient cannot provide live verification. Alternative documentation available?

The answer arrived after sixteen minutes.

No.

He typed:

Can payments be released against sender identity only?

No.

The agent added:

This process protects your recipient.

Thomas looked at that sentence for a long time.

Then he opened the secure app.

The last message from Olesya was three days old.

Received.

He typed:

Payment platform blocked. They require your passport and address proof. I am looking for another route.

The message delivered.

Olesya was awake.

No passport.

I know.

No face scan.

I know.

Why they need this?

Compliance.

What means?

Rules.

Her next answer came as separate lines.

Always rules.

Always my papers.

Always asking.

He watched the three lines stack on the screen. They were not dramatic. That made them harder to move past.

I will not send.

I know.

Then what?

I will find another way.

She did not answer.

He sat at the dining table until after midnight and searched.

Crypto to cash Azerbaijan.

Baku cash pickup digital asset.

USDT Baku broker.

Azerbaijan cash out verified.

The words left a film on the room.

The results came through forums, Telegram groups, payment pages without addresses, brokerage sites with English written in an over-clean register, YouTube comments, warnings, screenshots of payment proofs, men with profile photographs taken in cars. He opened a spreadsheet and began sorting names, fees, minimums, currencies, payout methods, and risk.

The European platform had worn a proper shirt. This route came bare-armed, smoking in a doorway.

At 01:40 he found a broker with a website, a company registration number in Georgia, and three years of forum references. The contact number led to a Telegram profile named Murad Cash. The profile picture showed the Maiden Tower at sunset.

Thomas wrote:

Need cash or bank payout in Baku. Regular amount. Recipient female. No document upload.

Murad replied after four minutes.

Amount?

Thomas gave the figure.

Crypto?

I can buy.

USDT only. Fee 18 cash, 12 bank.

Too high.

Then bank.

Recipient bank?

Yes.

May ask source.

Cash safer.

Thomas rubbed at the ridge between his eyebrows until the skin warmed beneath his fingers.

How cash delivered?

She meet my man. Public place.

Where?

Metro. Mall. Her choice.

No.

Then bank.

Thomas closed the chat, opened it again, and read the fee line.

Twelve percent.

The European platform had already been theft with manners. This had no manners left.

He opened a crypto exchange account he had used once in Norway when a colleague had insisted everyone should understand digital assets before dismissing them. The account still existed. It wanted identity confirmation, then a card, then another code. He gave it everything. The Delhi flat filled with the small sounds of verification: phone vibration, email chime, webcam click, the low fan in the laptop, Sarah turning in the other room.

By two thirty he owned a balance in USDT.

It looked like money.

It did not feel like money.

It sat in a wallet address, a string of letters and numbers copied to the clipboard. One wrong character would send it elsewhere. One fraud would take it whole. No bank officer would mark the case urgent. No support line would apologise.

He wrote to Olesya.

I have a route. Cash pickup in Baku possible. Public place.

Her reply arrived in the morning.

I will not meet a man for cash.

It is a known broker.

Known to who?

I checked references.

References from internet?

He stared at the question.

The cleaner came at ten and found him still at the table.

She had a key issued by the relocation agent. She entered after ringing twice. A narrow woman in a blue tunic, hair pinned tightly, carrying a plastic caddy with cloths and sprays arranged by colour. Thomas shut the laptop when she came in.

'Madam out?' she asked.

'Yes.'

'Sir want cleaning now?'

'Yes. Bedroom later.'

She nodded and began with the kitchen. The smell of lemon cleaner entered the room. She moved his empty water bottles into the recycling bag, wiped the counter where Sarah had left tea stains, lifted the toaster to clean beneath it. Her bangles made a soft glass sound each time she reached forward.

Thomas took the laptop to the balcony.

Outside, the compound trees held dusty leaves without movement. The guard at the gate stood with his shirt darkened under the arms. A delivery rider argued with security over a paper bag marked with a restaurant logo. Two children from the opposite block waited for a school car, their bags too large for their backs.

Olesya wrote again.

If someone sees me?

Use mall.

You think mall makes it clean?

He typed, deleted, typed again.

Bank deposit then.

Will they ask?

Possibly.

Then same.

It may pass.

May.

He did not answer that.

Inside, the cleaner had reached the dining table. She lifted Sarah's ceramic dish, wiped the wood beneath it, replaced it half an inch from where it had been. She saw the notepad with exchange calculations and turned it face down before wiping under it.

Thomas watched through the glass.

The household continued. The hidden thing had to move around it, under it, between its timed services and polished surfaces.

At noon, Sarah called from the car.

'I forgot my blue folder on the hall table. Can you photograph the invitation inside?'

'Now?'

'Please. The driver's taken the wrong turn and Meera wants the address.'

He went to the hall table. The blue folder lay beneath a scarf, beside the ceramic dish and a receipt from the tailor. He opened it. Invitations, menus, a note from the high commission, a printed map to a residence in Golf Links. He photographed the invitation and sent it.

Sarah replied with a thumbs-up.

He stood with the folder still open, looking at the thick card, the embossed address, the certainty of arrival. People in Sarah's Delhi printed directions to homes where guards expected them. Olesya was being asked to meet a stranger in a mall for cash.

He put the folder back.

At 12:34 he wrote to Murad.

Bank payout. Test amount first.

Murad sent a wallet address.

TRC20 only.

Thomas checked the network three times.

He sent a small amount.

The exchange warned him that blockchain transfers were irreversible.

He confirmed.

The transaction showed pending.

Then sent.

Then confirmed.

Murad wrote:

Got it.

Send recipient card.

Thomas sent Olesya's card number, name, and bank.

Murad answered:

One hour.

Thomas watched the clock.

At forty-three minutes, Olesya wrote:

Deposit came.

How much?

She gave the amount.

It was lower than expected.

Thomas checked the exchange rate, the fee, the hidden spread. Between his credit card, the crypto exchange, the network cost, Murad's commission, and the receiving bank's conversion, nearly a quarter had vanished.

He wrote to Murad:

Amount short.

Murad sent a calculation photographed from a notebook. Numbers in blue pen. Commission circled. Exchange at local rate. Bank fee. Handling.

Thomas could not tell whether it was dishonest or merely the price of the tunnel he had chosen.

He sent the remaining amount in three pieces.

The second transfer took two hours.

The third stalled at the exchange for manual review.

The fourth reached Murad, then disappeared into a payout queue.

Olesya sent:

Only first came.

More processing.

Need today.

I know.

Do you?

He placed the phone face down and kept his hand over it.

At three, a call from the office came in. A pressure anomaly on a pump package due for shipment. The supplier had substituted a valve model. The Delhi procurement team wanted approval to release it anyway. Thomas opened the technical sheet, read the tolerances, compared the operating envelope, and told them no.

'It will delay shipment,' the project manager said.

'Then delay it.'

'Client is pushing.'

'The valve is wrong.'
