'Can we accept with deviation?'

'No.'

He heard his own voice with the old clarity in it. This system still answered. The wrong part could be refused. A line could be held. A thing either met specification or did not.

On the table, his phone lit.

No second.

He muted the office call and wrote:

Checking.

Then unmuted.

'Send the rejection note to me first,' he said.

By evening the second deposit had arrived. The third remained under exchange review. The fourth showed completed on Murad's side and absent on Olesya's.

Murad wrote:

Bank delay.

Thomas wrote:

Proof?

Murad sent a screenshot with Olesya's name and a transaction number.

Olesya wrote:

Nothing.

Sarah came home carrying flowers wrapped in newspaper.

'The driver overcharged for parking,' she said, placing her bag on the hall table. 'Or the market did. I have no idea.'

She stopped when she saw his face.

'Again?'

'The platform failed.'

'And now?'

'Broker.'

Sarah set the flowers down slowly.

'What kind of broker?'

'Cash-out.'

'Thomas.'

'It is a bank deposit route.'

'That is not what I asked.'

He closed the laptop halfway.

Sarah looked at the screen before it shut. She saw enough: a wallet address, a transaction hash, Murad's messages, Olesya's card details.

'Is this legal?'

'It is not illegal to send money.'

'That was not what I asked either.'

The flowers lay between them. The newspaper had dampened where the stems were wrapped. Purple petals leaned out of the paper, already bruising at the edges from the heat.

'What would you have me do?' he said.

Sarah did not answer at once.

She took the flowers to the sink, unwrapped them, and trimmed the stems with kitchen scissors. Water ran into a glass vase the relocation agent had placed in the cupboard. She arranged the flowers badly. She had never cared for that kind of domestic neatness. The stems crossed too high, leaving gaps.

'I would have you know what you are doing,' she said.

'I do know.'

'No. You know the next payment.'

She carried the vase to the table. A drop of water fell on the wood and spread into a dark oval.

'There is more than one kind of debt here.'

He turned the laptop back towards himself.

'I have to finish this.'

'Yes,' she said.

She went to the bedroom and closed the door.

The third transfer cleared at 21:12.

The fourth arrived at 22:05, reduced by another fee Olesya could not identify.

She wrote:

Received.

Thomas waited for another line.

None came.

He typed:

Is Anya home?

No reply.

He typed:

Is the electricity stable?

No reply.

He typed:

I sent enough for the rent as well.

This time she answered.

Received.

The word looked identical to the first. Same spacing. Same capital letter. Same full stop absent from the end. It could have been generated by the app itself.

He scrolled up through their messages.

Years earlier, there had been other things.

The lift stuck again.

Anya says your English sounds like BBC man.

I made soup but too much dill.

You forgot your scarf.

These sat above photographs that no longer loaded, grey squares with broken icons in their centres. The app had kept the words and lost the faces.

He scrolled back down.

Received.

Received.

By the next month, the process had a sequence.

He bought USDT through the exchange, never more than the threshold that had triggered review last time. He sent it to Murad in pieces, copying the wallet address into a blank text file first so he could check the first six and last six characters. Murad confirmed with a thumbs-up or a single word. Thomas sent Olesya the expected payout, less known fees. Olesya waited.

Sometimes bank.

Sometimes cash to card.

Sometimes Murad used another man whose name appeared only as R.

Sometimes the payout split into four amounts that arrived out of order.

Sometimes Olesya wrote nothing until the final piece arrived.

Then:

Received.

The word became useful. That was the worst of it. It closed the loop. It gave him a point to enter in the spreadsheet. It let him archive the screenshots, reconcile the card balance, remove the red mark from the month's support line. It did not ask him to imagine the hand receiving the notification in Baku, the room around it, the person who had stopped saying what the money was for.

He learned to prefer it.

Once, Murad sent proof as a photograph, not a screenshot.

The image showed a receipt on a counter. Olesya's name was printed near the top, the amount below it, a stamp partly over the date. At the edge of the frame, too small to belong to the document, Thomas saw a woman's hand holding the paper flat. The nail on the index finger was torn short. There was a red mark across the knuckle, not deep, not fresh, only the kind of mark that came from a bag handle cutting into skin or a door pulled too hard.

He enlarged the photograph until the pixels broke apart.

The mark remained useless.

He wrote:

Is that your hand?

Olesya answered:

Received.

He wrote:

I asked about your hand.

No answer.

Murad wrote:

Proof sent. Next time include bank name in note.

Thomas saved the receipt in the folder and named it Month_18_Payout_Proof. The file sat beside others in order. Each one made the sequence look reasonable.

The next morning Sarah asked to use his printer login.

'Mine is still linked to Norway,' she said from the bedroom doorway. 'It keeps trying to send everything to the house.'

'Use guest.'

'Guest won't print the embassy form.'

He stood too quickly and crossed to the dining table.

'I'll do it.'

Sarah held out her laptop.

'It is just a form.'

'The printer software is awkward.'

She let him take the laptop. Her face gave nothing away, but she remained beside him whilst he typed the printer address and waited for the queue to load. On his own laptop, the payout folder sat open behind the browser. He closed it with his left hand, then clicked the printer dialogue on hers.

The embassy form printed in the study alcove. Five pages. Official crest. Boxes for passport number, address, purpose of travel, expected duration.

Sarah collected the pages and tapped them against the table to align the corners.

'Everything wants the same things,' she said.

'What?'

'Names. Addresses. Purpose.'

She put the pages into a folder and left the room.

Thomas reopened the payout folder.

Month_18_Payout_Proof.

He renamed it Receipt.

On a humid Thursday, stuck in traffic near Dhaula Kuan, he received one such confirmation whilst the company car sat between a cement mixer and a bus with a cracked rear window.

The air outside had gone the colour of old metal. Rain threatened but did not fall. Motorbikes pushed through gaps no wider than a shoulder. A man selling phone chargers moved between cars with his stock hanging from both arms, white cables swinging like loose tendons. The driver kept the air-conditioning high and a devotional song low on the radio.

Thomas had a call open on his laptop, hotspot tethered to his phone. Men in Norway discussed a schedule slip. Someone in Delhi had forgotten to mute and was eating near the microphone. A slide showed procurement milestones in green, amber, red.

His phone vibrated.

Received.

He looked at the word.

Around him the city inched forward by inches earned through horn and heat. A child tapped once on the window with a packet of tissues held up to the glass. The driver lifted his hand without turning. The child moved to the next car.

On the call, someone said his name.

'Thomas?'

He unmuted.

'Yes.'

'Can you confirm acceptance of the revised delivery sequence?'

He looked at the slide. Green. Amber. Red.

'Send it to me in writing.'

'We need verbal approval now.'

His phone dimmed. He touched the screen to keep it awake.

Received.

'Thomas?'

'No verbal approval,' he said. 'Send it in writing.'

He muted again.

The bus ahead released a cloud of black exhaust that rolled back over the bonnet and disappeared into the car's intake. The driver changed the air setting with two fingers.

Thomas opened the secure app and typed:

Anything else?

The message delivered.

No answer.

He waited.

Traffic moved one car length, then stopped.

The word stayed where she had left it.

Chapter 19

The word stayed where she had left it.

Received.

For three days Thomas did not write again.

He kept the app open in the background and let it show the last thing Olesya had given him. He moved through the Delhi flat, through office calls, through supplier notes and vehicle arrivals, through Sarah's packing for London, with the word resting beneath everything like a coin under a plate.

The app showed no new typing mark.

No missed call.

No photograph.

No small correction from Baku, no complaint about bank fees, no line from Anya using her mother's phone. Only the receipt, the delivery tick, the finished transaction.

Sarah left for London on the fourth morning.

Her suitcase stood beside the hall table at six, zipped and tagged. A black coat lay folded over the handle, absurd in the Delhi heat. She had dressed for the aircraft rather than the city: dark trousers, white shirt, scarf light enough to pass for decoration and warm enough to survive Heathrow. The driver waited downstairs, engine running too long in the compound until the guard knocked on the bonnet and told him to switch it off.

'You have the dinner with Rajiv on Thursday,' Sarah said.

'I know.'

'And the engineer from Stavanger arrives Friday.'

'Yes.'

She looked at him over the open lid of her handbag.

'You are saying yes too quickly.'

He checked the time on his watch.

'I have it in the calendar.'

'That is not the same thing.'

The driver rang once from downstairs. Sarah ignored it. She put her passport into the inside pocket of the handbag, then took it out again and checked the photo page, the visa pages, the boarding pass folded behind the cover.

'How long is London?'

'Six nights.'

'I thought four.'

'I told you six.'

He must have nodded. He had no memory of it.

Sarah closed the handbag.

'Try to sleep properly.'

'I will.'

'And eat something that is not from the office canteen.'

'All right.'

She waited, perhaps for him to step forward, perhaps only for the lift bell that had not yet sounded. Then she lifted the scarf from the suitcase handle and wrapped it once around her neck.

'Goodbye, Thomas.'

'Message when you land.'

'I always do.'

The door shut behind her with the soft hydraulic pull of expensive hinges.

The flat changed at once.

Her perfume remained near the hall mirror. The air-conditioning pushed it into the living room, where it thinned among the smells of cooled dust, old coffee, and the lemon cleaner the housekeeper had used on the table. A single earring back lay near the ceramic dish. He saw it only after the lift had taken her down.

He picked it up and placed it in the dish.

Then he opened the secure app.

Received.

He wrote:

Anything else?

The message delivered.

No answer.

By late afternoon he had checked the app eleven times. On the twelfth, the ticks turned blue.

Read.

No reply.

He placed the phone face down, then turned it over again. The blue ticks remained. There was a particular insult in their neatness, in the little mark the system gave when it could prove a message had arrived and leave untouched what came after. He had paid Murad. Murad had paid someone else. Someone had paid Olesya's bank or card or hand. The system had closed. She had seen the question and left it open.

He made coffee in the kitchen and forgot to put a cup under the machine.

The first run splashed into the drip tray, dark and thin, then overflowed onto the counter. He stood watching it reach the edge and fall in slow drops to the floor. The smell filled the kitchen with burned bitterness.

He cleaned it with paper towels.

The towels came away brown.

The next transfer was due on the eleventh.

He began the process early, partly to avoid the bank holiday Murad had warned him about, partly to give the money more time to surface if one of the pieces stalled. The sequence had become physical by then. Laptop open. Exchange account. Card code. Wallet address copied into a blank file. First six characters. Last six. Paste. Check again. Small send. Confirmation. Larger send. Screenshot. Message to Murad. Message to Olesya only after the broker had proof.

He did it from the dining table whilst rain gathered over Delhi and refused to break.

The sky lowered all afternoon. The compound trees turned their leaves pale side out. Air sat against the balcony glass. Down below, the guard dragged a chair beneath the overhang, then dragged it back out again when no rain came. The city seemed to wait without moving.

At 17:42 Murad wrote:

Paid.

He sent a screenshot. Blurred bank interface. Olesya's initials. Amount. Time stamp.

Thomas forwarded the amount to Olesya.

Sent.

No reply.

At 21:10 he wrote:

Confirm when received.

The message delivered.

No answer.

He ate at the table with the laptop open. Rice from a foil container, chicken gone soft in a sauce that had separated. Sarah had left two yoghurts in the fridge. He ate one standing by the sink and rinsed the spoon straight after.

At midnight the ticks turned blue.

Read.

No reply.

He called.

The app rang six times and ended.

He called again.

Declined.

He looked at the word until the screen dimmed.

The next morning he wrote:

Did the money arrive?

Blue ticks after seven minutes.

No reply.

Murad confirmed again:

Paid yesterday. Ask bank.

Thomas sent:

Need proof. Recipient not confirming.

Murad answered with a voice note.

Thomas did not play it. He wrote:

Text only.

A long pause.

Then:

Brother, paid. I cannot make woman answer phone.

Thomas set the phone down.

Outside, Delhi finally broke open. Rain struck the balcony in hard sheets, turned the opposite block into a grey wall, filled the gutters so quickly that water began to leap from the roof corners. The compound path vanished beneath a shallow skin. Gardeners ran for cover under a stairwell. A plastic chair tipped over and travelled a little before lodging against a drain.

His phone lay silent on the table.

He spent the day inside a pressure he could not name. The office call at ten ran through valve specifications. At eleven, a procurement dispute over storage charges. At twelve, a Norwegian engineer asked whether Delhi was always this loud in the background and laughed at his own line. Thomas answered each question. He made decisions. He corrected one calculation in a load sheet. He rejected a supplier deviation with two sentences.

The app stayed open beside the work.

Read.

No reply.

By the second day, the silence had shape.

It was not the old withdrawal, not the flat economy of Received. This silence had a boundary around it. Olesya had seen the messages. She had declined a call. She had not asked for more. She had not accused him. She had given him nothing to repair.

He began writing messages and deleting them.

Are you all right?

Deleted.

Please answer.

Deleted.

I need to know if the transfer arrived.

Deleted.

On the third night, Sarah called from London.

He had forgotten the time difference and answered with the laptop still open on the broker spreadsheet. It was early evening there, late in Delhi. Behind her, through the hotel room window, he could see the dull London sky and the pale reflection of a lamp.

'You look awful,' she said.

'Bad light.'

'It is always bad light with you now.'

He moved the phone away from the spreadsheet.

'How is London?'

'Wet. Efficient. Overpriced.'

'Meetings?'

'Useful enough.'

She was sitting on a hotel bed with her shoes still on. A room-service tray rested on the desk behind her, silver lid lifted, a folded napkin beside it. Visible life. Delivered food. Receipts that matched the room.

'What is happening?' she asked.

'Work.'

'Thomas.'

He rubbed his thumb along the edge of the phone case.

'Baku has gone quiet.'

Sarah's face changed only in the eyes. The rest held.

'How long?'

'Three days.'

'After a payment?'

'Yes.'

'Can you contact the daughter?'

'I have an old email.'

'Use it.'

'She is a child.'

'No, she is not.'

The correction struck cleanly. Anya had moved through the years whilst he had kept her fixed in school fees, asthma medicine, exam registration, shoes, winter clothes, all the categories that let a person remain smaller in the ledger than in life.

Sarah shifted on the bed. The tray rattled softly behind her.

'Use the email,' she said.

He did not answer.

'Thomas.'

'Yes.'

'Use it tonight.'

After the call ended, he searched his archive.

Anya's email sat inside an old school invoice from Baku, copied into the payment note field. He had used it once to send a scanned textbook when Olesya's connection failed during lockdown. The address had a string of numbers after her name. He copied it into a new message.

Subject:

Your mother.

He deleted the subject.

Subject:

Checking in.

He deleted that too.

In the end he left the subject blank.

Anya,

I have not been able to reach your mother. Please tell me if everything is in order.

Thomas.

He looked at the email for several minutes before sending it.

The reply arrived at 03:17.

He had fallen asleep at the table with the laptop open. The sound woke him. Not fully. Enough to lift his head and see the blue glow of the screen, the empty yoghurt pot near his elbow, the balcony glass gone black with his own reflection.

The email was from Anya.

Mr Thomas,

My mother is in hospital. Her heart. They took her first to state hospital. Now she is in intensive care. I did not know if I should write to you.

A.

He read it once.

Then again.

The words did not move.

He stood and the chair fell backwards onto the floor.

The sound reached the bedroom, then returned from it unchanged. Sarah was not there. The flat had no one in it to answer the noise.

He lifted the chair and sat down.

Which hospital?

He sent the email, then opened the secure app and called Olesya.

No answer.

He called again.

No answer.

He wrote to Anya:

Which hospital? Who is the doctor? What do they need?

The reply came after nine minutes.

State hospital first. Now cardiology centre. I am in corridor. They say no visitors at night. I will send name.

A photograph arrived.

It showed a corridor under greenish light. A row of plastic chairs bolted together. A vending machine with no water in the front row. A door at the end with a sign in Azerbaijani and Russian. The floor had been mopped recently, and the wet marks caught the fluorescent strips in long broken lines. In the lower corner of the image, Anya's shoe appeared. White trainer, scuffed near the toe.

No Olesya.

Thomas enlarged the door sign until the letters broke apart.

Anya sent the hospital name.

He searched it. State-run cardiology unit, private wing attached, mixed reviews, an address near a part of Baku he had driven through once without stopping. Photographs online showed a low building with narrow windows, a cracked step, a flag mounted above the entrance. Another image showed a polished reception desk in the private wing, bright enough to belong to a different country.

He called the number on the website.

The first call failed.

The second rang into a switchboard where a woman answered in Azerbaijani. Thomas asked for English. The line rustled, then another woman came on, older by the sound of her breathing, each word placed with care.

'Patient name.'

He gave Olesya's full name.

'Relation?'

The question arrived before he had prepared for it. It kept arriving. Bank, platform, card desk, hospital. A small gate, always the same size, always set at the point where money stopped being enough.

'I am paying for her care.'

'Relation?'

'I am overseas contact.'

'Family?'

He looked at the blank wall above the dining table. Sarah had wanted to buy a print for it. Something local, she had said. Something with colour. The wall remained empty.

'I am the contact.'

'We speak family only.'

'Her daughter is there. Anya.'

'Daughter can speak doctor morning.'

'Can you confirm she is alive?'

The line went quiet.

Not silence. He could hear movement near the receiver, a drawer closing, a voice further away, wheels crossing a hard floor.

'Patient in intensive care,' the woman said at last.

'Stable?'

'Doctor morning.'

'What does she need now?'

'Doctor morning.'

Then the line ended.

Thomas kept the phone to his ear after the call had gone dead. The flat around him seemed to have enlarged. The dining table, the laptop, the yoghurt pot, Sarah's empty chair, all of it suddenly too arranged, too ready for a life that could prove itself on request.

Anya wrote again.

They say I cannot stand near door.

Where are you now?

On stairs.

Send picture.

The photograph showed concrete steps with a metal rail painted brown. The paint had worn off where hands had touched it for years. A paper cup lay crushed near the wall. Someone had written a phone number in black marker beside a patch of flaking plaster. Anya's bag sat between her feet, unzipped, a charger cable hanging from one pocket.

He wrote:

Do you have food?

She answered:

Later.

Now?

No.

Buy something.

I don't want leave.

Ask nurse if there is cafeteria.

She did not reply for twelve minutes.

Then:

Tea only.

Buy tea. Stay where lights are.

The instruction sounded foolish after he sent it. Stay where lights are. She was eighteen, alone on hospital stairs, and he was telling her where to stand from a dining table in Delhi.

He opened a map of the hospital area and looked for cafes, pharmacies, ATMs. He marked them in a note and sent the names to Anya, each one with distance from the hospital entrance. She replied to none of them.

He wrote:

Can they move her to private wing?

They say deposit first. But doctor says not now. Maybe morning.

How much?

Anya sent the number.

Then:

There is also money owed downstairs.

What money?

A pause.

Mr Thomas, the men came again.

Which men?

Debt men.

He sat very still.

The rain had stopped. Somewhere outside, water dripped from an awning in steady intervals. The air-conditioning hummed above the silence.

Anya wrote again:

They found the flat. They knew when money came. Mama paid them. Then more came. They said old interest. They said they will come to school.

Thomas read the lines with both hands on the table.

The debt had not been outside the system.

It had been waiting inside it.

He asked her to send what she had.

The first photograph was of a folded paper pushed under a door. The edges were softened from being handled. The writing was in Russian and Azerbaijani, numbers circled, dates beneath them, a signature that looked less like a name than a scratch made with a blunt instrument.

The second was a message from an unknown number.

Today.

No delay.

School know address.

The third was a photograph of their living room taken from the doorway. Not the whole room. A corner. The place where the television had been, now marked by a rectangle of cleaner wall and two loose cables hanging down. On the floor beneath it sat a small bowl, a slipper, and a stack of school papers tied with string.

Thomas remembered the first time he had entered that flat in Baku. Olesya standing by the kitchen counter with her hands damp from washing cups. Anya smaller then, watching him with the solemn suspicion of a child trained not to ask adult questions. The television had been on low volume, a Russian cartoon or music channel, something bright that filled the room without entering it. He had thought of it as a poor object, not part of their breathing.

Now the wall showed where it had been taken.

The fourth photograph was not meant for him. Anya sent it and then wrote:

Sorry.

It showed Olesya from behind, months earlier perhaps, standing at the flat door in a cardigan, one hand on the handle, the other pressed flat against the metal to keep it shut. The chain was on. A man's shoe was visible beyond the threshold.

Thomas enlarged the photograph until the door grain broke into squares.

The shoe remained.

He wrote:

Who took this?

Me.

Why did you not tell me?

Mama said no.

Why?

She said you send and then men know.

He looked back at the spreadsheet.

The months were arranged without protest. Each transfer had landed and announced itself somewhere. Each arrival had put a mark on the water for people he had not seen.

He typed:

How did they know?

I don't know.

Then:

Maybe bank. Maybe neighbour. Maybe they watch.

The answer covered every route and no route. It was not a solvable fault. It was weather.

He typed:

How much has she paid them?

I don't know. Many times.

From the money I sent?

Yes.

The word landed without force. That made it worse. Yes. No defence. No apology. No attempt to soften the shape of it.

He opened the broker spreadsheet. Month after month arranged in rows. Rent. School. Utilities. Medical. Winter clothes. Exam registration. Emergency. Each label had been clean. Each label had hidden a mouth behind it, open and taking.

He wrote:

Does your mother owe them now?

Yes.

How much?

Anya sent a number, then another line.

Maybe more. They change.

He stood and went to the kitchen.

The tiles were cold under his feet. He opened the fridge and looked inside. Sarah's yoghurts. Bottled water. Half a lime in cling film. A glass container of rice he would not eat. The light inside the fridge made everything appear chosen, stored, preserved.

He closed it.

His phone vibrated on the table.

Doctor says medicines need bought outside. Pharmacy near hospital. I have list.

Send photograph.

The photograph came at an angle. A doctor's handwriting, pharmacy stamps, names he did not know, dosages, ampoules, one line circled twice. At the bottom, the hospital stamp cut into the page in blue ink.

He copied the medicine names into a search window and found little that helped.

He tried the hospital again.

This time a man answered, then transferred him to another extension where no one picked up. On the third attempt he reached someone in the private wing. The English was better there. The voice belonged to a receptionist, young, formal, reading from a line he had used often enough himself.

'For admission we require deposit and passport copy.'

'The patient is already in intensive care.'

'For transfer to private wing, deposit and passport copy.'

'Her daughter is there.'

'Daughter can present documents.'

'Can I pay directly by card?'

'Payment link only local.'

'Bank transfer?'

'Takes time.'

'She does not have time.'

'Sir, I am reception.'

The line held that last sentence between them. Not refusal. Boundary. He had reached a person whose job was to say where her job ended.

'What can be done now?'

'Medicines from outside pharmacy. Doctor morning. Deposit when office opens.'

'Can you tell the doctor payment is arranged?'

'Sir, I am reception.'

He thanked her.

After the call, he wrote the hospital's bank name, account number, and opening time in the notepad. The page already held exchange rates, card limits, Murad's fee, Anya's card number, the medicine amount, the debt number with a question mark beside it. In the margin, without planning to, he had drawn a small box around the word relation.

He tore the page out, folded it once, then unfolded it again.

How much?

Anya sent a photograph of a calculator screen.

He replied:

I am sending money to you directly. Your account.

I have only student card.

Send details.

It may not take international.

Send details.

The details arrived as a photograph of a card held against denim. Anya's thumb covered part of the number. The nail was bitten down to the quick. She sent a second photograph without him asking.

He opened every route he had.

The European platform was closed.

The bank transfer route still held its old refusal.

The broker would deposit, but only to Olesya's saved card unless he paid a higher commission for a new beneficiary.

The crypto exchange wanted review for a larger purchase.

His secondary account balance sat lower than he had expected. Fees had taken their own share for months. Sarah's London trip had not touched it, yet the number looked thinned, rubbed down by repeated charges.

He wrote to Murad:

New beneficiary. Student card. Urgent hospital. Amount large.

Murad answered:

New card 15 fee. Hospital no problem. Send.

Thomas wrote:

Need full paid today.

Then send fast.

He bought USDT with the credit card. Declined.

He opened the bank app.

Fraud warning.

He confirmed.

Tried again.

Declined.

He called the fraud desk and listened to music at 03:52, standing barefoot in the living room while the balcony glass cooled from the night rain. The agent asked him to confirm the last three transactions.

'Yes.'

'And this new attempted transaction is also yours?'

'Yes.'

'It is a high-risk merchant category.'

'Clear it.'

'I need the purpose of transaction.'

He looked at Anya's photograph of the hospital corridor.

'Medical support.'

'For yourself?'

'No.'

'For a family member?'

The word had become a toll booth.

'For a dependent.'

The agent typed.

'Relationship?'

Thomas pressed his fingers against the bridge of his nose.

'Personal dependent.'

'I will record medical support to dependent.'

'Clear it.'

'The hold is removed. Please allow ten minutes before retrying.'

He waited five.

The purchase went through.

He sent Murad the first tranche. Then the second. The third stuck pending network confirmation. He sent Anya the transaction plan in short lines, each amount and expected arrival.

She replied:

I am at pharmacy.

Then:

They want cash.

Murad can send cash pickup.

No men.

Bank then.

Pharmacy wants cash.

Thomas looked at the screen.

Where are you?

She sent a location pin.

He forwarded it to Murad.

Female recipient at pharmacy. No meeting in car. No private place. Cash handover inside pharmacy or bank deposit only.

Murad replied:

You want rules, use bank.

Thomas typed:

Inside pharmacy.

Fee 20.

Fine.

The word came too quickly. Fine. Twenty percent to place cash across a counter in a city where a young woman waited with a medicine list and a mother behind a closed door.

He sent the amount.

Murad wrote:

Thirty minutes.

At 04:41 Anya wrote:

A man called. He said outside pharmacy.

Inside only.

I told.

Wait near counter.

I am.

Her messages arrived without the formal Mr Thomas now. The corridor had stripped that away.

At 04:58:

Got cash.

Buy medicines now.

I am.

At 05:17:

Nurse took.

At 05:23:

Doctor says wait.

Thomas sat with the laptop open, both phones on the table, charger cables crossing over Sarah's place mat. Dawn lifted behind the curtains. The compound began in fragments: a gate chain, a broom on wet concrete, a kettle in another flat, the first horn from the road beyond the trees.

He called Sarah at 05:31 Delhi time, 01:01 in London.

She answered on the fourth ring.

'What happened?'

Her voice was thick with sleep, but the question was already awake.

'Olesya is in hospital.'

Sarah sat up. The hotel lamp came on. Its yellow light cut across her face.

'How bad?'

'Cardiac. Intensive care.'

'Are you going?'

He had not asked himself that yet. The word going opened too many doors at once: visa, flights, company notice, Sarah, the hospital corridor, Anya waiting with a phone in her hand, Olesya behind a door he had no right to enter and every claim to pay for.

'I don't know.'

Sarah closed her eyes once, then opened them.

'Is Anya alone?'

'Yes.'

'Then treat her as the person in front of you.'

'I am sending money.'

'That is not what I said.'

He looked at the table. Four cables. Two devices. The old notepad full of numbers. The ceramic dish holding Sarah's earring back.

'It is what I can do from here.'

Sarah was silent.

From London came the distant sound of a door closing in a corridor, someone rolling luggage over carpet, the hotel continuing around her.

'What did the money do?' she asked.

He looked up.

'What?'

'Before hospital. What did it do?'

He did not answer.

'Thomas.'

'Debt collectors.'

The word entered the room and stayed there.

'How much?'

'I don't know.'

'But your money?'

'Yes.'

Sarah pressed her hand over her mouth, then lowered it.

'And now?'

'Now I am sending more.'

Neither of them spoke for a while.

'I have to go,' he said.

'No,' Sarah said. 'You have to decide what going means.'

He ended the call before the line could ask anything else of him.

Anya wrote at 06:12.

They let me see her for one minute.

How is she?

She has tubes. She did not open eyes.

Did doctor speak?

Morning doctor comes eight.

Do you have money for deposit?

Some.

How much more?

Anya sent a number.

Thomas opened the secondary account and moved the remaining balance out. Then he took a cash advance from the credit card, accepted the fee, accepted the interest warning, accepted the message that this would accrue immediately. The app asked for confirmation. He gave it.

The number in the secondary account went close to zero.

He sent the money through Murad in two pieces and a bank deposit directly to Anya's card. The fees looked grotesque by then, too large to trust, too regular to be accidental. He stopped calculating them.

At eight, the office called.

He let it ring.

At eight twelve, the project manager messaged:

Supplier meeting moved to 09:00. Need you.

Thomas wrote:

Unavailable.

Medical emergency.

The project manager replied with a thumbs-up and no question.

That, too, was a kind of mercy.

At 08:43 Anya sent a photograph of a hospital payment receipt.

Deposit.

Private wing pending.

Doctor says wait.

He opened the photograph. The receipt had Anya's name printed as payer. Olesya's name as patient. The amount. The stamp. The hospital address. Everything the systems had wanted for months appeared on one page now, when it could do least.

He saved it.

For a file name he typed:

Hospital_Deposit_Anya.

Then he stopped.

The cursor blinked after Anya.

He deleted the whole thing and named the file:

Receipt.

At ten, Sarah messaged from London.

Any change?

He wrote:

Deposit paid. Waiting on doctor.

She replied:

Keep me informed.

He placed the phone beside the laptop.

Anya wrote:

Thank you.

Then:

They ask if relative will sign.

Who?

For consent.

You are daughter.

I am eighteen but they ask where husband.

Thomas read the line twice.

Where husband.

There it was, in a hospital corridor under greenish light, asked by a clerk behind glass, the same missing field from every failed form. The category no money had filled. The blank he had kept blank and called mercy, balance, privacy, equality, anything except absence.

He typed:

Can you sign?

She answered:

They say yes if no one else.

Then sign.

The ticks turned blue.

No reply.

At 10:27 she sent one word.

Signed.

Thomas sat in the Delhi flat with the air-conditioning running and the curtains half drawn against the morning heat. Outside, the compound had dried in patches. Gardeners swept leaves into wet heaps. A woman from the opposite block crossed the path carrying flowers in a plastic bucket, their stems wrapped in newspaper darkened by water.

His spreadsheet remained open.

Month.

Amount sent.

Fee.

Amount received.

Confirmation.

He moved the cursor to the next blank row.

For category, he typed Medical.

Then he deleted it.

He left the cell empty.

Chapter 20

The empty cell remained on the spreadsheet until evening.

Thomas worked around it.

He answered the supplier meeting by email. He approved a revised inspection schedule. He rejected a valve substitution for the second time and copied the project manager so there would be no further negotiation. He moved through the day with the spreadsheet open behind everything, its blank category visible each time he minimised a window.

Month.

Amount sent.

Fee.

Amount received.

Confirmation.

Category.

The last cell waited.

Medical had been too small.

Debt was too large.

Olesya was not a category.

At 18:40 Anya wrote:

Doctor says morning maybe better.

Then:

They let me sit near nurses.

Thomas typed:

Have you eaten?

After a delay:

Tea and bread.

Buy proper food.

No appetite.

He deleted his answer three times. The line he finally sent was practical and worse for it.

Keep receipts.

The reply came after eleven minutes.

Ok.

He closed the app and sat looking at the glass balcony door. Delhi had turned brown with evening dust. The gardeners had left the hoses coiled beside the path, loops of wet green plastic on concrete. In the opposite block, a woman shook a tablecloth over the rail and crumbs fell into the shrubbery below. The compound lights came on too early, pale against the last light in the sky.

The office sent another message.

Can you join fifteen minutes?

He wrote:

No.

Then he put the phone on silent.

Sarah called at ten from London.

Her hair was tied back. The hotel lamp lit only one side of her face. Behind her, the room had settled into the anonymous order of a place serviced by people she would never meet. White pillows. White duvet. A glass of water on a coaster. Shoes placed beside the chair.

'Any change?'

'Morning maybe better.'

'Maybe.'

'Anya is still there.'

'Alone?'

'Yes.'

Sarah looked away from the screen. When she came back, her voice had the careful flatness he had heard only a few times in their marriage, a flatness that did not soften itself to make room for him.

'And Olesya?'

'No direct update.'

'Has she spoken?'

'Not yet.'

'Can she?'

'I don't know.'

Sarah drew the duvet over her knees.

'I changed my flight,' she said.

'To when?'

'Tomorrow night.'

'You don't need to.'

'No. I don't.'

He could see the answer she did not say. She was not coming back for the hospital. She was coming back for the flat in Delhi, for the table, for whatever was sitting there now with its wires showing.

'I can manage,' he said.

'That sentence has done enough work.'

He looked at the screen. She did not look tired now. The hotel light had hardened her face into lines he knew and did not know.

'Sleep if you can,' she said.

'You too.'

The call ended.

Thomas returned to the spreadsheet.

In the empty category cell, he typed:

Hospital.

Then he deleted it.

He left the cell empty again.

At 02:09, Anya wrote:

She opened eyes.

Thomas sat up.

Did she speak?

No. Nurse said rest.

Does she know you are there?

She looked.

The line sat on the screen with no decoration. She looked. A whole corridor had passed into two words. The chair. The closed door. The green light. Anya standing where staff told her to stand. Olesya's eyes opening to the daughter who had signed where husband should have been.

Thomas typed:

Tell her I am here.

He deleted it.

Tell her I sent everything.

He deleted that too.

Tell her I am thinking of her.

He put the phone down.

The sentence had nowhere to go.

In the morning, Olesya sent a message from her own phone.

Home soon.

It arrived at 09:16, whilst Thomas was standing at the kitchen counter with coffee cooling beside his hand. The words appeared under months of transactions, payout confirmations, redirections, receipts, hospital deposits, all of it collapsing into two words that gave him less than a nurse had given Anya.

He answered:

Are you able to write?

After eight minutes:

Short.

How are you?

Tired.

What did doctor say?

Rest. Medicine. No stress.

The old instinct rose in him, quick and orderly.

Send full list. I will cover private nurse, medication, follow-up visits. Anything needed.

The ticks turned blue.

No reply.

At noon Anya wrote:

Doctor says maybe tomorrow discharge.

So soon?

She wants home.

Is that allowed?

They say if sign and buy medicine.

Who signs?

She signs.

Can she?

Anya did not answer.

At two, the private wing sent a receipt for another deposit and a discharge estimate. The paperwork had moved faster once money entered through a visible account. Olesya's name, Anya's signature, hospital stamp, payment time. Everything clear now. Everything legible when the event had already broken its skin.

Thomas paid.

Murad took his fee.

Anya bought the medicines.

Olesya went home the next afternoon.

The photograph Anya sent showed the flat entrance from inside. The metal door stood open. A plastic pharmacy bag hung from the handle. On the floor beside it, two pairs of shoes: Anya's white trainers and Olesya's black flats, placed neatly but too far from the wall. The corridor beyond was dim. A neighbour's laundry hung from a line and blocked part of the light.

No one appeared in the frame.

Home, Anya wrote.

Thomas enlarged the photograph and looked at the black flats.

The next three days were instructions.

Medicine morning.

Medicine night.

Blood pressure?

Send reading.

Doctor follow-up when?

Need food delivered.

No visitors.

Rest.

He created a recovery sheet and shared it with Anya through a link. She did not open it. He pasted the schedule into an email. She replied:

I know.

He sent a private nurse contact found through an expatriate forum in Baku. The nurse spoke Russian, charged in dollars, and asked for payment in advance.

Olesya answered from her own phone.

No nurse.

You need help.

No stranger in flat.

Professional nurse.

No.

You need monitoring.

Anya can.

She should be at school.

She is here.

That line ended the exchange.

Thomas sat with his hand on the mouse. The recovery sheet stayed open, its tidy boxes empty of the only people it concerned.

Sarah returned from London on the fourth night after discharge.

The driver brought her from the airport close to midnight. She entered quietly, wheeling the suitcase herself, coat over one arm, hair loosened by travel. The flat had the dry, over-cooled smell of rooms kept closed too long. Thomas had not turned on the hall lamp, and the city light from the balcony cut the floor into dull rectangles.

'You're awake,' she said.

'Yes.'

'I thought you might be.'

She left the suitcase by the door and went straight to the bedroom. A minute later water ran in the bathroom. He heard the drawer open where she kept her face cream, the lid turn, the small domestic sequence resuming without asking permission.

When she came back, she had changed into cotton trousers and a loose shirt. She looked younger with her face washed, and more remote.

'How is she?'

'Home.'

'That is not the same as better.'

'No.'

Sarah sat opposite him at the dining table. She saw the laptop, the recovery sheet, the open messages with Anya, the notepad with hospital amounts and debt numbers. She did not ask to see. He did not close anything.

'And the debt?'

'Still there.'

'How much?'

'No firm number.'

'That means large.'

'It means uncontrolled.'

Sarah folded her hands on the table. Her wedding ring had turned slightly, stone inward.

'What are you going to do?'

'Find the total.'

'And then?'

'Clear it.'

'With what?'

He looked at the notepad.

'I can restructure.'

Sarah's mouth moved once without sound.

'That word.'

'There are ways.'

'There are always ways with money until there aren't.'

He did not answer.

Sarah leaned back. The chair made a small sound against the floor.

'You are not sleeping, you are sending money through men you do not know, and now a young woman is signing hospital forms alone.'

'I know.'

'Do you?'

The question had no heat. It did not need any.

He looked at the screen.

'Remote support has failed.'

Sarah watched him.

'Say that again.'

'Remote support has failed.'

'Support.'

He recognised the trap in the word only after he had stepped into it. Support was clean. Support was transfers, nurses, fees, receipts, recovery schedules. Support could be archived.

'Provision,' he said.

'No.'

He waited.

Sarah stood and went to the kitchen for water. She took a bottle from the fridge, poured half into a glass, then stood with one hand on the counter. Her shoulders had dropped with the flight.

'What you are describing is a household with no house,' she said.

The line carried no blade. Its fatigue entered him.

She drank the water and set the glass down.

'I'm going to bed.'

'Sarah.'

She stopped.

'Not tonight.'

She went to the bedroom and closed the door.

Thomas stayed at the table.

A household with no house.

The phrase sat beside the spreadsheet and changed the shape of it.

He opened Olesya's messages.

How much total debt?

No answer.

He wrote again:

Need full number to clear. No judgement.

The reply came the next morning.

It does not clear.

Everything clears if total known.

No.

Send the number.

After twenty minutes:

You don't understand local.

Then explain.

They add.

Then we pay principal.

Already paid principal.

Interest then.

We negotiate.

Who we?

He read the question in the lift on the way down to the car.

The lift smelled of cleaning fluid and wet umbrellas. Someone had pressed all the buttons and wiped them with a damp cloth badly enough to leave streaks. His reflection appeared in the metal doors, divided by the join. Work shirt, laptop bag, phone in hand.

Who we?

The lift opened.

The driver stood by the car with the rear door open. Heat came up from the paving, carrying the smell of watered soil and diesel. Thomas got in and shut the door.

He typed:

I will handle it.

Olesya answered:

From Delhi?

He did not reply.

At the office, Delhi had already moved on. The supplier meeting had been rescheduled. The Stavanger engineer had arrived and wanted to visit the yard. A local manager came in with tea and a stack of printouts, smiling with the polite insistence of a man asking for decisions. Thomas made them. He initialled the drawings. He caught one error in a pressure rating and circled it with a red pen. The room around him accepted this version of him without question.

At lunch, he ate two spoonfuls of dal in the canteen and left the rest. Men at the next table discussed a cricket match with the full seriousness of technical arbitration. Someone laughed. A ceiling fan pushed warm air down over the steel trays.

His phone vibrated.

Olesya:

Anya missed school again.

He wrote:

She can return when you are stable.

No. She should not live like nurse.

Then accept nurse.

No.

Then what?

No answer.

The question remained under the last line.

Then what?

In the afternoon, the regional director called him in.

The office overlooked a road lined with trees covered in dust. From that height, the traffic had a pattern, though not a kind one. Cars, bikes, buses, rickshaws, each entering gaps that closed at once. The director was a broad Norwegian with pale eyelashes and a habit of tapping paper against the desk before speaking.

'We need to discuss your next placement.'

Thomas sat.

'Delhi is closing faster than expected.'

'How fast?'

'Six weeks for your scope, maybe eight for handover. Stavanger wants you back through Oslo first. There is a controls package in Hammerfest that needs adult supervision.'

Thomas looked at the paper.

'Norway.'

'Yes.'

'Permanent?'

'Nothing is permanent, but longer than Delhi. Sarah will like it, I think.'

The director smiled, not knowing what he had touched.

'Company housing?'

'Initially. Then you know how it works.'

'Visa sponsorship for dependants?'

'For Sarah, standard. HR will manage.'

Dependants.

The word came cleanly from the man across the desk, one of those office words that opened doors when placed beside the right name. Sarah. Standard. HR will manage.

'And local hiring?' Thomas asked.

'In Norway?'

'For admin support. Document control. Project office.'

The director frowned a little.

'Depends on budget. Why?'

'I know someone with project admin background in Baku.'

'Azerbaijan?'

'Yes.'

'For Hammerfest?'

'Potentially remote first, then relocation if needed.'

The director tapped the paper again.

'You would need HR, business case, salary band, qualifications. Norway is not India. You know that.'

'Yes.'

'Language?'

'English. Russian. Azerbaijani.'

'Norwegian?'

'No.'

'Then skilled category would be difficult unless the role is specialised.'

Specialised.

The word entered the room with a handle on it.

'Document control on international projects can be specialised,' Thomas said.

'Can be. Usually isn't.'

'If tied to Caspian vendor history, Russian documentation, legacy packages.'

The director watched him more closely.

'You are thinking ahead.'

'We will need support during handover.'

'Put it in a note. No promises.'

'Yes.'

The soft-pattern scan in his head caught the phrase too late. In the room it sounded ordinary, a man agreeing to a process.

He left with the paper folded in his hand.

In the car, he searched Norwegian skilled worker visa requirements.

The official immigration page loaded slowly through the phone signal. Blue and white, with language options at the top. Requirements sat in plain blocks.

Job offer.

Full-time position.

Pay and working conditions according to Norwegian standards.

Relevant qualifications.

Residence permit.

Family immigration.

He opened each page and saved it.

By the time the car reached the compound, the idea had begun arranging itself with the old obedience.

No more brokers.

No more men outside pharmacies.

No more Anya signing where another field had been left open.

A company role. A legal visa. Salary paid into a Norwegian bank account. Healthcare. Schooling or training for Anya if she came later. Debt negotiated from a place debt collectors could not reach. Not a transfer. A relocation.

A house.

He walked into the flat with the idea still incomplete enough to shine.

Sarah was on the balcony with a cup of tea. The evening had cooled slightly. A faint breeze moved the leaves of the compound trees and carried up the smell of fried onions from somewhere beyond the wall.

'Norway,' he said.

She turned.

'I know. HR emailed.'

'Six to eight weeks.'

'I saw.'

'Hammerfest first.'

'That part is bleak.'

He stood in the doorway.

'There may be a way to bring Olesya into the project.'

Sarah did not move.

Below them, a child crossed the path on a scooter, one foot pushing, one foot balanced. The wheels made a small plastic rattle over the paving.

'Say that carefully,' Sarah said.

'Document control. Caspian experience. Russian-language vendor records. Project handover.'

'A job.'

'A real job.'

'Created by you.'

'Justified by need.'

'For her.'

'For the project.'

Sarah looked out over the compound again.

'And Anya?'

'Later.'

'Later is where you put the hard part.'

He took the line and set it aside. Not discarded. Stored. The idea had too much momentum now.

'If Olesya is in Norway, the debt loses access.'

'Does it?'

'It loses proximity.'

'And what does she become there?'

'Employed.'

'No.'

He waited.

Sarah turned back to him.

'What does she become to you there?'

The question touched the same blank field as every form.

'Present,' he said.

Sarah closed her eyes once. When she opened them, the child below had reached the gate and was turning the scooter round with careful little steps.

'That is not an answer.'

'It is the only one I have.'

'Then it is not enough.'

She went inside, leaving the tea on the balcony ledge.

Thomas stayed outside until the cup stopped steaming.

That night he opened a new folder.

Norway.

Inside it, he created subfolders.

Role.

Visa.

Salary.

Debt.

Medical.

Anya.

He stared at the list, then renamed Anya to Family.

The folder looked more complete that way.

He began with the role.

The job description had to be specific enough to pass, but not so specific that HR would see the person before the vacancy. He wrote the first line, deleted it, wrote another.

Project document controller with Russian and Caspian vendor documentation experience.

He opened Olesya's old CV from Baku. It was six years out of date. The formatting had shifted, leaving bullet points floating under the wrong headings. Reception. Office assistant. Document filing. Vendor correspondence. English intermediate. Russian native. Azerbaijani fluent. No degree listed. No certifications.

He copied the relevant lines into a blank document and rewrote them in the language HR would recognise.

Vendor documentation support.

Multilingual project records.

Cross-border administrative liaison.

He stopped at liaison.

The word smelled like perfume over a stain.

He replaced it with coordinator.

Then he opened salary thresholds.

The official page gave figures in kroner. Skilled worker with bachelor's degree. Skilled worker without degree. Collective agreement. Normal pay for occupation. Full-time. Employer statement.

The numbers were higher than he expected.

He converted them to dollars, then to manat, then back to kroner. The conversions did nothing except give his hands something to do.

At 23:48 Olesya wrote:

Doctor says no stress. How to have no stress?

He looked at the Norway folder.

I may have a solution.

What solution?

Work route.

Where?

Norway.

The typing mark appeared, vanished, appeared again.

For me?

Yes.

What work?

Project documents. Your languages. My company.

Can Anya come?

Not at first. Maybe later.

Then no.

He sat back.

Read the whole idea first.

I read.

It is legal route.

Without Anya no.

We need to establish you first.

I cannot leave her with men here.

Then we solve debt first.

You said debt does not clear.

We negotiate from outside.

Who we?

The question returned. Smaller this time. Sharper.

He typed:

I will make the structure.

Olesya replied:

I need life, Thomas. Not structure.

He read it once.

Then he copied it into the Norway folder, into a note named Risks.

For the next hour he did not touch the role description.

He opened the debt note.

Principal.

Interest.

Collectors.

School threat.

Flat threat.

Hospital trigger.

He tried to arrange the entries in order. They would not stay there. Each line pulled the others crooked. The principal belonged to years he had not seen. Interest belonged to men who could add numbers faster than he could transfer them. School threat belonged to Anya. Hospital trigger belonged to Olesya's body, to the sealed door, to the wet corridor floor and the pharmacy counter.

He wrote:

Need legal settlement route.

Then:

Need third-party negotiator.

Then:

Need relocation before further contact.

He stopped at before further contact. It sounded clinical enough to pass inside a file and monstrous enough to show him its teeth.

Olesya wrote again near one.

You think Norway clean.

He typed:

It is safer.

She answered:

For who?

He held the phone without replying.

The question had layers. For her, leaving a flat where men could knock. For Anya, left behind at first. For Sarah, moved into a country where a second life might acquire paperwork. For him, no longer sending money through corridors and brokers and favours. Each answer cancelled another.

He wrote:

For the next step.

No answer came.

He returned to the immigration page and read the same paragraphs until the words lost their edges.

Relevant qualifications.

Pay and working conditions.

Employer responsibility.

Police certificate.

Passport.

Passport.

The word had stopped being an object and become a wall. Olesya had one. She would not want to hand it to him, to HR, to an office in Norway, to any system that could store her face and number in a database. After the hospital, after the collectors, after every request for documents had carried a cost, he was building a plan that began with the same demand.

He opened a blank message.

You will need passport scan for visa.

He did not send it.

At 01:22, Anya emailed.

Mr Thomas,

Mother sleeping. She says I must go school tomorrow. I do not know if I can leave her. The medicine makes her dizzy. The neighbour can check sometimes. I attached receipt.

A.

The attachment was a pharmacy receipt folded in half. Tablets. Ampoules. Syringes. Blood pressure strips. A handwritten item at the bottom, added after the total, with a price beside it and no printed code.

Thomas wrote:

Do not miss school if neighbour can sit with her.

Then he deleted the line.

He wrote:

What do you need to return to school tomorrow?

Anya answered:

Sleep.

He sat back.

From the bedroom came no sound. Sarah had turned off her lamp hours earlier. The flat held the dry, artificial chill of the air-conditioning and the faint smell of airport clothes from her open suitcase. In the hall, the suitcase still stood half unpacked, one wheel leaving a grey mark on the tile.

Sleep could not be transferred.

He sent money anyway.

The next morning he called a Norwegian immigration adviser from the car, a woman whose website had a photograph of a fjord behind her head and a booking calendar that charged by the half hour.

'This is preliminary,' he said.

'Everything is preliminary until the documents arrive,' she said.

Her accent was clipped, practical. He could hear a keyboard beneath her voice.

'Candidate is Azerbaijani resident. Project administration background. Russian documentation, Caspian vendor records. Potential role in northern Norway.'

'Degree?'

'No.'

'Formal vocational qualification?'

'No.'

'Years of experience?'

'Several.'

'Documented?'

'Some.'

'Norwegian employer?'

'Yes.'

'Salary?'

'Can be set to threshold.'

'Role must be genuine.'

'It will be.'

She paused.

'The authorities look at whether the job was created for the person.'

'Many jobs are created for a person with needed skills.'

'Then the need must be stronger than the person's name.'

He wrote the sentence down.

Need stronger than name.

'Family member?' she asked.

'No.'

'Partner?'

'No.'

'Any relationship to sponsor?'

The driver changed lanes and the horn from a bus filled the car. Thomas waited until it passed.

'Known professional contact.'

'Then disclose carefully if asked.'

'If asked?'

'They will ask.'

The line clicked with another call trying to come through. The office. He ignored it.

'What about dependent child?' he asked.

'For the candidate?'

'Yes.'

'Minor?'

'Eighteen.'

'Then not child in the usual family immigration sense. Separate basis required. Study, work, possibly other route. It depends.'

Separate basis required.

He wrote that down too.

'And medical history?'

'Not usually central unless it affects capacity for the role.'

'Recent hospitalisation?'

'Then you will need to be careful. A role that starts immediately after serious illness may raise questions from employer and candidate.'

'Questions are manageable.'

'Some are. Some reveal the application.'

The adviser gave him a list of documents. Passport. CV. Employment references. Education records. Job description. Salary statement. Housing plan. Health insurance. Police certificate. Application fee. Appointment at visa centre.

Each item placed another demand on Olesya's table in Baku.

'Put nothing informal in writing,' the adviser said.

'What do you mean?'

'No personal promises inside the employment process. No private support language. No suggestion that the job exists for rescue.'

Rescue.

He did not write that word down.

After the call, he sat in the parked car outside the office while the driver waited with both hands on the wheel.

'Sir?'

'One minute.'

The driver nodded and looked forward again.

Thomas added a new file to the Norway folder.

Immigration Adviser Notes.

Then he made another file.

Separate Route for Anya.

He did not open it.

The next morning, he requested a meeting with HR.

The HR manager in Delhi was a woman named Priya who wore steel-framed glasses and kept two phones on her desk, one face up, one face down. Her office smelled faintly of cardamom and printer heat. A stack of onboarding forms sat to her left, clipped by colour. On the wall behind her, a poster described company values in words no one used out loud.

'You want to create a role in Norway?' she said.

'Explore feasibility.'

'For someone already identified?'

'Potential candidate.'

'Internal?'

'External.'

'Nationality?'

'Azerbaijani resident.'

Priya made a note.

'Education?'

'Experience-based.'

'Degree?'

'No.'

Another note.

'Norwegian language?'

'No.'

'EU passport?'

'No.'

The notes grew smaller.

'Business justification?'

'Caspian project handover and Russian-language vendor document recovery.'

'Duration?'

'Twelve months minimum.'

'Budget owner?'

'My project.'

'Reporting line?'

He paused.

'To document control lead.'

'Not you?'

'No.'

Priya looked up.

For the first time in the meeting, she stopped writing.

'Thomas, if this is a real project need, we can examine it. If this is a personal relocation request dressed as a role, Norway will kill it in the first review.'

The room did not change. The air-conditioning hummed. A printer started somewhere beyond the glass. On Priya's desk, the face-up phone lit and went dark.

'It is a project need,' he said.

She held his eyes a second longer.

'Then write it like one.'

She gave him a checklist.

Role description.

Organisation chart.

Budget approval.

Salary justification.

Candidate CV.

Qualification evidence.

Immigration assessment.

Conflict declaration.

He took the paper.

Conflict declaration sat at the bottom.

'Standard,' Priya said.

'For all external hires?'

'For hires with prior relationship to staff.'

'What counts as prior relationship?'

'Anything that would make a reasonable person ask why this candidate.'

He folded the paper once, carefully.

'I see.'

The reply came too quickly.

Priya nodded.

'Send me the role first.'

Back at the flat that evening, Sarah found the checklist on the table.

She read it without touching it.

'Conflict declaration,' she said.

'Standard.'

'Isn't that the point?'

'I can manage disclosure.'

'Can you?'

He closed the laptop.

'I need the role to exist first.'

'You need many things to exist first.'

'Sarah.'

She looked at him across the table. There was no raised voice in her. That would have been easier.

'Does she want this?'

'She wants Anya protected.'

'That is not what I asked.'

'She will, if she sees the route.'

'There it is.'

'What?'

'Route.'

She went to the bedroom and returned with her laptop. She sat at the far end of the table and opened it. For an hour they worked in the same room without speaking. Her screen held flight options and housing links for Norway. His held immigration pages, salary thresholds, and Olesya's old CV.

Two futures built side by side.

Neither one named the other.

Close to midnight, Sarah shut her laptop.

'I am going to sleep.'

'All right.'

At the bedroom door she stopped.

'Do not put me in a form, Thomas.'

He looked up.

'What?'

'Whatever this becomes. Do not make me a category in it.'

She closed the door.

Thomas stayed with the official immigration page open.

Job offer.

Full-time position.

Pay and working conditions.

Relevant qualifications.

The page used plain language. That was part of its force. It did not know Olesya. It did not know Anya on the hospital stairs, or Sarah at the balcony, or the broker's fee, or the shoe beyond the door. It asked only for the criteria.

Criteria could be met.

He opened the role description again and placed the cursor beneath the heading.

Purpose of position.

The cursor waited.

Chapter 21

The cursor waited beneath Purpose of position until the Delhi assignment ended.

Thomas wrote around it for six weeks.

He built the role in clean pieces. Vendor records. Russian-language document recovery. Caspian package history. Handover support for legacy files that had entered the Norway project under poor naming conventions and worse scans. He wrote the need without writing Olesya. He wrote the skills without writing the flat door, the hospital stairs, the debt papers folded beneath a mattress in Baku.

Each sentence had to stand without her.

That was the first test.

Priya returned the draft twice.

Too candidate-specific.

Then:

Need stronger business justification.

Then:

Clarify reporting line independent from originator.

Originator.

He let the word sit in the document track changes for an hour before accepting it.

By the time the pack reached Stavanger HR, the Delhi flat was already half empty. Sarah had marked boxes with broad black tape. Books. Kitchen. Linen. Work. The relocation company wrapped plates in paper and placed them into cartons with more care than either of them had given the last month of meals. The housekeeper came on the final morning and wiped surfaces that would be cleaned again after they left.

At the hall table, Sarah checked passports and flight documents.

'Norway will be quiet,' she said.

'Yes.'

'You make quiet sound like a task.'

He looked up from his laptop.

'It might be.'

She placed his passport on top of his boarding pass and slid both towards him.

'Do not lose that.'

'I won't.'

Her hand rested on the folder with the Norway housing papers. Then she lifted it away and went to the kitchen, where the fridge stood open and empty except for one bottle of water no one wanted to carry.

The company house in Stavanger had glass on three sides.

It stood on a rise above a narrow road, the fjord visible between the shoulders of other houses. The roof was black, the walls pale, the windows tall enough to make the sky feel like another room. Everything had been measured. The gravel path held no loose stones. The front door sealed without a sound. Inside, underfloor heating rose through pale boards. Cupboards closed softly. The oven displayed the time before anyone had set it.

Norway did not flicker.

The first morning, Thomas woke before Sarah and walked through the house in socks. Outside, dawn came thin and blue over the water. The fjord lay like metal under the low cloud, no traffic noise reaching it, no horns, no generator, no dogs behind walls. A ferry moved across the distance with lit windows and no hurry. On the slope below the house, grass bent under frost.

His phone showed one message from Olesya.

Cold today.

He looked at the underfloor thermostat, set to twenty-two.

Send medicine list update, he wrote.

No reply.

Sarah came downstairs later in a wool jumper and bare feet, carrying two mugs.

'The view is indecent,' she said.

He took the coffee.

'It is good.'

'That was not what I said.'

She stood beside him at the glass. Their reflections hung over the fjord, close enough to belong to each other.

'HR emailed you?' she asked.

'Stavanger?'

'About the house forms.'

'Yes.'

'And your other forms?'

He did not answer immediately.

Sarah looked at the water.

'I am asking plainly.'

'Role is with HR.'

'For Olesya.'

'For the project.'

'Thomas.'

He kept his eyes on the ferry until it passed behind a headland.

'For both.'

Sarah nodded once, not agreement. A mark made in private.

On his first day at the Stavanger office, the reception desk gave him a visitor badge even though he was returning staff. The badge printed his name, company, date, time, and access level. A woman behind glass smiled and pointed him towards the lifts. The lobby smelled of wet wool and coffee. Employees hung coats in a line of identical lockers. Boots stood on black mats beneath them, paired and clean, each toe facing out.

The building made order look moral.

His desk overlooked the harbour. Cranes moved with slow precision over supply vessels. Yellow containers sat in rows. Men in high-visibility jackets walked along marked lanes and stopped at crossings even when no vehicle came. Beyond the port, water darkened under a cloud.

At nine, the HR meeting began.

Stavanger HR had three people on the call. An HR business partner named Ingrid, a compliance officer named Mads, and a document control manager from Hammerfest who joined with his camera off. Thomas sat in a small glass meeting room with a notepad, a role description, and Olesya's CV printed without photograph.

'We have reviewed the proposed role,' Ingrid said.

Her English was precise and unhurried. She had grey hair cut to the jaw and a silver watch with a narrow face.

'There may be a limited business case for language support on the legacy Caspian records. Limited. The weak point is role duration and qualification.'

'The records will affect handover for at least twelve months.'

'That may justify consultancy, not relocation.'

'Consultancy from Baku exposes us to the same document control gaps.'

Mads wrote something. His pen moved with the care of a man who expected notes to matter later.

'Candidate has no formal document control certification,' Ingrid said.

'Experience-based.'

'Evidence?'

'Project records from Baku office. Vendor correspondence. Internal file history.'

'References?'

'I can obtain.'

Mads looked up.

'From you?'

'From prior managers.'

'Are they still with the company?'

'Some.'

'Then use them. Not yourself.'

The document control manager spoke from the black square.

'Russian package recovery could help. I have three boxes of poor scans and no one wants to touch them.'

Thomas kept his face still.

The black square remained after he spoke, his name printed beneath it in white letters. No face. No room. Only the voice of a man somewhere north of them, nearer the work that had given Thomas the opening. For a few seconds the role existed cleanly. A need. A box of records. A language gap. A woman in Baku who could read what others could not.

Ingrid turned a page.

'Before we go further, relationship declaration.'

Mads slid a form across the table. It had already been printed. The company logo sat at the top. The paper was heavier than normal office stock.

Declaration of prior relationship or conflict of interest.

Thomas read the first line.

I declare that the proposed candidate is known to me in the following capacity.

The room waited.

'Standard process,' Ingrid said.

'Yes.'

'Complete it before we assess immigration.'

'What level of detail?'

Mads answered.

'Enough that a reviewer not connected to the project can assess whether the recruitment process is fair, whether there is undue influence, and whether any personal relationship creates company risk.'

Personal relationship.

The phrase sat in the same chair as all the others. Family. Dependent. Partner. Husband. Relation.

'Professional contact from Baku assignment,' Thomas said.

Mads did not blink.

'Put that in the form.'

Thomas picked up the pen.

The first word came easily.

Professional.

The second did not.

Contact.

He wrote both.

His handwriting looked smaller than usual.

After the meeting, he scanned the declaration and uploaded it to the HR portal. The file name was automatic.

COI_Declaration_THart.

He watched the upload bar fill.

In the afternoon, Olesya sent a photograph of her medicine packet. The brand name was Russian. The blister foil had been pressed through four times. Beside it lay a blood pressure monitor cuff and a plate with half a piece of bread.

Thomas wrote:

How is breathing today?

She answered:

Better.

Can you walk?

Kitchen.

Anya?

School.

Good.

The typing mark appeared and vanished.

Then:

Norway?

He looked through the glass wall of his meeting room. Outside, Ingrid walked past carrying a folder to a copier. Mads stood near the printer, talking to a man in a navy jumper. Each person moved inside a clear structure of task and permission.

In progress, Thomas wrote.

You need passport scan, CV, references, police certificate.

No.

The answer arrived before he had put the phone down.

Passport required.

No scan.

For visa.

No.

Can use secure portal.

No.

He sat back.

The office lights hummed softly above him. Beyond the harbour glass, rain began to move across the water, turning the surface to a grainy sheet.

Why not?

You know why.

He did.

Still, he typed:

Without documents there is no route.

After a long delay:

Then no route.

He closed the app and reopened the HR portal.

The declaration showed submitted.

That evening, Sarah unpacked the kitchen.

The house had more cupboards than they owned things. She placed plates where the relocation inventory suggested plates should go, glasses above the sink, pans in a drawer that slid open with a pressure so smooth it seemed insulting. On the counter, she lined up spices brought from Delhi, small jars with labels handwritten by a market seller who had insisted saffron was real.

'This place has no argument in it,' she said.

'What?'

'The house. Everything closes. Everything fits.'

He stood by the island with his laptop still under one arm.

'That's a complaint?'

'An observation.'

She opened another drawer. Empty.

'What did HR say?'

'Need documents.'

'And?'

'Olesya refuses passport scan.'

Sarah placed a stack of bowls on a shelf.

'That seems reasonable.'

'It blocks the process.'

'Both can be true.'

He set the laptop on the island.

'She wants protection without exposure.'

Sarah looked at him.

'Yes.'

The word closed more than it answered.

'That cannot work.'

'No.'

He waited for more, but Sarah had turned back to the cupboard. The bowls clicked softly against one another.

The next morning the municipality sent a letter addressed to both of them.

It arrived through the company relocation office in a white envelope with a transparent window and a return address printed in a font so plain it seemed beyond style. Sarah opened it with a kitchen knife, read the first page, and placed it beside his plate.

'Registration appointment,' she said.

'When?'

'Tuesday.'

The letter listed what they had to bring. Passport. Work contract. Housing confirmation. Marriage certificate. Tax number application. The documents were not requests in the way Baku documents had been requests, open to negotiation or delay. They were steps. Bring the thing, receive the next thing.

Sarah tapped the marriage certificate line.

'Do we have the original?'

'In the document wallet.'

'Where?'

'Study drawer.'

She stood and found it within a minute. The wallet held their life in plastic sleeves: passports, visas, marriage certificate, birth certificates neither of them used, tax records, vaccination cards, property papers, insurance policies. Sarah laid the marriage certificate on the table, smoothed it once with the side of her hand, then placed it back into its sleeve.

'Remarkably efficient, us on paper,' she said.

He looked at the certificate.

Names.

Date.

Place.

Witnesses.

A thing done once and still able to open doors years later.

'We need new passport photographs,' Sarah said.

'For what?'

'Registration office sometimes asks. The relocation woman said bring everything.'

'Fine.'

They went after lunch to a photo booth inside a small shopping centre near the harbour. Rain moved sideways along the street, making the pavement shine. Sarah held her coat closed at the throat. Inside, the booth smelled of warm dust and plastic curtain. She went first and came out with four photographs cut in a strip, each version of her face too white under the flash.

'Criminal,' she said, handing him the curtain.

He sat on the little stool and looked at the camera lens.

No smile.

Face forward.

Eyes open.

The machine flashed.

When the photographs printed, he held the strip beside Sarah's. Two official faces. Two people made acceptable by the same machine.

That evening, Olesya refused the passport scan again.

He had not asked badly. He had not pushed in the first line. He sent the official list from HR, reduced to its essentials, and wrote:

These are standard requirements. Everyone submits them.

She replied:

Everyone who trusts.

It is for work permit only.

Only is your word.

Norway is not Baku.

Men are men.

He put the phone down.

Across the room, Sarah was trimming their passport photographs with scissors after the booth cut one edge wrong. She placed the finished photographs in an envelope marked Registration. Her hands were steady. His own document strip lay untouched beside his laptop.

He opened Olesya's old CV again.

The photograph in the corner had been taken in Baku. She wore a white blouse and a black jacket, hair pulled back, chin lifted for a person behind a desk. The image was small and pixelated. Her eyes looked past the camera, not into it.

He enlarged it.

The face broke into squares.

He closed the file.

Over the next week, the role moved and did not move.

HR requested a revised job description.

He revised it.

Document control requested a sample of the Russian vendor records.

He sent three poor scans from an old Baku archive.

Compliance requested recruitment evidence.

He drafted a note on shortage of Russian-language project document specialists in northern Norway.

Finance requested budget approval.

He moved cost from contingency to project handover support.

Every request had a form. Every form had a place to put the answer. Norway accepted no darkness between boxes. If a fact existed, it had to sit under a heading. If it did not fit, it did not belong.

Olesya sent little.

Morning medicine.

BP 138.

Anya exam.

Neighbour came.

No answer to passport.

No answer to police certificate.

No answer to references.

Then, on a Wednesday, she sent:

Exit still blocked.

Thomas read it at his desk whilst snow began to fall outside the office. Not heavy snow. Light, dry flecks moving sideways in the harbour wind, vanishing against the glass. Men outside lifted their collars and kept walking.

What block?

Old debt. Border.

How much?

No answer.

Send number.

No answer.

He called.

She did not pick up.

He called Anya through the old email, then stopped before sending. The daughter had become a route too often.

At lunch he walked out to the harbour without a coat.

Cold entered through his shirt at once. The air smelled of salt, diesel, and wet rope. Gulls moved over the water without sound. A vessel backed away from the quay, its engines churning a pale wake. Everything on the dock had a line painted around it. Marked walking route. Loading area. Restricted access. Muster point.

He stood outside the marked lane until a port worker shouted something in Norwegian and pointed him back.

Thomas stepped behind the yellow line.

In the afternoon he called a debt lawyer in Baku whose number came through an old company driver.

The lawyer spoke English with a flat London edge learned somewhere expensive. He asked for documents. Thomas had none. He asked for the creditor name. Thomas had two names and a street rumour. He asked for court papers. Thomas had photographs sent by Anya and numbers changed by men who preferred phones to paper.

'Then you do not have debt,' the lawyer said. 'You have pressure.'

'Can pressure block exit?'

'If tied to court order, yes. If tied to tax, yes. If tied to private collection, not directly.'

'She says border is blocked.'

'Many people say border when they mean passport office, debt registry, police hold, or a reason not to approach the gate.'

'Can you check?'

'With power of attorney.'

'From her?'

'Yes.'

'She will not sign.'

'Then I cannot check.'

The same wall. Different country, different voice.

'What if the full debt is paid?'

'Full according to whom?'

Thomas had no answer.

The lawyer continued.

'If you pay private collectors without documents, you feed them. If you pay court debt, maybe clears. If there are penalties, separate. If passport block is administrative, separate again. You need documents.'

Documents.

He ended the call and wrote the word in his notebook.

Then he crossed it out until the paper tore.

He tried another route that evening.

Not the company. Not immigration. Not debt. A private consultant in Oslo who had once handled complicated relocations for oil workers from non-European jurisdictions. The consultant had a calm website, no photographs of people, only text and a phone number.

Thomas called from the study with the door closed.

'You are asking whether a person with debt can receive a residence permit,' the consultant said.

'I am asking what blocks it.'
