'The permit is one part. The employer is another. Exit from origin country is another. Debt enforcement is another. One person's need does not join them into one process.'

Thomas wrote:

Separate systems.

'If the employer is willing, can Norway approve?'

'Possibly. If the job is genuine and requirements met.'

'If origin country blocks exit?'

'Then Norway cannot make her leave.'

'If debt collectors are private?'

'Then they may not block legally.'

'May.'

'You need a local lawyer.'

'I have spoken to one.'

'Then listen to him.'

The consultant's voice gave no offence. It carried the bluntness of someone paid to remove fantasy early.

'Can family immigration apply after work permit?' Thomas asked.

'For spouse and children, yes, if conditions met.'

'Adult daughter?'

'Difficult.'

'Student route?'

'Possible if admitted, funded, documented.'

'So separate route.'

'Yes.'

'And if the mother will not come without her?'

The consultant paused.

'Then your immigration solution is not her solution.'

Thomas wrote the sentence down.

He did not save the note.

When he opened the study door, Sarah was in the hallway with a basket of laundry.

'I wasn't listening,' she said.

'I know.'

'I could still hear the shape of it.'

He stepped aside so she could pass.

'What shape?'

'You asking for one key. Everyone telling you there are different locks.'

She carried the basket upstairs.

He stood in the hall with the consultant's sentence still on the notepad.

Not her solution.

That night Olesya sent the number.

It arrived as a photograph, not text. A sheet of paper covered in rows of figures. Principal. Interest. Penalty. Legal fee. Enforcement fee. Storage fee. The words had been translated badly into English by someone or some app, leaving them stiff and wrong. At the bottom, a total sat in bold.

Thomas looked at it without moving.

The sum was not impossible in the abstract. It was impossible inside his life.

He could pay it by selling investments Sarah knew existed but did not track closely. He could take credit against the Norway house deposit before they purchased. He could move money from the joint account and explain it later. He could not do any of it quietly. Not at that scale. Not without creating a new debt in the visible life to feed the hidden one.

Olesya wrote:

This maybe not all.

Then:

They add.

He opened his financial files.

Norwegian account.

UK account.

Secondary account.

Credit card.

Joint savings.

Investment.

Each balance looked back at him with its own rule. Joint meant Sarah. Credit meant interest. Investment meant tax and records. Savings meant questions. The company salary could sustain payments. It could not swallow a decade of debt without leaving teeth marks.

Sarah came downstairs in a jumper and wool socks.

'What is it?'

He did not close the laptop.

She stood behind him and saw the number.

For a long time, neither of them spoke.

Outside, snow thickened against the glass, each flake visible for a second before the dark took it.

'Is that real?' she asked.

'Part of it.'

'Part.'

'There may be more.'

Sarah put one hand on the back of the chair. Her fingers pressed into the wood.

'Can you pay it?'

'Not without moving things.'

'Our things.'

He looked at the screen.

'Yes.'

She took her hand from the chair and folded both arms across her body.

'And if you pay it?'

'Then passport block may clear.'

'May.'

'I need documents to know.'

'And she will not give documents.'

'Not yet.'

'Thomas.'

He looked up.

Her face had gone pale in the window reflection, doubled over the fjord and falling snow.

'You cannot make a legal route out of people who will not be seen.'

He stood too quickly and the chair struck the table.

'She has reason not to trust systems.'

'Yes.'

'Then I have to build one she can trust.'

'With my money?'

The words landed plainly.

No drama. No raised voice. Only ownership entering a room where he had kept it out.

'With our resources,' he said.

Sarah's mouth tightened.

'There it is.'

'I did not mean it that way.'

'You did.'

She walked to the window and stood with her back to him. Snow gathered on the deck outside, covering the straight lines between boards.

'I am not refusing medicine,' she said. 'I am not refusing hospital. I am not refusing a child standing in a corridor. Do not make me that person.'

'I am not.'

'You are asking whether I will help buy your second household into existence.'

The house held the sentence.

Underfloor heating. Triple glass. Soft-closing cupboards. A view wide enough to make all human arrangements seem temporary and small.

Thomas sat down.

'I cannot leave her there.'

Sarah turned.

'You already did.'

He put both hands on the table.

The phone vibrated.

Olesya:

You see now.

He did not answer.

The next morning, compliance requested a formal background assessment before immigration work could continue.

Mads sent the email at 08:12.

Given proposed relocation and non-standard recruitment pathway, please provide consent for enhanced third-party screening of candidate, including debt registry, criminal record where available, sanctions, litigation, and politically exposed person checks.

Attach candidate consent and passport identification page.

Thomas read the email twice in the office kitchen while the coffee machine ground beans with a dry metallic rasp. A man beside him filled a bottle from the tap and nodded. Thomas nodded back.

Attach candidate consent.

Passport identification page.

Debt registry.

He forwarded the request to Olesya, removing the company names he could remove and leaving the parts that mattered.

Need consent for background check. Debt registry. Passport ID page. Required before visa.

Her answer came in pieces.

No.

They will see debt.

They must see to clear.

No.

Without this company cannot sponsor.

Then no company.

Olesya.

No.

He put the phone into his pocket and returned to his desk.

At ten, Ingrid called him into a meeting room. Mads was there already. No coffee. No papers except one folder between them.

'We have preliminary information,' Ingrid said.

Thomas sat.

Mads opened the folder.

'Candidate appears linked to significant unresolved civil debt and enforcement activity in Azerbaijan. We do not yet have consent for full verification, so we cannot establish scope.'

'Then wait for consent.'

'That is one issue,' Ingrid said.

'What is the other?'

Mads looked down at the folder, then back up.

'The role has a weak independent business case. Combined with prior relationship declaration, lack of formal qualification, unresolved debt indicators, and probable immigration complexity, our recommendation is to stop the requisition.'

The words were perfectly arranged.

Weak independent business case.

Prior relationship declaration.

Unresolved debt indicators.

Immigration complexity.

Stop the requisition.

No one in the room had raised a voice. No one had acted badly. The system had received the facts he was willing to give it and the facts it could find without him. It had assembled them into a result.

'The business case can be strengthened,' he said.

Ingrid shook her head once.

'Not enough.'

'The project needs the records.'

'Then we will source consultancy.'

'She can do the work.'

'That is not the full question.'

Mads closed the folder.

'There is also company risk if a sponsored employee arrives under financial pressure from unresolved collectors. It creates vulnerability. We cannot sponsor into that.'

Vulnerability.

The first word in the process that had come close to naming the person and still missed her.

'What if the debt is cleared?' Thomas asked.

'Documented clearance from recognised authority, not private collector, would change one factor,' Mads said. 'Not all.'

'If I obtain it?'

Ingrid leaned forward.

'Thomas, we are not asking you to obtain it. We are telling you this requisition should not proceed.'

The harbour behind her window was clean and grey. A vessel moved past with its deck lights on in daylight.

'Send the decision in writing,' he said.

'We will.'

He stood.

Mads spoke before he reached the door.

'You should also consider updating your conflict declaration if the relationship is more than professional.'

Thomas turned.

Mads did not soften the line. Ingrid looked at the folder.

'Is that a formal request?'

'Not at this stage.'

'Then put any request in writing.'

He left the room.

At his desk, the port carried on. Containers moved. Cranes lifted. Men crossed within painted lines. Every part of the system doing exactly what it had been built to do.

The written decision arrived at 12:46.

After review, requisition not approved for progression.

He saved it to the Norway folder.

Role.

Visa.

Salary.

Debt.

Medical.

Family.

The folders looked childish now.

At home, Sarah was not there. A message on the counter said she had gone for a walk. Her handwriting looked odd on the company notepad, too human for the kitchen. Outside, snow had stopped. The world had settled into a clarity that made every object stand apart from the next: deck chair, railing, water, stone, road, house.

Thomas sat at the dining table.

He opened the secure app.

Olesya's last message remained:

You see now.

He typed:

I cannot clear the debt.

Then he stopped.

The sentence was true and incomplete. He could not clear it without naming it, documenting it, bringing it into accounts Sarah could see, declarations HR could judge, forms Olesya would not sign, systems that would ask relation and receive nothing that could stand.

He added:

The visa is cancelled.

He looked at both lines.

I cannot clear the debt.

The visa is cancelled.

The fjord outside held its grey light. A gull landed on the deck railing, stayed only a second, then lifted away without sound.

Thomas pressed send.

Chapter 22

The message stayed on the screen after he sent it.

I cannot clear the debt.

The visa is cancelled.

The app gave him two grey ticks.

Delivered.

Thomas watched them until the phone dimmed in his hand. Outside, the fjord had turned from grey to black, and the last snow on the deck had hardened into a thin crust along the boards. The gull did not return. The railing held only a line of white where its feet had briefly marked the frost.

Sarah came back after seven.

He heard her key in the door, the pause whilst she took off her boots, the careful placement of them on the rubber mat. She had walked far enough for the cold to sit in her face. Her cheeks were raw. Her hair had flattened under the wool hat she carried in one hand.

'Did you send it?' she asked.

He looked up.

'Yes.'

She took off her coat and hung it in the hallway. Snowmelt darkened the hem.

'Did she answer?'

'No.'

Sarah came to the kitchen island and stood opposite him. The house lights reflected in the glass behind her, doubling the room, making a second Sarah stand faintly over the black fjord.

'What did you write?'

He turned the phone so she could see.

She read both lines.

Her mouth did not move.

'That is very little for what it ends,' she said.

'It does not end it.'

Sarah looked at him then.

'No?'

The phone vibrated on the table.

Olesya.

I know.

Two words.

He waited for more.

Nothing came.

Sarah saw the screen change. She stepped back from the island.

'I am making tea,' she said.

'Sarah.'

'Not now.'

She filled the kettle and stood with her back to him whilst it boiled. The kitchen was too quiet for a house with two people in it. Even the appliances seemed built to avoid disturbance. No rattle. No uneven compressor. No pipe knock in the wall. The kettle clicked off with one small sound and left the rest to them.

Olesya wrote again at 21:04.

You tried.

Thomas read the line and found no comfort in it. Tried belonged to failed machines and late payments, to men explaining why the result had not arrived. It had no place beside a hospital corridor, a debt paper, a daughter asked where husband was.

He typed:

There may be another way.

He deleted it.

He typed:

We can still clear part.

He deleted that too.

Sarah placed a mug beside him.

'Do not answer quickly,' she said.

He left the phone on the table.

In the morning, Olesya had written again.

I never expected you to clear it.

The message had arrived at 03:12. He found it whilst the sky over the fjord was still colourless and Sarah slept upstairs. The house held its warmth around him. Underfloor heat, sealed windows, a thermostat with a green light. He stood barefoot in the kitchen and read the line until the words separated.

I never expected you to clear it.

He answered:

Then why send the number?

The typing mark appeared, vanished, returned.

You asked.

He put the phone down.

The coffee machine ground beans. The sound filled the room with small violence. He placed a cup beneath the spout this time and watched the dark stream gather, obedient and hot.

Olesya sent:

You always ask for number.

He wrote:

Numbers can be solved.

Her reply came after a minute.

Not this.

Sarah came down in a grey jumper, hair tied back, feet in wool socks. She saw his face, then the phone.

'More?'

He nodded.

She poured coffee without asking him for one. Her hand moved slowly, not from sleep now, from caution.

'What is she saying?'

'That she never expected me to clear it.'

Sarah leaned against the counter.

'Did you?'

'Did I what?'

'Expect yourself to clear it.'

He looked at the water. The fjord sat under a low lid of cloud. On the road below, a cyclist passed in a yellow jacket, light blinking at the back of the helmet.

'I expected a route.'

Sarah closed her eyes briefly.

'Yes. You would.'

The phrase struck the soft scan in his head, absurdly, and then the moment moved past it.

'There is still money going out,' he said.

'Does she want it?'

'She needs it.'

'That is not the same.'

He opened the app again.

I will continue monthly support, he wrote.

Olesya did not answer for nearly an hour.

When she did, he was in the Stavanger office, sitting in a glass room with the door closed, watching snow move sideways across the harbour.

Keep.

One word.

He stared at it.

He typed:

For rent and medicine.

Keep.

You need recovery support.

No.

Olesya.

No.

He set the phone face down.

After lunch, she sent a photograph.

At first he thought it was a mistake. The image showed the corner of a room, badly lit, a cardboard box open on the floor. Inside were medicine packets, a folded cardigan, two cups wrapped in a towel, and the blood pressure cuff from the hospital photographs. The television wall appeared in the background, bare except for two hanging cables. On the windowsill stood a jar with coins in it and a dead plant in dry soil.

No caption.

He wrote:

What is this?

Packing.

For where?

No answer.

He enlarged the photograph. The box had once held cooking oil. The brand name ran across the side in red letters. Olesya had written MEDICINE on the flap in black marker, though the box held half the life of the flat.

He wrote:

Do not lift heavy things.

Anya lifts.

She has school.

Not today.

Why not?

Packing.

The answer did not accuse him. It did not need to. It placed Anya on the floor beside a box whilst he sat in a glass meeting room above the harbour, with a badge clipped to his belt and a calendar full of rooms reserved in his name.

He opened the recovery sheet he had once sent Anya.

Medicine morning.

Medicine night.

Blood pressure.

Rest.

No lifting.

No stress.

He closed it.

At three, Sarah messaged:

Do you want fish tonight?

He wrote:

Anything.

She replied:

That gives me nothing. I will buy fish.

When he came home, the kitchen smelled of butter, lemon, and the cold air that entered when Sarah opened the terrace door to cool the pan smoke. She had placed potatoes in a bowl and cut cucumbers with dill. The table was set for two.

'Before we eat,' he said.

Sarah turned from the stove.

'What now?'

'They are packing.'

The wooden spoon rested in her hand.

'Baku?'

'Yes.'

'To go where?'

'She won't say.'

Sarah turned off the stove and moved the pan from the heat.

'Then she has decided.'

'She is weak.'

'She may still have decided.'

'The debt men found the flat.'

'Then leaving the flat is not irrational.'

'Without telling me where?'

Sarah wiped her hand on a cloth and placed it beside the hob.

'Especially then.'

He looked at her.

'What does that mean?'

'It means she is removing the route.'

The words took a moment to enter him.

Sarah continued, quieter now.

'If the money brings the men, she has to break the path the money uses. That includes you.'

The fish cooled in the pan. Butter tightened around the edges.

'I am not the men.'

'No.'

'Then why treat me like them?'

Sarah took her time. She looked older in the kitchen light, not in the face exactly. In the way she stood without arranging herself for him.

'The path has the same shape from where she is standing.'

He picked up his phone.

Olesya had sent another photograph.

This one showed the hallway outside her flat. A suitcase stood near the door, old and soft at the corners. Beside it were two plastic shopping bags tied at the handles and a school backpack with a broken zip held closed by string. Anya's white trainers were on the threshold, toes pointing out.

Thomas wrote:

I can arrange proper transport.

No.

You cannot carry all this.

We carry.

Send address for pickup.

No.

Sarah watched him type and delete and type again.

'She is already answering you,' she said.

'She is refusing help.'

'She is refusing management.'

He looked at the phone.

The distinction was no use to him. It gave him nothing to do.

On the conference table in front of him lay the printed requisition decision from HR. After review, requisition not approved for progression. He had brought it to work without reason, folded into the back of his notebook. Now it sat there with the app beside it, two refusals in different languages.

At ten, the Hammerfest document control manager called.

'We are sourcing the Russian package recovery through consultancy,' he said. 'You know anyone legitimate?'

'No.'

'Unfortunate. Could have used that Baku person if compliance hadn't eaten it.'

'No?'

'No.'

'Fine.'

After the call, Thomas sat in the empty room with the harbour cranes moving behind the glass. A loader reversed below with a white light flashing on its roof. The office made its small, controlled sounds: door seals, keyboard taps, coffee cups on tables, the soft ventilation running through the ceiling.

His phone remained face down.

At lunch he went outside.

The harbour air entered his coat through every gap. Snow had become rain by then, needling across the quay. Workers moved in marked lanes, heads low, their reflective strips catching light even in daylight. Across the water, houses climbed the hill in pale blocks, each one with a roof pitched against weather, each one with windows lit from inside.

What do you mean keep?

No answer.

Are you leaving Baku?

No answer.

Do not move until we discuss.

He did not send it.

We owe. Also men came.

Yesterday.

No.

Mother says no.

She helps. Family.

The word sat on the screen. Family. It required no affidavit, no conflict declaration, no salary threshold, no visa criteria. It had a door in the north, a room with some space, perhaps a mattress on the floor, perhaps a stove, perhaps a woman who would not ask for an explanation before boiling water.

Knows what?

No reply.

He put the phone down and opened a map of Azerbaijan.

Baku on the coast.

Roads north.

Sumgait.

Quba.

Khachmaz.

Villages he had no memory of crossing, though he must have driven near some of them during site visits years before. The map gave him names, distances, travel times, fuel stations, border roads, little blue symbols for pharmacies and hospitals. It made the country look open. A line could be drawn from one place to another with the finger.

Olesya would not give him the line.

The line was factual. That gave it more weight.

Monthly support, full amount.

The next morning the transfer routes all failed.

Murad could not deposit to the old card. Cash pickup required a city and a name at a branch. Olesya would not provide either. The European platform remained closed. The bank transfer channel still demanded documentation. Crypto could move to Murad. Murad needed a recipient who would stand somewhere to receive it.

Thank you for hospital.

He looked at the line.

It carried no accusation. The absence made it harder. It was a receipt for an event already past.

The house kept the line overnight.

Thank you for hospital.

He saw it when he brushed his teeth, when he set the alarm, when he woke at 03:40 and reached for the phone before the room had settled into shape. It sat beneath unanswered questions and would not move. Hospital could be thanked. Transport could not be arranged. Debt could be counted. A location could be withheld.

At 04:12 Anya sent a photograph.

It was taken through a dirty bus window. Outside, the station lights spread yellow over wet concrete. Men stood smoking near the luggage compartment, collars raised, faces turned away from the camera. A woman in a padded coat held a sleeping child against her shoulder. In the reflection on the glass, Anya's face hovered over the scene, half-transparent, with Olesya beside her reduced to a dark shape and a hand on a bag.

Yes.

Which route?

No answer.

How is your mother?

Sleeping.

Medicine?

With me.

He wanted to write more. Do not let her sleep too long. Keep the tablets close. Watch the swelling in her feet. Send blood pressure when you stop. Tell the driver she is ill. Tell someone. Tell anyone. Each instruction lined up in him and found no right to cross.

He wrote:

Message when you arrive.

The ticks turned blue.

No answer.

At breakfast, Sarah saw him checking the phone between mouthfuls of toast he had not bitten.

'They left?'

'Bus.'

'North?'

'Yes.'

'Did she tell you where?'

'No.'

Sarah poured tea and sat down. Outside, the fjord had taken the first weak light. A ferry moved across it, windows lit, carrying people who had tickets, routes, arrivals.

'This may be the most information you get,' she said.

He looked at her.

'That is cruel.'

'No. It is plain.'

'It is not enough.'

'For you.'

He pushed the toast aside.

Sarah reached for the butter knife and aligned it with the edge of her plate.

'She is making a border you cannot process.'

The word border made him look at the phone again.

No new message.

At 09:18, Murad wrote:

Old card closed. New payout need new city.

Thomas deleted the chat thread from view, then restored it from archive thirty seconds later.

At 10:03, Anya wrote:

Stopped.

Where?

Tea place.

Send picture outside.

No.

He placed the phone on the table and covered it with his hand.

At the office that afternoon, the senior management meeting filled the big conference room. The table held carafes of water, coffee flasks, bowls of wrapped mints. A screen at the front showed project risk in green, amber, red. Men and women in wool jackets opened laptops, attached chargers, adjusted chairs. Outside, the harbour cranes moved without drama.

Thomas sat near the middle with his notebook open.

The regional director spoke about schedule exposure in Hammerfest. Finance spoke about cost recovery. HR spoke about recruitment restrictions. Someone mentioned consultancy for the Russian packages, and the room accepted it without looking at him.

His phone lay under the table on his knee.

No message.

The director asked him about the controls package.

Thomas looked at the slide.

'The sequence is acceptable if vendor documents arrive by week seven.'

'And if they don't?'

'Then we hold shipment.'

'Can we absorb that?'

'Better than accepting blind.'

Heads nodded. The answer fitted. The man at the table still worked.

The phone vibrated.

He looked down.

Olesya:

We go now.

He typed under the table:

Please send location.

The typing mark appeared.

Vanished.

Appeared again.

No.

He stood.

The room stopped moving.

'Excuse me,' he said.

No one asked why.

In the corridor outside, the carpet swallowed his steps. The office lights were bright and even. Through the glass wall, the meeting resumed without him, bodies leaning towards the screen, hands moving over keyboards, risks being named, assigned, reduced.

He called Olesya.

The app rang twice.

Declined.

He wrote:

I need to know where you are.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He wrote:

Olesya.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He called Anya.

No answer.

He emailed.

No reply.

He stood in the corridor with the phone in both hands. At the far end, a cleaner moved a trolley beside the glass doors. The wheels barely sounded. Every surface shone under the white office light.

After twenty minutes, one message arrived from Olesya.

Stop.

Then the app went still.

He went to his office and closed the door.

The harbour was already dark. Reflections of the office lights floated over the window, laying desks and ceiling panels across the water. He sat at the desk and opened the secure app again.

He wrote:

Tell me you arrived.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He waited.

The port worked on without him. A crane lowered a container onto a waiting lorry. The lorry's hazard lights blinked in steady amber, on and off, on and off, counting something down.

Chapter 23

The hazard lights counted down into winter.

On.

Off.

On.

Off.

Thomas sat in the Stavanger office until the port emptied of ordinary movement. The crane lights remained. The lorry left. Rain struck the window in small hard points, each one catching the office reflection before sliding into the dark. On his screen the secure app remained open.

Tell me you arrived.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

At 22:17 he wrote:

Olesya.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

At 23:03:

Please confirm Anya has medicine.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He did not send another. Three messages sat there like knocks on a door after the house had gone empty.

At home, Sarah had left a plate for him in the fridge. Fish, potatoes, cucumbers with dill. The plate was covered with cling film and labelled with a small square of masking tape.

Yours.

He took it out, stood with it in his hand, then put it back without lifting the film.

The house looked out over black water. Across the fjord, lights marked the opposite shore in a broken line. The windows held his reflection inside the room: coat still on, phone in one hand, the fridge light making the kitchen bright behind him. He looked like a man who had arrived somewhere and forgotten what for.

Sarah came downstairs just after midnight.

She had put on a jumper over her nightclothes. Her hair was loose. She stopped at the last stair and looked at him through the open kitchen.

'No message?'

'No.'

She came down the remaining steps.

'Did you eat?'

'Not yet.'

'That means no.'

He closed the fridge.

'They may be on the road.'

'They may be.'

'There may be no signal.'

'There may not be.'

She did not argue. That was worse.

Thomas opened the app again. Nothing had changed.

'Come to bed,' she said.

'Soon.'

Sarah looked at the phone, then at the black window.

'Soon has become a room you live in.'

She went back upstairs.

The first transfer after the move went out four days later.

Olesya had not asked. Anya had not sent an invoice, a photograph, or a list. They had both gone quiet. The transfer went out when the month turned and the old structure reached its date. Thomas woke before dawn, opened the payment folder, and prepared the amount by touch, the way a hand finds a switch in a familiar room.

Murad needed a recipient.

Thomas wrote:

Old card closed. Need alternative.

Murad replied:

No card no payout.

Cash pickup north?

Need town and name.

Have name.

Need town.

Thomas stared at the line.

Need town.

He wrote to Anya:

I can send support if you tell me which town.

No answer.

He wrote to Olesya:

I am not asking for address. Only town for transfer.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He waited until noon. Then he sent the money to Murad anyway, instructing him to hold it.

Murad replied:

Holding costs after three days.

Fine.

Fine, Thomas wrote, and paid the cost.

On the third day, Murad messaged:

Woman contacted from old number. She gave pickup location. Paid.

Which location?

Cannot share.

I am sender.

She said no.

Send proof.

Murad sent a photograph of a payout slip with most of the branch information covered by a thumb. Olesya's initials were visible. Amount. Date. Signature line, scrawled too quickly to read.

Thomas enlarged the signature until the pixels tore.

He wrote to Olesya:

Received?

No blue ticks.

He wrote to Anya:

Received?

No reply.

The money moved.

The line did not.

The second month was easier.

That made him close the laptop and sit back from it.

He no longer expected a message before sending. He opened the exchange, bought the amount in smaller parts, moved it to Murad, paid the holding fee, waited for proof. The proof came late, photographed from a counter somewhere with the branch name covered. A woman's sleeve appeared at the edge of the frame, dark wool, not enough to identify anyone.

He saved it.

Month 2.

The file name looked obscene.

The third month arrived with snow against the study window and Sarah packing for Oslo. She had meetings with a shipping insurer and a woman she knew from Delhi who had reappeared in Norway with a different surname and the same bracelets. Sarah folded a navy dress into a small suitcase, then unfolded it and hung it back in the cupboard.

'You should come,' she said.

'To Oslo?'

'Two nights. A hotel. People who do not know us.'

'I have Hammerfest calls.'

'You have calls everywhere.'

He looked at the phone.

'Not this week.'

Sarah sat on the edge of the bed.

'When, then?'

He did not answer.

She zipped the suitcase.

'I am not asking for a holiday now. I am asking whether there is still a version of you that can leave the house without waiting for a message.'

The phone lay face down on the bedside table. No sound came from it.

'There is no message,' he said.

'That is the message.'

She lifted the suitcase from the bed and set it on the floor.

After she left for Oslo, he sent the third payment. Murad confirmed pickup the following morning. No one wrote from the north.

Thomas added a tick to the spreadsheet.

The tick sat in the row, green and meaningless.

After that, the month learned a new rhythm.

He sent. Murad held. Someone, sometimes Olesya, sometimes Anya, sometimes a third person whose name Murad would not give, collected or redirected the money. Proof arrived with pieces covered: branch name, city, transaction code, sometimes the payer reference. Thomas paid fees for not knowing. Each payment reached a place he could not name.

In the spreadsheet, the confirmation column became useless.

He replaced Received with Proof.

Then with Paid.

Then with a tick.

The tick offended him less.

Stavanger moved into deeper winter. Mornings came late and with reluctance. The fjord changed colour by the hour, steel, slate, pewter, black. The house held heat and silence. Sarah bought heavier curtains for the bedroom. The room was warm. The glass still left her exposed at night.

'It's like sleeping in a display case,' she said, standing on a chair to clip the curtain ring.

'I can do that.'

'I know.'

She clipped the last ring herself.

They spoke more about the house than about anything else. Heating. Insurance. Car tyres. Whether to buy a proper dining table or continue with the company one. The conversation had become a way of walking across ice without looking down.

At dinner, Sarah asked:

'Have they answered?'

'No.'

'But the money is still going?'

'Yes.'

'How?'

'Broker.'

'Do you know where?'

'No.'

She set her fork down.

'Then you are funding a shape.'

He looked at his plate. Salmon, boiled potatoes, green beans. Food that made no demand.

'They need it.'

'Do you know that?'

'They collect it.'

'That is not the same thing.'

He had no answer that would stand.

Sarah did not press. She had stopped pressing. Pressing required a belief that something might open.

In February, Anya's email account replied.

Thank you.

No greeting. No explanation. No name beneath it.

Thomas answered within thirty seconds.

Are you both well?

The reply did not come.

He stared at the two words for so long that Sarah walked behind him and saw them.

'That is from Anya?'

'The account.'

'The account.'

'Yes.'

Sarah remained behind his chair.

'You don't know if she wrote it.'

'No.'

'You will treat it as hers.'

He closed the laptop.

'What would you have me do?'

Sarah moved away from the chair.

'I don't know anymore.'

Her honesty left no surface to strike. He preferred it when she had a position. Positions could be argued with, adjusted, worked around. This was only a person standing in the room with him, refusing to become an instrument.

The next morning he searched for private investigators in Azerbaijan.

He did it at the office before anyone arrived, sitting at his desk whilst the harbour lay under a colourless sky. The search results came in English, Russian, Azerbaijani. Matrimonial checks. Corporate due diligence. Debt tracing. Asset searches. Missing persons. Discreet enquiries. Photographs of men in suits, hands folded over desks, flags behind them, office plants with glossy leaves.

He closed three sites immediately.

One had a London number and a Baku partner. One listed corporate clients. One asked for a retainer before naming services. He called the London number.

A man answered on the second ring.

'Field enquiries in Azerbaijan?' Thomas asked.

'Depends on subject.'

'Two adults. Mother and daughter. No contact. Location unknown. Need welfare check without approaching.'

'Relationship?'

Thomas looked at the harbour.

'Financial sponsor.'

The words sounded wrong and exact.

'Legal matter?'

'No.'

'Police matter?'

'No.'

'Consent?'

'No.'

The man did not react.

'We can do passive location assessment if there is a lawful basis and no harassment. Open sources, local enquiries, address confirmation. No confrontation.'

'No contact.'

'All right.'

'No contact,' Thomas repeated.

'I heard you.'

The retainer was high. Thomas paid from the secondary account and sent what he had: old Baku addresses, Olesya's full name, Anya's full name, old card numbers, hospital name, partial debt papers, photographs of the old flat door, the bus window, the payout slips with covered branches, medicine packets, aunt reference, north.

North was not evidence.

He sent it anyway.

The intake form asked for purpose.

Thomas wrote:

Welfare confirmation.

Then it asked for limitations.

No direct contact.

No approach.

No disclosure of client.

No involvement with collectors.

The form asked for known risks. He wrote debt pressure, recent hospitalisation, possible relocation, and stopped before adding himself. The cursor waited at the end of the line. He moved to the next field.

Relationship to subject.

He sat with the form open for twenty minutes.

Financial sponsor would be read as charitable, perhaps transactional. Former employer contact would be too small and too false. Partner would create a claim Olesya had not accepted, and the word would not survive Sarah standing in the kitchen with the invoice in her hand. Family was a door in a different house.

He wrote:

Long-term personal contact.

The phrase looked like something written by a man avoiding a document.

He sent it.

The investigator replied:

Please confirm you do not require address release if subjects are located.

Thomas typed:

I require location confirmation only.

He did not press send.

He knew that if the exact address arrived, he would open it. He would map it, measure the distance to the bakery, to the nearest clinic, to the road south. He would turn it into a plan before the file had finished downloading.

He changed the line.

Do not release exact address unless urgent welfare issue observed.

Then he sent it.

The investigator's first update arrived after eight days.

Subject not at prior Baku address.

He already knew that.

The second update:

Former neighbour confirms departure after dispute with collectors. Direction north stated but no precise location given.

The third:

Possible connection through sister of subject's late mother. Village outside Qusar district. Checking.

Thomas read Qusar on the screen and opened a map.

North.

Mountains. Border roads. Villages along pale lines. Rivers thin as wires. The Caspian far behind, Baku reduced to a name at the edge of the map.

He did not write to Olesya.

The restraint cost him more than the retainer.

Sarah found the investigator invoice by accident.

It came through the printer when she was printing insurance documents. The printer tray held both sets of papers together. She brought the invoice to the kitchen island and placed it in front of him without folding it.

'What is this?'

He looked at the letterhead.

'Investigator.'

'For Olesya.'

'Passive. No contact.'

'You hired someone to find her.'

'To know she is alive.'

Sarah's face did not change. Her hand remained on the paper.

'Did she ask not to be found?'

'She asked me to stop.'

Sarah nodded once.

'And this is stopping?'

'I am not contacting her.'

'You are still entering the room.'

'There is no room.'

'Exactly.'

The invoice sat between them.

Outside, wind moved across the fjord and struck the glass with fine sleet. The house made one small sound as the weather pressed against it.

'If I do nothing,' he said, 'I have no way to know.'

'And if you know?'

He looked at the invoice.

Sarah waited.

'Then I know,' he said.

She lifted her hand from the paper.

'That may be all you get.'

For two days after that, he did not open the investigator portal.

He told himself this was restraint. The portal sent automated updates anyway, small grey notices that arrived in his inbox and sat above company mail.

Case active.

Field enquiry pending.

Local contact assigned.

He archived each one without opening it. Then he opened the archive and read the subject lines again.

At work, the Russian package consultancy began sending recovered files. Vendor names translated poorly. Equipment tags matched old Baku lists he had corrected years before. He saw Olesya's old file naming habits in some of them: dates reversed, Russian abbreviations left inside English folders, a space before a dash that no system would have inserted by itself.

He clicked through one archive after another.

For three seconds, in a folder from 2017, he found her initials in a metadata field.

OB.

Not her initials now, after all the naming and renaming. The name she had used in the office then. He copied the field into a note, then deleted it. The consultancy email waited for technical approval. He gave it.

Sarah began sleeping with the curtains fully drawn.

In the morning she opened them with a hard pull, letting the fjord back into the room all at once. Some days she dressed for the office she did not have, a blouse, trousers, earrings, then worked at the dining table on investment papers, travel documents, and committees that seemed to find her through every country. Other days she walked for hours and came back with wet hair, cheeks marked by wind, and no account of where she had gone.

'You should come with me,' she said one Saturday.

'Where?'

'Out.'

He looked at the laptop.

'I have something.'

'You always have something.'

She put on her coat.

'Does it ever finish?'

He nearly answered yes. The word was ready, trained by years of projects, milestones, handovers, punch lists, close-out reports.

He said nothing.

Sarah left.

After the door closed, he opened the investigator portal.

No final report yet.

Only a note:

Subject two observed in district centre. Employment likely informal.

He read it until the words lost their order.

The final report arrived three weeks later.

It came in a password-protected file at 06:38, whilst the house was still dark and Sarah slept. Thomas carried the laptop downstairs and opened it at the kitchen island. The password had been sent in a separate message.

The file contained twenty-two pages.

Executive summary.

Subject one located.

Subject two located.

Current residence: village outside Qusar district, exact address withheld pending client instruction due to sensitivity.

No direct contact made.

Observation period: five days.

Thomas scrolled slowly.

Photographs followed.

First, the house.

Cinderblock walls, one storey, metal roof patched in two colours. A wooden gate leaning inward. Snow gathered in dirty ridges along the path. A satellite dish fixed to the side wall with wire. Two windows, one with plastic taped across a cracked pane.

Second, Anya.

She stood outside a bakery entrance carrying a sack of flour against her hip. Her hair was tied back, face turned away from the camera. Flour dust marked the front of her dark coat and one cheek. She had grown thinner or the coat made her look so. Behind her, a man in a white apron held the door open with his foot.

Third, Olesya.

She stood at a washing line behind the house, lifting a sheet with both hands. The line sagged. Snow lay in patches near her shoes. Her hair was covered with a scarf. The cardigan hung loose on her shoulders. The photograph had been taken from a distance, through branches perhaps, the foreground blurred by something close to the lens.

Thomas stopped scrolling.

The kitchen lights reflected in the laptop screen. For a second Olesya's image lay under the reflection of his hands, pale on the black keys.

He zoomed in.

The image softened. Her face was turned downward, not visible. One hand held the sheet. The other reached for a peg. On the ground beside the basket lay a strip of cloth or bandage, white against wet earth.

He zoomed out.

The next photograph showed Anya at the bakery counter. A customer reached across with coins. Anya's hand was extended, palm up. The sleeve of her coat had flour at the cuff.

The report notes were brief.

Subject two appears employed informally at local bakery, early morning shifts.

Subject one observed mostly at residence, limited outside movement.

No visible male co-resident during observation period.

No collection activity observed during period.

Residence shared with older female thought to be relative.

Thomas read the lines twice.

No collection activity observed.

No visible male co-resident.

Older female thought to be relative.

The words gave him what he had asked for. They were alive. They had shelter. Anya had work. Olesya could stand at a washing line. There was no man at the door in the photographs, no hospital corridor, no visible collector.

The file should have released him.

It did not.

The photographs made a new enclosure. They proved distance. They proved ordinary damage. They proved that a life could continue outside his reach and still carry the marks of what had happened. They gave him a village without an address, a house without a door he could knock on, a daughter behind a counter, a woman under a scarf hanging sheets in snow.

Sarah came downstairs at seven.

She stopped when she saw the laptop.

'Is that it?'

'Yes.'

'Are they alive?'

'Yes.'

She stood on the other side of the island.

'May I see?'

He did not answer at once.

Then he turned the laptop.

Sarah looked through the photographs without touching the trackpad. Her eyes moved slowly, pausing at Anya with the flour, at Olesya with the sheet, at the house.

'She looks cold,' Sarah said.

It was the only thing she said.

Thomas turned the laptop back.

The report ended with recommendations.

Continue passive monitoring if required.

Obtain exact address with further field work if authorised.

Potential discreet courier route available through district centre.

He closed the file.

Then opened it again.

He went to the photograph of Olesya at the washing line and sat with it. Outside, Norway lightened by degrees. The fjord appeared, flat and colourless. A ferry moved on schedule. The house heating clicked somewhere beneath the floor.

Sarah made coffee.

The machine ground beans, louder than it needed to be. She placed a cup beside him and stood there, one hand on the island.

'What will you do with it?' she asked.

He looked at the report.

'Nothing.'

'Thomas.'

He closed the file.

'I said nothing.'

Sarah took her own cup to the window. Her reflection joined the morning glass, thin over the fjord.

He opened the secure app.

Olesya's last message remained:

Stop.

Below it, his own:

Tell me you arrived.

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He did not write.

He opened a blank message anyway.

I saw that you are working.

He deleted it.

I know where you are.

He deleted that faster.

I can still help if you let me.

The sentence stayed longer than the others. It looked modest on the screen. It was not. It carried a door, a route, a transaction, a man in a rented car, an office, a file, a search history, every concealed thing he had built around the word help until help had become another pressure.

He deleted it.

The app returned to Stop.

At the office, he opened the report twice more.

At the office, he opened the report twice more.

Once before the morning risk meeting.

Once after lunch, when the Russian package consultancy sent a sample translation and asked for approval.

Each time the photographs gave him the same facts.

House.

Bakery.

Washing line.

Snow.

No answer.

By evening, the file had become a place he entered.

He copied it into a folder named Q.

Then renamed the folder Archive.

Then moved it into Norway, under Debt, then moved it out again.

At home, Sarah had not made dinner. She had left bread, cheese, and a note.

Walking. Back later.

He stood at the island with the investigator file open and the note beside it.

The house was warm. The fjord was black. Somewhere in the walls, water moved through pipes with a low domestic rush.

He selected the file.

Delete.

The system asked:

Are you sure you want to move this item to the recycle bin?

He clicked yes.

The file disappeared.

For a minute, the folder was empty.

Then he opened the recycle bin and restored it.

He sat down.

The restored file appeared in the folder with its original name. The system did not ask why he had brought it back. It did not mark the hesitation. It kept no visible record of the minute in which he had attempted to remove what he had paid to know.

He opened the report again.

Potential discreet courier route available through district centre.

He had avoided that line before. It had sat at the bottom of the recommendations like a small hook. Courier route. District centre. A way to place something into their hands without requiring an account, a card, a branch name, a message read in blue. A way around the refusal that still looked enough like distance to let him call it respect.

He clicked the investigator's contact thread.

What does courier route mean?

The reply came the next morning.

Local delivery through trusted intermediary. Package only. No discussion. Subject may refuse.

Can money be delivered?

Cash not recommended. Medicine, documents, ticket, phone possible.

Ticket.

The word entered the room differently from money. It had direction built into it.

He wrote:

International ticket?

Possible if subject agrees. Needs identification details and route.

He did not answer.

For the rest of the day, he carried ticket through the house.

It sat with him while he emptied the dishwasher. It sat with him while Sarah spoke on the phone in the study, her voice low and efficient. It sat with him while snow slid from the roof in a soft collapse and broke apart on the deck.

At dinner, Sarah asked:

'Did the report give you what you wanted?'

He looked at her across the table. She had made lentils and rice, something warm and plain, and had opened a bottle of red wine she did not finish.

'It gave me where they are.'

'Not the address.'

'Enough.'

'Enough for what?'

He did not answer.

Sarah put down her glass.

'Thomas.'

'I don't know yet.'

'That is not true.'

He looked at the window. Their reflections sat in it, divided by the pendant light above the table.

'There may be a way to get her to Istanbul.'

Sarah did not move.

'Her and Anya?'

'Olesya first.'

The words came out and showed their damage.

Sarah stood and took her plate to the sink. She rinsed it carefully, though there was food still on it.

'You are doing it again.'

'No.'

'You are separating the hard part and calling it sequence.'

'If I can get Olesya out, Anya can follow.'

'Later.'

He closed his eyes.

'Do not use my words against me.'

'I am using your pattern.'

Water ran over the plate. Sarah turned it off.

'Has she asked for Istanbul?'

'No.'

'Has she answered you?'

'No.'

'Then who is this for?'

He stood.

'For the chance.'

Sarah dried her hands on a towel.

'A chance requires another person reaching for it.'

The house settled around the sentence.

The final intervention did not arrive as a thought. It arrived as an action.

He opened a flight search.

Stavanger to Istanbul.

Then he opened a second tab.

Baku to Istanbul.

He did not enter dates yet.

Outside, the deck light came on by motion sensor, though nothing visible had moved.

Chapter 24

The deck light went off after thirty seconds.

The dark returned to the glass.

Thomas sat with the flight searches open and did not enter dates. Stavanger to Istanbul waited in one tab. Baku to Istanbul waited in another. The cursor blinked inside the departure field, clean and patient, asking for the one thing he had spent years supplying: sequence.

Sarah's words remained in the kitchen.

A chance requires another person reaching for it.

He closed the Baku tab.

Opened it again.

Not Baku. Tbilisi.

He typed Tbilisi to Istanbul and watched the options populate. Morning departures, afternoon departures, one evening flight with a connection too long to be useful. He opened a third tab and searched bus routes from the northern district to the Georgian border. The names appeared on the screen in a clean column: Qusar, Quba, Shamakhi, Balakan, Lagodekhi. Roads he did not know. Towns that held no memory of him. Borders that did not care what he had meant.

The investigator replied before midnight.

Crossing via Georgia theoretically possible. Subject would need to travel south-west, then onward. Not recommended without willingness.

Thomas wrote:

If subject agrees, can courier deliver documents and funds?

Possible.

Can local contact assist with border payment?

Need clarity.

Secondary facilitation fee.

Delay.

Then:

Possible. Risk high. Subject may still refuse at any point.

Thomas looked at the last line.

Subject may still refuse at any point.

He copied it into the Istanbul folder and left it there.

The next morning, Sarah found him in the study.

He had not slept. The curtains were open, and a thin winter light had reached the desk without warming it. On the screen were maps, flight options, hotel pages, visa notes, courier instructions, exchange rates, border names, a spreadsheet titled Istanbul route.

Sarah stood in the doorway with a mug in both hands.

'You are doing it.'

He did not turn.

'I am costing it.'

'No.'

He looked at her then.

'I need to know what is possible.'

'You know.'

She came into the room and placed the mug on the desk, away from the laptop.

'Have you written to her?'

'Not yet.'

'Then write before you build the world around her answer.'

He rubbed both hands over his face. His jaw rasped under his palms.

'If I write before the route exists, she will say no.'

'If the route is the reason she says yes, what is she saying yes to?'

He looked back at the screen. Istanbul hotels offered warm rooms, airport transfers, views over water, breakfast included. The photographs were full of white linen and brass lamps. He closed one.

'A way out.'

'Out of what?'

'The village. The debt. The men.'

'Towards what?'

He did not answer.

Sarah lifted the mug again, held it briefly, then set it back down.

'I am going to the office space in town,' she said.

'I thought you had no meetings.'

'I don't.'

She left.

When the house door closed, the study seemed to lose scale. The map on his screen filled the room. Roads, borders, airports, transfer points. Each line made the next line possible. Each point required the next instruction.

He opened the secure app.

Olesya's last message remained:

Stop.

He typed:

I know you asked me to stop. I will ask once. There is a route to Istanbul. No documents sent to company. No visa. No Norway. You choose after arrival. I can arrange ticket from Tbilisi, hotel, doctor, money, and a route for Anya after.

He read it.

Then deleted Anya after.

He typed:

Anya can follow if you decide.

He deleted that too.

In the end, he sent:

There is a route to Istanbul. No company. No visa. No forms. If you want it, I can arrange everything. You can say no.

The message delivered.

No blue ticks.

For three days, nothing moved.

He went to the office. He returned to the house. He answered calls. He signed off the Russian package consultancy. He rejected a cost forecast with wrong assumptions. He ate what Sarah placed on the table and sometimes what she did not. At night he left the app open beside the bed until Sarah turned over and said nothing.

The message remained delivered.

On the second night he opened the Istanbul folder and removed half of what he had put inside it.

Debt lawyer.

Norway route.

Document recovery.

Salary threshold.

He deleted the files, then restored two of them, then moved all four into a subfolder called Later. Later made them worse. He renamed it Holding. That was worse too. In the end he left the folder untitled, a blank icon beneath Istanbul, and closed the window.

Sarah came into the study with a basket of folded washing.

'There are three shirts here that need ironing,' she said.

'Leave them.'

'For Turkey?'

He looked at her.

She set the basket on the chair.

'I am not asking as a trap.'

'I know.'

'Do you?'

He turned the phone face down beside the laptop.

'The message has not been read.'

'And you are already packing.'

'I am not packing.'

Sarah lifted one shirt from the basket and shook it open. The fabric snapped once in the quiet room.

'This is what packing looks like before it admits itself.'

She laid the shirt over the back of the chair and left the basket there.

He did not iron the shirts.

On the third morning the investigator sent an unsolicited update.

Courier route remains possible for limited package. Subject still in district. No contact attempted.

Thomas wrote:

Do not approach unless I confirm.

Confirmed.

He stared at confirmed. The investigator used the word as a lock. Thomas used it as a bridge. Olesya had once used Received until even that had disappeared.

At lunch he stood in the office kitchen with a sandwich in its paper wrapper and watched snow collect on the outer ledge. The wrapper grew damp at one corner from his hand. He threw it away without opening it.

On the fourth morning, the ticks turned blue.

He was in a meeting with Hammerfest, camera on, whilst a man in a wool jumper argued about schedule float. The blue marks appeared at the bottom of the app window on his second screen. Thomas stopped writing mid-word.

'Thomas?' someone said.

He looked at the meeting screen.

'Repeat that.'

The man repeated nothing important.

Olesya's typing mark appeared.

Vanished.

Appeared again.

The meeting continued around him, a room of faces speaking through headsets.

Her answer arrived in two lines.

Why Istanbul?

Why not here?

He did not answer until the meeting ended. He closed the door, lowered the blinds, and placed both hands flat on the desk.

Istanbul is possible, he wrote.

Here is also possible.

You are not out of reach there.

No place is out.

I can meet you in Istanbul and arrange next step.

What next?

Doctor. Rest. Debt lawyer. Documents if you choose.

Choose what?

He leaned back.

The office light hummed over him. Outside the glass wall, people passed with coffee cups, access cards, winter coats, lives arranged by badge and calendar.

He wrote:

What you want.

Her reply came after eight minutes.

I wanted home.

The line did not move.

He read it once, then once more, then locked the phone.

At home that evening, Sarah found him printing the itinerary.

Not a final itinerary. A draft, he told himself. A set of options. Stavanger to Istanbul. Tbilisi to Istanbul. A hotel near the airport, then another in KarakÃ¶y with better medical access. Contact details for a cardiologist recommended by an expatriate forum. Courier instructions. Cash amounts. Emergency numbers. Clinic addresses. The pages came out warm from the printer and curled slightly in the tray.

Sarah picked up the first page.

'She answered.'

'Yes.'

'And?'

'She asked why Istanbul.'

'That is not yes.'

'It is not no.'

Sarah placed the page down.

'You hear absence as permission.'

'I hear a door not closed.'

'No. You hear hinges.'

He gathered the pages and tapped them into order. Sarah watched the gesture.

'Does Anya come?'

'Not first.'

'Then you know what she will ask.'

'I know.'

'And your answer?'

'Later.'

Sarah turned away.

'You should be done with that word by now.'

He put the itinerary into a folder.

The next morning Olesya wrote:

Anya will not leave.

He answered too quickly.

You can come first. Then we bring her.

No.

If you stay there, nothing changes.

Something changed. We are away.

You are in a village, working through Anya, hiding from men.

We are not in street.

You need medical follow-up.

There is doctor.

Where?

No.

He stopped.

The word still had its old power. No. Small, exact, no explanation trailing behind it.

He wrote:

I will not ask where.

Good.

Will you consider Istanbul?

No answer for the rest of the day.

At 23:18, she wrote:

If I come, what am I there?

He stared at the question.

The house was silent. Sarah had gone upstairs an hour earlier. On the table sat the Istanbul folder, a half-empty glass of water, and the investigator's invoice. The fjord outside had no visible edge.

He typed:

Free.

He deleted it.

Out of reach.

He deleted it.

With me.

He deleted that too.

He wrote:

You decide after you arrive.

Her answer came within seconds.

Always after.

He stood from the table and walked to the window.

Always after.

After Baku. After transfers. After debt. After hospital. After Norway. After Olesya first, Anya later. Every route he had built required her to enter the first half and trust him for the second.

He returned to the phone.

I can book Anya too.

She will not.

Then I will keep route open for her.

No answer.

The next afternoon, Olesya wrote:

I will try crossing.

Thomas read it standing in the office kitchen with a paper cup under the coffee machine. The cup overflowed whilst he looked at the screen. Coffee ran over the counter and dripped onto the floor.

A woman from finance reached for napkins.

'You all right?'

'Yes.'

He took the napkins from her and cleaned the counter.

I will try crossing.

Not I will come.

Try.

He wrote:

I will arrange everything. Do not travel if chest pain. Keep medicine with you. Courier will bring documents and cash. Tbilisi flight will be flexible.

Her answer:

No many messages.

He stopped typing.

I understand.

He deleted the line.

Yes, he sent.

The next forty-eight hours became itinerary.

Courier through district centre. Cash in two envelopes. A phone SIM with Georgian roaming. Printed flight confirmation Tbilisi to Istanbul, flexible fare. Hotel booking under his name and hers separately, one suite, one adjoining room he added and removed and added again. A clinic appointment that could be cancelled. A driver in Tbilisi. A driver in Istanbul. Emergency funds. A contact at the crossing arranged through the investigator, described only as secondary facilitation.

Each item produced a document.

Each document produced a folder.

The courier pack went first.

The investigator refused cash over the agreed limit, then accepted a smaller amount, then accepted the larger amount after Thomas split it between travel and medical. The distinction was false, but the form required a label. Thomas printed the ticket confirmation twice. One copy for Olesya. One copy sealed in a second envelope marked duplicate. He added a small card with the Istanbul hotel address and his phone number written by hand.

He did not write anything personal on the card.

Then he wrote one line on the back.

I will be there.

He looked at it for a long time.

The line made a claim larger than the route. He tore the card in half and wrote a new one.

Hotel:

Flight:

Driver:

Emergency:

Facts only.

He placed the SIM card in a small plastic sleeve, then placed the sleeve in the envelope with the ticket. The phone itself became an argument with the investigator.

Too visible.

Too expensive.

Too much to carry.

Thomas ordered it anyway through the courier contact: a simple handset, charged, sealed, number written on the outside. The invoice came through with a photograph of the device on a wooden table. Black phone. White charger. A hand at the edge of the frame holding the receipt down.

For an hour he watched the package move by messages.

Collected.

In transit.

Delivered to intermediary.

Subject notified.

Then nothing.

He waited at the dining table whilst Sarah read across from him. She had opened a novel but had not turned the page in twenty minutes.

'Has it reached her?'

'Intermediary.'

'That is not her.'

'No.'

The next message arrived after midnight.

Package accepted.

He put the phone down carefully.

Sarah closed her book.

'Accepted is not coming.'

'I know.'

'Say it like you know what it means.'

He looked at her.

'Accepted is not coming.'

The words did not help. They only proved he could repeat them.

Sarah watched him build it.

'You are not even hiding it now,' she said.

'No.'

'That is not honesty.'

'What is it?'

'The end of stamina.'

He looked up.

She stood at the kitchen island with the Istanbul folder open in front of her. Her finger rested on the hotel confirmation.

'A suite?'

'And another room.'

'For Anya?'

'If she changes her mind.'

'You booked a room for a girl who has refused to come.'

'I kept an option.'

Sarah closed the folder.

'Options are how you keep people inside the plan after they leave it.'

He took the folder from her.

'I am going to Istanbul.'

'I know.'

'I have to be there if she comes.'

'And if she doesn't?'

He did not answer.

Sarah nodded. It was a small movement, nearly formal.

'What will you tell me?'

'Supply chain meeting.'

'Don't.'

He looked at her.

'Please,' she said. 'Do not make me listen to the sentence.'

So he did not.

He packed two shirts, one jacket, the Istanbul folder, a document wallet, and cash divided between envelopes and a flat money belt he had last used in Iraq. Sarah watched from the bedroom doorway. She did not ask flight time. She did not ask hotel. She had seen enough paper.

At the airport, Stavanger was all pale wood and glass, people queuing without noise, children in padded coats, a man removing his belt before security with practised calm. Thomas moved through it carrying only hand luggage. His phone stayed in his palm.

Olesya sent one message before boarding.

Courier came.

All correct?

Yes.

Medicine?

Yes.

He stopped himself from writing more.

In Istanbul, the air met him warm and damp at the aircraft door.

The airport was too bright, too large, full of polished stone and perfume, voices carrying under the high ceiling, screens repeating destinations in rows of green and white. He passed through passport control, collected no baggage, and found the driver holding a sign with his name.

The city moved outside the car in wet evening light. Flyovers, mosques in the distance, blocks of flats, billboards, traffic splitting and rejoining with a discipline that looked like disorder only from far away. The driver kept one hand on the wheel and one on the phone mounted near the vents. Turkish radio murmured beneath the engine.

Thomas checked Olesya's flight.

Tbilisi to Istanbul. Booked.

Not checked in.

He checked again at the hotel.

Not checked in.

The suite overlooked water, though not the view in the photographs. The windows were wide and sealed. A sofa in cream fabric faced a low table. The bed was too large. In the bathroom, towels were folded with a band of paper around them. A small bowl of fruit sat on the desk beside a card welcoming him by name.

He moved the fruit bowl to the floor.

Then he laid out the envelopes.

Medical.

Food.

Travel.

Anya.

He had written the labels in block capitals on the flight, then regretted them. Now he turned the envelopes over so the writing faced down.

He placed the second room key beside them.

The second room was two doors down.

He went there after midnight, carrying the key card and nothing else. The corridor smelled of carpet cleaner and warm air from the lift shaft. A room-service tray stood outside another door, two glasses empty, a napkin fallen partly over the plate. He opened the second room and switched on the light.

Smaller. Two single beds. A desk. A chair. No view worth selling. The room had been made for transit, for people arriving late and leaving before the city could attach itself to them.

He stood between the beds.

For Anya, he had told himself.

Anya had refused. Olesya had refused for her. Or perhaps Anya had refused for herself. The distinction had been turned away at the door.

He placed the spare key on the desk and left the room open while the corridor air entered. Then he turned off the light and closed the door.

Back in the suite, he opened the minibar and removed everything: little bottles, chocolate, nuts, a tin of Turkish delight, two glass bottles of tonic. He placed them all inside the wardrobe. The fridge light exposed the empty shelves.

He walked to a shop near the hotel and bought water, plain bread, yoghurt, apples, crackers, tea bags, a packet of sanitary pads he picked up too quickly and then held properly, forcing his hand not to hide it in the basket. The cashier did not look at him. Outside, a man hosed the pavement though rain had already washed it clean.

In the room, he put the food in the fridge and the pads in the bathroom drawer. Then he opened the drawer again and moved them to the counter where they could be seen.

Every act arranged a woman who had not arrived.

At 21:40, the investigator wrote:

Subject departed village. Travelling towards crossing.

Thomas sat on the sofa with his shoes still on.

At 23:05:

Subject in vehicle. Local contact confirms movement.

At 01:12:

No update.

He did not sleep. He lay on top of the bedspread and watched the ceiling change with passing headlights from the road below. The room smelled of hotel linen, citrus cleaner, and the faint damp of a city near water. The air-conditioning clicked on and off with a polite mechanical breath.

At 02:30, he called the front desk and asked if the kitchen could prepare soup in the morning.

'For breakfast, sir?'

'For later.'

'We have room service.'

'Yes.'

Yes.

Is she?

Not yet.

Then:

Do not confuse waiting with being owed.

Subject near crossing area.

Olesya did not write.

At 08:03, the investigator wrote:

Secondary fee paid.

Thomas read the words three times.

Paid.

The edge had been reached. Not imagined, not mapped, not arranged in a folder. Olesya was at the crossing point with cash he had sent, medicine in a bag, a flight waiting in Tbilisi, a hotel room in Istanbul, a second key on the desk.

He opened the flight check-in page.

Passenger not checked in.

At 09:20:

Delay at crossing.

At 10:11:

Subject still at crossing.

At 10:38:

Local contact reports subject stepped away from queue.

Thomas stood by the window. The city below had entered morning. Ferries moved over the water. A man on the pavement arranged simit rings on a cart, each one placed by hand into a neat circle. A delivery van reversed towards a loading bay and a hotel porter lifted one palm to guide it.

At 11:07:

Subject not answering local contact.

Thomas called Olesya.

The app rang once.

No answer.

He wrote:

Are you at crossing?

Sent.

No blue ticks.

He called the investigator.

'Tell me exactly,' Thomas said.

The man on the line had background noise behind him, traffic or a cafÃ©, voices he could not place.

'No.'

'No.'

The word did not echo. It removed the next room.

Thomas ended the call.

At 12:02, the Tbilisi flight closed check-in.

At 12:40, it departed.

Thomas watched the status change from boarding to departed. The screen did it without drama. A line of text moving to its next correct state.

Olesya wrote at 13:16.

I went back.

He sat down on the edge of the bed.

The room had been prepared for arrival.

That was what altered it. Her absence stood among the evidence of his preparation. The water in the fridge. The spare key. The second room two doors away. The cardigan he had not bought and should not have imagined. The clinic number written on hotel paper. The envelopes face down on the desk, each one a small, labelled answer to a question she had not asked.

He stood and gathered the envelopes into one pile.

Then he spread them out again.

Medical.

Food.

Travel.

Anya.

The labels were visible now. There was no use turning them over. He read them as a clerk might read a file, as Sarah might read them if she entered the room, as Olesya would have read them had she crossed the glass and metal and passport booths and arrived at the door.

Not wife.

Not home.

He opened the second room on the hotel app and cancelled it. A warning appeared: cancellation fee applies.

He accepted.

The app thanked him for updating his booking.

He put the phone down and looked at the door between the suite and the corridor. No one knocked. No luggage wheels stopped outside. No woman stood there with medicine in her bag and the crossing behind her.

Why?

The message delivered.

Blue ticks.

No reply.

He wrote:

Are you with Anya?

No reply.

He wrote:

Are you all right?

He deleted all right.

Are you out of reach?

No reply.

Why?

The typing mark appeared.

Vanished.

Appeared again.

You made room in Istanbul.

Then:

Not home.

He looked at the envelopes on the desk. Medical. Food. Travel. Anya. All face down, and still their labels seemed to rise through the paper.

It could become home.

No.

You did not even come to see.

I saw.

What did you see?

No answer.

He stood and went to the bathroom. The mirror showed him in a shirt creased from the flight, hair flattened on one side, eyes cut by the white hotel light. Behind him, through the open door, the envelopes lay on the desk in a row.

You make door. Always door. I stand outside with Anya.

Olesya.
